96. A 57-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of shortness of breath and swelling of his legs. He says his shortness of breath occurs most frequently when he lies down. During the past month, he has had the sensation of abdominal fullness and pulling and has noticed a progressive increase in his belt size. Five years ago, he received the diagnosis of bleeding esophageal varices and alcoholic cirrhosis. He underwent sclerosis of the varices and has abstained from alcohol since then. His temperature is 36.9°C (98.4°F). The abdomen is moderately tense and nontender with shifting dullness. There is 3+ pitting edema of the lower extremities. Paracentesis is performed. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Protein, total           2.8 g/dL</br>Albumin                  1.8 g/dL</br>Peritoneal fluid</br>Leukocyte count          900/mm^3</br>Segmented neutrophils    67%</br>Lymphocytes              28%</br>Protein, total           1.8 g/dL</br>Albumin                  1 g/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Echocardiography</br>b. Ultrasonography of the hepatic veins</br>c. Antibiotic therapy</br>d. Laparoscopy</br>e. Liver biopsy</br>f. CT angiography of the liver</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a.  Echocardiography  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key positive clues<br /></br>  – 1-week history of progressive shortness of breath, orthopnea (“occurs most frequently when he lies down”), and bilateral 3+ pitting edema → classic for new-onset congestive heart failure (CHF).<br /></br>  – Rapid increase in abdominal girth/ “belt size” → acute ascites rather than the chronic, slowly progressive fluid accumulation typical of long-standing cirrhosis.<br /></br>  – Past medical history of heavy alcohol use places him at risk not only for cirrhosis but also for alcoholic (dilated) cardiomyopathy.<br /></br>• Ascitic-fluid profile<br /></br>  – Serum albumin 1.8 g/dL, ascitic albumin 1.0 g/dL → SAAG ≈ 0.8 (&lt; 1.1). A low SAAG essentially rules OUT portal-hypertensive/cirrhotic ascites and should prompt an expanded differential.<br /></br>  – Total ascitic protein 1.8 g/dL (neither the very low values seen with cirrhosis nor the &gt; 2.5 g/dL classically seen in long-standing right-sided congestion) → supports that we are dealing with a <em>non-portal-hypertensive</em>, systemic process that has developed relatively quickly.<br /></br>  – Mild leukocytosis (900 cells/mm³) with a PMN fraction of 67 % is common in “cardiac ascites” or any rapidly accumulating transudate and, by itself, is not diagnostic of spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (culture and clinical picture are needed).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Orthopnea + peripheral edema in a 57-year-old → think CHF.<br /></br>2. New, tense ascites in a cirrhotic patient could still be portal hypertension, but the <strong>low SAAG</strong> argues strongly against that mechanism.<br /></br>3. An alternative that explains <em>all</em> findings (orthopnea, leg edema, ascites with low SAAG) is acute or subacute decompensated <em>right-sided</em> heart failure due to alcoholic (dilated) cardiomyopathy.<br /></br>4. The single best next test to confirm/exclude this hypothesis and guide management is a transthoracic <strong>echocardiogram</strong> (non-invasive, rapidly available, delineates EF, chamber size, and valvular function).  </p></br><p>Take-home message:<br /></br>In any patient with ascites, always calculate the SAAG. A SAAG &lt; 1.1 g/dL shifts the focus away from portal-hypertensive causes and makes you look for malignancy, infection, or systemic venous congestion (pericardial/heart disease). When orthopnea and peripheral edema coexist, rule out cardiac pathology first with an echocardiogram.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Alcoholic dilated cardiomyopathy → toxic injury to myocytes → eccentric ventricular dilation → ↓systolic function → ↑right- and left-sided pressures → venous congestion → peripheral edema and transudation of fluid into the peritoneal cavity.<br /></br>• Low albumin (malnutrition, chronic liver disease) can reduce the measured SAAG even when hydrostatic pressure is high, explaining the “borderline” gradient in this patient.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First step: transthoracic echocardiography to confirm systolic dysfunction, measure ejection fraction, and evaluate right-sided pressures.<br /></br>Subsequent management (once diagnosis confirmed):<br /></br>  • Intravenous diuretics (furosemide ± thiazide)<br /></br>  • Sodium and fluid restriction<br /></br>  • Guideline-directed medical therapy for HFrEF (ACE-I/ARB/ARNI, β-blocker, mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist)<br /></br>  • Possible consideration of alcohol abstinence counseling, implantable defibrillator, or advanced therapies depending on EF.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“New fluid + New orthopnea = New echo.”<br /></br>A low SAAG means “Not a Portal problem” (No Portal → think Pericardium/heart, Peritoneal carcinomatosis, Pancreas, Protein-loss nephrotic, Peritoneal TB).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Ultrasonography of the hepatic veins – Appropriate when suspecting Budd-Chiari (hepatic venous outflow obstruction) but that would present with a high SAAG (&gt; 1.1) and high ascitic protein (&gt; 2.5 g/dL), plus painful hepatomegaly.<br /></br>c. Antibiotic therapy – Would be correct for spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP), which requires an ascitic PMN count ≥ 250 cells/mm³ <em>and</em> a clinical picture of infection (fever, abdominal tenderness). This patient is afebrile, has a mildly elevated PMN count that is common in cardiac ascites, and lacks peritoneal signs.<br /></br>d. Laparoscopy – Used to diagnose malignant or tuberculous peritonitis when ascitic cytology or cultures are inconclusive; neither is strongly suggested here.<br /></br>e. Liver biopsy – Indicated for unexplained liver disease or malignancy; low utility in evaluating newly decompensated ascites with cardiopulmonary symptoms.<br /></br>f. CT angiography of the liver – Work-up for vascular abnormalities (e.g., portal vein thrombosis, hepatic artery aneurysm); clinical scenario and fluid profile point elsewhere.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: Orthopnea + bilateral edema + new tense ascites with a <em>low</em> SAAG should immediately raise concern for right-sided heart failure; echocardiography is the fastest, safest way to confirm the diagnosis and direct therapy.</p>
97. An asymptomatic 62-year-old man comes for a routine health maintenance examination. Physical examination shows splenomegaly. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 42%</br>Leukocyte count 46,000/mm3</br>Segmented neutrophils 35%</br>Bands 15%</br>Basophils 1%</br>Lymphocytes 16%</br>Monocytes 5%</br>Myeloblasts 1%</br>Promyelocytes 2%</br>Metamyelocytes 13%</br>Myelocytes 12%</br>Platelet count 650,000/mm3</br>Leukocyte alkaline phosphatase activity is decreased. Examination of bone marrow shows many immature white blood cell precursors. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Agnogenic myeloid metaplasia</br>b. Chronic myelogenous leukemia</br>c. Chronic myelomonocytic leukemia</br>d. Erythroleukemia</br>e. Essential thrombocytosis</br>f. Myelodysplasia</br>g. Polycythemia vera</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 62 y, insidious presentation: classic age range for CML (median ≈55 y).<br /></br>• Splenomegaly: due to extramedullary hematopoiesis from massive myeloid proliferation—common in CML.<br /></br>• Marked leukocytosis (46 000/µL) with a “left-shifted” differential: myeloblasts, promyelocytes, myelocytes, and metamyelocytes all present. This myeloid cell “spectrum” is a buzz-phrase for CML.<br /></br>• Thrombocytosis (650 000/µL): platelets are often ↑ in CML because the malignant clone involves the megakaryocytic as well as granulocytic lineages.<br /></br>• Leukocyte alkaline phosphatase (LAP) ↓: the single most testable laboratory discriminator between CML (low LAP in neoplastic neutrophils) and a benign leukemoid reaction (high LAP).<br /></br>• Bone-marrow packed with immature myeloid precursors: further confirms a chronic myeloproliferative neoplasm.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: No erythrocytosis (rules out polycythemia vera), no significant monocytosis, basophilia only mild, no tear-drop cells or cytopenias suggesting marrow fibrosis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify a chronic myeloproliferative picture: ↑ WBC + thrombocytosis + splenomegaly.<br /></br>2. Determine lineage predominance: mostly granulocytic with full spectrum of maturation.<br /></br>3. Check LAP score: low → malignant clone (CML) not reactive process.<br /></br>4. Arrive at CML—caused by BCR-ABL (t(9;22) Philadelphia chromosome).</p></br><p>Take-home message: Low LAP + marked leukocytosis with left shift in a middle-aged adult equals CML until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: “Leukocytosis 46 K with predominance of immature granulocytes and decreased LAP—consistent with CML. Plan: confirm BCR-ABL by PCR/FISH, start tyrosine kinase inhibitor after oncology consult.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: BCR-ABL fusion tyrosine kinase → constitutive myeloid proliferation.<br /></br>• Risk factors: ionizing radiation exposure; most cases sporadic.<br /></br>• Phases: chronic (↑ cells but functional), accelerated, blast crisis (resembles AML/ALL).<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: fatigue, weight loss, night sweats, splenic fullness; often found incidentally.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Review<br /></br>Confirmatory test: BCR-ABL detection via PCR or FISH.<br /></br>First-line therapy: Imatinib (or other TKIs: dasatinib, nilotinib).<br /></br>Other options: allogeneic stem-cell transplant (curative) for TKI-failure or blast crisis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CML is LOW”:<br /></br>L – Left shift (all myeloid stages)<br /></br>O – Over‐sized spleen<br /></br>W – Weak LAP (low score)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Agnogenic myeloid metaplasia (primary myelofibrosis)<br /></br>• Would show pancytopenia or variable counts, dramatic splenomegaly, tear-drop RBCs, “dry tap” due to marrow fibrosis; LAP not characteristically low. Not the picture given.  </p></br><p>c. Chronic myelomonocytic leukemia<br /></br>• Persistent monocytosis (&gt;1000/µL) is required. Here monocyes only 5% (≈2300/µL of 46 K) but differential dominated by neutrophilic precursors, not monocytes.  </p></br><p>d. Erythroleukemia (acute erythroid leukemia, AML-M6)<br /></br>• Presents with anemia, erythroblasts in peripheral blood, often pancytopenia—not leukocytosis with thrombocytosis.  </p></br><p>e. Essential thrombocytosis<br /></br>• Isolated marked thrombocytosis (&gt;450 K) with minimal leukocytosis and no left shift. WBC usually normal or only mildly ↑. LAP normal/high.  </p></br><p>f. Myelodysplasia (MDS)<br /></br>• Typically shows cytopenias (especially macrocytic anemia), dysplastic cells, normal or low WBC. Splenomegaly uncommon.  </p></br><p>g. Polycythemia vera<br /></br>• Hallmark is ↑ hematocrit/hemoglobin; patient’s Hct is normal (42 %). Also LAP is usually normal/high.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Low LAP + massive granulocytic proliferation with full maturation spectrum = Chronic myelogenous leukemia.</p>
98. A 5-year-old boy is brought to the office for a follow-up examination 2 days after he was discharged from the hospital following an episode of pyelonephritis. Medications are cefixime and acetaminophen. He is at the 50th percentile for height and 25th percentile for weight; BMI is at the 10th percentile. Temperature is 37.6°C (99.6°F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no other abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies from his hospital admission showed a leukocyte count within the reference range and urine positive for leukocyte esterase; urine culture grew Klebsiella pneumoniae susceptible to cephalosporins. Kidney ultrasonography showed mild hydronephrosis. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Continuous antibiotic prophylaxis</br>b. Kidney and bladder ultrasonography</br>c. Renal scintigraphy using dimercaptosuccinic acid (DMSA)</br>d. Repeat urine culture</br>e. Voiding cystourethrography</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Voiding cystourethrography (VCUG)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that narrow the differential:<br /></br>• “Episode of pyelonephritis” – a febrile, upper-tract UTI in a child automatically triggers an anatomic evaluation because vesicoureteral reflux (VUR) or obstruction is common.<br /></br>• “Kidney ultrasonography showed mild hydronephrosis” – an abnormal renal/bladder ultrasound (RBUS). According to AAP and AUA guidelines, any abnormal US after a febrile UTI is an indication for VCUG.<br /></br>• Age 5 yr, boy, afebrile now, on proper antibiotics – acute infection being treated; we are now in the work-up phase.<br /></br>Pertinent negative: no mention of prior UTIs; this is his first documented episode, so we have not yet documented VUR.</p></br><p>Pattern to remember:<br /></br>First febrile UTI → RBUS for everyone.<br /></br>If RBUS is abnormal OR infections are recurrent/atypical → VCUG.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Treat the infection (already done with cefixime).  </li></br><li>Evaluate for underlying structural abnormality.<br /></br>   • RBUS already done and was abnormal (hydronephrosis).  </li></br><li>Next diagnostic test: VCUG to look for reflux and/or posterior urethral valves.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Any abnormal ultrasound after a febrile UTI mandates a VCUG.</li></br></ol></br><p>Documentation: “Order and schedule voiding cystourethrography to evaluate for vesicoureteral reflux in the setting of hydronephrosis after febrile pyelonephritis.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Vesicoureteral reflux = retrograde flow of urine from bladder → ureter → renal pelvis.<br /></br>• Etiology: congenital incompetence of ureterovesical junction (primary) or increased bladder pressure (secondary).<br /></br>• Consequence: recurrent UTIs, renal scarring, hypertension, CKD.<br /></br>Hydronephrosis on US suggests either obstruction or high-grade reflux, hence the need for VCUG.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls  </p></br><p>Work-up algorithm after first febrile UTI (age 2-24 mo) – also applied to older children in most practice patterns:<br /></br>1. RBUS for everyone.<br /></br>2. VCUG if:<br /></br>   a) RBUS abnormal (hydronephrosis, scarring, duplication, etc.)<br /></br>   b) Recurrent febrile UTIs<br /></br>   c) Atypical organism (no improvement in 48 h, creatinine ↑, sepsis, etc.)  </p></br><p>Treatment of VUR once diagnosed:<br /></br>• Grades I–II: observation ± prophylactic antibiotics (TMP-SMX or nitrofurantoin) in high-risk patients.<br /></br>• Grades III–V or breakthrough UTIs: continuous antibiotic prophylaxis (CAP) and possible surgical re-implantation.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“FUR” mnemonic for post-UTI imaging:<br /></br>F – First febrile UTI → Ultrasound.<br /></br>U – Ultrasound abnormal → VCUG.<br /></br>R – Recurrent febrile UTIs → VCUG even if US normal.</p></br><p>Visualize a plumbing diagram: after the first flood (UTI) you look outside with an ultrasound; if you see the pipes bulging (hydronephrosis) you send a camera down the pipes (VCUG).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Continuous antibiotic prophylaxis – Appropriate only AFTER VUR is documented (especially grades III-V) or for recurrent UTIs. We have not yet established reflux or recurrence.<br /></br>b. Repeat kidney and bladder ultrasonography – Already done; repeating will not clarify reflux.<br /></br>c. Renal scintigraphy with DMSA – Used to detect cortical scarring or acute pyelonephritis focus, not first-line for reflux evaluation and wouldn’t change immediate management.<br /></br>d. Repeat urine culture – Useful if persistent symptoms or to confirm sterilization, but the child is asymptomatic and afebrile; culture would not address the structural question.<br /></br>e. Voiding cystourethrography – Correct: identifies VUR or obstructive lesions such as posterior urethral valves responsible for hydronephrosis and risk of recurrent pyelonephritis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>An abnormal renal/bladder ultrasound after a febrile UTI in children requires a voiding cystourethrogram to evaluate for vesicoureteral reflux—essential to prevent recurrent infections and renal scarring.</p>
99. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the emergency department immediately after she collapsed on her way to the bathroom at home. She has anorexia nervosa and has had an 18-kg (40-lb) weight loss during the past 16 months. Menarche was at the age of 11 years, but her last menstrual period was 14 months ago. She does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. On arrival, she appears cachectic. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 36 kg (80 lb); BMI is 13 kg/m^2. Her temperature is 36.4 °C (97.6 °F), pulse is 40/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 85/55 mm Hg. Physical examination shows fine hair over the skin. An ECG shows ST-segment depression and an increased U wave. This patient is most likely to have which of the following sets of serum electrolyte findings?  </br>Na+ (mEq/L)  K+ (mEq/L)  Cl- (mEq/L)  HCO3- (mEq/L)	Correct answer: C</br></br>a.  125        3.2         102          25  </br>b.  125        6.8         102          27  </br>c.  140        2.3         94           28  </br>d.  140        6.8         94           28  </br>e.  155        3.2         102          26</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c.  Na⁺ 140 mEq/L K⁺ 2.3 mEq/L Cl⁻ 94 mEq/L HCO₃⁻ 28 mEq/L  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Severe anorexia nervosa: 16-month 40-lb weight loss, BMI 13 kg/m² → high risk for electrolyte and acid–base disturbances from self-induced vomiting, laxatives, and diuretics.<br /></br>• Collapse + bradycardia (40/min), hypotension (85/55) → suggests volume depletion.<br /></br>• ECG: ST-segment depression + prominent U waves → classic for hypokalemia (&lt;3.5 mEq/L).<br /></br>• Fine lanugo hair → chronic malnutrition confirms eating-disorder context.<br /></br>Pertinent positives: hypovolemic vitals, U waves.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no alcohol/illicit drugs that could account for other patterns (e.g., alcoholic ketoacidosis).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify primary disturbance → hypokalemia signaled by U waves.<br /></br>Step 2: Determine acid–base status → chronic vomiting (or surreptitious diuretic use) causes loss of gastric HCl → metabolic alkalosis (↑HCO₃⁻) with low Cl⁻ (“chloride-responsive alkalosis”).<br /></br>Step 3: Assess Na⁺ → usually normal or mildly low; nothing in vignette suggests severe hyponatremia or hypernatremia.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In eating disorders with vomiting/diuretic abuse you expect a hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis with relatively normal sodium.  </p></br><p>Documentation point: Record orthostatic vitals, ECG, basic metabolic panel, and note suspected purging behaviors leading to hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Vomiting removes gastric H⁺ and Cl⁻ → kidneys retain HCO₃⁻ (metabolic alkalosis) and excrete H⁺ in exchange for K⁺, worsening hypokalemia.<br /></br>• Volume depletion activates RAAS → aldosterone ↑ distal Na⁺ reabsorption at the expense of K⁺ and H⁺ excretion.<br /></br>• Result: low Cl⁻, low K⁺, elevated HCO₃⁻. Sodium stays near normal unless water intake varies markedly.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>1. Continuous cardiac monitoring (risk of arrhythmia with K⁺ &lt;2.5).<br /></br>2. IV isotonic saline with KCl replacement (10–20 mEq/h).<br /></br>3. Screen for co-ingestions, measure Mg²⁺ (hypomagnesemia exacerbates K⁺ loss).<br /></br>Long-term: Multidisciplinary eating-disorder program, psychotherapy (CBT/family therapy), nutritional rehabilitation, possible SSRI for comorbid depression/anxiety.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Vomiting VACATES K⁺ &amp; Cl⁻”:<br /></br>V = Vomit<br /></br>A = Alkalosis (↑HCO₃⁻)<br /></br>C = low Chloride<br /></br>A = Aldosterone activated<br /></br>T = low poTassium<br /></br>E = ECG U waves<br /></br>S = Sodium Same (near normal)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Na⁺ 125 / K⁺ 3.2 / Cl⁻ 102 / HCO₃⁻ 25<br /></br>• Hyponatremia with normal Cl⁻/HCO₃⁻ → would suggest SIADH or water intoxication, not vomiting-induced alkalosis; K⁺ only mildly low (3.2) doesn’t fit ECG changes.  </p></br><p>b. Na⁺ 125 / K⁺ 6.8 / Cl⁻ 102 / HCO₃⁻ 27<br /></br>• Severe hyperkalemia (6.8) would give peaked T waves, not U waves; hyponatremia again inconsistent.  </p></br><p>d. Na⁺ 140 / K⁺ 6.8 / Cl⁻ 94 / HCO₃⁻ 28<br /></br>• Correct Na⁺, low Cl⁻, and alkalosis fit, but hyperkalemia contradicts ECG U waves and typical physiology of vomiting.  </p></br><p>e. Na⁺ 155 / K⁺ 3.2 / Cl⁻ 102 / HCO₃⁻ 26<br /></br>• Hypernatremia would require water loss &gt; salt loss (e.g., diabetes insipidus). Normal Cl⁻ and only slightly elevated HCO₃⁻ don’t match hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis pattern.  </p></br><p>Thus option c best matches hypokalemic, hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis with normal sodium seen in anorexia nervosa patients who purge.</p>
100. A 20-year-old female newborn was noted to have jaundice 1 hour ago. She was born at term to a 29-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, following an uncomplicated pregnancy and spontaneous vaginal delivery. The mother emigrated from Ethiopia 2 years ago. She received routine prenatal care. Examination of the patient shows jaundice to the level of the umbilicus. There is conjunctival icterus. No other abnormalities are noted. The maternal blood group is O, Rh-positive. Results of laboratory studies, including the patient’s blood group, are pending. Which of the following laboratory findings is most likely to be decreased in this patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Hematocrit</br>b. Leukocyte count</br>c. Platelet count</br>d. Serum direct bilirubin concentration</br>e. Serum indirect bilirubin concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Hematocrit  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Jaundice 1 hour ago … jaundice to the level of the umbilicus” – Jaundice appearing within the first 24 h of life is pathologic and almost always means hemolysis.<br /></br>• Mother’s blood group: O, Rh-positive – Immediately raises suspicion for ABO hemolytic disease of the newborn (most common cause of very‐early jaundice in term infants).<br /></br>• No other abnormalities – Sepsis, metabolic disease, and biliary atresia usually have additional findings.<br /></br>• Ethiopian ancestry is irrelevant to the mechanism but rules nothing out.<br /></br>Key takeaway: In a term infant with jaundice in the first few hours and an O-positive mother, think “ABO incompatibility → hemolytic anemia → ↑ indirect bilirubin.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Early-onset jaundice → must explain hemolysis or significant in utero red cell breakdown.<br /></br>2. The only maternal/neonatal blood‐group information given is maternal type O. Maternal anti-A &amp; anti-B IgG antibodies can cross the placenta regardless of prior sensitization.<br /></br>3. Hemolysis → anemia (↓ hematocrit/hemoglobin) + production of unconjugated bilirubin (↑ indirect bilirubin).<br /></br>4. Question asks which lab value will be DECREASED. The only value expected to fall is hematocrit.  </p></br><p>Core concept: ABO hemolytic disease → Coombs-positive hemolysis → anemia + jaundice within first 24 h.  </p></br><p>Documentation (real patient)<br /></br>• Newborn vitals, bilirubin level/trend, hematocrit/hemoglobin<br /></br>• DAT (direct Coombs) result<br /></br>• Plan for phototherapy vs exchange transfusion  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Mother type O makes IgG anti-A &amp; anti-B.<br /></br>• IgG crosses placenta → binds fetal type A or B RBCs → complement-mediated hemolysis.<br /></br>• Hemolysis →<br /></br>  – Anemia (↓ Hct)<br /></br>  – ↑ unconjugated (indirect) bilirubin that the immature neonatal liver cannot conjugate fast enough → jaundice.<br /></br>• Direct (conjugated) bilirubin remains normal or mildly increased only secondarily if any hepatic injury occurs (not expected here).<br /></br>• Platelets and leukocytes unaffected.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic Tests / Management (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Serum total &amp; direct bilirubin every 4–6 h.<br /></br>2. Blood type &amp; direct antiglobulin (Coombs) test.<br /></br>3. CBC to detect anemia (low Hct).<br /></br>4. Treatment:<br /></br>   • Phototherapy if bilirubin in high-risk zone.<br /></br>   • IVIG or exchange transfusion for severe hemolysis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABO = A Baby O mom hemolyzes”<br /></br>A – Appearance of jaundice &lt;24 h<br /></br>B – Bilirubin indirect ↑<br /></br>O mom – Mother is type O<br /></br>Hemolyzes – Hemolysis → ↓ Hct  </p></br><p>Alternate mnemonic: “O-mother’s IgG GETS BABY”<br /></br>G – IgG crosses placenta<br /></br>E – Early jaundice<br /></br>T – Total bilirubin (indirect) ↑<br /></br>S – Stained yellow skin<br /></br>BABY – Baby’s hematocrit ↓  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Leukocyte count – Hemolysis does not affect WBCs. Leukopenia would suggest infection or bone-marrow suppression.<br /></br>c. Platelet count – Thrombocytopenia occurs in sepsis, maternal ITP, or TORCH infections, not simple ABO incompatibility.<br /></br>d. Serum direct bilirubin – Would be normal or unchanged; conjugated fraction rises only in obstructive or hepatocellular disease.<br /></br>e. Serum indirect bilirubin – Hemolysis makes this go UP, not down.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Take-Home Message<br /></br>Pathologic jaundice appearing in the first day of life with an O-positive mother strongly suggests ABO hemolytic disease. Hemolysis → ↓ hematocrit and ↑ indirect bilirubin; no effect on platelets or leukocytes.</p>
101. A 48-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for management of acute respiratory failure. He has a 4-day history of fever, an increase in thick, yellow-green sputum production, and diffuse muscular aches. During the past 2 years, he has produced 2 to 3 tablespoons of yellow sputum daily. He has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 30 years. He is 183 cm (6 ft) tall and weighs 134 kg (295 lb); BMI is 40 kg/m2. On admission, his temperature is 38.6°C (101.5°F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 170/100 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 60%. On admission, an AP portable x-ray shows bilateral diffuse interstitial infiltrates without consolidation. He does not respond to high-flow oxygen therapy. He is intubated and mechanically ventilated. Examination shows hyperresonance to percussion and wheezes bilaterally. Ceftriaxone and levofloxacin are administered, and his symptoms improve. On hospital day 4, he is extubated. His temperature is 37.6°C (99.7°F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 150/96 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry while sitting shows an oxygen saturation greater than 95%. Six hours later, the patient’s oxygen saturation is 86%. The patient is lying in bed and says he feels fine. Physical examination shows no wheezing, crackles, or rhonchi. There is no peripheral edema. A repeat chest x-ray shows improvement. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management to improve this patient’s oxygen saturation?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Increase the oxygen concentration of inspired air  </br>b. Increase the positive end-expiratory pressure  </br>c. Place the patient in Trendelenburg position  </br>d. Place the patient's head on three pillows  </br>e. Return the patient to a sitting position</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Return the patient to a sitting position  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives:<br /></br>• Morbid obesity (BMI 40 kg/m²) → low functional residual capacity (FRC).<br /></br>• COPD/chronic bronchitis (30-pack-year smoker, daily sputum) → airways prone to closure when lung volumes fall.<br /></br>• Post-extubation: SpO₂ &gt; 95 % while sitting upright.<br /></br>• 6 h later: SpO₂ 86 % when lying flat; feels well; clear lungs; chest-x-ray improved.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives:<br /></br>• No new fever, crackles, wheezes, edema, or infiltrates → argues against pneumonia, pulmonary edema, bronchospasm or volume overload.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Obese/COPD patient desaturates only in the supine position → positional hypoxemia from loss of lung volume/atelectasis.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Supine posture + obesity pushes diaphragm cephalad → sharp drop in FRC.  </li></br><li>In COPD, closing capacity approaches FRC; when FRC drops below closing capacity, small airways shut early → dependent-lung atelectasis → V/Q mismatch → hypoxemia.  </li></br><li>Simply sitting the patient up immediately raises FRC, re-opens dependent alveoli, and corrects V/Q mismatch—fastest, easiest fix.<br /></br>Take-home: In obese or COPD patients who desaturate after lying down, first intervene with upright positioning before escalating therapy.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Documentation (real patient): “SpO₂ fell to 86 % while supine; lungs clear. Re-positioned upright; SpO₂ improved to ___ %. Plan: maintain upright posture, encourage ambulation.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Functional residual capacity = volume remaining after passive expiration.<br /></br>• Factors that ↓ FRC: supine position, anesthesia, obesity, pregnancy, ascites.<br /></br>• When FRC &lt; closing capacity, small airways close during normal tidal breathing → shunt physiology (perfused but non-ventilated alveoli) → hypoxemia refractory to simple FiO₂ increases if severe.<br /></br>• Upright posture restores FRC above closing capacity, relieving shunt.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics  </p></br><p>Immediate: Re-sit patient upright (or at least ≥ 45° head-of-bed).<br /></br>Supportive: Incentive spirometry, early ambulation, pulmonary toilet to prevent recurrent atelectasis.<br /></br>No new imaging or labs are necessary once oxygenation improves with position change.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip  </p></br><p>“Sat drops when they FLAT: Sit them STAT.”<br /></br>(If oxygen saturation falls when the patient is laid flat, the first move is to sit them up immediately.)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Increase the oxygen concentration of inspired air<br /></br>   – Adds FiO₂ but doesn’t address the mechanical collapse causing the shunt. Easier, quicker, and more physiologic to restore lung volume first.</p></br><p>b. Increase the positive end-expiratory pressure<br /></br>   – Requires re-intubation or NIV equipment; over-kill for simple positional atelectasis that resolves with upright posture.</p></br><p>c. Place the patient in Trendelenburg position<br /></br>   – Worsens diaphragmatic elevation, further decreases FRC, and can exacerbate aspiration risk.</p></br><p>d. Place the patient’s head on three pillows<br /></br>   – Slight elevation, but not as effective as full sitting (≥ 45–90° upright); NBME wants the most definitive, immediate correction.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway  </p></br><p>In obese or COPD patients, a drop in oxygen saturation that occurs only after lying flat is most often due to loss of FRC and dependent-lung atelectasis. First-line management is to return the patient to an upright sitting position.</p>
102. A 24-year-old female medical student sustains a deep laceration of her left index finger while assisting the surgeon during an operation on a patient with cervical cancer. The surgical patient has a history of multiple sexual partners and intravenous drug use; it is not yet known if she is HIV positive. Examination of the medical student shows a 2-cm, bleeding laceration; the bleeding stops with compression. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management pending results of HIV testing on the surgical patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Acyclovir therapy</br>b. Antiretroviral therapy</br>c. Corticosteroid therapy</br>d. Nafcillin therapy</br>e. No treatment is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Antiretroviral therapy (immediate HIV post-exposure prophylaxis, PEP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Deep laceration … while assisting the surgeon”  → percutaneous injury with a hollow-bore needle or sharp instrument is the highest-risk occupational exposure for HIV.<br /></br>• Surgical patient has “multiple sexual partners and IV drug use” → high epidemiologic risk for HIV infection.<br /></br>• “Not yet known if she is HIV positive” → source status is unknown at the moment; guidelines say act first, confirm later.<br /></br>• No other red-flag pathogens (e.g., active herpetic lesions) are mentioned.</p></br><p>Key pattern: High-risk source + percutaneous injury = start HIV PEP immediately, do not wait for source testing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Classify exposure → deep percutaneous injury with blood.<br /></br>2. Assess source risk → high-risk behaviors ⇒ treat as potentially HIV positive until proven otherwise.<br /></br>3. Time window → PEP is most effective when started ≤ 2 hours (acceptable up to 72 h).<br /></br>4. Choose therapy → combination antiretroviral therapy (e.g., 3-drug regimen: tenofovir + emtricitabine + either raltegravir or dolutegravir).<br /></br>Take-home: When in doubt, START PEP.</p></br><p>Documentation (real world): exposure details, patient &amp; source identifiers, consent for source HIV testing, counselling, ART regimen started, follow-up testing schedule (0, 6 wk, 3 mo, 6 mo).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• HIV enters via blood → infects CD4⁺ T-cells → systemic dissemination within days.<br /></br>• Early replication can be blocked if reverse transcriptase &amp; integrase are inhibited before viral DNA integrates into host genome, hence the urgency of PEP.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Immediate care: wash/irrigate wound, allow bleeding.<br /></br>2. Baseline labs on the exposed worker: HIV Ag/Ab, HBV, HCV, complete blood count, renal &amp; hepatic panels (for ART safety).<br /></br>3. Source testing: rapid HIV Ag/Ab combo assay; but do NOT delay PEP.<br /></br>4. PEP regimen:<br /></br>   • NRTIs: Tenofovir disoproxil fumarate + Emtricitabine (Truvada)<br /></br>   • Integrase inhibitor: Raltegravir or Dolutegravir<br /></br>   • Duration: 28 days<br /></br>5. Follow-up HIV testing at 6 wk, 3 mo, 6 mo (some guidelines add 12 mo if HCV co-infection present).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Needle + Unknown = NOW”<br /></br>N – Needle/puncture<br /></br>O – Occupational exposure<br /></br>W – Workup later, start drugs NOW (Triple ART ≤ 72 h)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acyclovir therapy<br /></br>   • Used for HSV/VZV, not HIV. No mention of vesicular lesions or herpetic exposure.<br /></br>c. Corticosteroid therapy<br /></br>   • Immunosuppressive; would worsen any viral infection risk. No indication post-injury.<br /></br>d. Nafcillin therapy<br /></br>   • Antistaphylococcal penicillin for MSSA skin infections. Laceration management requires only routine wound care plus tetanus; antibiotics are unnecessary unless gross contamination.<br /></br>e. No treatment is indicated<br /></br>   • Directly contradicts CDC/WHO guidelines: high-risk percutaneous exposure mandates immediate PEP while awaiting source results.</p></br><p>Core concept: For any percutaneous exposure from a potentially HIV-infected source, start combination antiretroviral PEP immediately; act first, test second.</p>
103. A 10-month-old female infant is brought to the office by her parents for a follow-up examination 3 weeks after undergoing staged repair of a left complete unilateral cleft lip and palate. The procedure and recovery were uncomplicated, and the infant was discharged home 2 days later. The parents report she is feeding well with a traditional bottle nipple and now also consumes some soft baby foods. She has not had fever, pain, or nasal regurgitation. She receives no medications. Today, she is at the 25th percentile for length and weight. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses a well-healing surgical incision on the left upper vermilion and philtrum; the palate also appears to be healing well without wound dehiscence or palatal holes. This patient's parents should be informed that she is at increased risk for developing which of the following?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Chronic colonization with group A streptococcus</br>b. Dyslexia</br>c. Peritonsillar abscess</br>d. Severe myopia</br>e. Velopharyngeal insufficiency</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Velopharyngeal insufficiency (VPI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Key clues<br /></br>   • “Left complete unilateral cleft lip and palate” → a congenital orofacial malformation.<br /></br>   • “Staged repair … 3 weeks ago … healing well” → early postoperative period after palatoplasty.<br /></br>   • “Feeding well … no nasal regurgitation (yet)” → short-term outcome is good, but long-term speech–swallow sequelae are still ahead.<br /></br>2. Pertinent positives/negatives<br /></br>   • Normal growth curves (25th %) → no failure to thrive.<br /></br>   • No fever, pain, wound dehiscence → rules out infection or fistula now.<br /></br>   • The question specifically asks about a FUTURE (long-term) risk in children with repaired cleft palate.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Cleft palate patients—even after technically successful repair—remain predisposed to velopharyngeal dysfunction due to intrinsic palatal muscle abnormalities, scarring, and anatomic shortening of the soft palate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why answer E?)<br /></br>• Normal speech requires the soft palate (velum) to elevate and seal against the posterior pharyngeal wall during oral phonation and swallowing.<br /></br>• Children with cleft palate have abnormal levator veli palatini musculature; repair cannot always restore complete competency.<br /></br>• As speech emerges (≈9–18 months), 15-30 % will demonstrate hypernasal speech, nasal air escape, or nasal regurgitation—classic signs of VPI.<br /></br>Take-home: The most common, clinically significant late complication of cleft palate repair is velopharyngeal insufficiency.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: At each well-child and surgical follow-up visit, document speech quality, presence of nasal emission/hypernasality, and any nasal regurgitation; refer to speech pathology or a cleft-craniofacial team if concerns arise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Failure of palatal shelves to fuse → cleft.<br /></br>• After repair, residual muscle malposition + scar contraction → short, stiff palate unable to contact posterior pharynx.<br /></br>• Results:<br /></br>  – Speech: hypernasal resonance, audible air escape, compensatory glottal stops.<br /></br>  – Swallowing: nasal regurgitation of liquids/solids.<br /></br>• Diagnosis: Nasopharyngoscopy or videofluoroscopic speech study.<br /></br>• Treatment: Speech therapy ± secondary surgery (e.g., pharyngeal flap, sphincter pharyngoplasty) if persistent.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line: Early speech therapy, articulation drills.<br /></br>If VPI persists &gt;6–12 months of therapy → imaging studies + surgical correction.<br /></br>No routine labs; focus on functional assessment (speech, swallowing, otologic status).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CLEFT” mnemonic for long-term issues after cleft palate repair:<br /></br>C – Chronic otitis media (eustachian tube dysfunction)<br /></br>L – Language delay → speech therapy<br /></br>E – Eustachian tube dysfunction (again)<br /></br>F – Fistula (palatal)<br /></br>T – Talking Through Nose (velopharyngeal insufficiency)  </p></br><p>Visual: Imagine soft palate as a drawbridge. In some repaired clefts, the bridge is too short—cars (air/food) fall into the river (nasopharynx) → hypernasal speech.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Chronic colonization with group A streptococcus<br /></br>   • GAS carriage typically involves the oropharynx of school-age children, unrelated to cleft anatomy or repair.</p></br><p>b. Dyslexia<br /></br>   • Reading/decoding disorder; not linked to cleft palate per se. Language‐based speech articulation problems ≠ reading disability.</p></br><p>c. Peritonsillar abscess<br /></br>   • A complication of tonsillitis in older children/adults. Cleft palate does not predispose to PTA more than general population.</p></br><p>d. Severe myopia<br /></br>   • Certain craniofacial syndromes (e.g., Stickler) combine cleft palate and myopia, but an isolated unilateral cleft lip/palate without syndromic features is not a proven risk factor for severe myopia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Even after “successful” cleft palate repair, 1 in 4 patients develops velopharyngeal insufficiency manifested by hypernasal speech and nasal regurgitation, necessitating ongoing surveillance by the cleft team.</p>
104. A 22-year-old man is brought to the physician by his parents because he has become newly preoccupied with religion during the past year. He lives with his parents and attends a community college. Two months ago, he stopped attending his classes. His parents report that he spends most of the day in his room alone reading religious scripture. He shows no interest in activities he once enjoyed, no longer spends time with his friends, masturbates frequently and obviously, and spends most of the night walking around his room laughing and talking to himself. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he has a depressed mood and animated affect. His speech is pressured, and responses to questions are tangential. He reports hearing a voice during the past 2 months that he believes is "the voice of God." He says that he feels confused and has thoughts about ending his life because he cannot understand if God truly wants him to become the next Messiah. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Delusional disorder</br>b. Obsessive-compulsive disorder</br>c. Schizoid personality disorder</br>d. Schizophrenia</br>e. Schizotypal personality disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Schizophrenia  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• Duration ≥ 12 months (“during the past year”) → exceeds the 6-month minimum for schizophrenia.<br /></br>• Positive symptoms:<br /></br>  – Third-person auditory hallucination (“voice of God”).<br /></br>  – Fixed, bizarre religious delusion (believes he may be the next Messiah).<br /></br>  – Disorganized thought/speech (tangential answers, pressured).<br /></br>• Negative symptoms: social withdrawal, anhedonia, loss of interest, nocturnal pacing.<br /></br>• Functional decline: stopped college, isolates in room.<br /></br>These elements together fulfill DSM-5 criteria for schizophrenia.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Time course – psychosis &gt; 6 mo → rules out brief psychotic disorder (&lt;1 mo) and schizophreniform (1–6 mo).<br /></br>Step 2: Symptom clusters – both positive and negative sx with functional deterioration → typical of schizophrenia, not a purely delusional state.<br /></br>Step 3: Rule out medical/medication causes – none given; vitals and exam normal.<br /></br>Take-home: Schizophrenia requires ≥ 2 core psychotic features (delusions, hallucinations, disorganized speech, disorganized behavior, negative symptoms) for ≥ 6 mo with social/occupational dysfunction.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note hallucination content, suicidal ideation, insight, and need for safety evaluation/hospitalization.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Snapshot<br /></br>• Hyperdopaminergic activity in the mesolimbic tract → positive symptoms.<br /></br>• Hypodopaminergic activity in the prefrontal cortex → negative/cognitive symptoms.<br /></br>• Neurodevelopmental + genetic vulnerabilities, precipitated by stressors.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment Highlights<br /></br>1. First-line: Second-generation antipsychotic (e.g., risperidone, olanzapine, quetiapine, ziprasidone, aripiprazole).<br /></br>2. Assess and manage suicidality; may require inpatient admission.<br /></br>3. Psychosocial interventions: CBT, family education, supported employment.<br /></br>4. Clozapine for treatment-resistant cases or persistent suicidality.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Tips<br /></br>• “SchizoPHrenia lasts PHorever” → symptoms ≥ 6 mo.<br /></br>• 2 P’s for schizophrenia: Positive + Persistent (&gt;6 mo).<br /></br>• 6 letters in SCHIZO ⇒ ≥ 6 months.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Delusional disorder<br /></br>– Only non-bizarre delusions, NO hallucinations or significant functional decline. This patient has hallucinations, bizarre religious delusion, disorganized speech, and marked dysfunction → not delusional disorder.</p></br><p>b. Obsessive-compulsive disorder<br /></br>– Obsessions are intrusive, ego-dystonic thoughts with insight; compulsions reduce anxiety. Here beliefs are ego-syntonic, fixed, and accompanied by hallucinations.</p></br><p>c. Schizoid personality disorder<br /></br>– Lifelong pattern of detachment and limited emotions, but NO psychosis. Sudden functional decline and hallucinations rule this out.</p></br><p>e. Schizotypal personality disorder<br /></br>– Eccentric behavior and odd beliefs with possible transient perceptual disturbances, but not persistent, well-formed hallucinations/delusions nor severe deterioration seen here.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>The combination of ≥ 1-year duration, positive and negative psychotic symptoms, and functional impairment makes schizophrenia the only diagnosis that fits all clinical criteria.</p>
105. A 26-year-old primigravid woman has spontaneous rupture of the membranes at 30 weeks’ gestation. She is afebrile and has no contractions. A sterile speculum examination shows a closed cervix and a pool of clear fluid in the vagina. Which of the following is the most likely complication?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Amniotic band syndrome</br>b. Fetal deformation</br>c. Fetal hypoplastic lungs</br>d. Preterm delivery</br>e. Umbilical cord occlusion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Preterm delivery  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Spontaneous rupture of the membranes at 30 weeks’ gestation” = preterm premature rupture of membranes (PPROM): rupture of membranes before 37 weeks and before the onset of labor.<br /></br>• No fever, no contractions, cervix closed → confirms rupture occurred first (pre-labor) and labor has not yet started.<br /></br>• Pool of clear fluid on sterile speculum = objective evidence of ruptured membranes.<br /></br>Key point: The single most common sequela of PPROM is the onset of labor within a short time frame. Roughly 50 % deliver within 24 h and &gt;90 % within 1 week.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify PPROM (prematurity + rupture).<br /></br>2. Ask: What happens most often after PPROM? → Spontaneous uterine contractions follow, culminating in delivery.<br /></br>3. Compare frequency of possible complications. Preterm birth is far more common than the others listed.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: PPROM is primarily a problem of prematurity; infection (chorioamnionitis) and placental abruption are next on the list, but the overwhelming likelihood is preterm labor/delivery.  </p></br><p>If this were a real patient encounter you would document: GA 30 wks, PPROM confirmed by pool/ferning, FHR, absence of contractions, maternal vitals, plan for latency antibiotics + corticosteroids, counseling re risk of preterm delivery.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Mechanism: Membrane weakness ± infection → rupture → prostaglandin release → uterine stimulatory cascade.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Prior PPROM, genital tract infections, smoking, polyhydramnios, multiple gestation.<br /></br>• Complications in descending order of frequency:<br /></br>  1. Preterm labor/delivery.<br /></br>  2. Chorioamnionitis.<br /></br>  3. Abruptio placentae.<br /></br>  4. Cord prolapse/compression, pulmonary hypoplasia, skeletal deformities (mostly when rupture &lt;24–26 weeks).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Immediate management at 30 weeks:<br /></br>• Latency antibiotics (IV ampicillin + erythromycin → PO amoxicillin + erythro).<br /></br>• Betamethasone (or dexamethasone) to accelerate lung maturity.<br /></br>• Strict observation—delivery only for fetal/maternal instability or ≥34 wks.<br /></br>• Magnesium sulfate for neuroprotection if &lt;32 wks.<br /></br>• NO digital cervical exam (↑infection risk).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PPROM = Preterm birth PRObably Minutes away.”<br /></br>Or think “rupture opens the exit door—most babies walk out soon.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amniotic band syndrome<br /></br>• Requires rupture of AMNION, not the chorion, typically in the first trimester → constriction rings, limb amputations. Far rarer than preterm labor in third-trimester PPROM.  </p></br><p>b. Fetal deformation<br /></br>• Oligohydramnios after mid-trimester can cause limb contractures or Potter facies, but significant skeletal deformation is usually from chronic severe fluid loss earlier (≤2 nd trimester). Not the leading outcome at 30 wks.  </p></br><p>c. Fetal hypoplastic lungs<br /></br>• Pulmonary hypoplasia occurs when membranes rupture before 24–26 weeks (critical lung-branching period). At 30 weeks the lungs are already canalicular/saccular; hypoplasia is much less likely.  </p></br><p>e. Umbilical cord occlusion (prolapse/compression)<br /></br>• Recognized complication, but incidence (≈1–2 %) is lower than the near-inevitable onset of labor.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In PPROM, remember “Prematurity first.” The most likely event after membranes rupture well before term is spontaneous preterm labor and delivery.</p>
106. An 8-year-old African American girl with sickle cell disease is brought to the physician by her mother because of a 12-hour history of cough and difficulty breathing. Her medications are penicillin and folic acid. Her temperature is 39°C (102.2°F), pulse is 130/min, respirations are 40/min, and blood pressure is 105/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 94%. On pulmonary examination, crackles are heard in the upper lobes. Breath sounds are decreased at the lung bases bilaterally. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. An x-ray of the chest is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Anti-Mycoplasma pneumoniae IgG titer</br>b. Rapid antigen detection test for respiratory syncytial virus</br>c. Blood culture</br>d. Spiral CT scan of the chest</br>e. Thoracentesis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Blood culture  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key demographics: 8-year-old African-American child with known sickle cell disease (SCD).<br /></br>• Presenting syndrome: 12 h of fever (39 °C), tachycardia, tachypnea, cough, hypoxia (but SpO₂ ≥ 94 %), new crackles and ↓ breath sounds, plus a NEW pulmonary infiltrate on the CXR (described in stem).<br /></br>• In SCD, the triad of (1) fever/respiratory sx + (2) hypoxia/tachypnea + (3) new infiltrate = Acute Chest Syndrome (ACS).  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: No mention of wheezing (asthma/bronchiolitis) or large pleural effusion.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Recognize the emergency: ACS is a leading cause of morbidity/mortality in SCD; treat empirically but also look for a causative infection.<br /></br>Step 2 – Decide what tests actually change management today:<br /></br>• SCD pts are functionally asplenic → high risk of sepsis from encapsulated organisms (Streptococcus pneumoniae, H. influenzae, Salmonella, etc.).<br /></br>• Blood culture is obtained immediately in any febrile SCD patient to identify bacteremia and guide antibiotic choices.  </p></br><p>Bottom line/Take-home: In a sickle-cell patient with ACS, the single most useful diagnostic next step is to obtain BLOOD CULTURES prior to, or concurrent with, starting broad-spectrum antibiotics.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): “Febrile SCD patient with suspected ACS; blood cultures drawn ×2 sets prior to antibiotics.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>ACS results from:<br /></br>• Infection (most common in children—Mycoplasma, Chlamydia, Strep pneumo)<br /></br>• Fat emboli from bone marrow infarct<br /></br>• Pulmonary infarction from vaso-occlusion<br /></br>Inflamed lung → intrapulmonary sickling → vicious cycle of hypoxia and further sickling. Early antibiotics, oxygen, and transfusion (simple or exchange) interrupt the cycle.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Snapshot<br /></br>Immediate management (often simultaneous):<br /></br>1. Ceftriaxone + Azithromycin (covers S. pneumo + atypicals)<br /></br>2. Incentive spirometry, O₂ supplementation<br /></br>3. ± Simple/exchange transfusion if severe hypoxia or Hb drop<br /></br>4. IV fluids, analgesia<br /></br>5. Blood cultures BEFORE the first antibiotic dose  </p></br><p>No advanced imaging is required unless the clinical picture changes (e.g., CT angiography only if PE strongly suspected and CXR is non-diagnostic).  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACS in SCD = A C S”<br /></br>A – Antibiotics ASAP (ceftriaxone + azithro)<br /></br>C – Culture the blood before the drugs<br /></br>S – Spirometry / Supportive care (O₂, fluids, transfusion)  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Anti-Mycoplasma pneumoniae IgG titer<br /></br>• IgG signifies PAST exposure; acute infection uses IgM or paired IgG serology. Serology results take days and will NOT change acute management.  </p></br><p>b. Rapid antigen test for RSV<br /></br>• RSV bronchiolitis is primarily in infants &lt; 2 y; school-aged child with focal crackles and infiltrate makes RSV unlikely.  </p></br><p>d. Spiral CT (CT angiography) of the chest<br /></br>• Used for pulmonary embolism. Although vaso-occlusion can cause in-situ thrombosis, the child has fever and crackles → infectious picture. CT exposes radiation and contrast without changing initial management.  </p></br><p>e. Thoracentesis<br /></br>• Only indicated if a moderate/large pleural effusion is present (blunting of costophrenic angle, meniscus sign). The stem describes decreased breath sounds at bases but does not mention effusion on CXR.  </p></br><p>Key difference: Blood culture is rapid, low-risk, guides therapy, and is standard of care in febrile SCD patients.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In any febrile sickle-cell patient with suspected acute chest syndrome, draw blood cultures immediately—then start broad-spectrum antibiotics and supportive care.</p>
107. A 58-year-old man is admitted to the hospital for treatment of anemia. Two days ago, he had excessive blood loss following colon polyp removal via colonoscopy. Today, his hemoglobin concentration is 6.5 g/dL and hematocrit is 19.5%. Two months ago, in order to decrease transfusion errors, a blood lock system was implemented in which the patient ID must be entered into the lock to open the blood container; the container cannot be opened without the correct ID. Requiring staff to enter the patient's ID to open the blood lock is best described as which of the following?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. An affordance</br>b. A forcing function</br>c. An interruption in care</br>d. Mapping of the design of the blood container</br>e. A policy leading to delays in care</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. A forcing function  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clue: “the container cannot be opened without the correct ID.”<br /></br>• This describes a built-in constraint—unless the user completes the required step (entering the correct patient identifier) the task (opening the cooler and transfusing blood) is physically impossible.<br /></br>• Purpose: eliminate wrong-patient transfusions, one of the most serious “never events.”  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify the safety goal: protect against mis-matching blood to patient.<br /></br>2. Note the design element: a lock that literally forces verification of the patient’s identifier before the next step can occur.<br /></br>3. Match to human-factors terminology: a “forcing function” (a.k.a. constraint) makes it impossible to proceed down the wrong path.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Forcing functions are the strongest reliability tool in the patient-safety hierarchy because they eliminate, rather than merely warn against, human error.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (Very brief because this is a systems-based Q)<br /></br>• Wrong-patient transfusions cause acute hemolysis → hypotension, DIC, renal failure, death.<br /></br>• Prevention hierarchy: forcing functions/physical constraints &gt; checklists &gt; reminders &gt; education.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Not applicable; the question tests systems design rather than clinical management.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Force before course”: If the design forces you to do X before you can continue the workflow, it’s a forcing function.<br /></br>OR<br /></br>“Dead-bolt = forcing function; door-sign = reminder.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Affordance – A design feature that makes the intended use obvious (e.g., a handle invites pulling). The lock does not suggest how to use the blood cooler; it prevents misuse.<br /></br>c. Interruption in care – An unplanned break in workflow (phone call, page). The lock is planned and integral, not an interruption.<br /></br>d. Mapping of design – Spatial/visual correspondence between controls and their effects (e.g., stove knobs arranged like burners). No spatial layout issue here.<br /></br>e. Policy leading to delays – Policies are rules; they can still be bypassed. Here, the physical lock enforces action regardless of policy and is specifically designed to reduce errors, not merely to slow things down.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>A physical or electronic lock that stops you from performing the next step until the correct identifier is entered exemplifies a forcing function—the most powerful error-proofing strategy in patient safety.</p>
108. Twenty-four hours after admission to the hospital for treatment of temperatures to 38.5°C (101.3°F) and abdominal pain, a 22-year-old man has a decrease in his temperature. Two weeks ago, he went on a 5-day trip to South America and had diarrhea for 7 days. He has no previous history of serious illness. On admission, a CT scan of the abdomen showed a 5-cm mass in the right lobe of the liver; the mass was filled with fluid. He has received metronidazole, ampicillin, and gentamicin since admission. His current temperature is 37.4°C (99.3°F), and blood pressure is 120/75 mm Hg. The abdomen is flat with moderate right upper quadrant tenderness and guarding. There are no peritoneal signs. A serum titer for antibodies to Entamoeba histolytica is positive at 1:128. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Continue the current antibiotic regimen for 10 days</br>b. Discontinue ampicillin and gentamicin and continue metronidazole</br>c. Perform examination of the stool for ova and parasites</br>d. Perform CT scan-guided aspiration of the mass</br>e. Perform surgical drainage of the mass</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Discontinue ampicillin and gentamicin and continue metronidazole</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Recent travel to South America + 7-day diarrheal illness → classic exposure risk for Entamoeba histolytica.<br /></br>• CT: solitary 5-cm fluid-filled mass in the right hepatic lobe → typical appearance of an amebic liver abscess (usually single, in right lobe, “anchovy‐paste” pus).<br /></br>• Rapid clinical improvement in &lt;24 h after starting metronidazole strongly suggests an amebic rather than a pyogenic abscess (pyogenic abscesses respond more slowly and require broader gram-negative/anaerobic coverage plus drainage).<br /></br>• Positive E. histolytica serology (1:128) virtually confirms the diagnosis (&gt;95 % sensitivity).</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: RUQ pain, fever, travel exposure, serology+, dramatic response to metronidazole.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No peritoneal signs or rupture, no history of biliary disease, no polymicrobial blood cultures → argue against pyogenic abscess.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach:</br>1. Identify liver abscess (imaging).<br /></br>2. Decide between pyogenic vs amebic.<br /></br>   – Travel, serology+, rapid response → amebic.<br /></br>3. Management for amebic abscess = tissue amebicide (metronidazole 500–750 mg PO/IV q8 h × 7–10 d) followed by a luminal agent (e.g., paromomycin or diiodohydroxyquin) to eradicate intestinal cysts.<br /></br>4. Broad gram-negative coverage (ampicillin, gentamicin) is unnecessary once pyogenic infection is ruled out; discontinue to avoid toxicity and resistance.<br /></br>Take-home: Metronidazole alone is sufficient in &gt;90 % of amebic liver abscesses; drainage is rarely required.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: Record positive serology, imaging characteristics, clinical improvement, and plan to stop unnecessary antibiotics and add luminal agent after metronidazole course.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)</br>• E. histolytica trophozoites invade colonic mucosa → enter portal circulation → hepatic abscess (weeks later).<br /></br>• Abscess content is sterile for bacteria; culture-negative.<br /></br>• Serology becomes positive by the time hepatic disease develops (stool O&amp;P often negative).<br /></br>Risk factors: travel to endemic areas, poor sanitation, men 20-40 y.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>First-line:<br /></br>1. Metronidazole 7–10 d (tissue amebicide).<br /></br>2. THEN paromomycin or iodoquinol 7–10 d (luminal amebicide).  </p></br><p>Drainage indications:<br /></br>• Impending rupture (especially left-lobe lesions), size &gt;10 cm, lack of clinical response to 72 h of therapy, uncertain diagnosis.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Metro takes care of the liver party, Paro cleans up the colon after.”<br /></br>Think: Travel → ‘ameba airplane’ lands in the liver; metronidazole is the fire-truck that puts out the single hot spot.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Continue current antibiotic regimen for 10 d<br /></br>   – Ampicillin/gentamicin target pyogenic (biliary/portal) abscess; unnecessary and exposes to nephro-/ototoxicity. Rapid response + serology+ favors amebic.</p></br><p>c. Stool O&amp;P for ova/parasites<br /></br>   – Low yield in hepatic disease (trophozoites rarely returned to lumen). Positive serology already establishes diagnosis; does not change management.</p></br><p>d. CT-guided aspiration<br /></br>   – Reserved for diagnostic uncertainty, very large (&gt;10 cm) lesions, poor response to therapy, or left-lobe lesions at risk of pericardial rupture. Not needed here.</p></br><p>e. Surgical drainage<br /></br>   – Even more invasive; indicated only if rupture or failure of percutaneous methods. Patient is improving.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Core Takeaway</br>Amebic liver abscess = travel history + solitary right-lobe lesion + positive serology; treat with metronidazole alone (followed by a luminal agent) and stop unnecessary broad-spectrum antibiotics.</p>
109. A previously healthy 23-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 20 minutes after the sudden onset of shortness of breath, moderate abdominal pain, nausea, and an itchy rash over the abdomen. Her symptoms began after she ate a sugar cookie given to her by a friend. The patient had a peanut allergy as a child and before eating the cookie, she asked her friend to taste it. Neither she nor her friend tasted or smelled peanuts. The patient’s only medication is an oral contraceptive. Her last menstrual period was 1 week ago. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 5 years. She drinks three 12-oz beers on weekends. She has been staying at different friends’ homes during the past 2 months while searching for an apartment and has been using unfamiliar laundry detergents. She is in mild respiratory distress but is able to speak in full sentences. Her pulse is 110/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 94%. A photograph of the rash is shown. Scattered wheezes are heard bilaterally. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. Abdominal examination shows mild, diffuse tenderness to palpation; there is no rebound or guarding. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Albuterol</br>b. Diphenhydramine</br>c. Epinephrine</br>d. Furosemide</br>e. Nitroglycerin</br>f. Prednisone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Epinephrine  (0.3–0.5 mg of 1:1,000 solution IM in the mid-anterolateral thigh)</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>1. Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Trigger: Cookie ingestion in a patient with prior peanut allergy ⟶ immediately suspicious for allergen exposure.<br /></br>• Multisystem involvement minutes later:<br /></br>  – Skin: itchy, urticarial rash (photo).<br /></br>  – Respiratory: dyspnea, wheezes, tachypnea.<br /></br>  – Gastrointestinal: abdominal pain, nausea.<br /></br>  – Hemodynamics: mild hypotension (100/60) + tachycardia.<br /></br>Meeting the NIAID criteria for anaphylaxis: acute illness with involvement of two organ systems after likely allergen exposure → diagnose anaphylaxis without needing labs.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: speaks full sentences (airway still patent), no rebound/guarding (not surgical abdomen) – but these do NOT rule out anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>2. Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why epinephrine first)<br /></br>Step-wise thinking:<br /></br>A) Recognize anaphylaxis.<br /></br>B) The life-threatening problems are upper-airway edema, bronchospasm, and distributive shock.<br /></br>C) First intervention that simultaneously:<br /></br>   • α1 agonism → peripheral vasoconstriction, raises BP, decreases mucosal edema.<br /></br>   • β1 agonism → ↑ cardiac output.<br /></br>   • β2 agonism → bronchodilation, ↓ mediator release from mast cells.<br /></br>Only epinephrine does all three quickly; onset is seconds to a few minutes when given IM.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: IM epinephrine is the FIRST and MOST IMPORTANT drug in every case of anaphylaxis—give it before antihistamines, steroids, or inhaled β-agonists.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Administered 0.3 mg 1:1,000 epinephrine IM to right lateral thigh at HH:MM; patient educated on need for epinephrine autoinjector.”</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>3. Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Allergen cross-links IgE on mast cells/basophils → explosive release of histamine, tryptase, prostaglandins, leukotrienes →<br /></br>• Vasodilation &amp; ↑ vascular permeability → hypotension, edema, urticaria.<br /></br>• Bronchial smooth-muscle contraction → wheeze, dyspnea.<br /></br>• GI smooth-muscle contraction &amp; edema → crampy abdominal pain, nausea.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>4. Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Immediate management:<br /></br>1. Epinephrine IM first.<br /></br>2. ABCs: high-flow O2, airway positioning, prepare for intubation if stridor/voice change.<br /></br>3. Large-bore IV, isotonic crystalloid bolus for hypotension.<br /></br>4. Adjuncts after epi:<br /></br>   • H1 blocker: diphenhydramine 25–50 mg IV.<br /></br>   • H2 blocker: ranitidine or famotidine.<br /></br>   • Glucocorticoid: methylprednisolone or prednisone to reduce biphasic reaction (hours later).<br /></br>   • Nebulized albuterol for persistent bronchospasm.<br /></br>Observe ≥4–6 hr (longer if severe) for biphasic anaphylaxis.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>5. Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Epi is the ‘ABC’ of anaphylaxis—Airway, Bronchospasm, Circulation—give it IMmediately.”<br /></br>Mnemonic for drug order: Epi THEN BAD (Benadryl, Albuterol, Dexamethasone).</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>6. Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Albuterol – Useful for bronchospasm but lacks α-adrenergic effects; cannot reverse hypotension or airway edema. Give only after epinephrine if wheeze persists.<br /></br>b. Diphenhydramine – H1 blockade relieves hives/pruritus but has slow onset (30–60 min IV/PO) and no effect on airway edema or shock.<br /></br>d. Furosemide – Treats volume overload; not indicated in distributive shock of anaphylaxis.<br /></br>e. Nitroglycerin – Vasodilator; would worsen hypotension. Used for angina or flash pulmonary edema, not allergic reactions.<br /></br>f. Prednisone – Reduces risk of late-phase reaction but takes ≥4 hours to work; never initial therapy.</p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Key Exam Point: The single best initial pharmacologic treatment for ANY patient meeting clinical criteria for anaphylaxis is intramuscular epinephrine.</p>
110. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician because he has noticed blood in his urine for the past 2 weeks. He has no prior history of blood in his urine. He has not had pain or any other symptoms. He has hypertension controlled with a diuretic and hypercholesterolemia controlled with a lipid-lowering agent. His blood pressure is 120/76 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. Urinalysis shows RBCs that are too numerous to count. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Renal scintigraphy</br>b. Retrograde urethrography</br>c. MRI of the pelvis</br>d. Cystoscopy</br>e. Renal biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: d. Cystoscopy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Painless, gross hematuria (“blood in his urine for the past 2 weeks”) in a 67-year-old man.<br /></br>• No dysuria, frequency, flank pain, or systemic symptoms → rules out infection or stones.<br /></br>• Urinalysis: RBCs “too numerous to count,” but no mention of dysmorphic RBCs, RBC casts, or heavy proteinuria → argues against a glomerular source.<br /></br>Bottom line: An older patient with painless gross hematuria should be assumed to have a urothelial (transitional-cell) malignancy until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Stepwise)<br /></br>1. Decide glomerular vs. non-glomerular source.<br /></br>   • Glomerular: dysmorphic RBCs, casts, proteinuria.<br /></br>   • Non-glomerular (urologic): normal RBCs, clots, painless bleeding.<br /></br>2. Non-glomerular source in a patient ≥40 y → mandated evaluation for bladder &amp; upper tract cancer.<br /></br>3. First diagnostic test that directly visualizes &amp; biopsies the bladder mucosa = cystoscopy.<br /></br>Core takeaway: “Painless hematuria in an older adult = scope the bladder.”</p></br><p>Documentation (real life): “Plan: schedule diagnostic cystoscopy with possible biopsy for evaluation of gross painless hematuria.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Urothelial (transitional-cell) carcinoma<br /></br>• Arises anywhere from renal pelvis → ureter → bladder → proximal urethra; bladder is most common site.<br /></br>• Risk factors: smoking (MC), aniline dyes, cyclophosphamide, phenacetin, chronic cystitis (Schistosoma in endemic areas).<br /></br>• Tumor bleeds easily → persistent or intermittent painless gross hematuria.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management<br /></br>Initial work-up of gross hematuria (per AUA/EAU guidelines)<br /></br>1. Cystoscopy ± biopsy (all patients ≥35-40 y).<br /></br>2. Upper-tract imaging (CT urogram or IVP) to evaluate kidneys &amp; ureters.<br /></br>3. Cytology or urine FISH for atypical cells (optional).<br /></br>Treatment depends on stage: TURBT + intravesical BCG for superficial, radical cystectomy ± chemo for muscle-invasive, etc.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Blood you can see after age 40? Go C-(see)-stoscopy.”<br /></br>or<br /></br>RED FLAG:<br /></br>R = Red urine<br /></br>E = Elderly (&gt;40 y)<br /></br>D = Do Cystoscopy</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Renal scintigraphy – Assesses renal perfusion or differential function (e.g., obstruction, renovascular disease); does NOT visualize urothelial tumors.<br /></br>b. Retrograde urethrography – Used to diagnose urethral trauma (pelvic fracture, straddle injury) → dysuria/urinary retention, blood at meatus.<br /></br>c. MRI of the pelvis – Helpful for staging once a pelvic mass is known; not the initial test to find the bleeding source.<br /></br>e. Renal biopsy – Indicated for suspected glomerular disease (proteinuria, casts, dysmorphic RBCs, systemic features). Urine here suggests a lower-tract, non-glomerular cause.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Exam Pearl<br /></br>Painless gross hematuria in an older adult is bladder cancer until proven otherwise; cystoscopy is always the first diagnostic step.</p>
111. A 57-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 3-week history of headaches that have been increasing in severity and frequency. He also has a 2-week history of progressive blurred vision in both eyes. He has not had pain in the eyes. His last visit to a physician was 10 years ago because of pain in his fingers; at that visit, he was told he had high blood pressure. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 90/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 200/110 mm Hg. Repeat blood pressure measurements are 210/112 mm Hg and 214/114 mm Hg. Findings on funduscopic examination are shown. Examination shows ulnar deviation of both hands and several swollen interphalangeal joints. His fasting serum glucose concentration is 160 mg/dL. Which of the following is the most likely underlying cause of this patient’s symptoms?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Diabetes mellitus</br>b. Glioblastoma multiforme</br>c. Hypertension</br>d. Idiopathic intracranial hypertension</br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c.
