Hypertension</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key positives<br /></br>  – 3-week history of progressively worsening headaches<br /></br>  – 2-week history of bilateral blurred vision without ocular pain<br /></br>  – Repeated very high blood pressures (≈ 210/110 mm Hg)<br /></br>  – Funduscopic photograph (not shown to you) that, in this clinical setting, almost certainly demonstrates grade IV hypertensive retinopathy (cotton-wool spots, flame hemorrhages, hard exudates, and papilledema).<br /></br>• Key negatives<br /></br>  – No focal neurologic deficit or seizures to suggest an intracranial tumor.<br /></br>  – No eye pain, redness, or photophobia to suggest an inflammatory ocular complication of rheumatoid arthritis.<br /></br>  – Duration is weeks, not years, arguing against slowly progressive diabetic retinopathy.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Acute severe hypertension + funduscopic evidence of end-organ damage + headache/visual changes = hypertensive emergency (malignant hypertension).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Symptoms (headache, blurred vision) localize to the eye/brain interface.<br /></br>2. Check vitals—markedly elevated BP → think of end-organ injury.<br /></br>3. Look at fundus—signs of retinopathy with papilledema confirm vascular injury from pressure, not a mass lesion.<br /></br>4. Conclusion: The high BP itself is the underlying cause of the neurologic/ocular symptoms.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Malignant (grade IV) hypertension produces acute target-organ damage—particularly papilledema, retinal hemorrhages, and headache—and must be recognized rapidly on exams and in practice.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>Chronic uncontrolled hypertension causes arteriolar thickening and sclerosis. When BP acutely rises to &gt;180/120 mm Hg, autoregulation fails, leading to hyperperfusion, endothelial damage, and leakage:<br /></br>• Retina → flame hemorrhages, cotton-wool spots, hard exudates, optic-disk edema.<br /></br>• Brain → elevated intracranial pressure → headaches, possible encephalopathy.<br /></br>• Kidneys &amp; heart simultaneously sustain injury (proteinuria, LV failure).</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostics</br>Real patient management would include:<br /></br>1. Immediate confirmation with repeat BP in both arms.<br /></br>2. IV antihypertensive titratable agent (e.g., nicardipine, clevidipine, labetalol) to lower MAP by ≤ 25 % within the first hour, then to 160/100 over next 2–6 h.<br /></br>3. Baseline labs (CMP, troponin, UA), ECG, and possible brain imaging if neurologic deficits develop.<br /></br>Document all end-organ findings, particularly the grade IV retinopathy (papilledema), in the chart.</p></br><p>Memory Tips</br>“Headache + Hazy discs + High pressure = Hypertensive emergency” (3 H’s).<br /></br>Grades of hypertensive retinopathy: “Silver, Nick, Cotton, Disk” → arteriolar narrowing/silver-wiring, AV nicking, cotton-wool spots, disk edema (papilledema).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Diabetes mellitus – Diabetic retinopathy is usually painless and slowly progressive over years, showing microaneurysms, dot-blot hemorrhages, and neovascularization, not acute papilledema with severe hypertension. Headache is not typical.<br /></br>b. Glioblastoma multiforme – Can give headaches and papilledema but BP would not be massively elevated; course is usually longer, with focal neurologic deficits or seizures.<br /></br>d. Idiopathic intracranial hypertension – Presents with headaches and papilledema but classically in obese women of child-bearing age; BP is normal.<br /></br>e. Rheumatoid arthritis – Can cause ocular complications (scleritis, episcleritis, keratoconjunctivitis sicca) associated with eye pain and redness, not papilledema or hypertensive findings.</p></br><p>Bottom line: The patient’s acute visual blurring and headaches arise from malignant hypertension causing grade IV hypertensive retinopathy.</p>
112. A case-control study is conducted to assess the effect of dietary fat intake on the occurrence of sudden cardiac death. One hundred fifty cases and controls are matched by age, sex assigned at birth, race, and history of hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and cigarette smoking. Dietary intake history of the cases is obtained from the next of kin. Results show an odds ratio of 1.6 with a 95% confidence interval of 1.2 to 1.9. The authors conclude that there is a relationship between dietary fat intake and sudden cardiac death. Which of the following raises the most concern about the authors' conclusion?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Differential bias in assessing dietary fat</br>b. Inadequate sample size</br>c. Lack of blinding</br>d. Misclassification of cases and controls</br>e. Weak statistical association</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>A. Differential bias in assessing dietary fat (recall / information bias)</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Study design: Case-control. Outcome (sudden cardiac death) is known first; investigators look back to compare past exposure (dietary fat).<br /></br>• Key clue: “Dietary intake history of the cases is obtained from the next of kin.” Controls are alive and therefore report their own diets.<br /></br>  – Cases → proxy recall.<br /></br>  – Controls → self-recall.<br /></br>• Matching was done for age, sex, race, HTN, hypercholesterolemia, and smoking, so confounding from these factors is minimized.<br /></br>• Reported result: OR 1.6 (95 % CI 1.2–1.9) = statistically significant, so the concern is not about power or precision.  </p></br><p>The single element that most threatens validity is the way the exposure was measured differently in the two groups.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise logic:<br /></br>1. In a case-control study, exposure must be ascertained <strong>in the same manner</strong> for cases and controls.<br /></br>2. Here, exposure data for cases come from next-of-kin recall (often incomplete or embellished after an unexpected death). Controls report firsthand.<br /></br>3. This introduces <strong>differential misclassification of the exposure</strong>—a form of information bias. Because the error differs between groups, it can artificially inflate or attenuate the odds ratio, undermining the conclusion that dietary fat truly raises risk.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: In observational studies, equal ascertainment of exposure in both groups is critical; otherwise differential (recall) bias can distort associations.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Not directly tested here; focus is epidemiologic methodology rather than cardiac pathophysiology.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Diagnostic / Treatment Pearl<br /></br>(N/A—methodologic question).</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Different people, different questions = differential bias.”<br /></br>Associate “next-of-kin recall” with <strong>recall bias</strong> → always red-flag in case-control studies of fatal outcomes.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Inadequate sample size – An imprecise study gives a wide CI crossing 1.0. CI is 1.2–1.9 (narrow, excludes 1), indicating adequate power.<br /></br>C. Lack of blinding – Blinding interviewers can help, but even with blinding, using proxies for cases and self-report for controls still causes information bias.<br /></br>D. Misclassification of cases and controls – Classification of outcome (sudden cardiac death) is unlikely to differ; diagnosis is usually well documented. Exposure misclassification (answer A) is the real issue.<br /></br>E. Weak statistical association – OR 1.6 with a significant CI represents a moderate association; strength alone is not the chief threat here—systematic exposure assessment error is.</p></br><p>–––––––––––––––––––––––––</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Because exposure information was gathered differently for the two groups, recall/information bias is the largest threat to the validity of the reported relationship between dietary fat intake and sudden cardiac death.</p>
113. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-month history of hoarseness. She has not had any other symptoms. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 45 years. Examination, including examination of the oropharynx, shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Follow-up examination in 6 weeks</br>b. X-rays of the maxillary sinuses</br>c. CT scan of the chest</br>d. Bronchodilator therapy</br>e. Indirect laryngoscopy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Indirect laryngoscopy</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• Age 67 years + 45-pack-year smoking history → high risk for head &amp; neck malignancy<br /></br>• Hoarseness persisting 2 months → exceeds “red-flag” cutoff of 2 weeks for voice change<br /></br>• No viral prodrome, cough, GERD symptoms, allergy, or medication trigger → lowers likelihood of benign laryngitis, post-nasal drip, ACE-I cough, etc.<br /></br>• Normal oropharyngeal exam → lesion is likely below the oropharynx (i.e., in the larynx)  </p></br><p>Pertinent positive: chronic hoarseness<br /></br>Pertinent negative: no acute URI, no oropharyngeal lesion visible  </p></br><p>Pattern: Any patient (especially smoker) with hoarseness &gt;2 weeks and no obvious benign cause requires direct visualization of the larynx to rule out laryngeal carcinoma or vocal-cord pathology.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step 1: Recognize alarm symptom: chronic hoarseness.<br /></br>Step 2: Apply guideline: &gt;2 weeks → visualize larynx.<br /></br>Step 3: Choose least invasive, office-based visualization method → indirect (mirror or flexible fiber-optic) laryngoscopy by ENT.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Chronic hoarseness warrants prompt laryngeal evaluation; never “watch and wait” in a smoker.</p></br><p>Documentation points (real patient): duration of hoarseness, smoking history, absence of URI/alcohol/GERD, recommendation for ENT referral and indirect laryngoscopy.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)  </p></br><p>• Laryngeal squamous-cell carcinoma arises from epithelial surfaces exposed to tobacco, alcohol, HPV, or irradiation.<br /></br>• Early lesion localizes to true vocal cords → presents with isolated, persistent hoarseness before dysphagia, otalgia, or airway compromise occur.<br /></br>• Early detection dramatically improves cure rate; thus guidelines emphasize visualization.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment  </p></br><p>Diagnostics:<br /></br>1. Indirect laryngoscopy → identifies mass, polyp, paresis.<br /></br>2. If suspicious lesion seen → direct laryngoscopy under anesthesia with biopsy.<br /></br>3. Staging CT/MRI of neck ± chest once a cancer diagnosis is established (look for local invasion, lung primary, mediastinal nodes).  </p></br><p>Treatment (after biopsy):<br /></br>• Early glottic cancer: endoscopic excision or radiation.<br /></br>• Advanced disease: partial/total laryngectomy, chemoradiation.<br /></br>• Voice therapy, smoking cessation essential.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“2-Week VOICE Rule”:<br /></br>Voice change lasting 2 weeks → Order Inspect of Cord Epithelium (Indirect/ENT).  </p></br><p>Visual mnemonic: Picture a red 2-week calendar stamped on a vocal cord— if it’s a smoker, the stamp flashes “ENT NOW.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Follow-up in 6 weeks<br /></br>• Delay risks missing an early, curable laryngeal cancer. Guidelines forbid simple observation beyond 2 weeks in high-risk patients.</p></br><p>b. X-rays of the maxillary sinuses<br /></br>• Sinus disease causes post-nasal drip &amp; throat clearing, not isolated hoarseness. No sinus symptoms here.</p></br><p>c. CT scan of the chest<br /></br>• Lung apex tumor can impinge recurrent laryngeal nerve → hoarseness, but initial step is to look at the larynx itself. CT chest is reserved if laryngoscopy reveals unilateral vocal-cord paralysis without laryngeal mass.</p></br><p>d. Bronchodilator therapy<br /></br>• Hoarseness is not caused by lower-airway bronchospasm. No wheeze, SOB, or COPD exacerbation present.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>Persistent hoarseness, especially in a long-term smoker, mandates immediate visualization of the larynx—start with indirect laryngoscopy to rule out laryngeal cancer.</p>
114. A 57-year-old man is admitted to the hospital because of fever, shortness of breath, and cough productive of yellow sputum. He has prostate cancer metastatic to the bone; his only symptom from the cancer is occasional back pain well controlled with oral acetaminophen and hydrocodone. At his last appointment 6 months ago, his serum prostate-specific antigen assay was stable, and his disease had not progressed. He plays golf several times each week and enjoys traveling with his wife. His advance directive states that he does not want intubation or mechanical ventilation unless he has an acute illness with a reasonable chance of recovery to a good quality of life. He is lethargic and unable to communicate. His temperature is 38.9°C (102°F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 148/92 mm Hg. Pulmonary examination shows shallow, rapid breathing. He is using accessory muscles of respiration. Inspiratory crackles are heard at the right upper and lower lung fields. Arterial blood gas analysis on 100% oxygen shows:</br>pH    7.36</br>Pco2  45 mm Hg</br>Po2   42 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows diffuse right-sided infiltrates. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Consult the hospital ethics committee</br>b. Begin noninvasive positive pressure ventilation by face mask</br>c. Continue oxygen by face mask and administer intravenous morphine for comfort</br>d. Intubate and begin mechanical ventilation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a.  Consult the hospital ethics committee  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Acute problem: high-fever, productive cough, diffuse R-sided infiltrates → severe community-acquired pneumonia/possible ARDS.<br /></br>• Gas exchange: PaO₂ 42 mm Hg on 100 % FiO₂ → life-threatening hypoxemic respiratory failure.<br /></br>• Decision-making capacity: “lethargic and unable to communicate” → lacks capacity.<br /></br>• Advance directive: “No intubation or mechanical ventilation unless he has an acute illness with a reasonable chance of recovery to a good quality of life.”<br /></br>  – Clause requires interpretation (“reasonable chance,” “good quality of life”).<br /></br>  – No surrogate decision maker is mentioned.<br /></br>• Ethical conflict: Clinically, intubation could reverse a likely treatable illness, but we are uncertain whether that meets the patient’s own criteria.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine decision-making capacity. He lacks it.<br /></br>2. Identify any written or oral advance directive. One exists but contains conditional language.<br /></br>3. If directive is ambiguous or no legally authorized surrogate is immediately available, obtain ethics consultation before providing or withholding a potentially life-saving treatment.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When an incapacitated patient’s directive is open to interpretation and no surrogate can speak for him, the next step is to involve the hospital ethics committee (or similar resource) to clarify the patient’s wishes and guide care.  </p></br><p>Documentation (real-world): incapacity assessment, contents of advance directive, attempts to reach family, reason for ethics consult.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>Community-acquired pneumonia can produce an inflammatory exudate → V/Q mismatch + shunt → hypoxemia refractory to supplemental O₂. If widespread, fulfills criteria for acute hypoxemic respiratory failure/ARDS.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Intubation + mechanical ventilation with low tidal volumes is standard for severe hypoxemia, but ethically permissible only if consistent with the patient’s goals.<br /></br>• Non-invasive positive-pressure ventilation (NIPPV) is still a form of mechanical ventilation and is often contraindicated in patients who are obtunded (aspiration risk).<br /></br>• Comfort-only measures (oxygen + morphine) are appropriate when the patient has clearly refused escalation or has end-stage disease with no reversible process. That is not yet established here.<br /></br>Ethics consult helps decide which of these divergent paths honors the patient’s expressed values.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“CAPACITY → DIRECTIVE → SURROGATE → ETHICS.”<br /></br>If patient lacks capacity and directive is unclear AND no surrogate = call ethics BEFORE acting.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Begin non-invasive positive-pressure ventilation<br /></br>• Still mechanical ventilation. Directive ambiguity remains; patient is obtunded (contraindication).<br /></br>c. Continue oxygen by face mask + IV morphine for comfort<br /></br>• Converts care to purely palliative without confirmation that this aligns with the patient’s conditional wishes; skips due process.<br /></br>d. Intubate and begin mechanical ventilation<br /></br>• May indeed help, but could violate the directive if later judged “unreasonable chance” or “poor quality of life.” Requires clarification first.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Assuming that any potentially reversible illness automatically nullifies a DNR/DNI clause. Always verify the exact wording and involve ethics or surrogates when the wording leaves room for interpretation.</p>
115. A 77-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-day history of passing air and fecal material during urination. He has a long-standing history of intermittent constipation. His last examination was 20 years ago. He has smoked two packs of cigarettes daily for 55 years. He is 183 cm (6 ft) tall and weighs 70 kg (155 lb); BMI is 21 kg/m2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Abdominal examination shows mild distention. Bowel sounds are decreased. Rectal examination shows a diffusely enlarged prostate. There is no stool in the rectal vault. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin         14 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count    9600/mm3</br>Platelet count     444,000/mm3</br>Urine</br>    WBC            >100/hpf</br>    Flora          mixed</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's symptoms?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Appendiceal abscess</br>b. Diverticular disease</br>c. Inflammatory bowel disease</br>d. Prostate cancer</br>e. Transitional cell carcinoma of the bladder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Diverticular disease (colonic diverticulitis leading to a colovesical fistula)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Passing air and fecal material during urination” = pneumaturia + fecaluria → classic for an enterovesical (colovesical) fistula.<br /></br>• Age 77, chronic constipation → strong risk for diverticulosis/diverticulitis.<br /></br>• Mixed flora &amp; &gt;100 WBC/hpf in urine → polymicrobial urinary tract infection with colonic organisms, another clue for colovesical communication.<br /></br>• No systemic inflammatory signs or Crohn history, making IBD less likely.<br /></br>• Enlarged prostate is incidental (very common at this age).  </p></br><p>Key Pattern: Older adult + constipation history + pneumaturia/fecaluria + polymicrobial UTI = diverticular colovesical fistula until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify symptom complex: pneumaturia &amp; fecaluria → bowel–bladder fistula.<br /></br>2. Ask “What diseases commonly create a tract between colon and bladder?”<br /></br>   – #1 cause in Western countries: sigmoid diverticulitis (≈ 65–75% of cases).<br /></br>   – Crohn disease, cancer, radiation, trauma, appendiceal abscess are far less common.<br /></br>3. Match demographics: elderly man with chronic constipation fits diverticular disease.<br /></br>4. Supporting data: polymicrobial urine &amp; recurrent UTI signs.<br /></br>Take-home: In an older patient, pneumaturia = diverticular fistula until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real-world): note pneumaturia, fecaluria, chronic constipation history, plan for CT abdomen/pelvis with oral/rectal contrast and surgery referral.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Diverticulosis = herniation of colonic mucosa through muscularis at vasa recta (sigmoid common).<br /></br>• Recurrent inflammation (diverticulitis) → pericolic abscess → erosion into adjacent bladder wall → fistulous tract → colovesical fistula.<br /></br>• Air &amp; stool track from high-pressure colon to low-pressure bladder → pneumaturia, fecaluria, recurrent polymicrobial UTIs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management (high-yield)<br /></br>1. CT scan with oral/rectal contrast or CT cystography: shows contrast or air in bladder, thickened sigmoid wall, tract.<br /></br>2. Cystoscopy may show fistula opening.<br /></br>3. Definitive Tx: elective surgical resection of diseased sigmoid with fistula takedown, often with primary anastomosis ± temporary diversion.<br /></br>4. Pre-op antibiotics for UTI (e.g., IV piperacillin-tazobactam then tailored to cultures).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Old man blowing bubbles in the toilet” → bubbles (air) in urine = diverticular colovesical fistula.<br /></br>Pneumaturia mnemonic: P.I.D.C.<br /></br>• P – Post-op/trauma<br /></br>• I – IBD (Crohn)<br /></br>• D – Diverticulitis (most common) ← remember “D”ominant cause<br /></br>• C – Cancer (colon, bladder)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Appendiceal abscess<br /></br>   • Usually affects younger patients, follows acute appendicitis in RLQ. Fistulas more often go to skin or adjacent small bowel, not bladder.<br /></br>   • No RLQ pain, fever, or appendicitis history here.</p></br><p>c. Inflammatory bowel disease (Crohn disease)<br /></br>   • Crohn fistulas occur, but average onset is teens–40s; look for chronic diarrhea, perianal disease, weight loss—none present.<br /></br>   • Age and constipation history fit diverticulitis better.</p></br><p>d. Prostate cancer<br /></br>   • Causes obstructive urinary symptoms or hematuria, not pneumaturia/fecaluria.<br /></br>   • Enlarged prostate here is likely benign prostatic hyperplasia (BPH), and prostate malignancy rarely erodes into colon.</p></br><p>e. Transitional cell carcinoma of the bladder<br /></br>   • Presents with painless gross hematuria.<br /></br>   • Tumor invasion can create fistulas, but pneumaturia/fecaluria are late findings and hematuria would dominate.<br /></br>   • No hematuria noted; mixed flora UTI points to bowel source, not cancer.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In an elderly patient, the triad of pneumaturia, fecaluria, and polymicrobial UTIs is virtually pathognomonic for a colovesical fistula, and the leading etiology is diverticular disease of the sigmoid colon.</p>
116. A 25-year-old man comes to the physician 2 hours after the sudden onset of sharp right buttock pain that radiates to the back of his knee. The pain worsens when he sits for a prolonged period or drives. He is able to walk. Use of over-the-counter ibuprofen has provided mild relief. He has no history of serious illness and takes no other medications. On examination, ipsilateral and contralateral straight-leg raise testing is positive. Muscle strength is normal throughout. Sensation is intact throughout. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>b. MRI of the lumbosacral spine</br>c. Myelography</br>d. X-ray of the lumbosacral spine</br>e. No further testing is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No further testing is indicated  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues<br /></br>• 25-year-old otherwise healthy man → very low pre-test probability of malignancy, fracture, infection, spinal stenosis<br /></br>• Acute onset (2 h) of sharp buttock pain radiating down the posterior thigh (“sciatica”) → classic for lumbar disk herniation with L5–S1 nerve-root irritation<br /></br>• Pain worsens with prolonged sitting/driving → flexed spine increases disk pressure, typical disk pain clue<br /></br>• Positive straight-leg-raise on both the symptomatic (ipsilateral) and opposite (crossed) leg → highly sensitive for disk herniation, but …<br /></br>• NORMAL neurologic exam (motor strength and sensory intact, no bowel/bladder or saddle anesthesia) → no red-flag neurologic deficit  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach<br /></br>1. Identify likely diagnosis: acute lumbosacral radiculopathy from a herniated disk.<br /></br>2. Check for “red flags” that would mandate immediate imaging:<br /></br>   – Age &gt;50 or &lt;20?  NO (25 y)<br /></br>   – History of cancer, weight loss, fever, IV drugs, immunosuppression?  NO<br /></br>   – Trauma or osteoporosis?  NO<br /></br>   – Progressive/severe motor deficit, cauda-equina syndrome?  NO<br /></br>3. With no red flags, first-line management is conservative (NSAIDs, heat, activity as tolerated, brief course of physical therapy). Imaging or electrodiagnostics are deferred until pain persists ≥6 weeks or new deficits appear.  </p></br><p>Take-home point: In routine acute low-back pain with or without mild radicular symptoms but no alarming features, the correct “test” is no test—treat empirically and re-evaluate. Document counseling on red-flag symptoms and follow-up plan.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Herniated nucleus pulposus compresses the traversing nerve root (e.g., L5 at L4–L5 disk). Pain before weakness/sensory loss is common. Straight-leg raise stretches the sciatic nerve root, reproducing pain. Most cases improve spontaneously within 4–6 weeks as inflammation subsides.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Initial therapy<br /></br>• NSAIDs (ibuprofen, naproxen)<br /></br>• Maintain activity; avoid prolonged rest<br /></br>• Heat/ice, optional short course oral steroids or muscle relaxants<br /></br>• Physical therapy exercises (McKenzie extension) if pain persists<br /></br>Escalate only if:<br /></br>• Red flags present → MRI or CT myelogram urgently<br /></br>• Persistent pain &gt;6 weeks despite therapy → MRI to plan epidural steroid or surgery<br /></br>• Progressive neurologic deficit → urgent MRI ± surgery  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“4-6-50 Rule” for acute back pain:<br /></br>4 red-flags (cancer/infection, fracture, cauda-equina, major neuro deficit) → image now.<br /></br>6 weeks → image later if still hurting.<br /></br>50 years old → age when routine x-ray/MRI becomes reasonable sooner.<br /></br>Everything else → no imaging.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Electromyography &amp; nerve-conduction studies (EMG/NCS) – Used to localize chronic radiculopathy (&gt;3 wk) or peripheral neuropathy; not helpful acutely and doesn’t change initial management.<br /></br>b. MRI lumbosacral spine – Gold-standard to visualize disk herniation but over-ordered; high false-positive rate in asymptomatic young adults. Reserved for red-flags or persistent symptoms ≥6 weeks.<br /></br>c. Myelography – Invasive CT contrast study now largely replaced by MRI; reserved when MRI contraindicated (e.g., pacemaker) and strong suspicion of cord/dural lesion.<br /></br>d. Plain x-ray lumbosacral spine – Good for fracture, spondylolisthesis, tumor, or ankylosing spondylitis, but offers no information on disks or nerves; unnecessary without trauma or age-related degeneration.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Ordering imaging “just to know” in a young patient with classic sciatica but no danger signs—leads to incidental findings, patient anxiety, and increased cost without benefit.</p>
117. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of an 8-month history of irregular menstrual periods, acne, and facial hair. Menarche was at the age of 12 years. Menses occur at irregular 35- to 42-day intervals; they last 1 week, and the flow is heavy. Moderate cramps on the first 2 days are relieved with ibuprofen therapy. The patient had recurrent urinary tract infections until the age of 10 years. She has no other history of serious illness and takes no other medications. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 77 kg (170 lb); BMI is 29 kg/m2. Her temperature is 36.9°C (98.4°F), pulse is 78/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 128/84 mm Hg. Examination shows scattered papules, pustules, and comedones over the face. There is sparse, coarse hair over the chin. The neck is supple; there is no thyromegaly. Breast examination shows no masses. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 5. Serum studies are most likely to show an increased concentration of which of the following?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Follicle-stimulating hormone</br>b. 11α-Hydroxyprogesterone</br>c. 21α-Hydroxylprogesterone</br>d. Luteinizing hormone</br>e. Thyroid-stimulating hormone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Luteinizing hormone (LH)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “16-year-old girl,” “irregular 35- to 42-day cycles,” “heavy flow,” “acne,” “facial hair,” “BMI 29 kg/m².”<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: Obesity, clinical hyperandrogenism (acne, hirsutism), chronic oligomenorrhea.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: Normal age at menarche, no virilization (deep voice, clitoromegaly), no thyroid mass or galactorrhea.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: Classic triad of polycystic ovary syndrome (PCOS) – (1) oligo-/anovulation, (2) hyperandrogenism, (3) ± polycystic ovaries/obesity.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. PCOS is driven by (a) ovarian theca cell hyper-responsiveness to LH and (b) peripheral insulin resistance.<br /></br>2. Excess LH → ↑ ovarian androgen production (testosterone, androstenedione) → acne &amp; hirsutism.<br /></br>3. LH rises disproportionately to FSH, producing a typical LH:FSH ratio ≥ 2:1.<br /></br>Thus, the serum analyte most likely to be elevated is LH.</p></br><p>Take-home: In any adolescent/young woman with chronic anovulatory cycles + hyperandrogenism, think PCOS and expect an elevated LH (and a high LH:FSH ratio).</p></br><p>Documentation point (real life): Record menstrual pattern, hirsutism score, BMI, BP, fasting glucose/lipids, and order labs (pregnancy test, TSH, prolactin, LH, FSH, total &amp; free testosterone, ± 17-OH-progesterone).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (PCOS)<br /></br>• Etiology: Multifactorial; genetic predisposition + insulin resistance.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Insulin resistance → hyperinsulinemia → suppresses hepatic sex-hormone–binding globulin (↑ free androgens) and stimulates theca cells (↑ androgen output). LH further amplifies theca androgen production. Inadequate FSH prevents granulosa aromatization → anovulation &amp; cystic follicles.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Oligo/anovulation, infertility, hirsutism/acne, obesity, acanthosis nigricans, ↑ risk endometrial hyperplasia/cancer.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>1. Weight loss/exercise (even 5–10% ↓ weight restores ovulation).<br /></br>2. Combined estrogen-progestin oral contraceptives (COCs) to regulate cycles &amp; protect endometrium.<br /></br>3. If pregnancy desired: letrozole (first-line ovulation induction), clomiphene, or metformin.<br /></br>4. Hirsutism: COCs ± spironolactone (after 6 months on COCs, assure contraception).<br /></br>Diagnostics: Pregnancy test → TSH, prolactin → LH/FSH ratio, total testosterone, DHEAS (rule out adrenal tumor), 17-OH-progesterone (screen CAH), pelvic US (string-of-pearls ovaries).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “POLY” for PCOS: P = Periods irregular, O = Obesity, L = LH↑, Y = hYperandrogenism.<br /></br>• LH:FSH “Two-to-One = Twenty-one” (age many patients present ~21) – easy recall of LH dominance.<br /></br>• Visual cue: A see-saw with LH sitting heavier than FSH.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Follicle-stimulating hormone (FSH)<br /></br>   – In PCOS, FSH is normal or low-normal because GnRH favors LH-pulse frequency. An elevated FSH would suggest ovarian failure (↑ FSH, ↑ LH, low estrogen) not seen here.  </p></br><p>b. 11α-Hydroxyprogesterone<br /></br>   – Would be high in 11-β-hydroxylase deficiency (a rarer congenital adrenal hyperplasia, CAH) causing virilization, hypertension, hypokalemia. This patient lacks HTN and severe virilization.  </p></br><p>c. 21α-Hydroxyprogesterone (17-hydroxyprogesterone)<br /></br>   – Elevated in classic 21-hydroxylase CAH presenting in infancy with salt-wasting or childhood with early virilization. Adolescents may have non-classic CAH, but cycles are usually shorter (&lt;21 d) and virilization more pronounced.  </p></br><p>e. Thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH)<br /></br>   – Hypothyroidism can cause menorrhagia and oligomenorrhea, but acne/hirsutism and an elevated testosterone are not typical. Normal exam (no goiter) and lack of other hypothyroid signs argue against it.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>PCOS is characterized by an elevated LH and a high LH:FSH ratio. Detecting this lab pattern in a patient with obesity, irregular menses, and mild hirsutism clinches the diagnosis and guides management.</p>
118. A 49-year-old woman comes to the physician for preoperative examination before she undergoes cholecystectomy. During the past 3 years, she has had recurrent cholecystitis and multiple gallstones. She has not had shortness of breath, difficulty swallowing, or hoarseness. She has rheumatoid arthritis with morning stiffness and widespread joint swelling. Her medications are methotrexate, folic acid, and prednisone. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows nodules over the extensor aspect of the upper extremities. There is swelling of the wrists, metacarpophalangeal joints of the hands, and knees; there are swan neck deformities of the fingers. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management to decrease this patient’s risk for operative complications?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Discontinuation of methotrexate</br>b. Discontinuation of prednisone</br>c. Indirect laryngoscopy</br>d. Pulmonary function testing</br>e. X-rays of the cervical spine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. X-rays of the cervical spine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key words: long-standing rheumatoid arthritis (RA), nodules, swan-neck deformities → indicates severe, erosive, multi-joint disease.<br /></br>• Surgery under general anesthesia → requires endotracheal intubation with neck manipulation.<br /></br>• No current neck pain/neurologic symptoms – BUT the question asks for the step that will “decrease the patient’s risk for operative complications,” not what she is currently symptomatic for.<br /></br>• Classic high-yield association: chronic RA → atlanto-axial (C1–C2) instability that may be clinically silent yet can cause acute spinal cord compression if the neck is hyper-extended during intubation.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify high-risk comorbidity (RA) in a surgical patient.<br /></br>2. Recall common RA extra-articular complication relevant to airway management (atlanto-axial subluxation).<br /></br>3. Determine preventive action: obtain flexion/extension cervical spine radiographs pre-op.<br /></br>4. If instability is present → special intubation techniques (fiber-optic) or neurosurgical stabilization first.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Every patient with long-standing, deforming RA who is to undergo general anesthesia should have cervical spine imaging to screen for atlanto-axial instability.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic synovitis in RA erodes the transverse ligament and odontoid process.<br /></br>• Result → excessive anterior movement of C1 on C2 when neck is flexed/extended.<br /></br>• Intubation can convert subclinical laxity into acute spinal cord compression → quadriparesis or respiratory arrest.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Flexion/extension lateral cervical spine X-rays (or CT/MRI if X-ray equivocal) identify &gt;3 mm anterior atlanto-dens interval.<br /></br>• If unstable: halo or posterior fusion before elective surgery; intubate with inline stabilization or fiber-optic scope.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“RA patients need a C-SPINE film Before you C-SPINE them (Collar + Scope).”<br /></br>Think “RA = Rheumatoid Atlanto-axial.”  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Discontinuation of methotrexate – Low-dose MTX is usually continued for elective surgery; stopping it increases RA flare risk without clear peri-op benefit.<br /></br>b. Discontinuation of prednisone – Abrupt cessation risks adrenal crisis; instead stress-dose steroids are given if long-term use.<br /></br>c. Indirect laryngoscopy – Would screen for cricoarytenoid arthritis causing hoarseness/airway obstruction; patient has no hoarseness or stridor.<br /></br>d. Pulmonary function testing – Consider if symptomatic interstitial lung disease or severe MTX toxicity; no respiratory complaints, normal vitals.  </p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Silent atlanto-axial instability is common in long-standing RA and can convert to catastrophic spinal cord injury during intubation; pre-operative cervical spine imaging is mandatory.</p>
119. A 32-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician because of spontaneous discharge from her nipples during the past 3 months. She discontinued breast-feeding her child 1 year ago. She takes no medications. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. On physical examination, a milky white discharge can be expressed from both nipples. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Her fasting serum prolactin concentration is 40 ng/mL. The most appropriate next step in diagnosis is measurement of which of the following serum concentrations?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a.  Cortisol</br>b.  Dopamine</br>c.  Estrogen</br>d.  Gonadotropin-releasing hormone</br>e.  Thyroid-stimulating hormone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key finding: Bilateral milky nipple discharge ≥3 mo after cessation of breast-feeding → galactorrhea is pathologic until proved otherwise.<br /></br>• Serum prolactin = 40 ng/mL (upper limit of normal ≈ 20–25 ng/mL). Mild, not massive.<br /></br>• Menses still q28 d → ovarian–pituitary axis intact; points away from large prolactinoma.<br /></br>• No culprit drugs, no visual field defects, no chest wall trauma.  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Mildly” elevated prolactin (&lt;100 ng/mL) + galactorrhea → think secondary causes (pregnancy, hypothyroidism, renal failure, medications). Pregnancy is unlikely because cycles are regular; next easiest, high-yield screen is hypothyroidism → order TSH.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to galactorrhea:<br /></br>1. Rule out pregnancy (β-hCG).<br /></br>2. Check TSH to evaluate primary hypothyroidism.<br /></br>   • Low T4 → ↑TRH from hypothalamus → TRH stimulates prolactin release → mild prolactin rise and galactorrhea.<br /></br>3. If TSH normal, look for medication causes, renal insufficiency, then pituitary MRI if prolactin markedly elevated or other signs.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Always evaluate thyroid function early in any unexplained hyperprolactinemia because hypothyroidism is common, easily missed, and easily treated.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Galactorrhea with mild hyperprolactinemia; plan – check serum TSH and free T4 to rule out primary hypothyroidism.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• TRH (thyrotropin-releasing hormone) from hypothalamus stimulates BOTH TSH and prolactin secretion.<br /></br>• Primary hypothyroidism → ↓T4/T3 → loss of negative feedback → ↑TRH → ↑TSH &amp; ↑prolactin → galactorrhea ± menstrual changes.<br /></br>• Prolactinomas typically produce prolactin &gt;200 ng/mL and often cause amenorrhea, infertility, headaches, visual field loss.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Snapshot<br /></br>If TSH elevated / T4 low → start levothyroxine; normalization of thyroid function usually normalizes prolactin and stops galactorrhea.<br /></br>If TSH normal → pursue other work-up (pregnancy test if not already done, drug review, kidney function, pituitary MRI).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Hi-TRH = Hi-Prolactin.”<br /></br>Think “THyroid first in THree letters: T-S-H” when prolactin is only modestly high.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cortisol – Adrenal insufficiency does not raise prolactin. ACTH/cortisol axis unrelated to galactorrhea.<br /></br>b. Dopamine – Dopamine inhibits prolactin but serum dopamine levels are not clinically measured; not useful diagnostically.<br /></br>c. Estrogen – Estrogen can increase prolactin but estrogen assays are not first-line and menstrual cycles are normal, arguing against estrogen excess.<br /></br>d. Gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) – Not measured directly in serum; pulsatile GnRH drives LH/FSH, but routine assays are not clinically performed and GnRH does not explain isolated mild prolactin rise.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Mild hyperprolactinemia with galactorrhea should prompt thyroid studies before jumping to pituitary imaging—primary hypothyroidism is a common, reversible cause via TRH-mediated prolactin stimulation.</p>
120. A 57-year-old man comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. One year ago, he sustained a myocardial infarction. He has coronary artery disease, hypertension, and type 2 diabetes mellitus. His medications are atorvastatin, lisinopril, metformin, metoprolol, and aspirin. He has smoked one-half pack of cigarettes daily for 25 years. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 97 kg (213 lb); BMI is 31 kg/m2. He feels well. His pulse is 62/min, and blood pressure is 145/78 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Laboratory studies show a hemoglobin A1c of 6.5% and serum LDL-cholesterol concentration of 110 mg/dL. Serum electrolyte, urea nitrogen, glucose, and creatinine concentrations are within the reference ranges. The physician recommends smoking cessation. Addition of which of the following is the most appropriate next step in pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Clonidine</br>b. Exenatide</br>c. Hydrochlorothiazide</br>d. Rosiglitazone</br>e. Sitagliptin</br>f. Spironolactone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Hydrochlorothiazide  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Persistent hypertension: BP 145/78 mm Hg on two proven, evidence-based agents for CAD/DM (lisinopril and metoprolol).<br /></br>• Target BP in a patient with diabetes and established ASCVD (atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease) is &lt;130/80 mm Hg (ACC/AHA, ADA).<br /></br>• Glycemic control is adequate (A1c = 6.5% on metformin).<br /></br>• Lipid goal is not yet met, but no LDL-lowering choice is offered.  </p></br><p>Key “pertinent positive” → sub-optimally controlled BP despite ACE-I + β-blocker → needs an additional first-line antihypertensive.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Confirm need to treat: BP &gt; goal in high-risk patient.<br /></br>2. Review current regimen:<br /></br>   – ACE inhibitor (lisinopril) ✔️<br /></br>   – β-blocker (metoprolol) ✔️ (post-MI)<br /></br>3. Choose guideline-preferred add-on: thiazide diuretic or calcium-channel blocker.<br /></br>4. Options offered → Hydrochlorothiazide is the only first-line add-on present.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In patients with DM/CAD whose BP remains above goal on an ACE-I (or ARB) ± β-blocker, the next evidence-based agent is a thiazide diuretic (chlorthalidone or HCTZ).  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Essential hypertension, uncontrolled on current therapy; will add thiazide diuretic. Will re-check BP and basic metabolic panel in 2 weeks.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Thiazides ↓ intravascular volume acutely and, chronically, reduce systemic vascular resistance by ↓ sodium content of arteriolar smooth muscle. In diabetes and CAD they:<br /></br>• Reduce stroke and MI risk.<br /></br>• Provide synergistic BP lowering with ACE-Is.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Monitoring<br /></br>• Hydrochlorothiazide 12.5–25 mg daily.<br /></br>• Check basic metabolic panel in 1–2 weeks (risk of hyponatremia, hypokalemia, mild ↑Ca²⁺, ↑uric acid).<br /></br>• Goal BP &lt;130/80 mm Hg.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABCD” for first-line HTN drugs:<br /></br>A – ACE/ARB<br /></br>B – β-blocker (post-MI, HFrEF)<br /></br>C – CCB<br /></br>D – Diuretic (thiazide)<br /></br>If already on A + B and still high, go to C or D (most often D).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Clonidine – Central α₂-agonist; used for resistant hypertension or hypertensive urgency; causes sedation, rebound HTN—NOT first- or second-line.  </p></br><p>b. Exenatide – GLP-1 receptor agonist; good for glycemic control and CV risk reduction, but BP, not A1c, is the uncontrolled parameter. Therapy escalation for diabetes isn’t indicated at A1c 6.5 %.  </p></br><p>d. Rosiglitazone – Thiazolidinedione; ↑ fluid retention, weight gain, HF risk; no BP benefit.  </p></br><p>e. Sitagliptin – DPP-4 inhibitor; same reasoning as exenatide but no proven CV benefit, and glycemic control is adequate.  </p></br><p>f. Spironolactone – Mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist; excellent add-on for resistant HTN or HFrEF with LVEF ≤ 35 %, but patient doesn’t have heart failure and is not yet “resistant” (needs a diuretic first).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept to Remember<br /></br>When BP remains &gt;130/80 mm Hg in a diabetic or CAD patient already on an ACE/ARB (±β-blocker), add a thiazide diuretic (or CCB) before moving to less-validated agents.</p>
121. A 60-year-old woman with osteoarthritis of the right knee comes to the office to discuss treatment options, including total knee replacement. Medications are naproxen and 81-mg aspirin. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows edema of the right knee; range of motion of the right knee is decreased. Results of laboratory studies are within the reference ranges. The patient asks the physician about the success and complication rates associated with total knee replacement at the local university hospital. Which of the following types of studies is most appropriate for the physician to reference to answer this patient’s question?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Case-control study  </br>b. Cross-sectional study  </br>c. Institutional case series  </br>d. Randomized controlled trial</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Institutional case series  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key question: “success and complication rates associated with total knee replacement at the local university hospital.”<br /></br>• Focus is LOCAL, procedure-specific outcomes that have already occurred in patients treated at that same institution.<br /></br>• No exposure vs. non-exposure comparison is requested; the patient simply wants historical results.<br /></br>• Pertinent negative: nothing in the stem implies the hospital is comparing TKRs to another procedure or randomizing patients.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify information needed → Past performance (frequency of success/complications) for one procedure at one site.<br /></br>Step 2: Choose study design that describes consecutive cases and their outcomes without a comparison group → case series.<br /></br>Step 3: Specify “institutional” because the interest is confined to that hospital’s experience.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When you need descriptive statistics about outcomes of a single intervention within one institution, an institutional case series is the appropriate design.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>(Not central to the design question, but briefly) Osteoarthritis → cartilage degeneration → pain, ↓ROM → TKR considered when conservative therapy fails. Outcomes of TKR measured by pain relief, function, complication rates (infection, DVT, prosthesis failure).  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• First-line OA management: NSAIDs (naproxen), weight loss, PT, intra-articular steroids.<br /></br>• Definitive option: Total knee arthroplasty. Success measured by validated scores (WOMAC, knee society scores), complications captured in peri-op records.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CASE series = Collection of All Same Events”<br /></br>Add the tag: “Institutional” when the data come from a single hospital/clinic.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Case-control study<br /></br>   – Compares patients with an outcome (e.g., TKR complications) to those without to find risk factors. Requires matched controls and odds ratios—not needed for simply quoting hospital success rates.  </p></br><p>b. Cross-sectional study<br /></br>   – Measures prevalence of an exposure or disease at one point in time across a population. Provides snapshots, not longitudinal outcome data for a procedure.  </p></br><p>d. Randomized controlled trial (RCT)<br /></br>   – Compares interventions prospectively with random allocation (e.g., TKR vs. unicompartmental knee). Overkill for describing existing institutional results; also impractical because the question is retrospective.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Thinking “higher level” evidence (RCT) is always better. The “best” design depends on the question. Descriptive institutional performance data ≠ comparative efficacy question.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: For historical outcome data about one procedure at one hospital, reference an institutional case series.</p>
122. A 72-year-old woman comes to the office with her son because of a "strange mole" on her back that she first noticed 6 months ago. The patient's son says he thinks it has enlarged during the past month. The patient says the mole is mildly itchy but not painful, and she has not had bleeding from the area. She has not had any weight loss or changes in appetite. Medical history is unremarkable, and she takes no medications. The patient has spent a lot of time outside by her son's new swimming pool during the past summer months. Vital signs are within normal limits. A photograph of the lesion is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate diagnostic study at this time?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Culture of the lesion</br>b. Full-thickness biopsy of the lesion</br>c. KOH testing of a scraping of the lesion</br>d. Shave biopsy of the lesion</br>e. No diagnostic study is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Full-thickness (excisional) biopsy of the lesion  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Strange mole,” “enlarged during the past month,” “mildly itchy” → evolution is the most prognostic “E” in the ABCDE criteria for melanoma (Asymmetry, Border irregularity, Color variation, Diameter &gt;6 mm, Evolution).<br /></br>• 72-year-old with significant cumulative sun exposure (“spent a lot of time outside”) → classic risk profile for cutaneous melanoma.<br /></br>• No systemic symptoms and a solitary pigmented lesion → focus is on proper dermatologic evaluation rather than staging work-up at this point.<br /></br>Key takeaway: Suspicious pigmented lesion = rule out melanoma first with the correct type of biopsy.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify malignant potential (ABCDE positives).<br /></br>2. Next step in management of any lesion that may be melanoma is HISTOLOGIC confirmation.<br /></br>3. Gold-standard technique = complete excisional (full-thickness) biopsy with 1–3 mm margins so Breslow depth can be measured accurately.<br /></br>4. Shave or partial biopsies risk transecting the tumor → inaccurate depth measurement → improper staging and management.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In suspected melanoma, always perform a full-thickness excisional biopsy.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): location, size, ABCDE positives, photo, consent for excision, anesthesia used, specimen orientation, sent in formalin “rule out melanoma.”</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Melanoma arises from malignant transformation of melanocytes (neural crest origin). UV-B–induced DNA damage + failure of repair → mutations (BRAF V600E most common). Breslow depth (vertical growth) is the single most important prognostic factor. Hence an intact specimen from epidermis down to subcutaneous fat is mandatory.</p></br><p>Risk factors: intense intermittent sunburns, fair skin, &gt;50 nevi, family or personal history, dysplastic nevi, immunosuppression.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>Diagnostic:<br /></br>• Full-thickness excisional biopsy (≤2 cm lesion: elliptic excision with 1–3 mm margin).<br /></br>• If lesion &gt;2 cm or in cosmetically sensitive area (face/digits) → narrow excisional or deep punch that includes entire depth.</p></br><p>Post-diagnosis:<br /></br>• Wide local excision margins based on Breslow depth (1 cm for ≤1 mm depth, 2 cm for 1–2 mm, etc.).<br /></br>• Sentinel lymph node biopsy for depth ≥0.8–1 mm or ulceration.<br /></br>• Adjuvant therapy for advanced disease (immune checkpoint inhibitors—pembrolizumab, nivolumab; BRAF/MEK inhibitors if mutation present).</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Melanoma? <strong>CUT IT OUT</strong> full-thickness.”<br /></br>• CUT = Complete, Unbroken, Through dermis.<br /></br>ABCDE + E = “E for Excisional biopsy.”</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Culture of the lesion<br /></br>• Appropriate for suspected infection (bacterial, fungal) but not for pigmented lesions. No exudate or cellulitis present.</p></br><p>c. KOH scraping<br /></br>• Used to identify dermatophytes (e.g., tinea) or Candida. A changing pigmented nevus does not look like fungal infection (no scaling annular plaque).</p></br><p>d. Shave biopsy<br /></br>• Acceptable for raised, superficial lesions (seborrheic keratosis, BCC) but contraindicated for suspected melanoma because it can transect the deepest malignant cells, leading to underestimation of Breslow depth and stage.</p></br><p>e. No diagnostic study<br /></br>• Observation risky; delay in melanoma diagnosis worsens prognosis (depth increases at ≈0.1 mm/month).</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Choosing a shave biopsy because it is quicker and widely used for other skin cancers. Always think “Breslow depth” → need full thickness.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Any lesion suspicious for melanoma requires an immediate full-thickness excisional biopsy to permit accurate histologic diagnosis and Breslow depth measurement, guiding definitive therapy and prognosis.</p>
123. A 27-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of fever, pain with urination, and progressive generalized weakness. She has systemic lupus erythematosus complicated by nephritis. Current medications include prednisone and cyclophosphamide. Her temperature is 39.4°C (103°F), pulse is 122/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 88/62 mm Hg. Examination shows warm, flushed skin. There is moderate suprapubic and right flank tenderness. Her leukocyte count is 18,000/mm^3 (80% segmented neutrophils, 5% bands, 1% eosinophils, 1% basophils, 10% lymphocytes, and 3% monocytes). Urinalysis shows 100 WBC/hpf. A Gram stain shows numerous gram-negative rods. Intravenous administration of piperacillin and tazobactam and 2 liters of 0.9% saline is started. Thirty minutes later, her pulse is 124/min, and blood pressure is 92/68 mm Hg. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administration of ciprofloxacin</br>b. Administration of gentamicin</br>c. Administration of hydrocortisone</br>d. Administration of metoprolol</br>e. Cardioversion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Administration of hydrocortisone  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Fever, dysuria, flank pain → acute pyelonephritis/urosepsis (Gram-negative rods in urine).<br /></br>• Temp 39.4 °C, HR 122 → SIRS. BP 88/62 despite 2 L NS → septic (distributive) shock.<br /></br>• “Warm, flushed skin” = hallmark of early distributive shock.<br /></br>• Chronic prednisone + cyclophosphamide for SLE nephritis → long-term glucocorticoid therapy → hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis suppression.<br /></br>• 30 min after fluids + broad-spectrum antibiotics, BP remains low (92/68) → inadequate stress response likely due to relative adrenal insufficiency.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: chronic steroid use, persistent hypotension after fluid resuscitation.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no arrhythmia, no evidence of inadequate antibiotic coverage, no indication for rate control.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Recognize septic shock: infection + hypotension + ≥2 SIRS criteria.<br /></br>2. Initial management:<br /></br>   – Broad-spectrum antibiotics (piperacillin-tazobactam) ✅<br /></br>   – Aggressive IV crystalloid (30 mL/kg; 2 L just given) ✅<br /></br>3. If MAP &lt;65 mm Hg after fluids → look for vasopressor need or adrenal crisis.<br /></br>4. Chronic exogenous steroids → secondary adrenal insufficiency; patients cannot mount the cortisol surge needed for severe physiologic stress.<br /></br>5. Give stress-dose IV hydrocortisone (e.g., 100 mg bolus, then 50 mg q6h) to correct cortisol deficiency and improve vascular tone.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any patient on chronic glucocorticoids who develops refractory hypotension during severe illness should immediately receive stress-dose steroids.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Persistent hypotension after 2 L crystalloid in patient on chronic prednisone—concern for adrenal insufficiency; administered 100 mg IV hydrocortisone.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Exogenous glucocorticoids suppress CRH/ACTH → adrenal atrophy → impaired cortisol release.<br /></br>• In septic shock, cytokine-mediated vasodilation and capillary leak lower SVR; cortisol is needed to up-regulate α-adrenergic receptors and maintain vascular tone.<br /></br>• Relative adrenal insufficiency → vasopressor-resistant hypotension. Hydrocortisone rapidly restores responsiveness to catecholamines.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line septic shock sequence:<br /></br>1. Airway/O2, 2. IV fluids, 3. Broad-spectrum antibiotics, 4. Vasopressors (norepinephrine) if MAP &lt;65, 5. IV hydrocortisone if adrenal insufficiency suspected or shock refractory.<br /></br>Labs: draw baseline cortisol if time allows, but do NOT delay empiric steroids in unstable patient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PRESS” for chronic steroid users in septic shock:<br /></br>P – Persistent hypotension<br /></br>R – Recent steroid use<br /></br>E – Early antibiotics/fluids done<br /></br>S – Suspect adrenal insufficiency<br /></br>S – Stress-dose Steroids (hydrocortisone)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Ciprofloxacin – Redundant coverage; piperacillin-tazobactam already covers gram-negatives (incl. Pseudomonas). Antibiotic change not necessary within 30 min.<br /></br>b. Gentamicin – Adds nephrotoxicity (bad in lupus nephritis) and is not first-line escalation; BP problem, not coverage gap.<br /></br>d. Metoprolol – Would worsen hypotension by blunting sympathetic compensation. Indicated for rate control in tachyarrhythmias, not septic shock.<br /></br>e. Cardioversion – Reserved for unstable tachyarrhythmias (e.g., AF with RVR, VT). Patient is in sinus tachycardia secondary to shock, not an arrhythmia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In a septic, chronically steroid-treated patient who remains hypotensive after adequate fluids and antibiotics, immediately administer IV hydrocortisone to treat possible adrenal crisis and improve hemodynamics.</p>
124. A 72-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of progressive shortness of breath, nonproductive cough, and fatigue. During this period, he has had early satiety resulting in a 2.3-kg (5-lb) weight loss. He has no known sick contacts. He has a 20-year history of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease and a 10-year history of hypertension. Current medications are an inhaled bronchodilator and an oral thiazide diuretic. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 50 years. He is in mild respiratory distress. He is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 52 kg (115 lb); BMI is 19 kg/m2. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 103/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 130/90 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 87%. Examination shows no scleral icterus. There is jugular venous distention. Breath sounds are distant. There is a right ventricular heave and a loud S2. A grade 2/6 systolic murmur is heard best over the left sternal border. The liver is tender, smooth, and pulsatile. There is no peripheral edema. His hematocrit is 50%, leukocyte count is 4500/mm3, and platelet count is 200,000/mm3. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Digoxin therapy</br>b. Furosemide therapy</br>c. Incentive spirometry</br>d. Continuous positive airway pressure</br>e. Home oxygen therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Home oxygen therapy</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Older man with severe COPD (50-pack-year smoker) → chronic lung disease.<br /></br>• Room-air oxygen saturation 87 % (≤88 %) → meets hypoxemia criterion.<br /></br>• Physical signs of cor pulmonale: JVD, loud P2, RV heave, pulsatile tender liver, mild systolic murmur (tricuspid regurg).<br /></br>• Secondary polycythemia (Hct 50 %).<br /></br>Key pattern: COPD + chronic hypoxemia + evidence of pulmonary hypertension/right-heart strain.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Long-term (≥15 h/day) domiciliary oxygen is the only intervention proven to prolong life in COPD patients with chronic hypoxemia. Prescription criteria:<br /></br>• PaO₂ ≤55 mm Hg or SaO₂ ≤88 % on room air, OR<br /></br>• PaO₂ 56–59 mm Hg / SaO₂ 89 % PLUS pulmonary hypertension, peripheral edema, or Hct &gt;55 %.<br /></br>This patient’s SaO₂ is 87 % and he already has cor pulmonale and polycythemia, so immediate home oxygen is indicated.</p></br><p>Take-home: In COPD, once resting saturation falls to ≤88 % (or there is cor pulmonale), start long-term oxygen—this improves survival, exercise tolerance, and quality of life.</p></br><p>Documentation point: record resting SpO₂/ABG and indication for long-term oxygen (Medicare/insurance requirement).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Chronic alveolar hypoxia → hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction → pulmonary arterial remodeling → pulmonary hypertension → RV hypertrophy/failure (cor pulmonale). Hypoxia also increases erythropoietin → secondary polycythemia. Supplemental O₂ reverses vasoconstriction and drops pulmonary arterial pressures, reducing RV strain and improving survival.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• First-line treatment for stable cor pulmonale from COPD: long-term supplemental O₂.<br /></br>• Diuretics may relieve volume overload but do NOT alter disease course.<br /></br>• Digoxin has no mortality benefit in cor pulmonale due to COPD and may be harmful unless there is concurrent left-sided systolic dysfunction or atrial fibrillation.<br /></br>• CPAP treats obstructive sleep apnea, not daytime hypoxemia from COPD.<br /></br>• Incentive spirometry prevents post-operative atelectasis; irrelevant here.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“O₂ SAVES COPD lives”<br /></br>O₂ ≤88 % → Start O₂<br /></br>S aturation<br /></br>A bove 88? No ⇒ O₂<br /></br>V ascular strain (cor pulmonale)<br /></br>E rythrocytosis (Hct &gt;55 %)<br /></br>S urvival improved</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Digoxin therapy – Helpful for LV systolic failure or rate-control in AF. It does not improve survival or pulmonary pressures in isolated RV failure from COPD.<br /></br>b. Furosemide therapy – Would reduce peripheral edema/volume, but this patient is not fluid-overloaded (no edema). Does not address the underlying hypoxemia driving cor pulmonale.<br /></br>c. Incentive spirometry – Prevents/ treats atelectasis post-op; does not correct chronic COPD hypoxemia.<br /></br>d. Continuous positive airway pressure – Standard for obstructive sleep apnea; does not provide continuous high-flow O₂ nor does it address daytime hypoxemia from COPD.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Resting SpO₂ ≤88 % (or PaO₂ ≤55 mm Hg) in a COPD patient mandates long-term home oxygen, the only therapy shown to improve survival by correcting chronic hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction and preventing progression of cor pulmonale.</p>
125. Four days after admission to the hospital for treatment of a myocardial infarction, a 72-year-old woman has the sudden onset of shortness of breath and confusion. She has atrial fibrillation, type 2 diabetes mellitus, essential hypertension, and hypercholesterolemia. Her medications are diltiazem, quinapril, metformin, and simvastatin. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 50 years and has drunk one glass of wine daily for 52 years. She is anxious and oriented to person but not to place or time. Her temperature is 35°C (95°F), pulse is 116/min and irregularly irregular, respirations are 30/min, and blood pressure is 92/58 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 100% oxygen by face mask shows an oxygen saturation of 91%. The skin is cool to the touch. Examination shows jugular venous distention. Decreased breath sounds are heard in the lung bases bilaterally. Scattered expiratory wheezes and crackles are heard bilaterally. A rub is heard in the lower lobe of the left lung. There is 2+ pretibial and pedal edema bilaterally. Laboratory studies show:  </br>Hemoglobin                         11.6 g/dL  </br>Leukocyte count                  11,000/mm3  </br>Segmented neutrophils          65%  </br>Bands                                3%  </br>Lymphocytes                      30%  </br>Monocytes                         2%  </br>Serum  </br>Na+                                  136 mEq/L  </br>K+                                   3.7 mEq/L  </br>Cl–                                  105 mEq/L  </br>HCO3–                                14 mEq/L  </br>Glucose                             86 mg/dL  </br>Creatine kinase                   68 U/L  </br>Lactic acid                         3.6 mEq/L (N=0.5–2)  </br>Arterial blood gas analysis shows:  </br>pH                                    7.29  </br>Pco2                                 30 mm Hg  </br>Po2                                  64 mm Hg  </br>An ECG is shown. A chest x-ray shows cardiomegaly and pulmonary infiltrates, and pleural effusions bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Cardiac catheterization  </br>b. CT angiography  </br>c. Echocardiography  </br>d. Measurement of serum brain natriuretic peptide concentration  </br>e. Radionuclide myocardial scan</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Cardiac catheterization (placement of a pulmonary-artery [Swan-Ganz] catheter)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Time course: 4 days after an acute MI – classic window for mechanical or hemodynamic complications.<br /></br>• Sudden dyspnea + altered mentation → indicates acute decompensation/shock.<br /></br>• Vital signs: T 35 °C (cool), BP 92/58 mm Hg, RR 30, O₂ sat only 91 % on 100 % O₂ –‐ severe circulatory failure.<br /></br>• Physical exam: cool skin, jugular venous distention, pulmonary crackles &amp; effusions → elevated venous and left-sided filling pressures.<br /></br>• Labs/ABG: high lactate (3.6 mEq/L) and metabolic acidosis (HCO₃⁻ 14, pH 7.29) → tissue hypoperfusion.<br /></br>→ All point toward cardiogenic shock as the most likely etiology of the acute collapse.</p></br><p>Key negatives that make septic or hypovolemic shock less likely: hypothermia rather than fever, cold/clammy rather than warm extremities, clear bleeding history absent.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why the catheter is next)<br /></br>1. The patient is in undifferentiated shock after an MI; management hinges on knowing the exact hemodynamics.<br /></br>   • Cardiogenic shock = low cardiac output + high systemic vascular resistance + high pulmonary-capillary wedge pressure (PCWP).<br /></br>   • Mechanical complications (papillary-muscle rupture, ventricular-septal rupture, free-wall rupture/tamponade) each produce a different pressure profile (e.g., giant v-waves in MR, “step-up” in O₂ sat in RV for VSD, equalization of diastolic pressures in tamponade).<br /></br>2. A pulmonary-artery catheter provides immediate, quantitative data: cardiac output/index, PCWP, right-sided pressures, and mixed venous O₂ saturation—information that directly guides pressors, inotropes, afterload reduction, balloon pump, or emergency surgery.<br /></br>3. The patient is unstable (hypotensive, hypoxic); catheter insertion can be done at the bedside within minutes—safer and faster than CT and gives more actionable information than a screening test such as BNP.</p></br><p>Take-home core concept<br /></br>In a hemodynamically unstable post-MI patient, bedside pulmonary-artery catheterization is the fastest way to confirm cardiogenic shock, distinguish specific mechanical complications, and tailor immediate therapy.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real-life)<br /></br>• Rationale for emergent Swan-Ganz catheter to quantify filling pressures/output.<br /></br>• Shock vitals, lactate, ABG, CXR findings.<br /></br>• Rapid initiation of vasopressors/inotropes while awaiting catheter readings.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>Post-MI cardiogenic shock can result from:<br /></br>• Severe LV systolic dysfunction (large infarct).<br /></br>• Papillary-muscle rupture → acute severe mitral regurgitation → massive ↑ LA &amp; pulmonary pressures.<br /></br>• Ventricular-septal rupture → left-to-right shunt → RV overload and ↓ systemic output.<br /></br>• Free-wall rupture → tamponade → obstructive form of cardiogenic shock.<br /></br>All share: ↓ stroke volume → ↓ CO → hypotension → reflex ↑ SVR &amp; end-organ hypoperfusion (↑ lactate).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>Diagnostic sequence once catheter is placed:<br /></br>• CI &lt; 2.2 L/min/m² confirms cardiogenic shock.<br /></br>• PCWP &gt; 18 mm Hg supports LV failure or MR.<br /></br>• Large v-waves → papillary-muscle rupture; O₂ step-up in RV → VSD; equalization of diastolic pressures → tamponade.</p></br><p>Therapy (while/after data obtained)<br /></br>1. Pharmacologic: norepinephrine ± dobutamine for pressure &amp; contractility support.<br /></br>2. Mechanical: intra-aortic balloon pump or Impella if refractory.<br /></br>3. Surgical: prompt repair of papillary-muscle or septal rupture; pericardiocentesis/surgery for tamponade.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Crash Swan in SHOCK”<br /></br>• C – Cold/clammy skin<br /></br>• R – Recent MI<br /></br>• A – Altered mental status<br /></br>• S – Sudden dyspnea<br /></br>• H – Hypotension<br /></br>When you see this cluster, “Swan” (Swan-Ganz catheter) is the first diagnostic move.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>• CT angiography – Excellent for pulmonary embolism, but patient is too unstable and already shows pulmonary edema, not clear, hyperlucent lungs typical for PE.<br /></br>• Echocardiography – Very useful for structural lesions, but in NBME logic the immediate need is quantitative hemodynamics; echo can follow once the patient is better perfused.<br /></br>• Serum BNP – Helps distinguish CHF vs pulmonary causes in stable dyspnea; adds nothing urgently here.<br /></br>• Radionuclide myocardial scan – Outpatient test for perfusion/viability, far too slow and not helpful in shock.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom line<br /></br>In a crashing post-MI patient, place a pulmonary-artery/“cardiac” catheter first to confirm cardiogenic shock, unmask mechanical complications, and guide lifesaving therapy.</p>
126. A 2-month-old girl is brought to the clinic by her mother for a well-child examination. The mother says her daughter is exclusively breast-fed; it takes 1 hour to complete a feeding, and her daughter sweats during feedings. The patient is at the 10th percentile for weight; she was at the 50th percentile at her last examination 6 weeks ago. Other growth parameters have remained stable at the 50th percentile. Vital signs are within normal limits. The lungs are clear to auscultation. On cardiac examination, a grade 3/6 systolic murmur is heard best at the lower left sternal border. The liver edge is palpated 5 cm below the right costal margin. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's failure to thrive?	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. Atrial septal defect</br>b. Congenital hypothyroidism</br>c. Fabry disease</br>d. Glycogen storage disease, type II (Pompe disease)</br>e. Inadequate breast milk supply</br>f. Inadequate breast-feeding technique</br>g. Ventricular septal defect</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>g. Ventricular septal defect (VSD)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “Sweats during feedings” + “takes 1 hour to complete a feeding” → classic clues for congestive heart failure (CHF) in infants; they burn calories just trying to eat.<br /></br>• Weight dropped from 50th → 10th percentile while length/head circumference remain 50th → FTT from ↑ metabolic demand rather than global malnutrition.<br /></br>• Hepatomegaly (liver edge 5 cm below R costal margin) → systemic venous congestion from right-sided CHF.<br /></br>• Harsh grade 3/6 systolic murmur best at the lower left sternal border → textbook description of a VSD.<br /></br>• Clear lungs &amp; normal vitals do not exclude mild-to-moderate CHF in infants; hepatomegaly is often the earliest sign.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Infant has features of CHF (diaphoresis with feeds, prolonged feeds, hepatomegaly, FTT).<br /></br>Step 2: What lesion in a 2-month-old most commonly causes CHF + holosystolic murmur at LL sternal border? → VSD.<br /></br>Take-home: Moderate-to-large VSDs create a L→R shunt that increases pulmonary blood flow, raises metabolic demands, and eventually causes CHF/FTT by 6–8 weeks of age when pulmonary vascular resistance drops.</p></br><p>What would be documented: Murmur location/quality, hepatomegaly, growth chart percentiles, feeding history (time to feed, diaphoresis), plan for echocardiography and nutritional supplementation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• At birth PVR ≈ SVR → small shunt. Over weeks PVR falls → large L→R flow across VSD → volume overload of LA/LV → pulmonary over-circulation → right-sided congestion → hepatomegaly.<br /></br>• Sympathetic activation during feeds causes diaphoresis; increased work of breathing &amp; tachycardia raise caloric expenditure, leading to FTT.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment</br>Diagnostics: Echocardiogram (defines size/location, estimates Qp/Qs).<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>1. Medical stabilization – furosemide, +/- ACE inhibitor, ↑-calorie formula or fortified breast milk (120-150 kcal/kg/d).<br /></br>2. Surgical closure or device repair if failure to thrive, pulmonary HTN, or persistent significant shunt beyond 6–12 mo.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“VSD = Very Sweaty Diner” – infants get sweaty eating.<br /></br>Murmur mnemonic: “HoLo at the LoWer border” – Holosystolic murmur at Lower Left sternal border.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a.
