Atrial septal defect – Produces a systolic ejection murmur at the upper left sternal border + fixed split S2. CHF/FTT rare in infancy because shunt is low-pressure.<br /></br>b. Congenital hypothyroidism – Causes constipation, lethargy, macroglossia, large fontanelles—not a harsh murmur or hepatomegaly.<br /></br>c. Fabry disease – X-linked lysosomal disorder presenting later with neuropathic pain, angiokeratomas, renal failure; not early-infant CHF/FTT.<br /></br>d. Pompe (Glycogen storage II) – Can present at 2 mo with cardiomegaly and hypotonia, but usually shows cardiomyopathy (S3, gallop, not an isolated holosystolic VSD murmur) and profound muscle weakness.<br /></br>e. Inadequate breast-milk supply – Would not cause hepatomegaly or characteristic murmur; all growth parameters (length, HC) would typically fall together.<br /></br>f. Inadequate breast-feeding technique – Same reasoning as (e); no CHF signs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Concept</br>In a 4- to 8-week-old who becomes sweaty with feeds, falls off the weight curve, and has a harsh holosystolic LL sternal border murmur with hepatomegaly, think moderate-to-large VSD causing congestive heart failure and failure to thrive.</p>
127. A 67-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 12-hour history of increasingly severe generalized, cramping pain that began in his left groin and spread to his abdomen. The patient rates the pain as a 10 on a 10-point scale. He previously has noted a hernia in the left groin area. He says, "Normally I can push it back in, but this time I couldn't. I was going to wait and make an appointment with my primary care doctor, but I couldn't tolerate the pain." He has hypertension treated with verapamil and hydrochlorothiazide. The patient appears uncomfortable. Temperature is 37.4°C (99.3°F), pulse is 96/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 140/95 mm Hg. Cardiac examination discloses an S4 gallop. Bowel sounds are increased, and the abdomen is soft with moderate, diffuse tenderness. A tender mass is present in the left groin extending into the scrotal sac, and the skin overlying the groin is erythematous. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Blood</br>Hematocrit 45%</br>Hemoglobin 15 g/dL</br>WBC 18,000/mm3</br>Neutrophils, segmented 70%</br>Neutrophils, bands 20%</br>Lymphocytes 8%</br>Monocytes 2%</br>Arterial blood gas analysis</br>PO2 75 mm Hg</br>PCO2 34 mm Hg</br>pH 7.35</br>The physician contacts the on-call surgeon to see the patient. The surgeon instructs the physician to attempt reduction of the hernia and to call back if unsuccessful. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administer hydromorphone and attempt manual reduction of the hernia</br>b. Contact the hospital administrator and report an Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act violation</br>c. Inform the surgeon that the patient needs to be evaluated</br>d. Obtain CT scan of the abdomen and call the surgeon back if there are signs of bowel obstruction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Administer hydromorphone and attempt manual reduction of the hernia  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Known hernia” + “cannot push it back in” + exquisitely tender, erythematous groin mass → incarcerated inguinal hernia (possible early strangulation).<br /></br>• Pain is 10/10, abdomen is soft (no peritonitis) ⇒ reduction can still be attempted.<br /></br>• Surgeon has been contacted and explicitly instructs: “attempt reduction and call back if unsuccessful.”<br /></br>• Key negatives: no fever &gt;38 °C, no guarding/rigidity, no severe metabolic acidosis—strangulation is possible but not yet proven.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Stabilize/analgesia: Treat the patient’s severe pain (10/10).<br /></br>2. Attempt reduction per surgical directive. Sedation or analgesia (e.g., an opioid) relaxes abdominal wall musculature, making manual reduction (taxic reduction) more likely to succeed.<br /></br>3. If reduction fails or signs of strangulation develop (peritonitis, systemic toxicity, lactic acidosis), call surgeon back for emergent OR.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In an incarcerated hernia without overt strangulation, immediate gentle reduction after adequate analgesia/sedation is recommended; it should not be delayed by imaging or administrative complaints.  </p></br><p>Documentation (real life): Pain score, medication administered, patient consent, description of reduction attempt, time surgeon notified.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Incarceration = non-reducible hernia; blood supply may still be intact.<br /></br>• Strangulation = compromised blood supply → ischemia, necrosis, peritonitis; mandates emergent surgery.<br /></br>• Early strangulation presents with leukocytosis and severe pain but before peritoneal signs appear a reduction attempt is reasonable. Successful reduction converts the case to an elective repair later.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line at this stage:<br /></br>1. Analgesia (IV opioid such as hydromorphone or morphine).<br /></br>2. Possible sedation (midazolam) and Trendelenburg position, ice packs, gentle steady pressure toward the internal ring.<br /></br>3. If reduction unsuccessful or suspicion of strangulation rises → immediate surgical exploration (no imaging delay).<br /></br>CT scan is reserved for unclear groin masses or suspected bowel obstruction when diagnosis is uncertain—not necessary here.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pain-Push-Page” for incarcerated hernia management:<br /></br>1. Pain control<br /></br>2. Push (attempt reduction)<br /></br>3. Page surgeon again if it won’t go back or if peritoneal signs appear.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Report an EMTALA violation<br /></br>• EMTALA requires that an on-call specialist provide appropriate medical screening and stabilizing treatment. The surgeon responded and provided a treatment plan; no violation.  </p></br><p>c. Inform the surgeon that the patient needs to be evaluated<br /></br>• The surgeon already gave a plan contingent on the ED physician’s attempt. Jumping to demand immediate bedside evaluation disregards standard, efficient care and the agreed protocol.  </p></br><p>d. Obtain CT scan of the abdomen and call back if there are signs of bowel obstruction<br /></br>• Imaging delays definitive management, exposes patient to contrast/radiation, and is unnecessary when diagnosis is clear on exam. Regulation of pain and reduction attempt should precede imaging.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Severe pain from an incarcerated inguinal hernia should first be treated with adequate analgesia, then a gentle manual reduction attempted as directed by the surgeon. If reduction fails or strangulation is evident, immediate surgical intervention is required.</p>
128. A 55-year-old man comes to the office because of a 2-month history of moderate right shoulder pain. The patient says the pain occurs whenever he raises his right arm straight up in front of him or up from his side. He also reports decreased strength in his right shoulder. He says he injured his right shoulder 35 years ago during a college football game when he was hit by another player and landed shoulder-first on the ground. After the injury, he had pain in the shoulder that resolved in 3 days without treatment. He has not had any subsequent injuries to the shoulder. Medical history otherwise is unremarkable, and he takes no routine medications. Vital signs are normal. Physical examination discloses inability to resist downward pressure on the right arm when it is held straight out in front with the thumb pointing downward. Which of the following imaging studies of the right shoulder is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. AP and lateral x-rays</br>b. Arthrography</br>c. MRI</br>d. Ultrasonography</br>e. No imaging studies are necessary to confirm the diagnosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: AP and lateral shoulder x-rays  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 55-year-old with 2-month history of moderate shoulder pain and weakness.<br /></br>• Pain is provoked by forward flexion or abduction (“raises his arm straight up in front of him or up from his side”).<br /></br>• Cannot resist downward pressure with the arm held straight out in front, thumb-down → positive “empty-can” test, localising the problem to the supraspinatus component of the rotator cuff.<br /></br>• Remote shoulder trauma 35 years ago → predisposes to chronic degenerative changes around the acromion/AC joint and to rotator-cuff pathology.<br /></br>Key pattern: middle-aged/older patient with impingement-type pain and supraspinatus weakness after a remote injury = chronic degenerative rotator-cuff disease / impingement syndrome.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify syndrome: Rotator-cuff tendinopathy/partial tear with impingement.<br /></br>Step 2 – Decide what imaging, if any, is needed to CONFIRM that diagnosis:<br /></br>   • First-line study in virtually every shoulder complaint is plain radiography (AP and lateral).<br /></br>   • X-rays confirm chronic degenerative changes (subacromial osteophytes, sclerosis of the greater tuberosity, superior migration of the humeral head) that underlie supraspinatus impingement.<br /></br>   • They also rule out alternative bone pathology (fracture, dislocation, arthritis, calcific tendinitis).  </p></br><p>Take-home: In a patient with suspected chronic rotator-cuff disease, plain films are the initial and usually sufficient confirmatory study; advanced imaging is reserved for refractory cases or pre-operative planning.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Repetitive overhead motion or prior trauma → acromial/AC-joint osteophytes + intrinsic tendon degeneration.<br /></br>• Result = impingement of supraspinatus tendon in the subacromial space → pain on forward flexion/abduction, positive empty-can, variable weakness.<br /></br>• Chronic disease can cause superior migration of the humeral head (seen on AP view).  </p></br><p>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>Diagnostics (step-wise):<br /></br>1. AP &amp; lateral x-rays (first-line).<br /></br>2. If full-thickness tear suspected or surgery considered → MRI or ultrasound.<br /></br>Treatment:<br /></br>• Initial: NSAIDs + activity modification + structured physical therapy focusing on rotator-cuff and scapular stabilisers.<br /></br>• Subacromial corticosteroid injection if symptoms persist.<br /></br>• Definitive: Arthroscopic subacromial decompression and/or rotator-cuff repair for refractory or full-thickness tears.  </p></br><p>Memorisation Tip<br /></br>“R-C-X”: Rotator-Cuff complaints start with X-rays (AP &amp; lateral) before pricey scans. Visualise an “X” drawn across a cuff-sleeve.  </p></br><p>Why the Other Options Are Incorrect<br /></br>b. Arthrography – Historically used, now largely obsolete. Reserved only when MRI is contraindicated and full-thickness tear must be proven. Not first-line.<br /></br>c. MRI – Gold standard for delineating a tear but not necessary to confirm the clinical diagnosis in an uncomplicated presentation; obtained only if surgery is contemplated or symptoms fail conservative care.<br /></br>d. Ultrasound – Useful office-based screen but operator-dependent and, like MRI, usually ordered after plain films.<br /></br>e. No imaging necessary – While many mild impingement cases can be managed empirically, NBME expects initial plain films in a 55-year-old with weakness to rule out structural bone disease and document degenerative changes.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core takeaway: For suspected chronic rotator-cuff disease/impingement, start with AP &amp; lateral shoulder x-rays; reserve ultrasound or MRI for refractory cases or operative planning.</p>
129. A 25-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 45 minutes after the onset of dizziness. Her symptom began after she consumed 1800 mL of water during a contest in which participants tried to drink the most water in 15 minutes. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. On arrival, she is alert and fully oriented. Her pulse is 88/min and regular, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 110/80 mm Hg. Examination, including neurologic examination, shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na+ 128 mEq/L</br>K+ 3.8 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 5 mg/dL</br>Glucose 80 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 0.7 mg/dL</br>Urine specific gravity 1.005</br>Administration of which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. ADH (vasopressin)</br>b. Furosemide</br>c. 0.9% Saline</br>d. 3% Saline</br>e. No pharmacotherapy is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No pharmacotherapy is indicated  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Drinking 1.8 L of water in 15 min → acute, large free-water load.<br /></br>• Mild, asymptomatic hyponatremia (Na⁺ 128 mEq/L).<br /></br>• Kidneys are responding appropriately: urine specific gravity 1.005 (maximally dilute) ⇒ ADH is suppressed and free water is being excreted.<br /></br>• Normal vital signs and completely normal neurologic exam (dizziness has already resolved on arrival).  </p></br><p>Key takeaway: This is classic primary polydipsia (“water intoxication”) with intact renal water excretion and only mild hyponatremia.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Determine severity of hyponatremia and symptoms.<br /></br>   • Na⁺ 128 = mild (125-135 mEq/L).<br /></br>   • No seizures, confusion, vomiting, or focal deficits.<br /></br>2. Identify etiology.<br /></br>   • Low urine specific gravity + recent water challenge = dilutional hyponatremia from excess intake, not SIADH or adrenal/renal failure.<br /></br>3. Management principle.<br /></br>   • In mild, asymptomatic dilutional hyponatremia, simply restrict free water; the kidneys will correct Na⁺ rapidly on their own.<br /></br>   • Pharmacologic or hypertonic therapy risks over-correction → osmotic demyelination.  </p></br><p>Documentation point: “Educated patient to stop fluid challenge, monitored for symptom progression; no IV fluids or medications started.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>Primary polydipsia<br /></br>• Etiology: Excess water intake overwhelms kidney’s excretory capacity (~15–20 L/day).<br /></br>• Consequence: Dilutional hyponatremia, low plasma osmolality, very dilute urine (Uosm &lt;100 mOsm/kg; USG &lt;1.006).<br /></br>• ADH appropriately suppressed. Contrast with SIADH (inappropriately concentrated urine).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line: Stop water intake ± mild fluid restriction.<br /></br>Monitoring: Serial Na⁺ q2–4 h; neuro status check.<br /></br>Escalate only if:<br /></br>• Moderate/severe symptoms (seizure, confusion) OR<br /></br>• Na⁺ &lt;120 mEq/L with any neuro sign → 3% saline slowly (≤8 mEq/L per 24 h).<br /></br>Avoid DDAVP or vasopressin; avoid loop diuretics unless volume overloaded.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“P​o​l​y​d​i​p​s​i​a = Pee Dilute”: if the urine is very dilute and patient just chugged water, treatment is simply to Pass on drugs.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. ADH (vasopressin): Would worsen hyponatremia by preventing free-water excretion. Indicated for diabetes insipidus, not here.<br /></br>b. Furosemide: Loops can raise Na⁺ in SIADH by promoting free-water loss, but unnecessary and could deplete volume/electrolytes in an already euvolemic patient.<br /></br>c. 0.9 % Saline: Isotonic but still adds water; kidneys would quickly excrete Na⁺ and you may not correct serum Na⁺. Not needed.<br /></br>d. 3 % Saline: Reserved for severe or symptomatic (seizure/coma) hyponatremia. Over-treatment here could raise Na⁺ too rapidly → osmotic demyelination syndrome.<br /></br>e. No pharmacotherapy: Correct because mild, asymptomatic, and kidney response is intact.  </p></br><p>Core concept: In primary polydipsia with mild, asymptomatic hyponatremia and maximally dilute urine, simply stop the water—no drugs, no saline.</p>
130. A previously healthy 57-year-old man is brought to the emergency department immediately after being involved in a head-on motor vehicle collision. He was the driver and was wearing a seat belt with a shoulder strap. On arrival, he is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. He says that he does not think he had loss of consciousness but feels sore all over his body. His temperature is 36.7°C (98°F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 140/70 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 2 L/min of oxygen via nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 99%. Physical examination and an ECG show no abnormalities. A chest x-ray is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Aortic disruption</br>b. Aspiration pneumonia</br>c. Perforation of the esophagus</br>d. Perforation of an intra-abdominal viscus</br>e. Pericardial effusion</br>f. Pneumothorax</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Aortic disruption (blunt traumatic thoracic aortic injury, BTAI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• High-energy, head-on motor vehicle collision → classic deceleration mechanism.<br /></br>• Seat belt/shoulder strap → protects abdomen but does NOT prevent shearing forces on mediastinal vessels.<br /></br>• Immediately post-crash yet hemodynamically stable → BTAI classically has a “lucid interval”; patients can look well until the aorta ruptures completely.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray provided (not shown in text) is the only study mentioned, implying the diagnosis hinges on its appearance. Board questions virtually always pair “high-speed MVC + abnormal CXR (widened mediastinum, loss of aortic knob, left apical cap, tracheal deviation)” with traumatic aortic injury.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: normal lung exam &amp; SpO₂ → argues against pneumothorax or aspiration pneumonia; normal ECG &amp; no tamponade physiology → argues against pericardial effusion; no peritoneal signs or free air under diaphragm → argues against intra-abdominal perforation; no severe odynophagia/subcutaneous emphysema → argues against esophageal rupture.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Deceleration + widened mediastinum” = think aortic disruption until proven otherwise.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Mechanism: Sudden deceleration causes shear stress at the aortic isthmus (just distal to left subclavian).  </li></br><li>Screening test: Upright CXR. Widened mediastinum (&gt;8 cm), indistinct aortic knob, left pleural effusion (hemothorax) are red flags.  </li></br><li>Next diagnostic step (real life): Urgent CT angiography of chest or trans-esophageal echo if unstable.  </li></br><li>Management: Blood pressure control (IV esmolol ± nitroprusside) to reduce shear, then endovascular stent-graft repair (preferred) or open repair.</li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home: Any high-speed deceleration injury + abnormal mediastinum on CXR → presume aortic injury.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: mechanism, vitals, neuro-vascular exam, CXR findings, plan for CT-angiogram and BP control.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Site: Aortic isthmus tethered by ligamentum arteriosum → maximal shear.<br /></br>• Types: Intimal tear → pseudoaneurysm → contained rupture → free rupture (rapid exsanguination).<br /></br>• Up to 80 % die at scene; survivors usually have contained rupture.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Quick Review  </p></br><ol></br><li>Imaging hierarchy: CXR → CT-angiogram (gold standard in stable pt) → TEE in OR/ED for unstable pt.  </li></br><li>BP/HR goals: SBP 100–120 mm Hg, HR &lt;80 bpm. Use IV β-blocker first (esmolol, labetalol).  </li></br><li>Definitive repair: Thoracic endovascular aortic repair (TEVAR).  </li></br><li>Concomitant injuries common (rib, spine, pulmonary contusion)—maintain high index of suspicion.</li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“WIST”:<br /></br>W – Widened mediastinum<br /></br>I – Isthmus shear injury<br /></br>S – Seat-belt/Speed (deceleration)<br /></br>T – TEVAR after Tight BP control  </p></br><p>Visual mnemonic: Picture the aorta as a garden hose; slamming on the brakes causes it to kink and bulge at the fixed point (isthmus).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>b. Aspiration pneumonia – Would present hours-days later with fever, rales, infiltrates in dependent lung fields, not immediately after crash and CXR would show parenchymal opacities, not mediastinal widening.</p></br><p>c. Esophageal perforation (Boerhaave) – Typically follows forceful vomiting; presents with severe chest pain, subcutaneous emphysema, mediastinal air. CXR would show pneumomediastinum or pleural effusion, not classic widened mediastinum after MVC.</p></br><p>d. Perforation of an intra-abdominal viscus – Would cause acute abdominal pain, peritoneal signs, and free air under diaphragm on upright CXR; none described.</p></br><p>e. Pericardial effusion – Requires fluid around heart; would give low voltage on ECG, possibly muffled heart sounds and/or tamponade physiology (hypotension, JVD); none present and CXR would show “water-bottle” cardiac silhouette, not widened mediastinum.</p></br><p>f. Pneumothorax – Presents with unilateral chest pain, decreased breath sounds, possibly hypoxia; CXR would show collapsed lung &amp; pleural line, not mediastinal widening. Normal pulse oximetry and lung exam argue against it.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway  </p></br><p>In high-speed deceleration injuries, any abnormal mediastinum on CXR = presume traumatic aortic disruption, control blood pressure, and obtain emergent CT-angiography/TEVAR.</p>
131. A 77-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of increasing back pain. Eight years ago, he was diagnosed with prostate cancer. Gonadotropin inhibitor therapy was begun, and he was asymptomatic until 2 months ago; metastatic lesions at L3 and T8 were confirmed at that time. Ibuprofen therapy sufficiently controlled the pain for 1 month, after which oxycodone (5 mg every 4 hours) and laxatives were added to the medication regimen. At his last visit 1 month ago, he reported his pain as between 0 and 2 on a 10-point scale. He now describes his pain as 4 to 5 on a 10-point scale. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Massage therapy and reevaluation in 1 week</br>b. Add oral acetaminophen therapy to the regimen</br>c. Begin intravenous morphine therapy</br>d. Begin oral propoxyphene therapy</br>e. Increase the oxycodone dosage by 25% to 50%</br>f. Switch from codeine to meperidine therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Increase the oxycodone dosage by 25 %–50 %</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Cancer type &amp; stage: Metastatic prostate cancer to the spine → chronic cancer pain expected.<br /></br>• Current regimen:<br /></br>  – Non-opioid: ibuprofen (NSAID)<br /></br>  – Short-acting strong opioid: oxycodone 5 mg q4 h (≈ 30 mg/day) + bowel regimen<br /></br>• Pain course: Previously 0–2/10, now 4–5/10 for the past 2 weeks → persistent but still moderate pain, no red-flag neurologic changes.<br /></br>• Key clue: Patient is already on Step 3 of the WHO cancer-pain ladder but at a LOW dose; inadequate control suggests simple dose-escalation rather than drug/class change or parenteral therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Confirm that the pain is still opioid-responsive and not neuropathic or new cord compression (no new neuro deficits).<br /></br>2. WHO &amp; NCCN cancer-pain guidelines:<br /></br>   • If pain ≥ 4/10 persists → ↑ current opioid by 25 %–50 %.<br /></br>   • Reassess in 24–48 h.<br /></br>3. Because the patient is tolerating oxycodone and has no uncontrolled adverse effects, the next logical step is dose escalation.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Chronic cancer pain is titrated by increasing the current opioid 25 %–50 % until pain ≤ 3/10 or dose-limiting side-effects occur.</p></br><p>Documentation (real-life): pain score, current meds &amp; side-effects, new dose prescribed, follow-up plan.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Cancer pain is often nociceptive from bone metastases (periosteal irritation, inflammatory mediators).<br /></br>• Opioid tolerance is pharmacodynamic—µ-receptor desensitization—requiring higher doses over time.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line: Short-acting opioid titrated q2–3 d until controlled, then convert to long-acting formulation; keep breakthrough doses (10 %–15 % of total daily).<br /></br>Adjuvants: NSAID or corticosteroid for bone pain; bisphosphonates or denosumab for structural control.<br /></br>No additional labs/imaging required solely for dose escalation when pain pattern unchanged.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“2-4-6 Rule” for cancer pain titration:<br /></br>Pain score 0–3 → keep dose, 4–6 → ↑ 25 %–50 %, 7–10 → ↑ 50 %–100 % or switch route.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Massage &amp; reevaluate: Comfort measure alone; violates guideline—pharmacologic escalation required for ≥ 4/10 pain.<br /></br>b. Add acetaminophen: Already on NSAID; acetaminophen adds minimal benefit and risks hepatotoxicity; still does not address opioid tolerance.<br /></br>c. IV morphine: Reserved for severe pain or inability to take PO; unnecessary escalation in route/setting.<br /></br>d. Propoxyphene: Weak opioid with inferior analgesia; removed from U.S. market for cardiac toxicity.<br /></br>f. Switch to meperidine: Short half-life, neurotoxic metabolite (normeperidine); contraindicated for chronic therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>For persistent moderate cancer pain in a patient already taking an opioid without limiting side-effects, increase the current opioid dose by 25 %–50 % before changing drugs or routes.</p>
132. An 18-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has a 5-year history of asthma treated with inhaled albuterol as needed and inhaled beclomethasone daily. She has never required treatment in the hospital for an acute episode. She says that her asthma is worse after she spends prolonged periods of time indoors and after she visits homes of friends who own cats. Her house has hardwood floors, which she and her family clean every week. She sleeps with her windows closed and changes her bedding regularly. She does not smoke or drink alcohol.</br>Examination shows no abnormalities. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Skin testing shows allergies to dust mites and cat dander. The physician advises the patient to avoid direct contact with cats. Which of the following environmental changes is likely to be most helpful in preventing exacerbations of this patient's asthma?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Humidifier use</br>b. Placement of carpet in her bedroom</br>c. Sleeping with the windows open</br>d. Use of allergen-impermeable mattress and pillow covers</br>e. Vacuuming the floors of her house daily</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Use of allergen-impermeable mattress and pillow covers  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Trigger identification: “worse … indoors” + positive skin tests to dust-mites and cat dander ⇒ classic indoor allergen–induced asthma.<br /></br>• Home details: hardwood floors cleaned weekly (already low-risk), bedding changed regularly, windows closed (↓ outdoor pollen). Mattress and pillows, however, remain a large dust-mite reservoir.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: never hospitalized, no smoking, no pets at home → suggests allergen avoidance rather than medication escalation is the intervention being tested.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Confirm allergen (dust mites) → focus on bedroom/bedding (patient spends 1/3 of life there).<br /></br>2. Highest yield dust-mite reduction = physical barrier (encasing mattress &amp; pillows in impermeable covers) + hot-water wash ≥55 °C.<br /></br>3. Other measures (vacuuming, de-humidifying, removing carpets) can help but are less effective or impractical.<br /></br>Take-home: For dust-mite–driven asthma, bedroom encasement is the single most effective environmental change.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Dust mites live in soft fabrics (mattress, pillows, carpets) and need humidity &gt;50%. Fecal particles become airborne, are inhaled, bind IgE on mast cells → bronchoconstriction, mucous hypersecretion, airway inflammation.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics (brief)<br /></br>• Pharmacologic: Inhaled corticosteroid (beclomethasone) remains controller; SABA (albuterol) for rescue.<br /></br>• Non-pharm: 1) Allergen-impermeable encasements, 2) weekly hot-water wash, 3) maintain indoor humidity 30–50 %, 4) HEPA vacuum or remove carpets, 5) consider allergen immunotherapy if incomplete control.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Dust-mite D’s”:<br /></br>1. Dress the bed (encase mattress/pillow) – MOST important.<br /></br>2. Dry the air (&lt;50 % humidity).<br /></br>3. Dump the rugs/carpets.<br /></br>Picture a “D-shield” wrapped around the bed keeping mites out.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Humidifier use – Raises indoor humidity, actually INCREASES dust-mite growth and mold; counterproductive.<br /></br>b. Placement of carpet – Carpets = prime dust-mite habitat → worsens symptoms.<br /></br>c. Sleeping with windows open – Minimal effect on mites; may add outdoor pollen/mold exposures.<br /></br>e. Vacuuming floors daily – Ordinary vacuuming aerosolizes mite antigen; only HEPA vacuum helps, and even then is less effective than encasing bedding.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For dust-mite–induced asthma, sealing the mattress and pillows with allergen-impermeable covers is the highest-yield single intervention.</p>
133. A 44-year-old man comes to the office because of a 6-month history of intermittent moderate headache. He has had one to two headaches weekly since residents of the neighboring apartment got a dog that barks a lot. He says he has had recurrent thoughts about killing the dog during the past 6 months, but he has no intent or plan to harm the animal. He worries that his headaches are punishment for these thoughts about killing; he says he realizes this does not make sense but remains concerned. He vacuums 2 hours daily because he is worried that dog hair is coming into his apartment. He has no history of serious illness. His only medication is acetaminophen, which resolves his headaches. He drinks two to three 12-oz beers on weekends. His pulse is 72/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 150/105 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no other abnormalities. Mental status examination shows an anxious affect. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Delusional disorder</br>b. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder</br>d. Paranoid personality disorder</br>e. Somatic symptom disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Delusional disorder  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Time course: 6-month history → meets the ≥1-month requirement for delusional disorder.<br /></br>• Core belief: “Dog hair is coming into my apartment” (despite closed doors/windows) and “my headaches are punishment for my thoughts.” These are fixed, idiosyncratic explanations for his symptoms, not shared by others.<br /></br>• Degree of conviction: He vacuums 2 hours every day exclusively to address the putative dog-hair invasion. This persistent behavior shows that the belief is sufficiently fixed to drive daily functioning.<br /></br>• Insight: He concedes that the headache-as-punishment idea “does not make sense” yet continues to be pre-occupied; partial insight (common in delusional disorder) does not rule the diagnosis out.<br /></br>• Functioning: Aside from time spent vacuuming, no social, occupational, or global impairment is described—typical for delusional disorder.<br /></br>• Absence of other psychotic symptoms: No hallucinations, disorganized speech, or negative symptoms.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify the primary psychopathology (a persistent, circumscribed false belief).<br /></br>2. Check duration (≥1 month).<br /></br>3. Ensure schizophrenia criteria are not met (no hallucinations, prominent thought disorder, or negative symptoms).<br /></br>4. Evaluate global functioning (largely preserved).<br /></br>Taken together, these features fulfill DSM-5 criteria for delusional disorder.  </p></br><p>Key takeaway: A single, non-bizarre, long-standing, fixed false belief in an otherwise well-functioning adult with no other psychotic features = delusional disorder. In real practice, the belief, level of insight, effect on functioning, and absence of additional psychotic manifestations would be documented.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology remains unclear; hypotheses include dysregulated dopamine pathways and impaired frontal-striatal circuitry.<br /></br>• Risk factors: middle age, social isolation, immigration, sensory impairment.<br /></br>• Subtype: Somatic (concern about contamination/headaches) and Persecutory (dog “invading” space).  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>1. Antipsychotic medication (e.g., risperidone, olanzapine, aripiprazole).<br /></br>2. Supportive or cognitive-behavioral therapy aimed at improving insight and coping.<br /></br>Diagnostics: Basic medical work-up (TSH, CBC, CMP, toxicology) and neuroimaging only if red-flags (focal deficits, late-onset psychosis) exist.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Delusion = fixed conclusion.”<br /></br>If the false belief stands alone and the life otherwise goes on, think D-elusional D-isorder (Double D).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Generalized anxiety disorder – Would show pervasive, free-floating worry about multiple life areas for ≥6 months plus physical symptoms (restlessness, fatigue, muscle tension). Here the concern is narrowly focused on dog hair/headaches, not “everything.”<br /></br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder – Requires intrusive, ego-dystonic obsessions that the patient actively attempts to suppress, plus clearly defined compulsions aimed at neutralizing the obsession. In OCD the patient generally recognizes the thoughts as irrational and experiences significant distress. This patient’s belief in dog-hair contamination is held with delusional conviction (partial insight), not merely as an unwanted, intrusive thought he knows is false.<br /></br>d. Paranoid personality disorder – Lifelong pattern beginning by early adulthood, involving pervasive distrust and suspiciousness across many contexts, not a single fixed belief onset in mid-life.<br /></br>e. Somatic symptom disorder – Characterized by excessive thoughts/feelings about ≥1 somatic complaints. Here, the core issue is the contamination/punishment belief, not disproportionate concern with the headache itself.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing a circumscribed fixed false belief (delusion) that leads to repetitive behavior with an obsession/compulsion pair in OCD. Always ask: “Does the patient actually believe the content to be true?” If yes, think delusion; if no (they recognize it’s irrational and fight it), think OCD.</p>
134. A 27-year-old primigravid woman at 32 weeks' gestation comes to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of progressive drooping of the right side of her face, an inability to close her right eye, and right-sided facial pain. She has not had loss of hearing or balance. Her pregnancy had been uncomplicated. She has no personal or family history of serious illness. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 88/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 140/82 mm Hg. Examination of the face shows paralysis on the right side and an inability to close the right eye. The patient moves her head stiffly; there is no nuchal rigidity. Heart sounds are normal. A grade 2/6 holosystolic murmur is heard best at the apex. The abdomen is soft. The fundal height is 32 cm. There is mild edema of the lower extremities. Muscle strength is normal. Deep tendon reflexes are 2+. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Carotid ultrasonography</br>b. CT scan of the head</br>c. Corticosteroid therapy</br>d. Angiography of the brain</br>e. Nerve conduction studies</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Corticosteroid therapy (eg, oral prednisone started within 72 h)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Abrupt-onset unilateral facial weakness: “progressive drooping … inability to close her right eye.”<br /></br>• Entire right face involved (forehead + lower face) → lower motor neuron lesion of CN VII, classic for Bell palsy.<br /></br>• No other focal neurologic deficits, no hearing loss/vertigo (rules out vestibular schwannoma), no nuchal rigidity (rules out meningitis), normal strength/reflexes (rules out stroke or Guillain-Barré).<br /></br>• Third-trimester pregnancy—well-known risk factor for Bell palsy due to hormonal-induced fluid retention and nerve edema.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify pattern → isolated LMN facial nerve palsy.<br /></br>Step 2: Recognize high-risk group (pregnancy) and timing (&lt;72 h).<br /></br>Step 3: Evidence-based therapy → high-dose oral glucocorticoids improve likelihood of full recovery; benefit outweighs minimal fetal risk.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Bell palsy is a clinical diagnosis; treat promptly with steroids rather than ordering imaging or invasive tests.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): onset time, complete facial involvement, absence of other neuro signs, eye-closure inability, plan for prednisone ± eye protection, OB consultation for medication safety.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Idiopathic inflammation/edema of CN VII as it traverses the facial canal.<br /></br>• Thought to be viral (HSV-1 reactivation) or immune mediated.<br /></br>• Pregnancy, diabetes, and recent viral illness increase risk.<br /></br>• LMN lesion → ipsilateral total facial paralysis (forehead and lower face) + possible loss of taste anterior 2/3 tongue &amp; hyperacusis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line (start within 72 h):<br /></br>1. Oral prednisone 60–80 mg/day × 5 d, then taper.<br /></br>2. ± Acyclovir/valacyclovir (mixed evidence, often added when viral trigger suspected).<br /></br>3. Eye care: artificial tears, nighttime eye patch, to prevent exposure keratitis.  </p></br><p>No imaging or electrophysiologic studies are required unless atypical features (recurrent, gradual onset, bilateral, or additional cranial neuropathies).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bell Rings the ‘PReD’ alarm”<br /></br>• Bell palsy<br /></br>• Rapid onset<br /></br>• Prednisone Required within Days (72 h)</p></br><p>UMN vs LMN face: “Forehead spared? Think Cortex (stroke); Forehead down? Think Bells (LMN).”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answers<br /></br>a. Carotid ultrasonography – Used for carotid stenosis causing cortical (UMN) deficits (contralateral lower face weakness sparing forehead) or TIAs; not indicated in isolated LMN palsy.<br /></br>b. CT scan of the head – Would be ordered for intracranial hemorrhage, mass, or stroke; none fit the presentation and would delay therapy.<br /></br>d. Angiography of the brain – Evaluates aneurysms/vascular malformations; unnecessary here.<br /></br>e. Nerve conduction studies – Helpful in chronic or unclear peripheral neuropathies; Bell palsy is diagnosed clinically and treated empirically.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>An otherwise healthy pregnant woman with acute, complete unilateral facial paralysis has Bell palsy. Prompt corticosteroid therapy is the evidence-based next step.</p>
135. A 73-year-old woman is brought to the clinic by her husband because of a 6-month history of progressive memory difficulty. During this time, she has misplaced objects more frequently than usual and has had trouble following recipes. Six days ago, she became lost while walking their dog in their neighborhood. Today, she forgot that their daughter had called the previous day. The patient has type 2 diabetes mellitus and hypertension. Her medications are metformin and lisinopril. She does not smoke cigarettes or drink alcohol. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she is oriented to person but not to place or time. She recalls zero of three objects after 5 minutes. She makes four errors when performing serial sevens. She can name the US President and Vice President. She can name eight animals in 1 minute. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of a complete blood count, her erythrocyte sedimentation rate, and results of liver and thyroid function tests are within the reference ranges. VDRL testing for syphilis is negative. MRIs of the brain are shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Deep brain stimulation</br>b. Placement of a ventriculoperitoneal shunt</br>c. Transcranial magnetic stimulation</br>d. Treatment with an acetylcholine precursor</br>e. Treatment with a cholinesterase inhibitor</br>f. Treatment with a postsynaptic choline receptor agonist</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Treatment with a cholinesterase inhibitor (eg, donepezil, rivastigmine, galantamine)</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• “73-year-old woman … 6-month history of progressive memory difficulty” – insidious onset in an elderly patient immediately raises concern for a neurodegenerative dementia.<br /></br>• Prominent early short-term memory loss: misplacing objects, repeating questions, forgetting recent phone call.<br /></br>• Visuospatial disorientation: became lost in her own neighborhood.<br /></br>• Cognitive testing: impaired orientation to time/place, 0/3 recall at 5 min, serial-seven errors – global cortical deficits typical of Alzheimer disease (AD). Naming animals and political figures relatively spared, which fits an early AD pattern.<br /></br>• Normal neuro exam and labs – rules out metabolic, infectious, or structural mimics (eg, B12, hypothyroid, neurosyphilis).<br /></br>• MRI (not shown here) classically would demonstrate diffuse cortical and hippocampal atrophy with enlarged ventricles—another point toward AD.</p></br><p>Key positives: progressive episodic memory loss + visuospatial deficits + normal neuro exam/labs.<br /></br>Key negatives: no gait disturbance, urinary incontinence, focal neuro deficits—arguments against other dementias such as normal-pressure hydrocephalus (NPH) or vascular dementia.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Elderly patient with gradual short-term memory loss → consider Alzheimer disease first.<br /></br>2. Rule out reversible causes (TSH, B12, VDRL, CBC, LFTs) – all normal.<br /></br>3. MRI excludes structural lesion and supports diffuse atrophy pattern.<br /></br>4. First-line symptomatic therapy for mild-to-moderate AD = acetylcholinesterase inhibitor. Core takeaway: AD = cortical cholinergic deficit → boost acetylcholine.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: cognitive screening results, functional decline, discussion of diagnosis, and initiation of donepezil (or rivastigmine/galantamine); counsel on disease course and caregiver support.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• AD is characterized by β-amyloid plaques and neurofibrillary tangles causing neuronal loss, particularly in the basal forebrain (nucleus basalis of Meynert) where cholinergic neurons reside.<br /></br>• Result = cortical acetylcholine deficiency → memory and executive dysfunction.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests</br>1. Cholinesterase inhibitors:<br /></br>   • Donepezil (daily), rivastigmine (oral/patch), galantamine.<br /></br>   • Provide modest improvement/slow decline in cognition and function.<br /></br>2. NMDA receptor antagonist (memantine) added for moderate–severe disease.<br /></br>3. Supportive measures: safety (wandering), advanced directives, caregiver education.<br /></br>No additional diagnostic tests are required at this point after a typical work-up.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“AD = A-cetylcholine Down” → Give a drug that “A-cts to Deliver” acetylcholine (Donepezil).<br /></br>Or: “DRaG your memory back” – Donepezil, Rivastigmine, Galantamine.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Deep brain stimulation – Used for Parkinson disease, essential tremor, refractory OCD; does not improve cortical dementia symptoms.<br /></br>b. Ventriculoperitoneal shunt – Treats normal-pressure hydrocephalus (gait disturbance, urinary incontinence, magnetic gait). Gait impairment is absent here.<br /></br>c. Transcranial magnetic stimulation – Adjunct for treatment-resistant major depressive disorder; not proven for AD cognition.<br /></br>d. Acetylcholine precursor (eg, lecithin/choline supplements) – Ineffective; does not cross BBB sufficiently or address enzymatic breakdown.<br /></br>f. Postsynaptic cholinergic receptor agonist (eg, nicotine) – Poor CNS selectivity, rapid tolerance, significant side effects; not recommended for AD.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing AD with NPH—remember NPH triad “wet, wobbly, wacky”; early prominent gait disturbance and imaging shows enlarged ventricles without cortical atrophy.</p></br><p>──────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>Alzheimer disease presents with progressive episodic memory loss and visuospatial difficulties in an elderly patient with normal basic labs/structural imaging. First-line therapy is a cholinesterase inhibitor to increase central acetylcholine levels and slow cognitive decline.</p>
136. A previously healthy 77-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 6-hour history of increasingly severe, nonradiating lower abdominal pain. He also has had urinary urgency during this time but has not been able to void for 8 hours. He has not had fever, chills, diarrhea, nausea, or vomiting. Vital signs are within normal limits. On examination, there is fullness in the midline from the umbilicus to the pubic symphysis; the area is tender to palpation, but there are no peritoneal signs. Rectal examination shows an enlarged prostate; sphincter tone is normal, and no masses are palpable. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Bacterial prostatitis</br>b. Benign prostatic hyperplasia</br>c. Cecal volvulus</br>d. Prostatic adenocarcinoma</br>e. Sigmoid volvulus</br>f. Urinary tract infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Benign prostatic hyperplasia (BPH)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 77-year-old man → classic age group for BPH.<br /></br>• 6-hour history of suprapubic/“lower abdominal” pain + palpable midline fullness that extends from the umbilicus to the pubic symphysis → distended urinary bladder.<br /></br>• Urinary urgency but inability to void for 8 hours → acute urinary retention.<br /></br>• No fever, chills, nausea, vomiting → argues against infectious or peritonitic process.<br /></br>• Rectal exam: enlarged prostate, normal sphincter tone, no nodules → benign-appearing gland.</p></br><p>Taken together, these details point to obstruction of bladder outflow by an enlarged, benign prostate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify chief problem: Acute urinary retention (painful suprapubic fullness + inability to void).<br /></br>2. Generate differential for retention in an older man: BPH, obstructing prostate cancer, acute bacterial prostatitis, neurogenic bladder, urethral stricture, medications, severe constipation.<br /></br>3. Use positives/negatives:<br /></br>   • Enlarged but nontender, smooth prostate → favors BPH.<br /></br>   • Afebrile, normal vitals → argues against prostatitis.<br /></br>   • No nodularity → argues against prostate cancer.<br /></br>4. Rule out non-urologic causes: Volvulus or UTI would not give a palpable distended bladder and classically do not create urinary retention.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In elderly men, the most common cause of acute urinary retention is benign prostatic hyperplasia.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Suprapubic palpable bladder; DRE – smooth, diffusely enlarged prostate without nodules; impression – acute urinary retention 2° BPH.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• BPH = non-malignant hyperplasia of periurethral (transitional zone) glands driven by DHT (dihydrotestosterone).<br /></br>• Progressive narrowing of the prostatic urethra → increased resistance to flow → obstructive LUTS (lower urinary tract symptoms: hesitancy, weak stream, incomplete emptying) which can culminate in complete retention.<br /></br>• Bladder overdistension triggers suprapubic pain and fullness.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Place Foley catheter to decompress bladder.<br /></br>Long-term:<br /></br>• α-1 antagonists (e.g., tamsulosin) for rapid symptom relief.<br /></br>• 5-α-reductase inhibitors (e.g., finasteride) to shrink prostate.<br /></br>• Surgical options (TURP) if medical therapy fails or complications (recurrent retention, infections, stones, renal insufficiency).</p></br><p>Work-up: UA to rule out infection/hematuria, creatinine to assess post-renal obstruction, ultrasound (if concern for hydronephrosis).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Memory Tips<br /></br>“SUPer Pubic pain = Super full bladder from Smooth, Uniform Prostate” → think BPH.<br /></br>BPH = “Big Puffy Homogenous Prostate.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Acute bacterial prostatitis<br /></br>• Would present with fever, chills, systemic toxicity; prostate is exquisitely tender/boggy on DRE.</p></br><p>c. Cecal volvulus<br /></br>• Causes colicky abdominal pain, distension, obstructive GI symptoms (nausea, vomiting) with tympanitic mass in right upper quadrant, not a midline suprapubic fullness; no urinary urgency.</p></br><p>d. Prostatic adenocarcinoma<br /></br>• Typically asymptomatic or presents with hard, nodular, asymmetric prostate; acute retention uncommon.</p></br><p>e. Sigmoid volvulus<br /></br>• Massive abdominal distension, “coffee-bean” sign on x-ray; no urinary symptoms.</p></br><p>f. Urinary tract infection<br /></br>• Presents with dysuria, frequency, urgency, possibly fever; does not usually cause inability to void and would not create a palpable bladder.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Takeaway<br /></br>An elderly man with acute urinary retention, suprapubic pain, and a smooth enlarged prostate is classic for BPH—always think “BPH until proven otherwise” on NBME/Step-2 questions involving painless urinary obstruction in older males.</p>
137. A 6-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his parents because of a 2-h history of severe right leg pain and refusal to walk. They say he has intermittently reported pain in his leg during the past 2 weeks. He has no history of trauma or serious illness and receives no medications. Growth and development are appropriate for age. His temperature is 38.1°C (100.5°F), pulse is 100/min, and blood pressure is 100/80 mm Hg. There is warmth and tenderness to palpation of the proximal aspect of the right tibia with an associated firm mass. Distal pulses are intact. Range of motion of the right knee is limited by pain. Muscle strength in the right lower extremity is 4/5 because of pain and 5/5 elsewhere. Sensation is intact. Laboratory studies show a hemoglobin concentration of 10 g/dL, leukocyte count of 20,000/mm³, and erythrocyte sedimentation rate of 10 mm/h. X-rays show a 6-cm, poorly defined lytic lesion in the proximal tibial metaphysis with adjacent periosteal reaction. The results of a biopsy are shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Acute lymphoblastic leukemia</br>b. Eosinophilic granuloma</br>c. Ewing sarcoma</br>d. Osteoid osteoma</br>e. Osteomyelitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Age 6 years – peak age for ALL (2–10 yr).<br /></br>• Bone symptoms: 2-week history of intermittent leg pain now acutely severe, firm metaphyseal mass, warmth/tenderness ➔ marrow/periosteal infiltration common in ALL and can mimic infection or primary bone tumor.<br /></br>• Systemic findings: Low-grade fever, pallor (Hgb 10 g/dL), marked leukocytosis (20 000/µL) without an elevated ESR (10 mm/h). In infectious osteomyelitis or Ewing sarcoma the ESR/CRP is usually markedly high.<br /></br>• Imaging: Large, ill-defined lytic metaphyseal lesion with periosteal reaction. Leukemic infiltration often produces metaphyseal lucencies and periosteal new bone.<br /></br>• Biopsy slide (not shown here): Sheets of small, immature lymphoid “blasts” with scant cytoplasm, high N:C ratio, PAS-positive cytoplasm, TdT+, CD10+ – classic for ALL.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. High fever + bone pain ➔ infection vs malignant marrow process.<br /></br>2. Normal ESR argues against pyogenic osteomyelitis (ESR classically &gt;40–60 mm/h).<br /></br>3. Large metaphyseal lesion in a young child plus anemia &amp; leukocytosis ➔ consider marrow-based malignancy (ALL, occasionally metastatic neuroblastoma).<br /></br>4. Histology clinches the diagnosis: lymphoblasts = ALL.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Leukemic marrow infiltration frequently presents with focal bone pain, lytic metaphyseal lesions, and a normal or only mildly elevated ESR, distinguishing it from pyogenic osteomyelitis or primary bone tumors.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• ALL = malignant proliferation of immature B- or T-cell precursors.<br /></br>• Marrow overcrowding → anemia, neutropenia or leukocytosis, thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• Leukemic cells extend through the cortex → periosteal irritation and lytic lesions, causing localized pain and swelling.</p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests</br>1. Confirm with peripheral smear + bone-marrow aspiration showing ≥25 % lymphoblasts.<br /></br>2. Flow cytometry: TdT+, CD10/CD19 (B-cell) or CD3 (T-cell).<br /></br>3. Therapy: multi-agent chemotherapy (vincristine + prednisone, add daunorubicin, pegaspargase) with CNS prophylaxis (intrathecal methotrexate ± cytarabine). Bone pain resolves rapidly once treatment begins.</p></br><p>Mnemonic</br>“ALL little legs can Ache”:<br /></br>A – Age 2-10<br /></br>L – Leukocytosis ± blasts<br /></br>L – Lucent metaphyseal lesions<br /></br>Ache – Bone pain/swelling</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Eosinophilic granuloma (Langerhans cell histiocytosis) – Usually well-circumscribed punched-out lesion; mild/no systemic symptoms; biopsy shows Birbeck-granule Langerhans cells, not lymphoblasts.<br /></br>c. Ewing sarcoma – Onion-skin diaphyseal lesion in 10–20-yr-olds; ESR high; biopsy small round blue cells with t(11;22) EWS-FLI1 fusion.<br /></br>d. Osteoid osteoma – &lt;2 cm nidus, nocturnal pain dramatically relieved by NSAIDs; radiograph shows central radiolucent nidus with surrounding sclerosis.<br /></br>e. Osteomyelitis – Metaphyseal lytic focus + periosteal reaction and warmth fit, but ESR and CRP are high, not 10 mm/h; biopsy shows necrotic bone with neutrophils, not blasts.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip</br>Think “ALL can fool us all” – childhood leukemia can masquerade as infection or bone tumors; a NORMAL ESR in the face of striking bone findings should raise suspicion for marrow malignancy rather than osteomyelitis.</p>
138. A 39-year-old man comes to the office because of a 1-month history of intermittent regurgitation and constipation. He has regurgitated undigested food four times weekly. He previously had daily bowel movements; during the past month, he has had a bowel movement every 3 to 4 days. He also has a 1-year history of progressive difficulty swallowing. He says initially he had difficulty swallowing solids. He now also has difficulty swallowing liquids. When he eats, he occasionally has moderate pain in the lower sternal area that radiates upward. He emigrated from Brazil 5 years ago. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. A chest x-ray shows a dilated esophagus with an air-fluid level. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Barrett esophagus</br>b. Nutcracker esophagus</br>c. Schatzki ring</br>d. South American trypanosomiasis</br>e. Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. South American trypanosomiasis (Chagas disease)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• Progressive dysphagia: began with solids ➜ now includes liquids → suggests a motility disorder (achalasia pattern).<br /></br>• Regurgitation of UNDIGESTED food &amp; chest pain → esophageal stasis, typical of achalasia/mega-esophagus.<br /></br>• CXR: dilated esophagus with an air-fluid level = “mega-esophagus.”<br /></br>• Brazilian immigrant → endemic area for Trypanosoma cruzi.  </p></br><p>Taken together, the vignette is classic for achalasia secondary to chronic T. cruzi infection (Chagas disease).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Dysphagia solids → liquids = obstruction evolving into global motility failure.<br /></br>2. Imaging shows massively dilated esophagus = outflow obstruction at the LES.<br /></br>3. Differential for achalasia: idiopathic vs secondary (Chagas, malignancy).<br /></br>4. Epidemiology (Brazil) rules-in Chagas and essentially rules-out malignancy in a healthy 39-y/o.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Always think “Chagas” when you see mega-esophagus/megacolon + South American origin.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real patient): progressive dysphagia pattern, regurgitation frequency, weight change, travel/immigration history, CXR findings.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Trypanosoma cruzi invades and destroys the Auerbach (myenteric) plexus.<br /></br>• Loss of inhibitory neurons → LES fails to relax, leading to functional obstruction.<br /></br>• Proximal esophagus dilates over time (“mega-esophagus”).<br /></br>• Same neuronal destruction can cause megacolon and cardiomyopathy (dilated or arrhythmogenic).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (quick board-relevant points)<br /></br>Diagnostics: Barium swallow (bird-beak), manometry (↑ LES resting pressure, absent peristalsis).<br /></br>Acute/structural management: <br /></br> • Pneumatic dilation, Heller myotomy, or LES botulinum toxin.<br /></br>Antiparasitic therapy (benznidazole, nifurtimox) is useful early but does not reverse established neuronal loss.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Chagas = Cruzi Causes ‘C’s” → Cardiac (dilated), Colon (mega-), and esophagus (mega-).<br /></br>Picture a map of Brazil shaped like a dilated esophagus to recall South American link.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Barrett esophagus<br /></br>• Complication of chronic GERD → heartburn, not progressive dysphagia until strictures/cancer.<br /></br>• CXR usually normal; no mega-esophagus.</p></br><p>b. Nutcracker (hypertensive) esophagus<br /></br>• Manometry shows high-pressure, coordinated contractions → episodic chest pain/dysphagia, NOT progressive and no esophageal dilation.</p></br><p>c. Schatzki ring<br /></br>• Intermittent solid-food (“steakhouse”) dysphagia, sudden obstruction episodes.<br /></br>• Thin mucosal ring at GE junction; imaging would not show massive dilation or air-fluid level.</p></br><p>e. Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)<br /></br>• Atrophy of smooth muscle → ↓ LES tone → severe reflux, not LES hypertension.<br /></br>• Frequently accompanied by Raynaud, skin thickening; imaging shows a patulous (wide-open) rather than obstructed esophagus.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>An immigrant from Brazil with progressive dysphagia (solids → liquids), regurgitation, and mega-esophagus on CXR has achalasia caused by Chagas disease—South American trypanosomiasis.</p>
139. A hospitalized 27-year-old woman with sickle cell crisis has shortness of breath and nonproductive cough 4 hours after transfusion of 1 unit of packed red blood cells. Her only medication is morphine. Her temperature is 38°C (100.4°F), pulse is 126/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 98/58 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 88%. Examination shows central cyanosis. Diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. Heart sounds are normal. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH 7.47</br>Pco2 38 mm Hg</br>Po2 55 mm Hg</br>A chest x-ray shows bilateral pulmonary infiltrates. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. ABO incompatibility</br>b. Anaphylactic transfusion reaction</br>c. Fluid overload</br>d. Hemolytic transfusion reaction</br>e. Transfusion-related acute lung injury</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – How we know it’s TRALI<br /></br>1. Timing: Acute respiratory distress develops 4 hours after starting the transfusion (TRALI classically begins during the transfusion or within 6 hrs).<br /></br>2. Respiratory findings:<br /></br>   • Severe hypoxemia (SpO₂ 88 % on room air; PaO₂ 55 mm Hg).<br /></br>   • Bilateral crackles + bilateral pulmonary infiltrates on CXR → non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema/ARDS picture.<br /></br>3. Vital signs: Fever (38 °C) and hypotension are common in TRALI.<br /></br>4. Volume status: Only 1 unit was given; no JVD, S3, or hypertension → argues against fluid overload (TACO).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Young patient + ≤6 h post-transfusion + ARDS with normal heart exam = TRALI.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>Step 1: New hypoxemia + diffuse infiltrates → think ARDS vs. cardiogenic pulmonary edema.<br /></br>Step 2: Temporal link to transfusion (&lt;6 h) narrows list to TRALI or TACO.<br /></br>Step 3: Hypotension, only 1 unit transfused, no signs of volume overload → favors TRALI.<br /></br>Core takeaway: TRALI is the most common cause of transfusion-associated acute respiratory failure and presents as sudden non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema within 6 h of any blood product.</p></br><p>What would need to be documented: time of symptom onset, transfusion start/stop times &amp; product ID, vitals/O₂ sats, CXR, volume status, and notification of blood bank (mandatory TRALI report).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Donor anti-leukocyte (anti-HLA or anti-HNA) antibodies bind recipient neutrophils → neutrophil activation in pulmonary microvasculature → cytokine release → capillary leakage → non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema (essentially a transfusion-triggered ARDS).<br /></br>• Occurs with any plasma-containing product (RBCs, platelets, FFP).<br /></br>Risk factors: multiparous female donors (higher anti-HLA Ab load), critically ill recipients.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate management:<br /></br>1. Stop transfusion; keep IV line open with normal saline.<br /></br>2. Supportive care—high-flow O₂, may need intubation + PEEP like standard ARDS.<br /></br>3. Notify blood bank; donor units from implicated donor are quarantined.<br /></br>No role for diuretics (not volume-mediated) or steroids (no proven benefit).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“TRALI = TRAnsfusion → Lung Injury”<br /></br>• 6 letters → starts within 6 hours.<br /></br>• Think “Leukocyte antibodies Leak fluid into Lungs.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. ABO incompatibility (acute hemolytic reaction)<br /></br>– Timing: minutes after starting transfusion.<br /></br>– Presentation: high fever, flank/back pain, hemoglobinuria, DIC, NOT isolated respiratory failure or infiltrates.</p></br><p>b. Anaphylactic transfusion reaction<br /></br>– Seconds–minutes after transfusion.<br /></br>– IgA-deficient recipient with anti-IgA antibodies.<br /></br>– Features: hypotension, wheezing, angioedema, urticaria; CXR usually clear.</p></br><p>c. Fluid overload (TACO)<br /></br>– Cardiogenic pulmonary edema; usually &gt;2 units transfused or rapid infusion.<br /></br>– Hypertension, S3, JVD, elevated BNP; improves quickly with IV furosemide.<br /></br>– This patient is hypotensive and received only 1 unit.</p></br><p>d. Hemolytic transfusion reaction (delayed)<br /></br>– Occurs days to weeks later; fever + jaundice, falling hematocrit, positive direct Coombs; lungs typically normal.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In any patient who develops acute hypoxemic respiratory failure and bilateral infiltrates within 6 hours of a transfusion—with no evidence of circulatory overload—think TRALI and stop the transfusion immediately.</p>
140. A 22-year-old man comes to the physician because of a severe nosebleed and a generalized rash for the past 24 hours. He has a 1-month history of fatigue and generalized weakness. His temperature is 36.7°C (98°F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 142/72 mm Hg. Examination of the skin shows diffuse petechiae over the entire body. There is diffuse lymphadenopathy and mild hepatosplenomegaly. The nares are packed with gauze, and there is no active bleeding. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit                                        28%</br>Leukocyte count                            36,000/mm³</br>Segmented neutrophils                   10%</br>Lymphocytes                                    25%</br>Lymphoblasts and other immature forms   65%</br>Platelet count                                  15,000/mm³</br>Serum  </br>Urea nitrogen                              20 mg/dL  </br>Creatinine                                     1.2 mg/dL  </br>Uric acid                                       15.2 mg/dL  </br>A biopsy specimen of bone marrow shows acute lymphoblastic leukemia. In addition to vigorous hydration and alkalinization of the urine, which of the following is most appropriate to administer to prevent acute renal failure during chemotherapy in this patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Acetylcysteine</br>b. Allopurinol</br>c. Lisinopril</br>d. Low-dose dopamine</br>e. Mesna</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Allopurinol</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young adult with newly diagnosed acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL) → very rapid cell turnover.<br /></br>• Extremely high WBC count with 65 % blasts and marked thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• Baseline hyperuricemia (uric acid 15.2 mg/dL).<br /></br>• Question asks for prophylaxis “to prevent acute renal failure during chemotherapy.”<br /></br>Key takeaway: chemolysis of a large tumor burden → Tumor Lysis Syndrome (TLS) → precipitation of uric acid crystals in renal tubules → obstructive AKI unless prevented.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Anticipate TLS whenever chemotherapy is started for a high-grade leukemia/lymphoma with very high cell burden.<br /></br>2. Core metabolic derangements: ↑K⁺, ↑PO₄³⁻, ↓Ca²⁺, ↑uric acid.<br /></br>3. Renal injury results primarily from uric acid (and calcium-phosphate) crystal deposition.<br /></br>4. Mainstays of prophylaxis:<br /></br>   • Vigorous IV hydration + urine alkalinization (already ordered).<br /></br>   • Xanthine-oxidase inhibition (allopurinol, febuxostat) or direct urate oxidation (rasburicase).<br /></br>Therefore, the most appropriate additional drug is allopurinol.</p></br><p>Take-home message: Whenever you see “high blast count + high uric acid” and the stem asks about preventing chemo-induced AKI, think ALL / Burkitt lymphoma → give allopurinol (or rasburicase).</p></br><p>Documentation point: note TLS risk, baseline chem panel, start allopurinol 300 mg PO daily (dose-adjust if CKD), monitor uric acid, K⁺, Ca²⁺, creatinine every 6–8 h.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Cell lysis releases nucleic acids → purine catabolism → hypoxanthine → xanthine → uric acid (via xanthine oxidase).<br /></br>• Acidic distal tubules/collecting ducts favor uric acid crystallization → intratubular obstruction &amp; inflammatory injury → acute kidney injury (AKI).<br /></br>• Allopurinol is a competitive inhibitor of xanthine oxidase → stops formation of uric acid; xanthine &amp; hypoxanthine are more water-soluble and easily excreted.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>First-line TLS prophylaxis:<br /></br>1. Hydration (2–3 L/m²/day).<br /></br>2. Allopurinol 300 mg PO daily (or IV if NPO). Start 1–2 days before chemo.<br /></br>3. Urine alkalinization (controversial but still appears in exam stems).<br /></br>Second-line / high-risk: rasburicase (recombinant urate oxidase) rapidly converts uric acid to allantoin.<br /></br>Labs: baseline BMP, uric acid, LDH; monitor q6–12 h for 24–48 h after chemo.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ALL blasts ALL lyse – give ALLO-purinol.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>TLS triad mnemonic: “PUKe”<br /></br>P = Phosphate ↑<br /></br>U = Uric acid ↑<br /></br>K = Potassium ↑<br /></br>Avoid the “Puke” in kidneys with ALLOpurinol/RASburicase + fluids.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acetylcysteine – Replenishes glutathione; used for acetaminophen toxicity and as a mucolytic, not for TLS.<br /></br>c. Lisinopril – ACE inhibitor; lowers intraglomerular pressure and treats proteinuric CKD/HTN. Would worsen, not prevent, AKI during volume shifts.<br /></br>d. Low-dose dopamine – Once postulated renal vasodilator; studies show no benefit in preventing AKI. Not recommended.<br /></br>e. Mesna – Thiol compound that binds acrolein to prevent hemorrhagic cystitis from cyclophosphamide/ifosfamide, unrelated to uric acid nephropathy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>High-burden leukemias → expect tumor lysis. Combine hydration + urine alkalinization with allopurinol (or rasburicase) to block uric acid–mediated AKI.</p>
141. A 25-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by ambulance 25 minutes after his wife found him unconscious on the floor at home this morning. He was discharged from the military 3 months ago after he sustained a traumatic brain injury and underwent right below-the-knee amputation. Since his return, his family says he has had mood swings, aggressive behavior, and anxiety, which have prevented him from interacting with people. He has major depressive disorder and severe limb pain. His medications are amitriptyline, sertraline, oxycodone, and acetaminophen. Two days ago, he began a short-term course of alprazolam for persistent anxiety. His family says he generally has been adherent to his medication and cognitive behavioral therapy regimens. He does not smoke cigarettes. He drank four 12-oz beers weekly as a teenager; now he drinks eight 12-oz beers weekly. On arrival, he is somnolent and responds to painful stimuli only. Temperature is 36.7°C (98.0°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 10/min and shallow, and blood pressure is 85/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 4 L/min of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 92%. Examination shows warm, dry, flushed skin. The pupils are mydriatic with nystagmus. Air movement is decreased in the lungs. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft. Bowel sounds are decreased. Patellar deep tendon reflexes are 3+ bilaterally. An overdose of which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Acetaminophen</br>b. Alcohol</br>c. Alprazolam</br>d. Amitriptyline</br>e. Oxycodone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Amitriptyline (tricyclic antidepressant)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recently discharged combat veteran with major depressive disorder who is prescribed a tricyclic antidepressant (amitriptyline) for neuropathic limb pain.<br /></br>• Found unconscious; vital-sign pattern: tachycardia (110/min) + hypotension (85/60 mm Hg) + hypoventilation (10/min, ↓ air movement).<br /></br>• Classic anticholinergic/antihistaminic findings: “warm, dry, flushed” skin, mydriasis, ↓ bowel sounds, urinary retention is often present (not tested here).<br /></br>• CNS excitation despite somnolence: nystagmus and brisk (3+) deep-tendon reflexes suggest possible impending seizures.<br /></br>Key negatives: no pinpoint pupils (rules out opioid), no hypoglycemia or right-upper-quadrant tenderness (rules out early acetaminophen toxicity), no ataxia with normal vitals (rules out simple benzodiazepine or alcohol overdose).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify toxidrome: anticholinergic + Na-channel blockade.<br /></br>Step 2 – Match meds taken at home to toxidrome: only tricyclics fit.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Tricyclic antidepressant overdose presents with anticholinergic signs (hot, dry, tachycardic, mydriatic, ileus), CNS excitation/seizures, and cardiovascular instability (hypotension, QRS widening). Immediate airway support and IV sodium bicarbonate are life-saving.</p></br><p>Documentation note: “Patient with suspected TCA overdose—airway protected, EKG ordered to assess QRS/QT, IV NaHCO₃ administered for hypotension and anticipated cardiotoxicity.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• TCAs block muscarinic, histamine-1, and α-1 adrenergic receptors → anticholinergic symptoms &amp; peripheral vasodilation.<br /></br>• They inhibit fast myocardial Na⁺ channels → QRS widening, ventricular arrhythmias, hypotension.<br /></br>• GABA antagonism lowers seizure threshold → hyperreflexia, seizures.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Airway/ventilation (he is hypoventilating).<br /></br>2. IV sodium bicarbonate: alkalinizes serum &amp; increases Na⁺ gradient to overcome channel blockade; also narrows QRS &amp; raises BP.<br /></br>3. Activated charcoal if &lt;2 h ingestion and airway protected.<br /></br>4. Benzodiazepines for seizures (NOT phenytoin; it worsens Na-blockade).<br /></br>5. Continuous cardiac monitoring; consider lipid emulsion for refractory shock.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Tri-C” for TCA overdose:<br /></br>T – Tachycardia &amp; hypotension (cardiac)<br /></br>R – Reduced bowel/urine (anticholinergic) + Reflexes brisk → seizures<br /></br>I – Increased temperature, dry skin<br /></br>C – Coma + wide Complex QRS<br /></br>Picture a red-flushed soldier holding three “C” signs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acetaminophen – Early phase is usually asymptomatic or mild nausea; vitals normal. Anticholinergic signs absent. Hepatic injury appears 24–48 h later.<br /></br>b. Alcohol – Causes CNS depression, hyporeflexia, slurred speech, vasodilation but not anticholinergic dryness or mydriasis; respirations usually fast (metabolic acidosis) rather than slow.<br /></br>c. Alprazolam (benzodiazepine) – Produces sedation, ataxia, normal vital signs; skin moist, pupils normal; reflexes diminished, not brisk. Severe toxicity requires co-ingestant.<br /></br>e. Oxycodone (opioid) – Classic triad: miosis, respiratory depression, CNS depression. Pupils would be pinpoint, not dilated; skin often cool/clammy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Point<br /></br>Dilated pupils + dry flushed skin + ileus + tachycardia + hypotension + CNS excitation in an antidepressant-treated patient = TCA overdose. Treat with airway support and IV sodium bicarbonate immediately.</p>
142. A 29-year-old woman comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Menses occur at regular 28-day intervals, last 5 days, and are accompanied by mild cramps. Her last menstrual period was 3 weeks ago. She is sexually active and monogamous with one male partner. She has a copper IUD in place. Her blood pressure is 118/72 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no abnormalities.
