Pelvic examination shows an IUD string at the cervical os and a mildly tender right adnexal mass.</br>Transvaginal ultrasonography shows a normal uterus and left adnexa and a 5-cm hemorrhagic cyst in the right adnexa. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Ceftriaxone and doxycycline therapy</br>b. Oral contraceptive therapy</br>c. Ovarian cystectomy</br>d. Removal of the IUD</br>e. Repeat transvaginal ultrasonography in 6 weeks</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Ceftriaxone and doxycycline therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Mildly tender right adnexal mass” or uterine/adnexal tenderness = pelvic inflammatory disease (PID) until proved otherwise.<br /></br>• Copper IUD in place → important risk factor for PID, especially within the first few months after insertion (but can occur later).<br /></br>• Ultrasound shows only a 5-cm hemorrhagic cyst (a common, typically self-resolving functional cyst), not a complex mass; radiology does not rule out PID because most PID is a clinical diagnosis.<br /></br>• No systemic toxicity → outpatient PID regimen is appropriate.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>US Preventive Services Task Force and CDC guidelines: Treat empirically for PID in any sexually active woman who has pelvic or lower-abdominal pain plus cervical motion, uterine, or adnexal tenderness when no other cause is identified. Waiting for cultures, imaging changes, or lab confirmation delays care and risks infertility/ectopic pregnancy.<br /></br>Outpatient regimen:<br /></br>• Ceftriaxone 500 mg IM (3rd-generation cephalosporin; N. gonorrhoeae coverage)<br /></br>• Doxycycline 100 mg PO bid × 14 days (C. trachomatis)<br /></br>± Metronidazole 500 mg PO bid × 14 days (anaerobes) – many protocols now include it, but question stem focuses on the two-drug core.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>PID = ascending infection from cervix → endometrium → fallopian tubes/ovaries. Untreated infection causes scarring, infertility, chronic pelvic pain, and ectopic pregnancy. IUD strings can facilitate ascent of pathogens, especially the first 3 mo but risk persists.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Treat immediately when clinical criteria met.<br /></br>• Remove IUD only if no clinical improvement after 48–72 h or if severe infection/toxic.<br /></br>• Imaging (TVUS, CT) reserved for complications (tubo-ovarian abscess) or diagnostic uncertainty.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Cef + Doxy before it’s foxy”: If pelvic tenderness + IUD (or sex risk), give Ceftriaxone + Doxycycline immediately.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Oral contraceptive therapy – Combined OCPs can prevent new functional cysts, but they do NOT treat PID. They also conflict with her preference for copper IUD contraception.<br /></br>c. Ovarian cystectomy – Functional (hemorrhagic) cysts &lt;8 cm in a premenopausal woman usually resolve; surgery is reserved for persistent, large, torsed, or suspicious masses.<br /></br>d. Removal of the IUD – Not first-line. Current guidelines: leave IUD in place during antibiotic therapy unless the patient is severely ill or fails to improve within 72 h.<br /></br>e. Repeat TVUS in 6 weeks – Reasonable for simple hemorrhagic cyst follow-up, but inadequate here because you must not delay empiric PID treatment.  </p></br><p>Key Takeaway<br /></br>In any reproductive-age woman with pelvic/adnexal tenderness—especially with an IUD—empirically treat PID with ceftriaxone plus doxycycline without delaying for imaging or observation.</p>
143. A 20-year-old primigravid woman at 28 weeks’ gestation is brought to the emergency department after she was involved in a high-speed motor vehicle collision. She was the restrained front seat passenger. She has no history of serious illness. Her pregnancy had been uncomplicated. She has moderate neck and back pain and abdominal tenderness. She appears confused. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 100/58 mm Hg. The fetal heart rate is 140–150/min. Examination shows multiple facial contusions. Laboratory studies show a hematocrit of 32%, leukocyte count of 9000/mm^3, and platelet count of 210,000/mm^3. In addition to continuous fetal monitoring, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. CT scan of the head and neck</br>b. Administration of Rho (D) immune globulin</br>c. Terbutaline tocolysis</br>d. Cesarean delivery</br>e. Exploratory laparotomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. CT scan of the head and neck  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Trauma mechanism: “high-speed motor-vehicle collision,” “restrained front-seat passenger.”<br /></br>• Maternal red flags: confusion (possible intracranial injury), neck &amp; back pain (possible cervical spine injury), facial contusions.<br /></br>• Vital signs: tachycardia 120/min and borderline hypotension 100/58 → could be early hemorrhagic shock or neuro-genic etiology, but still stable enough for imaging.<br /></br>• Pregnancy data: 28 weeks, reassuring fetal heart rate 140–150/min, no contractions reported.  </p></br><p>These findings mandate a standard adult trauma work-up—ABCs first, then an urgent search for maternal life-threatening injuries (head/neck). Fetal monitoring has already begun; the next priority is maternal evaluation with the imaging that best visualizes life-threatening pathology.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. ATLS protocol: after airway, breathing, circulation are addressed, the next step is to look for occult bleeding or neurologic injury in the mother.<br /></br>2. The patient is confused → suspect intracranial hemorrhage. Neck pain after high-speed MVC demands cervical spine clearance.<br /></br>3. In pregnancy, necessary diagnostic imaging that changes management is NOT withheld—radiation dose from a head/neck CT is far below the teratogenic threshold and is outside the maternal abdomen.<br /></br>4. Therefore, the most appropriate immediate action—after initiating continuous fetal monitoring—is CT of the head and neck.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In maternal trauma, stabilize and thoroughly evaluate the mother first; appropriate imaging is never delayed because of pregnancy.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record neurologic status, C-spine precautions, informed consent for imaging risks/benefits, and fetal monitoring strip.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• High-energy blunt trauma can cause subdural, epidural, or intracerebral hemorrhage → altered mental status.<br /></br>• Cervical spine fractures/dislocations can cause spinal cord injury; rapid identification with CT is standard.<br /></br>• Ionizing radiation risk: A single head CT ≈ 0.05–0.1 mGy to the fetus (well below 50 mGy threshold for teratogenesis).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>First-line imaging: Non-contrast CT head ± CT angiography if vascular injury suspected; CT C-spine with thin cuts.<br /></br>Adjuncts: Type &amp; screen, Kleihauer-Betke (for fetomaternal hemorrhage) once mother is stable; Rho(D) immune globulin if Rh-negative.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Mom first, baby always benefits”: Stabilize/diagnose maternal injuries according to standard trauma protocol—fetal care follows.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Rho(D) immune globulin<br /></br>• Indicated after trauma to an Rh-negative mother to prevent alloimmunization.<br /></br>• Her Rh status is not provided; even if Rh-negative, administration is important but not as urgent as ruling out life-threatening maternal head/neck injury.</p></br><p>c. Terbutaline tocolysis<br /></br>• Used for preterm labor or uterine tetany from abruption. No contractions, no bleeding, and reassuring fetal tracing → not indicated now.</p></br><p>d. Cesarean delivery<br /></br>• Reserved for non-reassuring fetal status refractory to resuscitation or maternal instability precluding continued pregnancy. Fetal heart rate is reassuring; maternal evaluation should proceed first.</p></br><p>e. Exploratory laparotomy<br /></br>• Indicated for penetrating trauma or signs of intra-abdominal catastrophe (peritonitis, free air, uncontrolled hemorrhage). Abdominal tenderness is mild; no evidence of hemodynamic collapse—CT head/neck is higher priority.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In pregnant trauma patients, follow standard trauma algorithms: once ABCs are addressed and fetal monitoring begun, promptly obtain the imaging necessary to detect maternal life-threatening injuries—even if it involves radiation—because maternal stabilization is the best way to protect the fetus.</p>
144. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of fatigue since starting his freshman year of high school 3 months ago. He often falls asleep during class. He urinates four to five times nightly and often has difficulty falling asleep again. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He is at the 20th percentile for height and above the 95th percentile for weight and BMI. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a velvety, hyperpigmented macular rash over the neck and axillae. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 39%</br>Leukocyte count 5900/mm3</br>Platelet count 401,000/mm3</br>Serum</br>Na+ 133 mEq/L</br>K+ 4.3 mEq/L</br>Cl- 98 mEq/L</br>HCO3- 22 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen 14 mg/dL</br>Glucose 148 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 0.6 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>pH 5</br>Specific gravity 1.035</br>Glucose 2+</br>Ketones negative</br>This patient is at increased risk for which of the following complications of his current condition during the next 15 years?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Adenocarcinoma of the bowel</br>b. Adrenal insufficiency</br>c. Coronary artery disease</br>d. Diabetes insipidus</br>e. Ruptured cervical disc</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Coronary artery disease (CAD)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key findings: 15-year-old, obese (BMI &gt;95th percentile), nocturia/polyuria, random glucose 148 mg/dL, 2+ glucosuria, acanthosis nigricans.<br /></br>• These findings = insulin resistance → early-onset type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM).<br /></br>• No ketones → not DKA; normal vitals and electrolytes further support uncomplicated, newly recognized T2DM.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1 – Recognize the disease: Obese adolescent + acanthosis nigricans + hyperglycaemia = type 2 DM.<br /></br>Step 2 – Ask what the NBME loves to test: “What kills or disables patients with T2DM the fastest?” → Macrovascular disease (MI, stroke, peripheral arterial disease).<br /></br>Step 3 – Apply the 15-year window: Starting at age 15, by age 30 the most likely serious complication is premature atherosclerotic CAD. Microvascular complications (retinopathy, nephropathy, neuropathy) also occur, but frank MI is both common and test-favored.</p></br><p>Take-home: Cardiovascular disease is the leading cause of morbidity and mortality in all diabetics, and risk begins soon after diagnosis—even in teenagers.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Insulin resistance → hyperinsulinaemia, hyperglycaemia, dyslipidaemia, endothelial dysfunction.<br /></br>• Accelerated atherogenesis: ↑LDL oxidation, ↓HDL, chronic inflammation → earlier plaque formation and destabilisation → MI/angina in 20- to 30-year-olds with juvenile-onset T2DM.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Monitoring (high-yield points)</br>1. Intensive lifestyle modification (diet, exercise, weight loss).<br /></br>2. Metformin first-line; add GLP-1 agonist (liraglutide) or SGLT2 inhibitor (empagliflozin) if needed.<br /></br>3. Aggressive cardiovascular risk reduction:</br>   • Statin starting at age &gt;10 yrs if LDL ≥100 mg/dL or additional risk factors.<br /></br>   • ACE inhibitor/ARB for BP &gt;130/80 mm Hg or microalbuminuria.<br /></br>   • Annual fasting lipid profile, HbA1c q3 mo, BP each visit.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip</br>“Diabetics die of DISease of large DIStributors (large arteries).”<br /></br>Visualize a teenage “2” (type 2) holding a big heart—his biggest problem is the heart.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Adenocarcinoma of the bowel – DM modestly increases colorectal cancer risk, but not as dramatically or as early as CAD; 15-year horizon favors CAD.<br /></br>b. Adrenal insufficiency – Autoimmune Addison disease is linked to type 1 DM (autoimmune), not type 2 insulin-resistant DM.<br /></br>d. Diabetes insipidus – Unrelated to insulin resistance; presents with dilute urine (low specific gravity), not 1.035.<br /></br>e. Ruptured cervical disc – Obesity is a risk factor for lumbar (not cervical) disc disease; far less common and not directly tied to DM.</p></br><p>Bottom Line</br>In adolescents with newly diagnosed type 2 diabetes, premature coronary artery disease is the most likely serious complication they will face over the next decade-plus.</p>
145. A previously healthy 3-year-old boy who is scheduled for an elective operation begins to cry and cling tightly to his mother on arrival at the hospital. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this behavior?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Fear of mutilation</br>b. Fear of pain</br>c. Fear of separation from parent</br>d. Panic disorder</br>e. Stranger anxiety</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>Fear of mutilation (bodily injury)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 3 years → developmentally in the early preschool period (≈3–6 yr).<br /></br>• Situation: Elective surgery/hospital setting.<br /></br>• Behavior: Crying and clinging to mother immediately on arrival – a classic stress response in a preschooler facing a medical procedure.</p></br><p>Key developmental clue: Preschoolers have vivid imaginations but poorly defined body boundaries, so anything involving needles, scalpels, or “cuts” is perceived as literal loss of body parts (“mutilation”). That fear overrides other anxieties once they know a procedure is coming.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify developmental stage.<br /></br>2. Recall the predominant hospital-related fear for that stage.<br /></br>3. Match to choices.</p></br><p>Core takeaway:<br /></br>• Infants (6–18 mo): stranger anxiety<br /></br>• Toddlers (1–3 yr): separation anxiety<br /></br>• Preschoolers (3–6 yr): fear of bodily injury/mutilation<br /></br>• School-age (7–11 yr): loss of control, physical disability<br /></br>• Adolescents: loss of independence, body image</p></br><p>What to document if this were real: “Child demonstrates developmentally appropriate fear of bodily injury before procedure; will employ age-appropriate preparation, distraction, parental presence.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology / Developmental Review<br /></br>• Cognition in preschoolers is pre-operational (Piaget): magical thinking + limited concept of body integrity.<br /></br>• A needle stick or incision is interpreted as permanent removal of body parts.<br /></br>• Stress manifests as clinging, crying, or resistance.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Management Pearls<br /></br>1. Provide simple concrete explanations (“The doctor will fix your arm; your body will stay all together”).<br /></br>2. Use therapeutic play (dolls, drawings) to demonstrate the procedure.<br /></br>3. Allow parental presence during induction when possible.<br /></br>4. Offer choices to give a sense of control (e.g., which flavor mask).<br /></br>No labs or imaging needed—this is anticipatory guidance.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“3 to 6 = Fix-Sticks.”<br /></br>Imagine a 3-year-old counting six band-aids, afraid each one hides a missing piece of skin.</p></br><p>OR use the ladder:<br /></br>• Stranger (Infant) → Separation (Toddler) → Surgery cuts (Preschool).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Fear of pain – Pain is real, but preschoolers focus more on <em>what the needle will do</em> (mutilate) than the sensation itself.<br /></br>b. Fear of separation from parent – Peaks 9–18 months, fades by 3 yr; he clings because he thinks mom can “keep him in one piece,” not primarily because he fears her absence.<br /></br>c. Panic disorder – Requires recurrent unexpected panic attacks in older children/adults; not developmentally appropriate here.<br /></br>d. Stranger anxiety – Peaks at 6–12 months. A 3-year-old usually tolerates strangers unless they threaten bodily integrity.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In the hospital, a 3-year-old’s dominant concern is that procedures will “take parts away.” Address that fear directly with clear, simple, reassuring explanations and parental presence.</p>
146. A 19-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department by her mother 30 minutes after ingesting an unknown liquid she found in her parents' utility room following an argument with her boyfriend. Immediately after swallowing the liquid, she telephoned her mother at work, who arrived at home 20 minutes later and found her daughter drooling and reporting severe upper chest pain. On arrival at the emergency department, the patient is in obvious distress and is drooling and unable to swallow. Temperature is 37.0°C (98.6°F), pulse is 120/min and regular, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 148/90 mm Hg. Physical examination shows profuse clear drainage from the mouth and erythema and edema of the posterior oropharynx. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. Two large-bore intravenous catheters are inserted for fluid administration and a dose of intravenous hydromorphone is administered. The patient begins to feel more comfortable. Results of laboratory studies are within the reference ranges. Chest x-ray shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administration of activated charcoal</br>b. Administration of oral bicarbonate</br>c. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy</br>d. Induction of vomiting</br>e. Nasogastric tube placement</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy (EGD) within the first 12–24 hours</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Unknown liquid … utility room” → likely a caustic (strong acid/alkali, e.g., drain cleaner).<br /></br>• Immediate pain, drooling, inability to swallow, posterior-pharyngeal erythema/edema → classic caustic burn of the oropharynx/esophagus.<br /></br>• Normal chest x-ray &amp; vitals stable → no radiographic evidence of perforation or mediastinitis, but mucosal injury still likely.<br /></br>• Key negatives: no mention of pill fragments (rules against tablet overdose), no charcoal-amenable toxin, no altered mental status (airway is patent).<br /></br>These findings narrow the differential to caustic ingestion requiring mucosal injury grading.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Stabilize ABCs (done: IV access, analgesia).<br /></br>Step 2 – Evaluate extent of GI injury. The gold standard is diagnostic EGD performed early (ideally within 12–24 h) before edema/fibrosis obscure visualization.<br /></br>Step 3 – Management thereafter (NPO, proton-pump inhibitor, surgical consult) depends on EGD grade.<br /></br>Take-home: Any symptomatic caustic ingestion → early endoscopic evaluation.</p></br><p>Document in the chart: time of ingestion, substance (if known), airway status, hemodynamics, exam of oropharynx, plan for NPO &amp; urgent EGD.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Alkalis (most household cleaners) cause liquefaction necrosis → deep transmural injury; acids cause coagulation necrosis. The severity cannot be predicted by symptoms alone; hence direct visualization is required.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic Tests &amp; Treatment Review<br /></br>• EGD within 12–24 h (contraindicated if frank perforation or severe hemodynamic instability).<br /></br>• NPO, IV fluids, IV PPI to reduce gastric acid stimulation of injured tissue.<br /></br>• Surgical evaluation for perforation (free air, severe pain, peritonitis).<br /></br>• Long-term: stricture surveillance.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Caustic = Camera” – If it burns on the way down, look with a scope ASAP (unless perforated).<br /></br>AVOID “CAN” mnemonic (Charcoal, Alkali-neutralization, NG tube/Nausea-induction) – all worsen outcome.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Activated charcoal – Ineffective for corrosives; adheres poorly and obscures endoscopic view. Risk of vomiting/aspiration.<br /></br>b. Oral bicarbonate (neutralization) – Exothermic reaction may worsen mucosal injury; delays EGD.<br /></br>d. Induction of vomiting – Re-exposes esophagus/oropharynx to the caustic, increasing burn depth and perforation risk.<br /></br>e. Nasogastric tube placement – Blind insertion can perforate a friable esophagus and pushes caustic further into stomach; placed only after EGD under direct visualization if needed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Symptomatic caustic ingestion requires rapid airway assessment and early endoscopic evaluation (within 24 h) to grade injury and guide further management; do NOT give charcoal, neutralizing agents, induce emesis, or blindly insert tubes.</p>
147. A 23-year-old male college student comes to the clinic for a follow-up visit 14 days after undergoing emergent splenectomy following a motor vehicle collision. The patient has resumed his regular diet and is ambulating normally. Medical history otherwise is unremarkable, and his only medication is acetaminophen as needed. The patient completed his Haemophilus influenzae type b vaccinations by age 18 months and his varicella, polio, and measles-mumps-rubella vaccinations by age 5 years; he received a human papillomavirus vaccination at age 11 years. Temperature is 37.0°C (98.6°F), pulse is 82/min, and blood pressure is 116/54 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows a well-healing midline incision. Which of the following vaccinations is most appropriate to administer to this patient today?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Haemophilus influenzae type b</br>b. Human papillomavirus</br>c. Inactivated poliovirus</br>d. Measles, mumps, rubella</br>e. Varicella</br>f. No vaccine is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Haemophilus influenzae type b (Hib) vaccine  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clue: “14 days after emergent splenectomy.”  Anatomic (or functional) asplenia places patients at lifelong, markedly increased risk of fulminant sepsis with encapsulated bacteria.<br /></br>• Timing: ACIP/IDSA recommend giving certain vaccines ≥14 days before or after splenectomy; today is exactly 14 days post-op, so administration is appropriate now.<br /></br>• Encapsulated organisms of greatest concern: Streptococcus pneumoniae, Neisseria meningitidis, and Haemophilus influenzae type b.<br /></br>• Only one of the vaccines in the answer list protects against an encapsulated bacterium—Hib. Therefore, Hib is the best choice among the options provided.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify risk: Newly asplenic adult → overwhelming postsplenectomy infection (OPSI).<br /></br>2. Recall required vaccines:<br /></br>   • Pneumococcal series (PCV13 followed 8 wk later by PPSV23)<br /></br>   • Meningococcal ACWY (and MenB series)<br /></br>   • One-time Hib booster<br /></br>3. Scan answer choices: Only Hib is present; therefore select it.<br /></br>Take-home: Every asplenic patient needs targeted encapsulated-organism immunization, with Hib given once ≥14 days peri-splenectomy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• The spleen removes opsonized, encapsulated bacteria from the bloodstream.<br /></br>• After splenectomy, IgM-producing marginal-zone B cells and splenic macrophages are lost → impaired complement activation and poor clearance of encapsulated organisms → risk of rapid septicemia.<br /></br>• Vaccination provides protective IgG that can opsonize these pathogens in the absence of splenic function.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Give vaccines at least 14 days before elective splenectomy; if not possible, give ≥14 days after emergent splenectomy (as in this patient).<br /></br>• Long-term: some clinicians prescribe prophylactic oral penicillin for the first 1–5 years or for life in high-risk adults; all patients need emergency antibiotics at first fever.<br /></br>• Patients must carry a medical alert bracelet or card indicating “Asplenia.”</p></br><p>Memory Tips<br /></br>“ SHiN OPSI” – Spleen lost → Haemophilus influenzae, Neisseria meningitidis, Strep pneumoniae cause Overwhelming Post-Splenectomy Infection. Vaccinate for all three.<br /></br>“14-day Rule” – Give the asplenia vaccines ≥14 days before or after surgery.</p></br><p>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Human papillomavirus: The patient already began the HPV series at age 11. Additional doses are needed only if the series is incomplete; OPSI risk is unrelated to HPV.<br /></br>c. Inactivated poliovirus: Routine childhood series completed; no special indication with asplenia.<br /></br>d. Measles, mumps, rubella (MMR): Already immune from childhood series; MMR does not address encapsulated bacterial risk.<br /></br>e. Varicella: Completed in childhood; unrelated to splenic function.<br /></br>f. No vaccine indicated: Incorrect—newly asplenic patients absolutely require vaccines against encapsulated organisms; delaying vaccination increases mortality from OPSI.</p></br><p>Core Concept<br /></br>Newly asplenic patients need targeted vaccination against encapsulated bacteria. At or beyond the 14-day postoperative mark, a one-time Hib booster (along with pneumococcal and meningococcal vaccines) is essential to prevent overwhelming postsplenectomy infection.</p>
148. A previously healthy 19-year-old college student comes to the emergency department because of a 3-day history of lower abdominal pain and vaginal spotting. Menses usually occur at regular 28-day intervals, but she cannot recall the date of her last menstrual period. She takes no medications. She is sexually active and uses condoms inconsistently. She appears pale but is alert and answers questions appropriately. Her pulse is 110/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 80/50 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows lower quadrant tenderness with marked guarding. Pelvic examination shows a soft uterus and adnexal tenderness greater on the right than on the left. Her hemoglobin concentration is 6.2 g/dL, hematocrit is 18%, and serum β-hCG concentration is 6645 mIU/mL. Urinalysis shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. CT scan of the abdomen</br>c. Intravenous antibiotic therapy</br>d. Culdocentesis</br>e. Exploratory laparotomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Exploratory laparotomy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• 19-year-old woman with lower abdominal pain + vaginal spotting → early-pregnancy complication on differential.<br /></br>• Cannot recall LMP + β-hCG 6645 mIU/mL → pregnancy confirmed, ~5–6 gestational weeks.<br /></br>• Vital signs: BP 80/50 mm Hg, HR 110 → hemodynamic instability / class III hemorrhagic shock.<br /></br>• Exam: Marked guarding, unilateral (R&gt;L) adnexal tenderness → peritoneal irritation, suggests ruptured ectopic rather than intra-uterine pregnancy.<br /></br>• Profound anemia (Hgb 6.2 g/dL, Hct 18 %) → acute intra-abdominal blood loss (hemoperitoneum).<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No UTI findings on UA; tenderness out of proportion to a typical threatened abortion; no fever or cervical motion tenderness to suggest PID.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Positive pregnancy test + unilateral pelvic pain + hypotension/acute anemia” = ruptured ectopic pregnancy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Diagnose the emergency – ruptured ectopic pregnancy with intraperitoneal bleeding.<br /></br>Step 2: Assess stability – patient is unstable (hypotensive, tachycardic, Hgb 6).<br /></br>Step 3: Management algorithm –<br /></br>• Unstable or peritoneal signs → immediate surgical intervention to control bleeding (exploratory laparotomy or laparoscopy) + IV fluids, blood transfusion.<br /></br>• Stable → confirm dx with transvaginal US &amp; treat (methotrexate vs. lap surgery).<br /></br>Because time to definitive hemorrhage control is life-saving, imaging studies or expectant measures are contraindicated.  </p></br><p>Take-home core concept: In a pregnant patient with suspected ruptured ectopic and hemodynamic instability, go straight to the OR.  </p></br><p>Documentation must include: suspicion of ruptured ectopic, hemodynamic parameters, resuscitative measures initiated, informed consent for emergent surgery.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Ectopic pregnancy = implantation outside uterine cavity (≈95 % fallopian tube). Tubal wall cannot expand → trophoblastic invasion → luminal rupture ≈6-8 weeks gestation → intra-abdominal hemorrhage → peritoneal irritation &amp; hypovolemic shock. Risk factors: prior PID, tubal surgery, smoking, but 50 % have none (as in this patient).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (when patient is stable)<br /></br>• β-hCG ≥1500 mIU/mL but empty uterus on transvaginal US strongly suggests ectopic.<br /></br>• Medical: IM methotrexate if stable, small mass (&lt;3.5 cm), no fetal cardiac activity.<br /></br>• Surgical: Laparoscopic salpingostomy or salpingectomy if contraindications to MTX, failed MTX, mass &gt;3.5 cm, or rupture.<br /></br>Unstable or ruptured → emergent laparotomy/laparoscopy + salpingectomy, IV fluids, type-specific blood.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Mnemonics / Memory Aid<br /></br>“Ruptured ECTOPIC = ECTOPIC CRASH”<br /></br>E – Ectopic suspected<br /></br>C – Clinical shock (BP ↓, HR ↑)<br /></br>T – Tender abdomen with guarding<br /></br>O – Out-of-uterus β-hCG<br /></br>P – Pale &amp; anemic<br /></br>I – Immediate<br /></br>C – Cut (surgical)  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Observation only – Delays definitive care; mortality from ongoing intraperitoneal bleed.<br /></br>b. CT scan abdomen – Time-consuming, lower sensitivity for ectopic; unstable patients go directly to OR, not to radiology.<br /></br>c. Intravenous antibiotic therapy – Appropriate for pelvic inflammatory disease or tubo-ovarian abscess (would have fever, purulent discharge, cervical motion tenderness). Does not treat hemorrhage.<br /></br>d. Culdocentesis – Old diagnostic test (needle into posterior fornix to aspirate blood). In the modern era, unstable patients require emergent surgery, not bedside diagnostic procedures.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Ordering a pelvic ultrasound first. Imaging is correct only if the patient is hemodynamically stable; instability trumps diagnostics.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Pregnant patient + unilateral pain + signs of intraperitoneal hemorrhage = ruptured ectopic → stabilize and proceed to emergent exploratory laparotomy/laparoscopy to control bleeding and save the patient’s life.</p>
149. A 17-year-old boy is brought to the office by his parents because of changes in his behavior during the past 6 months. They say he has become withdrawn and moody and that his grades have declined. He has returned home from parties intoxicated three times during the past month. During a wedding reception 3 weeks ago, he was delirious, incoherent, and imbalanced. The patient says he drinks alcohol but that his drinking is not a problem and he can stop drinking whenever he wants. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he is calm and cooperative. He reports no insomnia, hypersomnia, or suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Alcohol use disorder</br>b. Conduct disorder</br>c. Major depressive disorder</br>d. Normal adolescent behavior</br>e. Recreational alcohol use</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Alcohol use disorder (AUD)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age/setting: 17-year-old adolescent.<br /></br>• Pertinent positives<br /></br>  – Recurrent intoxication: “returned home from parties intoxicated three times during the past month.”<br /></br>  – Alcohol-related impairment: “grades have declined,” “withdrawn and moody,” episode of marked intoxication (“delirious, incoherent, imbalanced” at wedding).<br /></br>  – Lack of insight/denial: “drinking is not a problem and he can stop whenever he wants.”<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No evidence of depression (no insomnia/hypersomnia, suicidality), no aggressive/illegal behavior, normal vitals/exam.<br /></br>Pattern: Recurrent, maladaptive alcohol use causing social/academic impairment → classic for Alcohol Use Disorder.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>DSM-5 criteria for AUD = ≥2 of 11 features occurring within 12 months. This patient meets at least three:<br /></br>1. Recurrent use resulting in failure to fulfill major role obligations (declining grades).<br /></br>2. Recurrent use in physically hazardous situations (severe intoxication at wedding).<br /></br>3. Continued use despite social/interpersonal problems (parents concerned, withdrawal from others).<br /></br>Severity: 2-3 criteria = mild AUD; ≥4 = moderate; ≥6 = severe.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Any substance use that repeatedly causes functional impairment or distress in ≤12 months = “use disorder,” even in adolescents.</p></br><p>Documentation tip: List specific DSM criteria met, note functional impairment, recommend counseling / substance-use evaluation.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Repeated ethanol exposure depletes GABA receptors and up-regulates NMDA glutamate receptors → tolerance, withdrawal risk.<br /></br>• Adolescents are particularly vulnerable to reward-pathway sensitization (dopamine in nucleus accumbens), promoting continued use despite consequences.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Brief motivational interviewing (first step).<br /></br>2. Family therapy, school involvement.<br /></br>3. If ≥18 y or severe dependence, consider pharmacologic aids (naltrexone, acamprosate, disulfiram).<br /></br>4. Screen for co-occurring psych disorders.<br /></br>No additional labs required to make DSM diagnosis, but baseline CMP, LFTs often ordered.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>CAGE + Fail:<br /></br>C – Cut down?<br /></br>A – Annoyed criticism?<br /></br>G – Guilty?<br /></br>E – Eye-opener?<br /></br>Any “YES” plus measurable Failure in school/work = AUD.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answers Explained<br /></br>b. Conduct disorder – Requires ≥3 behaviors such as aggression to people/animals, destruction, theft, serious rule violations. None are present.<br /></br>c. Major depressive disorder – Must have ≥5 SIGECAPS symptoms for ≥2 weeks with depressed mood or anhedonia. He denies sleep change, suicidality, etc. Mood change here is better explained by substance use.<br /></br>d. Normal adolescent behavior – Occasional experimentation may occur, but repeated intoxication with academic decline exceeds “normal.” Functional impairment rules this out.<br /></br>e. Recreational alcohol use – Defined by infrequent, controlled use without social/academic/legal consequences. The patient has clear negative consequences and impaired control → therefore “recreational” is incorrect.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Any adolescent with repeated alcohol intoxication causing school or social problems meets criteria for Alcohol Use Disorder—label it as such and intervene early.</p>
150. A 47-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 9-month history of hot flashes, night sweats, and pain during sexual intercourse. Her last menstrual period was 1 year ago. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Pelvic examination shows vaginal atrophy and no cervical mucus. A Pap smear and mammography show no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Pregnancy test</br>b. Measurement of serum luteinizing hormone concentration</br>c. Hormone therapy</br>d. Levothyroxine therapy</br>e. Vaginal corticosteroid therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Pregnancy test  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 47-year-old (perimenopausal age), 12-month amenorrhea, vasomotor symptoms (hot flashes, night sweats), genitourinary syndrome of menopause (vaginal atrophy, dyspareunia), normal Pap/mammogram.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No systemic signs of thyroid disease, no vulvar dermatoses, no palpable masses.<br /></br>• Pattern: Classic menopausal presentation, but in any woman with recent amenorrhea—even if menopause is likely—pregnancy must be ruled out before starting therapy that contains estrogen or progestin.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Does the history meet clinical criteria for menopause?<br /></br>– Yes: ≥12 mo of amenorrhea in a woman &gt;45 yr plus typical symptoms → menopause is very likely.<br /></br>Step 2: Can pregnancy still occur at this age?<br /></br>– Yes. Ovarian function is diminished but not zero; sporadic ovulation can still happen in the perimenopausal window.<br /></br>Step 3: Would the contemplated treatment change if she were pregnant?<br /></br>– Absolutely. Systemic estrogen ± progestin (hormone therapy) is contraindicated in pregnancy. Therefore, you must “pee before you prescribe.”<br /></br>Thus the single most appropriate next step is a urine (or serum) β-hCG test.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Menopause = ovarian follicular depletion → ↓ estrogen &amp; inhibin → loss of negative feedback → ↑ FSH/LH.<br /></br>• Vasomotor symptoms result from thermoregulatory center instability triggered by estrogen withdrawal.<br /></br>• Vaginal atrophy: Estrogen loss → thinner epithelium, ↓ glycogen, ↓ lubrication → dyspareunia.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Confirm not pregnant.<br /></br>2. If negative and symptoms are moderate–severe:<br /></br>   • Systemic menopausal hormone therapy (MHT): estrogen + progestin if uterus present; estrogen alone if post-hysterectomy.<br /></br>   • Contraindications: pregnancy, estrogen-dependent malignancy, unexplained vaginal bleeding, active thromboembolic disease, severe liver disease.<br /></br>3. For isolated genitourinary symptoms: topical estrogen can be used.<br /></br>4. Non-hormonal options (e.g., SSRIs, SNRIs, gabapentin, clonidine) for vasomotor symptoms if MHT contraindicated or declined.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Rule of P.E.E.” for menopause management<br /></br>P – Perimenopausal/menopausal woman<br /></br>E – Evaluate for Estrogen contraindications<br /></br>E – Exclude pregnancy FIRST<br /></br>Visual: Picture a woman standing at a pharmacy counter holding estrogen, but the pharmacist hands her a urine cup first.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Serum LH: FSH/LH levels fluctuate; after 12 mo amenorrhea in a woman &gt;45, hormone levels are unnecessary for diagnosis and do not influence immediate management.<br /></br>c. Hormone therapy: Appropriate definitive therapy for symptomatic menopause, but you must exclude pregnancy before initiation. Jumping straight to MHT is unsafe.<br /></br>d. Levothyroxine: Hypothyroidism can cause menstrual changes, but patient lacks symptoms (fatigue, weight gain, cold intolerance) and TSH wasn’t suggested abnormal. Thyroid disease does not cause hot flashes or vaginal atrophy.<br /></br>e. Vaginal corticosteroid therapy: Used for inflammatory vulvar dermatoses (e.g., lichen sclerosus). Atrophic vaginitis from estrogen deficiency responds to topical estrogen, not steroids.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>In any woman with recent amenorrhea—no matter how “menopausal” she looks—you must document a negative pregnancy test before prescribing systemic estrogen/progestin therapy.</p>
151. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-month history of perirectal pain that worsens with bowel movements and a thick, yellow rectal discharge. She has hypertension and psoriasis. Her medications are metoprolol and topical fluocinonide cream. There is no family history of serious illness. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 136/70 mm Hg. The abdomen is soft, nontender, and nondistended. Rectal examination shows brown stool. The skin of the posterior perineal wall is erythematous with a draining sinus tract. Test of the stool for occult blood is positive. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Clostridium difficile colitis</br>b. Colon cancer</br>c. Crohn disease</br>d. Diverticular abscess</br>e. Ulcerative colitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: c. Crohn disease  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>1. “Perirectal pain that worsens with bowel movements” + “thick, yellow rectal discharge”<br /></br>   • Yellow, purulent material suggests a chronic, draining tract (fistula).<br /></br>2. “Skin of posterior perineal wall is erythematous with a draining sinus tract”<br /></br>   • Classic description of a perianal fistula.<br /></br>3. Stool guaiac positive<br /></br>   • Occult blood can occur with any inflammatory colitis but is common in Crohn disease because of patchy ulcerations.<br /></br>Key negatives<br /></br>• No recent antibiotics, no watery diarrhea → argues against C difficile.<br /></br>• No systemic toxicity or high fever → argues against diverticular abscess.<br /></br>• Perianal disease is rare in ulcerative colitis and essentially never caused by colon cancer.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Elderly age is less typical, but perianal fistulas are overwhelmingly associated with transmural IBD—i.e., Crohn disease.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thinking:<br /></br>1. Identify an anorectal fistula (pain + purulent drainage + external opening).<br /></br>2. Ask “What conditions commonly create fistulas?”<br /></br>   • Crohn disease (transmural inflammation)<br /></br>   • Trauma, anorectal abscess, radiation, tuberculosis, cancer (much less common)<br /></br>3. Combine with occult GI bleeding → favors inflammatory bowel disease.<br /></br>4. Among IBD subtypes, ONLY Crohn disease has transmural inflammation → fistulas.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: A draining perianal sinus/fistula in any age should trigger the reflex consideration “Crohn disease until proven otherwise.”</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): Characterize location of external opening, discharge description, pain severity, impact on continence, and any IBD history.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Crohn disease = relapsing-remitting, transmural granulomatous inflammation.<br /></br>• Transmural nature → deep ulcers, fissures, and sinus tracts that can bore through skin (perianal fistulas), bladder (enterovesical), or vagina (enterovaginal).<br /></br>• Skip lesions can occur anywhere mouth-to-anus; terminal ileum and perianal region most common.<br /></br>• Extraintestinal: arthritis, uveitis, erythema nodosum, nephrolithiasis.</p></br><p>Risk factors: Smoking, family history, Caucasian/ASH, but new onset can occur at any age.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)</br>Immediate work-up:<br /></br>• Colonoscopy with terminal ileum intubation + biopsy → transmural inflammation, skip lesions, non-caseating granulomas.<br /></br>• MRI pelvis or endoanal ultrasound to define fistula anatomy.</p></br><p>Management:<br /></br>1. Acute perianal fistula:<br /></br>   • Seton placement ± abscess drainage (surgery).<br /></br>   • Antibiotics (ciprofloxacin + metronidazole) for secondary infection.<br /></br>2. Induce &amp; maintain remission of Crohn disease:<br /></br>   • Induction: Corticosteroids.<br /></br>   • Maintenance/ fistulizing disease: Anti-TNF biologics (infliximab, adalimumab) ± azathioprine.<br /></br>   • Nutritional support, smoking cessation.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Crohn Carves Completely through the wall → C-for-Crohn = C-for-Complete transmural → fistulas &amp; perforations.”<br /></br>Picture a “crow” (Crohn) pecking holes straight through a pipe (the bowel wall) forming tunnels (fistulas).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Clostridioides difficile colitis<br /></br>   • Presents with recent antibiotic use, watery (not purulent) diarrhea, leukocytosis, pseudomembranes; does NOT produce external perianal tracts.</p></br><p>b. Colon cancer<br /></br>   • Causes hematochezia/occult blood, change in bowel habits, weight loss; fistulas are extraordinarily rare without adjacent organ invasion.</p></br><p>d. Diverticular abscess<br /></br>   • Complication of diverticulitis; would produce LLQ pain, fever, leukocytosis ± intraperitoneal abscess, not an external sinus on perineal skin.</p></br><p>e. Ulcerative colitis<br /></br>   • Inflammation limited to mucosa/submucosa, starts in rectum and extends proximally, NO transmural involvement; therefore, fistulas are not a feature.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>Perianal fistula + GI bleeding = think Crohn disease due to its hallmark transmural inflammation.</p>
152. Twelve hours after discharge from the hospital, a 2-day-old male newborn is brought to the emergency department after having a seizure. His parents report that he stiffened, and his eyes rolled back for 3 or 4 minutes; then he went limp. He was delivered vaginally at term to a 20-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, following an uncomplicated pregnancy; his weight on delivery was 3062 g (6 lb 12 oz). The patient’s mother used to drink two glasses of wine each evening with dinner but stopped drinking alcohol when she learned she was pregnant at 4 weeks’ gestation. She has never smoked cigarettes or used illicit drugs. On arrival, the infant is awake but sluggish. Examination shows hypertelorism and low-set ears. The lungs are clear to auscultation. There are no murmurs. A chest x-ray shows a narrow mediastinum and a right-sided aortic arch. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Down syndrome</br>b. Severe combined immunodeficiency</br>c. Thymic-parathyroid dysplasia</br>d. Trisomy 13 syndrome</br>e. Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Thymic-parathyroid dysplasia (DiGeorge syndrome, 22q11.2 deletion)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that jump out:<br /></br>• 2-day-old newborn with tonic–clonic-type seizure → think acute neonatal hypocalcemia.<br /></br>• Dysmorphic facies: hypertelorism, low-set ears → facial midline/branchial arch maldevelopment.<br /></br>• Chest-x-ray: “narrow mediastinum” + right-sided aortic arch → absent thymic shadow and aberrant great-vessel development.<br /></br>• Completely normal pregnancy and delivery, no teratogenic exposures after 4 wk GA → points away from alcohol/valproate etiologies.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: clear lungs, no murmurs (structural heart disease can still be present in DiGeorge, but lack of loud murmur doesn’t rule it out). Absence of infection history → less likely severe combined immunodeficiency (SCID).</p></br><p>Pattern: Facial anomalies + hypocalcemic seizure + absent thymus + conotruncal/aortic arch defect = classic DiGeorge (CATCH-22).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</p></br><ol></br><li>Seizure shortly after birth → evaluate metabolic causes first; hypocalcemia is most common.  </li></br><li>Ask “why hypocalcemic?” → Parathyroid failure/aplasia in DiGeorge.  </li></br><li>What syndrome gives BOTH parathyroid aplasia AND thymic aplasia AND conotruncal defects? → 22q11.2 deletion, aka thymic-parathyroid dysplasia.  </li></br><li>Cross-check with facial features and right-sided aortic arch — both fit.</li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: On NBME/Step questions, the triad of neonatal hypocalcemic seizures + absent thymus (narrow mediastinum) + characteristic facies = DiGeorge.</p></br><p>Documentation note (real patient): record serum calcium, PTH, chromosome FISH for 22q11.2, immune work-up (T-cell counts), echocardiogram, start IV calcium gluconate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Embryology: Failure of 3rd &amp; 4th pharyngeal (branchial) pouches to develop →<br /></br>  – 3rd pouch: thymus + inferior parathyroids<br /></br>  – 4th pouch: superior parathyroids + portions of conotruncal heart outflow<br /></br>• Loss of parathyroids → ↓PTH → hypocalcemia → tetany/seizures.<br /></br>• Loss of thymus → profound T-cell deficiency (viral/fungal infections later).<br /></br>• Conotruncal anomalies (TOF, interrupted aortic arch, right-sided arch).<br /></br>• Genetic: 22q11.2 micro-deletion; mnemonic “CATCH-22”: Cardiac, Abnormal facies, Thymic aplasia, Cleft palate, Hypocalcemia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>Immediate:<br /></br>• Check total/ionized Ca²⁺, PTH, Mg²⁺; give IV calcium gluconate for seizures.<br /></br>• Start calcitriol ± oral calcium to maintain levels.  </p></br><p>Long-term:<br /></br>• Echocardiogram for structural heart disease.<br /></br>• Flow cytometry for T-cell counts; potential prophylactic TMP-SMX, avoid live vaccines if severe T-cell deficiency.<br /></br>• Genetic counseling; confirm deletion via FISH or microarray.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Di George CATCHes 22 calcium-deficient seizures.”<br /></br>C – Conotruncal/Cardiac (right-sided arch, TOF)<br /></br>A – Abnormal facies (hypertelorism, low-set ears)<br /></br>T – Thymic aplasia (narrow mediastinum)<br /></br>C – Cleft palate<br /></br>H – Hypocalcemia (seizures/tetany)<br /></br>22 – Chromosome 22q11 deletion  </p></br><p>Visual: imagine a baseball glove (thymus) missing from the mediastinum “catching” a ball (calcium) but dropping it—so the calcium is low.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Down syndrome (trisomy 21)<br /></br>   • Dysmorphic facies (up-slanting palpebral fissures, epicanthal folds), single palmar crease, hypotonia; heart defects = endocardial cushion (AV canal), not thymic absence. Calcium normal; no narrow mediastinum.  </p></br><p>b. Severe combined immunodeficiency (SCID)<br /></br>   • Severe infections after birth, chronic diarrhea, FTT. No specific facial anomalies, parathyroid function intact; calcium normal; mediastinum can be small but not classic right-sided arch.  </p></br><p>d. Trisomy 13 (Patau)<br /></br>   • Cleft lip/palate, holoprosencephaly, polydactyly, cutis aplasia, severe cardiac defects. Thymus/parathyroids intact; seizures usually from CNS malformations rather than hypocalcemia.  </p></br><p>e. Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome<br /></br>   • X-linked; triad of eczema, thrombocytopenia (bleeding), and recurrent infections due to combined B/T-cell dysfunction. Presents later; no neonatal hypocalcemic seizures or absent thymic shadow.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>Neonatal hypocalcemic seizure + absent thymic shadow + facial anomalies = DiGeorge (thymic-parathyroid dysplasia, 22q11.2). Think “CATCH-22.”</p>
153. A 2-year-old girl is brought to the physician for a well-child examination. She has had two urinary tract infections since birth. She has not had pain with urination or urinary frequency or urgency. Examination shows no abnormalities. Renal ultrasonography shows no obstruction. Voiding cystourethrography shows grade II vesicoureteral reflux. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial step to prevent future urinary tract infections in this patient?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Monitor temperature curves daily</br>b. Urine cultures weekly</br>c. Oral antibiotic prophylaxis</br>d. 2-Week course of intravenous antibiotic therapy</br>e. Vesicoureteral reimplantation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Oral antibiotic prophylaxis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recurrent UTIs: two episodes by age 2.<br /></br>• Imaging: grade II vesicoureteral reflux (VUR); no anatomic obstruction.<br /></br>• Age: toddler (highest risk period for renal scarring after febrile UTI).<br /></br>Pertinent positives: low-grade VUR, history of infection.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no obstruction, no voiding complaints, normal exam.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise reasoning:<br /></br>1. VUR grades I–II have a &gt;70 % spontaneous resolution rate; therefore, surgery is usually not first-line.<br /></br>2. However, any child &lt;3 y with VUR + ≥1 febrile or symptomatic UTI is at risk for renal parenchymal scarring.<br /></br>3. Goal: prevent additional infections while waiting for reflux to resolve.<br /></br>4. Guideline-supported first step = continuous low-dose antibiotic prophylaxis until patient is toilet-trained, reflux resolves, or breakthrough infections dictate escalation.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Low-grade VUR → conservative management + daily prophylaxis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>VUR = retrograde flow of urine from bladder to ureter/renal pelvis. Recurrent reflux of infected urine predisposes to pyelonephritis → inflammation and eventual cortical scarring. Risk correlates with:<br /></br>• Younger age (immature immune &amp; renal systems)<br /></br>• Febrile UTIs<br /></br>• Higher reflux grade<br /></br>Stopping infections early prevents irreversible nephron loss and future hypertension/CKD.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line prophylactic agents:<br /></br>• Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole 2 mg/kg TMP once nightly<br /></br>• Nitrofurantoin 1–2 mg/kg once nightly<br /></br>Follow-up: repeat VCUG or radionuclide cystography every 12–24 mo; prompt urine culture for any fever ≥38 °C.<br /></br>Escalate to endoscopic bulking or ureteral reimplantation only for:<br /></br>• Persistent grade III–V beyond age 6–7 y<br /></br>• Breakthrough febrile UTIs despite perfect compliance.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Low grade, Low dose”: Grades I–II = nightly Low-dose antibiotics.<br /></br>“High grade, High intervention”: Grades IV–V often need surgery.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Monitor temperature curves daily – Detects, but does not prevent, infection; offers no prophylactic benefit.<br /></br>b. Weekly urine cultures – Expensive, uncomfortable, high false-positive risk; does not prevent infection.<br /></br>d. Two-week IV antibiotics – Reserved for an active febrile UTI/pyelonephritis, not prophylaxis.<br /></br>e. Vesicoureteral reimplantation – Invasive; indicated for high-grade (IV–V) reflux or breakthrough infections despite prophylaxis, not initial therapy for grade II disease.</p></br><p>Key Point: In young children with grade I–II VUR and prior UTIs, nightly oral antibiotic prophylaxis is the evidence-based first step to prevent further infections and renal scarring while allowing time for spontaneous resolution.</p>
154. A 4-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his mother because she is concerned that he is having difficulty making friends. The mother reports that he attends preschool but does not socialize with the other children and spends most of the time playing by himself. At school, he is only interested in blocks, reports how tall he can build towers, and resists other activities. At home, he spends hours building towers out of blocks in his room and becomes very upset if he is interrupted. He does not talk about any of his classmates; when his mother invites another child to the house, he has little interest in socializing. His mother reports that he understands directions and communicates without difficulty but has never been very affectionate. He is generally well behaved but will have a temper tantrum if a daily routine is altered or if furniture is rearranged. Pregnancy, labor, and delivery were uncomplicated, and he has reached all appropriate motor milestones for his age. His mother does not recall if language emerged somewhat later in him than it did for her daughter but notes that he makes his needs and wants known without any problem. He is neatly dressed. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he makes no eye contact with the physician and perseverates about the towers that he built in the waiting room. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder</br>b. Expressive language disorder</br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder</br>d. Pervasive developmental disorder</br>e. Social anxiety disorder (social phobia)</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Pervasive developmental disorder (autism-spectrum disorder)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key social clues<br /></br>  – “Does not socialize … spends most of the time playing by himself”<br /></br>  – “Little interest in socializing” even when peers are invited to his home<br /></br>  – “Has never been very affectionate,” “no eye contact”<br /></br>• Restricted/repetitive behaviors<br /></br>  – Hours building block towers, “perseverates about the towers”<br /></br>  – Marked distress if routine is altered or furniture is moved<br /></br>• Normal overall cognition &amp; language adequate for basic needs (“communicates without difficulty”) but possible mild language delay noted by mom<br /></br>• Normal motor milestones; normal physical examination</p></br><p>Pattern: Impaired social reciprocity + restricted, repetitive, inflexible behaviors beginning in early childhood → classic autism-spectrum picture (DSM-5 umbrella term for former “pervasive developmental disorders” such as autistic disorder and Asperger disorder).</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>– No hyperactivity, impulsivity, distractibility (rules out ADHD)<br /></br>– No obsessive thoughts that are ego-dystonic or recognized as excessive (rules out OCD)<br /></br>– Speech adequate (rules out pure expressive language disorder)<br /></br>– Anxiety is not the limiting factor; he lacks desire for social interaction rather than fearing it (rules out social anxiety disorder)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Core deficit = social communication/interaction (limited eye contact, absent peer relationships, lack of shared enjoyment).<br /></br>2. Plus ≥2 restricted/repetitive patterns (highly circumscribed interest in block towers, inflexible adherence to routines).<br /></br>3. Symptoms present in early developmental period and cause functional impairment.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Autism spectrum = “social disconnect + narrow, repetitive interests/behaviors.”</p></br><p>Documentation in chart: Social communication deficits, repetitive behaviors, onset in early childhood, effect on functioning, parental concerns, normal hearing/vision screen, normal neurologic exam.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Multifactorial—strong genetic component (high concordance in monozygotic twins); associated with advanced paternal age, some genetic syndromes (Fragile X, Rett).<br /></br>• Abnormal brain connectivity &amp; atypical synaptic pruning thought to underlie social-communication deficits.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Management</br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Primarily clinical; screen with M-CHAT at 18 &amp; 24 mo.<br /></br>• Hearing evaluation, speech-language assessment, ± genetic testing to rule out syndromic causes.</p></br><p>Treatment (no cure—early intervention is key)<br /></br>1. Intensive behavioral therapy (Applied Behavior Analysis).<br /></br>2. Speech-language and occupational therapy.<br /></br>3. Structured educational environment.<br /></br>4. Medications for associated symptoms only: risperidone/aripiprazole for severe irritability, SSRIs for anxiety, stimulants for comorbid ADHD features.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“ABC of Autism”<br /></br>A – Alone (social isolation)<br /></br>B – Bizarre, narrow interests / repetitive Behaviors<br /></br>C – Can’t Change routine<br /></br>Visualize a child sitting “alone in a tower,” refusing to come down when mom tries to move the blocks.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder – Would show hyperactivity, impulsivity, inattention across settings. This child is able to focus for hours on blocks (hyper-focus, not distractibility) and is not disruptive.<br /></br>b. Expressive language disorder – Main feature is impaired speech production with relatively normal social reciprocity. Here, language is functional; the problem is social use, not production.<br /></br>c. Obsessive-compulsive disorder – In OCD, repetitive behaviors are driven by intrusive ego-dystonic obsessions and the patient usually recognizes them as excessive. Young child rarely has such insight, and core social deficits are not explained by OCD.<br /></br>e. Social anxiety disorder – These children desire friendships but avoid interactions because of fear of embarrassment. Our patient lacks interest rather than having performance anxiety; moreover, repetitive behaviors/routines point away from SAD.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Early childhood presentation of impaired social interaction plus restricted, repetitive interests and inflexibility = autism spectrum disorder (previously categorized as a pervasive developmental disorder).</p>
155. Two days after undergoing urgent cesarean delivery because of a nonreassuring fetal heart tracing, a 37-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, has the onset of dysphoria, insomnia, and easy distractibility. Pregnancy was uncomplicated. She has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. Her paternal cousin had major depressive disorder, postpartum onset. The patient’s vital signs are within normal limits. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 97%. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she has an irritable mood and tired, concerned affect. Without treatment, which of the following is most likely to develop in this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Alopecia</br>b. Dissociative amnesia with dissociative fugue</br>c. Loss of maternal bond with newborn</br>d. Resolution of symptoms within 2 weeks</br>e. Suicidality</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Resolution of symptoms within 2 weeks  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Timing: Symptoms began “2 days after” delivery—classic window for postpartum blues (starts 2–3 days postpartum, peaks day 5).<br /></br>• Symptoms: Dysphoria, irritability, insomnia, easy distractibility—mild mood lability without psychosis.<br /></br>• Severity: No suicidal thoughts, no thoughts of harming baby, still oriented; exam otherwise normal.<br /></br>• Risk factors absent: No personal psychiatric history; one distant relative with depression is not strongly predictive.  </p></br><p>Together these point to POSTPARTUM BLUES, not postpartum depression or psychosis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Post-delivery mood complaints → classify by timing &amp; severity.<br /></br>2. &lt; 14 days and mild → postpartum blues.<br /></br>3. Natural history → self-limited; resolves spontaneously within 2 weeks with reassurance and sleep.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Mild mood symptoms that start within a few days of delivery almost always remit on their own within 2 weeks.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record mood screen (PHQ-2/EPDS score), counsel on expectations, arrange follow-up in 2 weeks to ensure resolution.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Rapid fall in estrogen and progesterone + sleep deprivation and new-parent stress → transient dysregulation of serotonin &amp; dopamine pathways.<br /></br>• Up to 70% of postpartum patients experience blues; only ~20% progress to major depression.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Postpartum blues:<br /></br>• First-line: Reassurance, education, optimize sleep/support.<br /></br>• No pharmacotherapy unless symptoms persist &gt; 2 weeks (then evaluate for postpartum depression).<br /></br>Diagnostics: None needed acutely; screening tool at follow-up.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Baby BLUES are Brief (Less than two weeks) and Less severe.”<br /></br>Visual: Picture a 14-day calendar colored light blue that fades away.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Alopecia – Telogen effluvium hair loss occurs 2–5 months postpartum due to estrogen withdrawal, not within days, and is unrelated to mood disorder trajectory.<br /></br>b. Dissociative amnesia with fugue – Rare, unrelated to hormonal shifts of childbirth; would entail sudden travel and identity loss, which is absent here.<br /></br>c. Loss of maternal bond with newborn – Suggests postpartum depression/psychosis. Blues patients remain engaged and affectionate despite tearfulness.<br /></br>e. Suicidality – Hallmark of postpartum depression or psychosis (emergency). No suicidal ideation given, and blues typically do not progress to suicidality within first 2 weeks.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>This patient has postpartum blues, a benign, self-limited condition expected to resolve spontaneously within 14 days. Prompt reassurance and follow-up are key; if symptoms persist or worsen, reevaluate for postpartum depression.</p>
156. A 32-year-old woman with asthma comes to the physician because of a 3-month history of progressive wheezing and a cough that is occasionally productive of plugs of brownish sputum. She received the diagnosis of asthma 15 years ago. She has been taking an inhaled long-acting β-adrenergic agonist and inhaled glucocorticoid daily for 8 years and had good control of her symptoms until 3 months ago. She has no pets. She has no recent history of travel abroad or new occupational exposure. She appears well and is not in respiratory distress. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 96%. Examination shows no cyanosis. Scattered inspiratory and expiratory wheezes are heard. There is no clubbing or peripheral edema. Her leukocyte count is 20,000/mm3 (45% segmented neutrophils, 40% eosinophils, 12% lymphocytes, and 3% monocytes). Her serum IgE concentration is 2800 IU/mL. A chest x-ray shows alveolar infiltrates in the upper lungs bilaterally. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis</br>b. Eosinophilic leukemia</br>c. Hypereosinophilic syndrome</br>d. Polyarteritis nodosa</br>e. Strongyloides stercoralis infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a.  Allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis (ABPA)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Long-standing asthma that had been well-controlled until 3 months ago → classic host for ABPA (almost always occurs in patients with asthma or cystic fibrosis).<br /></br>• “Progressive wheezing and cough productive of brownish plugs” → dark mucus plugs are pathognomonic for ABPA (matted Aspergillus hyphae mixed with eosinophils).<br /></br>• Marked eosinophilia (40 % of 20 000 ≈ 8 000/µL) and sky-high total IgE (2 800 IU/mL; normal &lt; 100–150) → hypersensitivity picture.<br /></br>• Transient alveolar infiltrates in the upper lobes → migrating/patchy infiltrates typical of ABPA (often central/upper lung fields).<br /></br>• Absence of systemic illness, travel, or pet exposure helps rule out parasites and vasculitis.</p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no fever, weight loss, liver/spleen enlargement, or skin nodules; O2 saturation normal → argues against parasitic hyper-infection, vasculitis, or leukemia.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Asthma + worsening control + brown plugs + eosinophilia + very high IgE + fleeting infiltrates = ABPA.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)</br>1. Identify host: asthmatic.<br /></br>2. Look for sudden deterioration despite appropriate inhaler regimen.<br /></br>3. Find hypersensitivity markers (IgE, eosinophilia).<br /></br>4. Imaging shows transient/central infiltrates.<br /></br>5. Combine → colonization of bronchi by Aspergillus fumigatus triggering type I &amp; III hypersensitivity = ABPA.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: When an asthmatic (or CF patient) suddenly worsens, think ABPA if you see brown sputum, eosinophilia, and very high IgE.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note sputum description, total/absolute eosinophil count, total IgE, radiographic infiltrates, and plan for systemic glucocorticoids ± antifungal.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: Hypersensitivity (type I/IgE-mediated &amp; type III immune-complex) reaction to Aspergillus fumigatus that transiently colonizes bronchi.<br /></br>• Repeated inflammation → mucus plugging, bronchiectasis (often central/upper), and progressive obstructive lung disease.<br /></br>• Hallmarks: asthma/CF host, ↑↑IgE (&gt;1000 IU/mL), peripheral eosinophilia, positive Aspergillus skin test/specific IgE, bronchiectasis or fleeting infiltrates.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>First line:<br /></br>1. Oral corticosteroids (e.g., prednisone 0.5 mg/kg daily, slow taper) to control inflammation.<br /></br>2. Add itraconazole or voriconazole in refractory or relapsing cases (reduces fungal burden &amp; steroid dose).</p></br><p>Diagnostics often ordered:<br /></br>• Serum Aspergillus-specific IgE &amp; IgG (precipitins)<br /></br>• Chest CT for central bronchiectasis (“finger-in-glove” mucus plugging).<br /></br>• Sputum culture rarely shows invasive disease but may grow Aspergillus.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“ABPA = A-B-P-A”<br /></br>A: Asthma host<br /></br>B: Brown mucus plugs / Bronchiectasis (central)<br /></br>P: Peripheral eosinoPhilia &amp; Patchy infiltrates<br /></br>A: crazy ↑↑ IgE (Allergic)</p></br><p>Picture a brown “asp” coiled in a bronchus plugging it up, surrounded by eosinophil explosions.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>b. Eosinophilic leukemia<br /></br>• Would show clonal, often immature eosinophils, constitutional symptoms, and often splenomegaly; IgE not massively elevated; brown mucus plugs &amp; asthma link absent.</p></br><p>c. Hypereosinophilic syndrome<br /></br>• Requires persistent eosinophilia &gt; 1500/µL for &gt; 6 months with multi-organ damage (cardiac, skin, nervous system). IgE usually only mildly ↑. No abrupt asthma worsening or transient infiltrates.</p></br><p>d. Polyarteritis nodosa<br /></br>• Necrotizing vasculitis of medium vessels; presents with systemic constitutional symptoms, skin nodules, renal infarcts, abdominal pain, mononeuritis multiplex. Asthma/eosinophilia/IgE are not defining features (that’s Churg-Strauss/Eosinophilic granulomatosis with polyangiitis, which still wouldn’t give brown plugs).</p></br><p>e. Strongyloides stercoralis infection<br /></br>• Pulmonary “Löffler” phase can cause wheeze &amp; eosinophilia, but patients usually have GI complaints, travel to endemic areas, larva currens rash. IgE not as high; brown mucus plugs uncommon.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>High-Yield Wrap-Up</br>Asthma/CF patient + acute loss of control + brown sputum + marked eosinophilia + IgE &gt; 1000 + fleeting infiltrates = Allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis. Treat with oral steroids, add azole if needed.</p>
157. A 24-year-old woman comes to the office for an annual employment physical examination. She feels well. Medical history is unremarkable and she takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. As the physician is preparing to leave the examination room the patient asks, "Do you think having a romantic relationship with my gynecologist is all right?" Which of the following is the most appropriate response?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. "I am under no obligation to report any romantic relationship to a disciplinary board."</br>b. "If you switch gynecologists it would be acceptable for your former gynecologist to begin a romantic relationship with you immediately."</br>c. "A mutually consensual romantic relationship between patient and physician can sometimes be justified."</br>d. "A romantic relationship in this setting is always unethical."</br>e. "Would you mind telling me who your gynecologist is?"</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. “A romantic relationship in this setting is always unethical.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clue: The woman is asking about a “romantic relationship with my gynecologist,” i.e., an ongoing physician–patient relationship.<br /></br>• No mitigating circumstances (e.g., the physician–patient relationship has formally ended, a significant cooling-off period, transfer of care, etc.) are mentioned.<br /></br>• Therefore the question tests the ethical rule governing sexual/romantic relationships between a physician and a CURRENT patient.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the relationship status: patient ⇄ current physician.<br /></br>2. Recall AMA Code of Medical Ethics Opinion 9.1.1: sexual or romantic relationships with current patients are unethical because they exploit the inherent power imbalance and may compromise care.<br /></br>3. Apply rule → must advise that the relationship is not acceptable under any circumstances.<br /></br>4. Provide clear, firm guidance to the patient.</p></br><p>Take-home core concept: A sexual or romantic relationship with a current patient is ALWAYS unethical, irrespective of mutual consent.</p></br><p>Documentation note (real life): You would record the patient’s question and your counseling that such relationships are unethical and advised termination of the relationship or transfer of care if needed.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology / Ethics Rationale<br /></br>• Power differential: Physicians possess specialized knowledge, control access to medical care, and often information about patient vulnerabilities → inability to ensure truly voluntary consent.<br /></br>• Risk to patient welfare: Objectivity may be lost, leading to compromised diagnosis or treatment.<br /></br>• Professional responsibility: The physician’s fiduciary duty obligates acting solely in the patient’s best interest; personal romantic involvement conflicts with this duty.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. “I am under no obligation to report …”<br /></br>   – Wrong: While self-reporting is not the immediate issue, the primary duty is to tell the patient the relationship is unethical. This response sidesteps the real question and gives the impression it might be permissible.</p></br><p>b. “If you switch gynecologists it would be acceptable … immediately.”<br /></br>   – Wrong: Simply transferring care does NOT immediately remove the ethical barrier. The AMA still discourages relationships with former patients and requires a substantial time interval and assurance that power imbalance has resolved. Immediate dating remains unethical.</p></br><p>c. “A mutually consensual relationship … can sometimes be justified.”<br /></br>   – Wrong: “Mutual consent” does not override the ethical prohibition when the physician–patient relationship is current. The power differential makes true consent questionable.</p></br><p>e. “Would you mind telling me who your gynecologist is?”<br /></br>   – Wrong: Irrelevant to the ethical principle. The patient’s physician identity does not change the rule; asking may feel intrusive and avoids giving clear guidance.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “Current = Can’t” → If the patient is CURRENT, you CAN’T date.<br /></br>• Remember the 3 P’s of why: Power imbalance, Patient welfare risk, Professional duty.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A physician must never engage in a romantic or sexual relationship with a current patient. Mutual consent or switching doctors does not make it acceptable immediately.</p>
158. A healthy 52-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine total colonoscopy. He has no history of blood in his stool or rectal bleeding.
