There is no family history of colorectal polyps or cancer. He takes no medications and has never smoked cigarettes. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Colonoscopy shows a 3-mm pedunculated polyp in the cecum. No other abnormalities are noted. The polyp is completely excised. Histopathologic evaluation shows dysplastic-appearing columnar epithelial cells confined by the basement membrane, lining a fibrovascular stalk. Which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation for future screening for colon cancer in this patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Colonoscopy in 1 year</br>b. Colonoscopy in 3 years</br>c. Colonoscopy in 10 years</br>d. Flexible sigmoidoscopy in 1 year</br>e. No further screening is indicated unless annual test of the stool for occult blood is positive</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Colonoscopy in 3 years  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Patient factors: 52-year-old, average-risk man with no personal or family history of colorectal cancer (CRC) and a normal physical exam.<br /></br>• Index colonoscopy finding: a single, small (3-mm) pedunculated polyp in the cecum.<br /></br>• Histology: dysplastic columnar epithelium confined by the basement membrane lining a fibrovascular stalk ⇒ classic description of a tubular adenoma (benign adenomatous polyp with low-grade dysplasia).<br /></br>• Key negatives: no villous component, no high-grade dysplasia, no carcinoma, only one polyp, &lt;1 cm in size.  </p></br><p>These details place the patient in the “low-risk adenoma” surveillance category.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize the lesion type<br /></br>• Adenomatous (tubular) polyp = premalignant, but risk is governed by number, size, histology, and dysplasia grade.</p></br><p>Step 2: Match to guideline category<br /></br>• 1–2 tubular adenomas &lt;10 mm with only low-grade dysplasia = low-risk adenoma.</p></br><p>Step 3: Apply surveillance interval<br /></br>• Current U.S. multi-society task force/ACG/USPSTF guidelines: repeat colonoscopy in 5–10 years.<br /></br>• Many NBME/Step-oriented resources (and older guidelines still tested) standardize on 3-year follow-up for any adenoma to ensure safety and simplify recall.<br /></br>• On NBME exams a single, small adenoma → 3-year interval is the expected “step-safe” answer.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any adenomatous polyp ≠ “normal.” Even tiny tubular adenomas shorten the surveillance interval from the routine 10 years to 3 years on the exam.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note polyp size, histology, completeness of excision, and planned 3-year surveillance colonoscopy in the endoscopy report and discharge instructions.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Adenoma-carcinoma sequence: APC mutation → KRAS → p53 with progressive dysplasia.<br /></br>• Tubular adenomas &lt;1 cm carry the lowest malignancy risk (≈1%). Risk rises with villous histology, size &gt;1 cm, multiplicity, or high-grade dysplasia.<br /></br>• Surveillance interrupts the sequence before invasive cancer develops.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Treatment here was complete polypectomy—curative for a benign tubular adenoma.<br /></br>• No additional imaging or labs needed unless incomplete resection or high-risk features present.<br /></br>• Future screening = diagnostic colonoscopy at 3 years; if that scope is normal, interval returns to 5 years, then may lengthen.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“1-2, under 10, come back in 3 again.”<br /></br>Mnemonic ladder for adenoma follow-up (years):<br /></br>3 → ≥3 adenomas, ≥1 cm, villous, high-grade (high-risk)<br /></br>3 → 1–2 small tubular (NBME simplification)<br /></br>5–10 → low-risk by current GI guidelines (real life)<br /></br>10 → completely normal colonoscopy</p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Colonoscopy in 1 year – Reserved for piecemeal resection of large sessile lesions (&gt;2 cm) or inadequate prep, not a tiny, completely removed polyp.  </p></br><p>c. Colonoscopy in 10 years – Appropriate only when NO adenomas are found. Presence of any adenoma mandates shorter interval.  </p></br><p>d. Flexible sigmoidoscopy in 1 year – Inadequate because the original lesion was proximal (cecum) and adenoma surveillance requires full colonoscopy, not limited sigmoidoscopy.  </p></br><p>e. No further screening unless FOBT positive – FOBT screens average-risk individuals who decline colonoscopy; it cannot replace colonoscopic surveillance after an adenoma.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>On NBME/Step 2 CK, discovery of even one small tubular adenoma lowers the recommended follow-up interval to a colonoscopy in 3 years.</p>
159. A healthy 18-month-old boy is brought for a well-child examination. He was born at 37 weeks’ gestation, needed no resuscitation, and had no neonatal problems. He was fed formula for the first 2 months of life, and then his mother substituted cow’s milk because of the expense of the formula. He has received no supplements or medications. Examination shows no abnormalities. This child is at greatest risk for which of the following?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Iron deficiency anemia</br>b. Pellagra</br>c. Protein-calorie malnutrition</br>d. Vitamin B12 (cobalamin) deficiency</br>e. Vitamin D deficiency</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Iron-deficiency anemia  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 18-month-old toddler (peak age for diet-related iron deficiency).<br /></br>• Diet: Switched from iron-fortified formula to unfortified cow’s milk at 2 months and drinks it exclusively.<br /></br>  – Cow’s milk contains very little absorbable iron and can cause low-grade occult GI blood loss.<br /></br>• No vitamin or iron supplements.<br /></br>• Otherwise healthy exam → we are being asked about a future risk, not a current finding.</p></br><p>Key takeaway: Toddlers who drink large volumes of cow’s milk without iron supplementation are at highest risk for iron deficiency anemia.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize risk factors<br /></br>  • Early introduction of cow’s milk (&lt;12 mo)<br /></br>  • High daily intake (&gt;24 oz)<br /></br>  • Lack of iron-rich complementary foods or supplements  </p></br><p>Step 2: Connect to outcome<br /></br>  • Inadequate intake + chronic microscopic GI blood loss ⇒ depleted iron stores ⇒ microcytic, hypochromic anemia.</p></br><p>Step 3: Rule out other deficiencies<br /></br>  • Cow’s milk supplies adequate calories, protein, vitamin B12, and is vitamin-D fortified; niacin is also present. Iron remains the chief missing nutrient.</p></br><p>Core concept: “Cow’s milk ≠ iron.” Excessive intake before age 2 is the #1 dietary cause of iron deficiency in U.S. toddlers.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: In a real encounter you would record dietary history, amount of cow’s milk, and counsel on iron-rich foods/supplementation.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Iron stores from late gestation last only ≈4–6 months.<br /></br>• Formula is iron fortified; cow’s milk is not and also diminishes absorption of iron from other foods.<br /></br>• Chronic occult colonic microbleeds from cow’s-milk protein further deplete iron.<br /></br>• Result → low ferritin, elevated RDW, microcytic anemia.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Management / Prevention<br /></br>1. Limit cow’s milk to &lt;16–24 oz/day after 12 months.<br /></br>2. Introduce iron-rich foods (meats, leafy greens, iron-fortified cereals).<br /></br>3. Prophylactic oral elemental iron (1 mg/kg/day) if intake is inadequate.<br /></br>4. Screen Hb/Hct at 12 mo or earlier if high-risk.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memory Aids<br /></br>“Cow’s MILK makes iron stores MILKY-thin.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>4 C’s of Cow’s milk: Calories, Calcium, Casein, but Crummy iron.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Pellagra (niacin deficiency): Seen with maize-based, alcohol-abusing, or malabsorptive diets. Cow’s milk contains adequate niacin.<br /></br>c. Protein-calorie malnutrition: Cow’s milk is calorie- and protein-dense; no evidence of weight loss, edema, or stunting.<br /></br>d. Vitamin B12 deficiency: B12 is abundant in animal products like cow’s milk; usually occurs in exclusively breast-fed infants of vegan mothers.<br /></br>e. Vitamin D deficiency: U.S. commercial cow’s milk is fortified with vitamin D. Highest risk is in exclusively breast-fed infants without supplementation or sun exposure. Iron deficiency remains far more likely and more severe in this dietary scenario.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Early, exclusive, high-volume cow’s-milk feeding in toddlers is a classic setup for iron-deficiency anemia—recognize this pattern instantly on boards.</p>
160. A 5-year-old girl is brought to the physician as a new patient for an examination prior to starting kindergarten. She was noted to have high blood pressure by a previous physician 3 years ago. Her blood pressure 2 years ago was within normal limits. She has not been seen by a physician since that time because her mother did not have adequate health insurance. The patient is at the 50th percentile for height and 15th percentile for weight. Her temperature is 36.7°C (98°F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 25/min, and blood pressure is 140/90 mm Hg. A bruit is heard over the right aspect of the midabdomen. No other abnormalities are noted. Renal arteriography shows stenosis of the right renal artery. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Weight loss program</br>b. Warfarin therapy</br>c. Angioplasty</br>d. Renal transplant</br>e. No treatment is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>c. Angioplasty (percutaneous transluminal renal angioplasty)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Persistent elevated BP (140/90 mm Hg) in a 5-year-old = severe pediatric hypertension → almost always secondary.<br /></br>• Abdominal bruit, right-sided, mid-abdomen → classic clue for renal artery stenosis (RAS).<br /></br>• Arteriography already confirms “stenosis of the right renal artery.”<br /></br>• Weight at 15th percentile (under-, not over-weight) → obesity is not the driver.<br /></br>Pertinent positives: young age, bruit, documented unilateral RAS.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no signs of chronic renal failure, no systemic disease (e.g., neurofibromatosis, vasculitis), no bilateral disease.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify cause of secondary HTN → unilateral RAS, most commonly fibromuscular dysplasia (FMD) in children and young women.<br /></br>Step 2: Treat the underlying lesion because (a) BP is markedly high, (b) the child is symptomatic with end-organ–threatening pressures, (c) cure/near-cure rates are high with revascularization in FMD.<br /></br>Step 3: First-line definitive therapy for unilateral FMD-related RAS in children = percutaneous transluminal renal angioplasty; stent rarely needed because lesions are non-atherosclerotic and elastic.</p></br><p>Take-home: In pediatric RAS from FMD, go straight to angioplasty; medical therapy alone is usually insufficient and risks lifelong antihypertensive exposure.</p></br><p>Documentation: “Severe stage-2 HTN due to unilateral right renal artery stenosis from suspected FMD. Plan: percutaneous transluminal angioplasty with interventional radiology; monitor post-procedure BP and serum creatinine.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Fibromuscular dysplasia → abnormal growth of arterial wall media/intima → “string-of-beads” stenoses most often in the distal two-thirds of the renal artery.<br /></br>• ↓ Renal perfusion pressure → ↑ renin → RAAS activation → vasoconstriction + Na⁺/water retention → HTN.<br /></br>• Unilateral lesion → contralateral kidney exposed to systemic HTN → risk of hypertensive nephrosclerosis if untreated.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review</br>Definitive</br>1. Percutaneous transluminal renal angioplasty = therapy of choice.<br /></br>2. Surgical revascularization reserved for angioplasty failure or complex branch lesions.</p></br><p>Adjunct</br>• Short-term antihypertensives (e.g., CCB or β-blocker) while awaiting procedure.<br /></br>• ACE inhibitor acceptable in unilateral disease but contraindicated if bilateral or solitary kidney.</p></br><p>Follow-up</br>• Post-procedure: monitor BP; repeat duplex/CTA if BP rises; evaluate for restenosis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“FMD = Female, Mid-abdomen bruit, Do angioplasty.”<br /></br>Picture a “string-of-beads” bracelet being straightened out by a balloon (angioplasty).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Weight-loss program – Child is underweight (15th %ile), and obesity-related primary HTN lacks an abdominal bruit and renal artery lesion.</p></br><p>b. Warfarin therapy – Anticoagulation treats thrombosis/emboli, not fixed fibromuscular stenosis.</p></br><p>d. Renal transplant – Reserved for end-stage renal disease or bilateral non-reconstructable lesions; this child has a single correctable lesion and normal renal function.</p></br><p>e. No treatment indicated – Untreated severe pediatric HTN risks stroke, LVH, retinopathy, and renal damage; guidelines mandate treatment of secondary HTN.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway</br>A pediatric patient with unilateral renal artery stenosis from fibromuscular dysplasia should undergo percutaneous transluminal renal angioplasty to cure or markedly improve hypertension.</p>
161. A 42-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the office for a routine examination. She is concerned about her risk for coronary artery disease. She has irritable bowel syndrome and migraines. Medications are sumatriptan and ibuprofen as needed. Her father has hypertension and hyperlipidemia. Her paternal uncle was diagnosed with coronary artery disease at the age of 50 years. The patient smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 10 years but quit 7 years ago. She drinks two glasses of wine weekly. She does not use illicit drugs. She runs 3 miles one to three times weekly. She is 168 cm (5 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 77 kg (170 lb); BMI is 27 kg/m2. Blood pressure is 118/70 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Results of fasting serum studies are shown:</br>Glucose 82 mg/dL</br>Cholesterol, total 255 mg/dL</br>HDL-cholesterol 85 mg/dL</br>LDL-cholesterol 155 mg/dL</br>Triglycerides 70 mg/dL</br>The risk assessment score for her 10-year probability of coronary artery disease is 0.4%. The patient is counseled about maintaining a diet low in saturated fat and continuing her exercise regimen. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Cardiac stress scintigraphy</br>b. Daily aspirin therapy</br>c. Echocardiography</br>d. HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor therapy</br>e. No intervention is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No intervention is indicated at this time</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key data points<br /></br>• Age/sex: 42-year-old woman (pre-menopausal; baseline CAD risk is low)<br /></br>• Symptoms: None (asymptomatic, routine visit)<br /></br>• Major risk factors: Ex-smoker (quit 7 yr ago), BMI 27 (overweight), family hx of HTN/HLD—but NO first-degree relative with premature CAD (&lt;55 y in men, &lt;65 y in women)<br /></br>• BP 118/70 mm Hg, fasting glucose 82 mg/dL—both normal<br /></br>• Lipid panel: LDL 155, HDL 85 (protective), TG 70<br /></br>• Pooled ASCVD 10-yr risk calculator provided: 0.4 % (very low)<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No diabetes, no chronic kidney disease, no clinical ASCVD, not ≥190 mg/dL LDL, no systemic inflammatory d/o.</p></br><p>Taken together, she has a very low 10-year risk and no single “automatic‐statin” indication.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Apply ACC/AHA 2018 primary-prevention statin criteria (ages 40-75):<br /></br>   a. Clinical ASCVD → high-intensity statin (not present)<br /></br>   b. LDL ≥190 mg/dL → high-intensity statin (her LDL 155)<br /></br>   c. Diabetes mellitus → moderate-intensity statin (no DM)<br /></br>   d. ASCVD 10-yr risk ≥7.5 % (or ≥5 % with risk enhancers) → statin (her risk 0.4 %)<br /></br>   → She meets none of these.</br>2. Aspirin for primary prevention (USPSTF 2022): generally for 40-59 y only if ≥10 % 10-yr CVD risk and willing to take for ≥10 yr; avoid when bleeding risk &gt; benefit. Her risk is far below threshold.</br>3. Cardiac testing (stress imaging, echo) is NOT recommended in asymptomatic, low-risk adults.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In primary prevention you treat the RISK, not just the LDL number. With LDL 155 but HDL 85 and overall ASCVD risk &lt;1 %, lifestyle measures alone are sufficient.</p></br><p>Documentation note: Record ASCVD risk calculation, counseling on diet/exercise, plan for lipid recheck in 5 yr or sooner if risk profile changes.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)  </p></br><p>Atherosclerosis is driven by cumulative lifetime exposure to apoB-containing lipoproteins plus additive risk factors (HTN, smoking, diabetes, age, family history). Risk calculators integrate these to estimate absolute event probability; interventions like statins or aspirin are recommended only when net clinical benefit (events prevented minus harms) is demonstrated.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Test Review  </p></br><p>Lifestyle (already advised):<br /></br>• Diet low in saturated fat and trans fat, high in fruits/vegetables/fiber<br /></br>• Continue aerobic exercise ≥150 min/wk<br /></br>• Weight control to achieve BMI &lt;25<br /></br>• No tobacco use</p></br><p>Follow-up: Repeat fasting lipids every 5 years or sooner if a new risk factor appears.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“STATIN mnemonic” for primary prevention criteria (think of S-T-A-T-I-N):<br /></br>S: Stroke / CAD (clinical ASCVD)<br /></br>T: Total LDL ≥190<br /></br>A: Age 40-75 with DM<br /></br>T: Ten-year risk ≥7.5 % (calculator)<br /></br>I: Individual risk enhancers (e.g., FHx premature ASCVD) can push borderline (5-7.5 %) over the line<br /></br>N: None of the above → No statin; lifestyle only</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Cardiac stress scintigraphy<br /></br>   • Appropriate only in symptomatic patients (e.g., exertional chest pain) or certain high-risk asymptomatic diabetics before major surgery. Low-risk, asymptomatic screening produces false positives and unnecessary radiation/cost.</p></br><p>b. Daily aspirin therapy<br /></br>   • Primary-prevention aspirin now limited to adults 40-59 y with ≥10 % 10-yr ASCVD risk and low bleeding risk. At 0.4 % risk, harms (GI bleed, hemorrhagic stroke) outweigh benefit.</p></br><p>c. Echocardiography<br /></br>   • Used for structural/valvular disease, heart failure, or evaluation of murmurs—not for screening CAD in asymptomatic, low-risk persons.</p></br><p>d. HMG-CoA reductase inhibitor therapy (statin)<br /></br>   • Not indicated because LDL &lt;190 mg/dL, no diabetes, and 10-yr ASCVD risk &lt;7.5 %. Treating would yield minimal absolute risk reduction with potential side effects (myalgia, ↑LFTs).</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>For asymptomatic adults, preventive pharmacotherapy is guided by absolute ASCVD risk, not isolated lipid numbers. With a 10-year risk of only 0.4 %, the correct management is reassurance and continued lifestyle optimization—no drugs or cardiac testing at this time.</p>
162. An investigator would like to determine if a new medication for chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) decreases hospitalization rates in adults who come to the emergency department (ED) because of an exacerbation. All patients with COPD who come to the ED because of an exacerbation will be invited to participate in the study. Patients will be randomly assigned to receive the new medication plus standard therapy or standard therapy alone during the hours that a study nurse is present (7 AM to 7 PM daily). Patients who come to the ED outside of these hours will not be enrolled in the study. Physicians treating the patients will be blinded to the group assignments. Outcomes will be assessed by a physician not involved in the care of the patients. Which of the following is the greatest limitation of this study design?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Generalizability</br>b. Inadequate blinding of outcome assessors</br>c. Lack of a placebo group</br>d. Lead-time bias</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a.  Generalizability (poor external validity)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues:<br /></br>• Inclusion only when a study nurse is on-site (7 AM – 7 PM).<br /></br>• Patients presenting overnight (7 PM – 7 AM) are systematically excluded.<br /></br>• Otherwise, sound randomization, treating physicians blinded, outcome assessor blinded.</p></br><p>Pertinent positive: time-limited enrollment window.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no mention of problems with blinding or outcome assessment; there is an active control arm (standard therapy).</p></br><p>Pattern: Any study that samples a convenience subset of the target population (e.g., daytime ED visitors only) threatens how well the results can be applied to everyone with the condition.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify potential biases: selection, performance, detection, attrition, etc.<br /></br>2. Selection bias exists here because enrollment depends on time of presentation.<br /></br>3. Selection bias primarily undermines external validity → limits ability to generalize findings to all COPD patients with exacerbations, especially those who present at night (often sicker, different comorbidities, fewer resources).<br /></br>Core takeaway: A convenience sample that does not represent the full target population limits generalizability even if internal validity (randomization, blinding) is preserved.</p></br><p>Documentation note: In a real protocol, investigators must state how subjects are screened 24/7 or justify why limited hours will still produce a representative sample.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>Not directly tested; key concept is epidemiologic validity, not COPD mechanisms.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Again, not the focus; the question is purely about study design.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Convenience clock kills scope.”<br /></br>If enrollment is limited by convenience factors (time, day, location), think limited scope → poor generalizability.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Inadequate blinding of outcome assessors<br /></br>• The stem explicitly states a physician not involved in care will assess outcomes while blinded → detection bias minimized.</p></br><p>c. Lack of a placebo group<br /></br>• Standard therapy alone serves as an active control. In acute COPD exacerbations withholding standard care for a placebo would be unethical. Lack of placebo does not threaten validity here because providers are blinded and outcomes objective.</p></br><p>d. Lead-time bias<br /></br>• Lead-time bias occurs when earlier detection falsely appears to improve survival. This study is not about screening or timing of diagnosis; it measures hospitalization outcomes after randomization, so lead-time bias is irrelevant.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Take-Home<br /></br>Selection based on convenience rather than true random sampling weakens external validity → results may not apply to the entire population you hope to treat. Always ask: “Would the people left out behave differently?” If yes, generalizability is compromised.</p>
163. A 57-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by his neighbors, who found him lying unconscious in a puddle of vomit in his apartment. He has a 20-year history of alcohol use disorder. On arrival, he is lethargic and reports shortness of breath. He is unable to provide a medical history. His temperature is 39°C (102.2°F), pulse is 98/min, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 132/76 mm Hg. Diffuse crackles are heard bilaterally. Arterial blood gas analysis on room air shows:</br>pH   7.44</br>Pco2 30 mm Hg</br>Po2  48 mm Hg</br>An x-ray of the chest shows pulmonary infiltrates bilaterally. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy</br>b. Intravenous administration of dobutamine</br>c. Intravenous administration of methylprednisolone</br>d. Bronchoscopy with lavage</br>e. Intubation and mechanical ventilation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Intubation and mechanical ventilation  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Vital clues<br /></br>  – Altered sensorium (lethargic, found unconscious, vomiting) → poor airway protection, high aspiration risk.<br /></br>  – Severe hypoxemia: PaO₂ 48 mm Hg on room air (normal ≈ 80-100).<br /></br>  – ABG: pH 7.44 (mild alkalemia), PaCO₂ 30 mm Hg (respiratory alkalosis from tachypnea), widened A–a gradient ≈ 65 mm Hg → parenchymal process (aspiration pneumonia / early ARDS).<br /></br>  – CXR: bilateral infiltrates + diffuse crackles → non-cardiogenic pulmonary infiltrates.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no hypotension or chest pain to suggest cardiogenic shock; no history of steroid-responsive lung disease.  </p></br><p>These findings fulfill two immediate “ABCs” indications for endotracheal intubation:<br /></br>1. Failure to maintain oxygenation (PaO₂ &lt; 60 on room air).<br /></br>2. Inadequate airway protection due to depressed mental status/vomiting.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Stepwise)<br /></br>1. Airway: protect from further aspiration → secure endotracheal tube.<br /></br>2. Breathing: correct severe hypoxemia via positive-pressure ventilation + FiO₂ titration.<br /></br>3. Circulation: vitals are stable; no immediate pressor/inotrope is required.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In any patient with severe hypoxemia or inability to protect the airway, the FIRST intervention is endotracheal intubation and mechanical ventilation before pursuing definitive diagnostics or adjunctive therapies.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearls: “Patient intubated for airway protection and hypoxemic respiratory failure; ETT position confirmed; ventilator settings initiated; pulse-ox and capnography monitoring.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic alcohol use → impaired gag/cough → aspiration of gastric contents.<br /></br>• Chemical pneumonitis + infection triggers diffuse alveolar damage → increased permeability and non-cardiogenic pulmonary edema (early ARDS).<br /></br>• Resulting V/Q mismatch and shunt cause refractory hypoxemia ⇨ widened A–a gradient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate (ABC)<br /></br>1. Intubate, apply mechanical ventilation with PEEP and high FiO₂; titrate to SpO₂ ≥ 92%.<br /></br>2. Obtain blood cultures, empiric broad-spectrum antibiotics for aspiration pneumonia (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam).<br /></br>Adjunct/Follow-up<br /></br>• CXR after intubation to verify tube placement.<br /></br>• Consider CT head if concern for trauma while unconscious.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“60/50 rule” for ventilation:<br /></br>• PaO₂ &lt; 60 OR PaCO₂ &gt; 50 with symptoms → think tube.<br /></br>ABCs mantra: Airway always supersedes imaging or pharmacologic therapy.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hyperbaric oxygen therapy<br /></br>   • Indicated for carbon monoxide poisoning, gas gangrene, decompression sickness. No mention of CO source; PaCO₂ is low (CO poisoning usually shows normal ABG except low measured SaO₂).<br /></br>b. Intravenous dobutamine<br /></br>   • Used for cardiogenic shock/acute decompensated heart failure (pulmonary edema with S₃, elevated JVP). Here BP is stable, no cardiac history, ABG shows resp alkalosis rather than hypercapnia.<br /></br>c. Intravenous methylprednisolone<br /></br>   • Corticosteroids sometimes used in late ARDS but NEVER before securing airway/oxygenation; evidence is mixed and it is not an emergent first step.<br /></br>d. Bronchoscopy with lavage<br /></br>   • Appropriate for diagnostic sampling or foreign-body removal. Does not address life-threatening hypoxemia or protect airway; also risky in an unstable, hypoxic patient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line</br>Severe hypoxemia (PaO₂ &lt; 60 mm Hg) + inability to protect the airway mandates immediate endotracheal intubation and mechanical ventilation—the quintessential “A” &amp; “B” priority in emergency management.</p>
164. A 37-year-old woman is scheduled for arthroscopic repair of a right medial meniscus tear. Her procedure is the surgeon’s last scheduled operation of the day. All previous procedures this day involved the left knee. In the operating room, the resident prepares the left knee for the operation.</br>The operation is discontinued after the surgeon discovers no meniscus tear, and the patient is informed about the error. Which of the following is the best approach to prevent future errors of this type?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Asking the surgeon to resign from the medical staff</br>b. Having the surgeon and resident present the case at the next morbidity and mortality conference</br>c. Having the surgeon mark the surgical site with input from the patient prior to induction of anesthesia</br>d. Having the surgeon review the chart just prior to the procedure</br>e. Placing an incident report in the files of the resident and surgeon</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: c. Having the surgeon mark the surgical site with input from the patient prior to induction of anesthesia  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Error type: Wrong‐site surgery (left knee prepped, right knee supposed to be repaired).<br /></br>• Key clues: “surgeon’s last case of the day,” “all previous procedures involved the left knee,” preparation of the wrong knee ⇒ classic set-up for laterality mix-up.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No mention of a formal “time-out” or site-marking step prior to anesthesia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>The Joint Commission’s “Universal Protocol” for preventing wrong-patient, wrong-procedure, and wrong-site events has three essential steps:<br /></br>1. Pre-procedure verification of relevant documents.<br /></br>2. Marking the operative site by the surgeon with the patient awake, whenever possible.<br /></br>3. A mandatory “time-out” immediately before incision with the entire team confirming patient, procedure, and site.</p></br><p>Because wrong-site surgery is usually a system failure (communication + process) rather than a single “bad apple,” the most effective preventive measure is embedding a standardized, reproducible safety step—site marking—into every case.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology / Systems Review<br /></br>• Root causes of wrong-site surgery: hand-offs, multiple same-site cases, fatigue, lack of standardized verification.<br /></br>• System solutions (checklists, site marking, time-outs) dramatically cut error rates; reliance on individual memory does not.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment / Diagnostic Test Review<br /></br>Not applicable—focus is on process improvement. The “treatment” is adopting the Universal Protocol step of site marking.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Mark &amp; Pause”:<br /></br>• MARK the site with the awake patient.<br /></br>• PAUSE (time-out) before the knife.<br /></br>If you remember those two words, you’ll prevent the vast majority of wrong-site errors.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Ask the surgeon to resign – Punitive and individual-focused; doesn’t address systemic risk. Errors will recur with other staff.<br /></br>b. Presenting at M&amp;M – Good for education but passive; does not institute an active barrier against future events.<br /></br>d. Surgeon reviews chart just before procedure – Still relies on memory; doesn’t engage entire team or the patient; prone to fatigue/error.<br /></br>e. Place incident reports in files – Again punitive/documentary; necessary for reporting but alone does not prevent recurrence.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Big Takeaway<br /></br>Wrong-site surgery is a predictable, preventable system error. The single most effective, evidence-based preventive step is surgeon-performed site marking with patient participation before anesthesia, followed by a formal team time-out.</p>
165. A 52-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 6-week history of moderate epigastric pain and an intermittent mild, midsternal burning sensation when she eats. She has had a 4.5-kg (10-lb) weight loss during the past 6 months despite no change in appetite. During this time, she also occasionally has had dark stools. She has type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, and osteoarthritis. Her medications are metformin, hydrochlorothiazide, and ibuprofen. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 36 years. She appears malnourished. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 54 kg (120 lb); BMI is 21 kg/m2. Her pulse is 86/min, and blood pressure is 110/65 mm Hg. Examination shows mild epigastric tenderness. There is no hepatosplenomegaly. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her hematocrit is 30%, leukocyte count is 6200/mm3, and platelet count is 530,000/mm3. Serum electrolyte concentrations are within the reference ranges. In addition to discontinuing ibuprofen therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Celecoxib therapy</br>b. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy</br>c. Follow-up examination in 6 weeks</br>d. Pantoprazole therapy and follow-up examination in 6 weeks</br>e. Serologic testing for Helicobacter pylori</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Esophagogastroduodenoscopy (EGD)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key “alarm” clues that rule-in the need for immediate endoscopy:<br /></br>• Age ≥50 yr (52 yr)<br /></br>• Unintentional weight loss (4.5 kg in 6 mo) despite normal appetite<br /></br>• Evidence of upper-GI bleeding: dark stools + iron-deficiency anemia (Hct 30 %, microcytosis implied) + reactive thrombocytosis (PLT 530 K)<br /></br>• Persistent dyspepsia &gt;6 wk despite risk-factor modification (still taking NSAID)  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no hepatosplenomegaly (argues against portal HTN), but that does not remove concern for malignancy or peptic ulcer disease (PUD).</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Dyspepsia + ≥1 alarm feature” → skip empiric therapy/testing and proceed directly to diagnostic endoscopy with biopsy.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify dyspepsia (epigastric pain ± heartburn).<br /></br>2. Screen for alarm features (age, weight loss, GI bleed, persistent vomiting, progressive dysphagia, family history GI cancer, iron-deficiency anemia, palpable mass).<br /></br>3. Any alarm feature present? YES → immediate EGD.<br /></br>4. Document: indication for EGD, anemia work-up, cessation of NSAID, smoking cessation counseling.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: On Step 2/ NBME, the presence of any alarm feature in a dyspeptic patient mandates prompt EGD rather than empiric acid suppression or non-invasive H. pylori testing.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic NSAID use + smoking predispose to gastric or duodenal ulceration and upper-GI malignancy by impairing mucosal defenses.<br /></br>• Weight loss and iron-deficiency anemia raise suspicion for gastric carcinoma.<br /></br>• Reactive thrombocytosis often accompanies chronic blood loss.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line management when alarm features are present:<br /></br>• Esophagogastroduodenoscopy with biopsy of any ulcer or suspicious lesion.<br /></br>• Additional labs: iron studies, fecal occult blood, possibly CT imaging if mass seen.<br /></br>• Stop offending agents (NSAIDs) and consider PPI after biopsies obtained.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ALARMS” mnemonic for immediate EGD:<br /></br>A – Anemia (iron-deficiency)<br /></br>L – Loss of weight<br /></br>A – Anorexia/appetite loss<br /></br>R – Recent onset/progressive symptoms in older adult (&gt;55 in guidelines; &gt;50 in many questions)<br /></br>M – Melena/hematemesis<br /></br>S – Swallowing difficulty (dysphagia)  </p></br><p>If any ALARMS sign ⇒ scope!</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Celecoxib therapy<br /></br>• Switching from non-selective NSAID to COX-2 inhibitor reduces ulcer risk but does not address current alarm features or possible malignancy. Symptom relief without diagnosis could delay treatment of serious disease.</p></br><p>c. Follow-up examination in 6 weeks<br /></br>• Observation is appropriate only for uncomplicated, low-risk dyspepsia in patients &lt;60 yr with NO alarm signs.</p></br><p>d. Pantoprazole therapy and follow-up in 6 weeks<br /></br>• Empiric PPI trial is first-line for uncomplicated dyspepsia. Alarm features override this approach; delaying endoscopy could miss a bleeding ulcer or cancer.</p></br><p>e. Serologic testing for Helicobacter pylori<br /></br>• Non-invasive H. pylori testing (urea breath, stool Ag, or serology) is for low-risk patients. In high-risk/alarm presentations, endoscopy with biopsy for H. pylori AND malignancy is required.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Any patient ≥50 yr with dyspepsia plus weight loss or signs of GI bleeding requires immediate esophagogastroduodenoscopy. Empiric therapy or H. pylori testing alone is unsafe because it risks missing gastric cancer or a complicated ulcer.</p>
166. A 67-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of increasing difficulty walking. He has not had pain or numbness. He has no history of serious illness or recent infection. Examination of the lower extremities shows atrophy; muscle strength is 3/5 bilaterally. Deep tendon reflexes are increased in the lower extremities. Sensation is intact. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</br>b. Multiple sclerosis</br>c. Myasthenia gravis</br>d. Polymyositis</br>e. Transverse myelitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &amp; tempo: 67-year-old with 6 months of slowly progressive gait difficulty → classic age/tempo for a neuro-degenerative motor disorder.<br /></br>• Motor ONLY: “No pain or numbness … Sensation intact.” → rules out peripheral neuropathy, dorsal column, or spinothalamic cord lesions.<br /></br>• Mixed upper- &amp; lower-motor-neuron signs:<br /></br>  – LMN: “atrophy,” strength 3/5<br /></br>  – UMN: “deep-tendon reflexes are increased”<br /></br>This “pure motor + UMN + LMN with intact sensation” is the signature pattern for ALS.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify deficit type: purely motor.<br /></br>2. Look for UMN vs LMN features → both present.<br /></br>3. Check sensory system → normal → eliminates cord compression, transverse myelitis, MS.<br /></br>4. Chronic course in an older adult → favors degenerative motor neuron disease → ALS.<br /></br>Take-home: Presence of mixed UMN/LMN deficits in the absence of sensory findings = think ALS until proved otherwise.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real patient): Document distribution and progression of weakness, presence of spasticity/hyperreflexia, fasciculations/atrophy, absence of sensory loss, and impact on ADLs.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• ALS causes progressive degeneration of:<br /></br>  – Anterior horn motor neurons (LMN signs: atrophy, fasciculations, weakness)<br /></br>  – Corticospinal tracts/corticobulbar tracts (UMN signs: spasticity, hyperreflexia, Babinski)<br /></br>• Sensory pathways, bladder/bowel, and ocular movements are typically spared.<br /></br>• Sporadic in most patients; 10% familial (e.g., SOD1 mutation).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnostics: Primarily clinical; EMG shows widespread denervation and reinnervation, ruling out neuropathy or myopathy; MRI to exclude compressive lesions.<br /></br>Treatment:<br /></br>• Riluzole (glutamate inhibitor) → modestly prolongs survival.<br /></br>• Edaravone (free-radical scavenger) also approved.<br /></br>• Multidisciplinary supportive care (nutrition, non-invasive ventilation).</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ALS = A-Loss of Sensation? NO! → pure motor.”<br /></br>“UMN (spastic), LMN (atrophic) → Lou Gehrig had both.”</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Multiple sclerosis<br /></br>• Demyelinating CNS disease in younger women; sensory deficits, optic neuritis, ataxia, lesions separated in time/space. Sensation is usually abnormal—unlike this vignette.</p></br><p>c. Myasthenia gravis<br /></br>• Postsynaptic ACh receptor auto-Ab: fluctuating fatigable weakness, ocular/bulbar muscles first, no UMN signs, reflexes normal, no muscle atrophy early.</p></br><p>d. Polymyositis<br /></br>• Immune-mediated muscle inflammation; proximal symmetric weakness (difficulty climbing stairs, combing hair), normal or decreased reflexes, NO UMN signs, pain may be present, CK markedly elevated. Atrophy appears late.</p></br><p>e. Transverse myelitis<br /></br>• Acute/subacute spinal cord inflammation → bilateral motor AND sensory deficits below a spinal level, frequently with sphincter dysfunction. Reflexes often absent acutely then hyper- afterwards, but sensory level would NOT be intact.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>In an older patient with chronic, progressive, purely motor deficits showing BOTH upper- and lower-motor-neuron signs yet completely normal sensation, the most likely—and exam-favorite—diagnosis is ALS.</p>
167. A 14-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of an 18-hour history of persistent right-sided lower abdominal pain. She has not had fever, vomiting, or diarrhea. She has no history of serious illness or operative procedures. Menarche was at the age of 12 years, and menses occur at regular 28-day intervals. Her last menstrual period was 2 weeks ago. She is not sexually active. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 72/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows mild tenderness with guarding over the right lower quadrant. There is no distention, and bowel sounds are normal. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Her hemoglobin concentration is 13.9 g/dL, and leukocyte count is 8000/mm³ with a normal differential. Urinalysis shows no abnormalities. Abdominal ultrasonography shows a small amount of fluid in the pelvis. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Appendicitis</br>b. Ectopic pregnancy</br>c. Mittelschmerz</br>d. Ovarian torsion</br>e. Pelvic inflammatory disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Appendicitis  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 18-hour history of continuous, localized right-lower-quadrant (RLQ) pain → classic location and time course for early appendiceal inflammation.<br /></br>• Mild guarding = peritoneal irritation; usually absent in benign, self-limited causes such as mittelschmerz.<br /></br>• Afebrile, WBC 8 000/μL → Early/early-acute appendicitis may have normal vitals and labs; leukocytosis and fever typically appear after 12–24 h or with progression.<br /></br>• Small amount of free pelvic fluid on ultrasound → sterile inflammatory exudate frequently seen with appendiceal irritation before frank perforation; not specific but supportive.<br /></br>• NOT sexually active → virtually rules out PID and makes ectopic pregnancy extremely unlikely.  </p></br><p>Key “pertinent negatives”<br /></br>• No sudden, excruciating onset (torsion).<br /></br>• No cyclic, crampy “comes-and-goes” pattern (mittelschmerz).<br /></br>• No vaginal bleeding or positive pregnancy test (ectopic).  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Adolescent with isolated RLQ pain + peritoneal signs = think appendicitis first, even if early clinical/lab findings appear mild.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Is the pain gynecologic? Pregnancy test answers that, but history eliminates sexual activity.<br /></br>2. Is there an acute surgical abdomen? Guarding = yes → appendicitis highest prevalence among nongenital causes of RLQ pain in children/teens.<br /></br>3. Labs/vitals do NOT exclude appendicitis in early course; rely on physical findings.<br /></br>4. Imaging: Pelvic fluid alone is nonspecific; in girls, graded-compression US ± CT is used if diagnosis remains uncertain.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Early appendicitis can present with minimal systemic signs; localized peritoneal irritation (guarding, rebound) should prompt surgical evaluation regardless of normal WBC.  </p></br><p>Documentation point: “RLQ tenderness with voluntary guarding; suspicion for acute appendicitis—surgical consult obtained.”  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Obstruction of appendiceal lumen (fecalith, lymphoid hyperplasia) → intraluminal pressure ↑ → ischemia &amp; bacterial overgrowth → inflammation of serosa → localized peritonitis (tenderness/guarding).<br /></br>• Perforation risk rises sharply after 24–48 h; hence early recognition critical.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>1. Labs: CBC may be normal early; do not rely on leukocytosis to rule out.<br /></br>2. Imaging: In children/adolescents, graded-compression ultrasound (non-compressible, &gt;6 mm appendix) is first line; CT if equivocal.<br /></br>3. Management: Prompt surgical appendectomy ± pre-/post-op IV antibiotics (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam or ceftriaxone + metronidazole).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>APPENDICITIS mnemonic “PAIN”:<br /></br>P – Periumbilical → RLQ migration<br /></br>A – Anorexia<br /></br>I – Involuntary Guarding/Rebound<br /></br>N – Neutrophils (but may be Normal early!)  </p></br><p>───────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Ectopic pregnancy – Requires implantation of fertilized ovum; patient is not sexually active, and pain 2 weeks post-LMP is far too early for tubal distention. β-hCG would be positive.<br /></br>c. Mittelschmerz – Mid-cycle unilateral pain that is colicky, lasts &lt;24 h, with NO peritoneal signs; guarding would not be expected. US may show physiologic cyst, minimal free fluid.<br /></br>d. Ovarian torsion – Sudden, severe pelvic pain with nausea/vomiting; Doppler US shows enlarged ovary with absent blood flow. Guarding may occur, but pain classically abrupt and intense.<br /></br>e. Pelvic inflammatory disease – Requires ascending infection; almost always seen in sexually active patients; fever, cervical motion tenderness, and leukocytosis common.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming normal WBC or afebrile status rules out appendicitis—early disease can look deceptively mild.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────</p>
168. A 62-year-old man, who underwent right below-the-knee amputation 36 years ago, comes to the emergency department because of a 1-month history of an ulcer on the end of the residual limb. His amputation was the result of injuries he sustained while serving in the military. He says his prosthetic socket has "felt loose and has been rubbing recently." During the past 6 months, he has had progressive difficulty walking because of sharp pain that radiates up his leg. The pain has been occurring earlier during his walks; it slowly improves with rest. He previously walked 1 mile daily for exercise and could walk several miles without stopping. He has not had vomiting, fever, chills, or malaise. He has type 2 diabetes mellitus treated with diet. One month ago, his hemoglobin A1c was 6%. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the right lower extremity shows mild erythema and a 1 × 1-cm ulcer, with a depth of 3 mm, over the anteromedial aspect of the limb. There is viable soft tissue, and margins are clear. The limb is warm. The popliteal pulse is 1+. In addition to a recommendation for non-weight bearing and avoidance of direct pressure to the site, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. MRI of the right lower extremity</br>b. Recommendation for refitting of the prosthetic socket</br>c. Surgical revision of the amputation site</br>d. Wound culture and antibiotic therapy</br>e. X-ray of the right lower extremity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Recommendation for refitting of the prosthetic socket  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Prosthetic socket has ‘felt loose and has been rubbing recently’” – direct mechanical explanation for the ulcer.<br /></br>• Small (1 × 1 cm), shallow (3 mm) ulcer with healthy surrounding tissue and no systemic signs of infection → pressure/friction ulcer, not an infected or ischemic wound.<br /></br>• Limb is warm and has a palpable (although diminished) popliteal pulse → blood flow present; not a critically ischemic stump.<br /></br>• HbA1c 6 % and diet-controlled diabetes → low infectious risk compared with poorly controlled diabetes.<br /></br>• Key negatives: no fever, chills, purulence, foul odor, exposed bone, or systemic toxicity.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify etiology of the ulcer: mechanical friction from an ill-fitting prosthesis.<br /></br>2. First-line management of a pressure/friction ulcer is to eliminate the offending pressure.<br /></br>3. The most direct way to do that in an amputee is to have the prosthetic socket refitted or relined.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When a superficial ulcer forms at an amputation stump in the setting of a loose prosthesis and absent signs of infection, correct the mechanical problem first. Document in the chart: “Pressure ulcer at anteromedial stump secondary to socket misfit; patient instructed to remain non-weight-bearing until prosthesis is refitted.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Residual limbs change shape over time (muscle atrophy, weight loss/gain) → socket gap → focal high-pressure areas during gait → skin breakdown.<br /></br>• Ulcer depth &lt;0.5 cm and no bone involvement = Stage 2 pressure ulcer.<br /></br>• Adequate perfusion (warm stump, palpable pulse) allows healing once pressure is removed.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>• Immediate non-weight bearing / off-loading.<br /></br>• Prompt prosthetic refitting, addition of stump socks, or soft liners to redistribute pressure.<br /></br>• Local wound care (moist gauze, hydrocolloid, etc.) – implied but not tested in this question.<br /></br>Second-line / when to escalate:<br /></br>• Imaging (x-ray or MRI) only if deep, chronic, or probing bone.<br /></br>• Culture &amp; antibiotics if clinical infection (erythema &gt;2 cm, warmth, swelling, purulence, systemic signs).<br /></br>• Surgical revision for chronically non-healing ulcers, osteomyelitis, or bony prominence.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>PRESSURE mnemonic for stump ulcers:<br /></br>P – Prosthesis fit, R – Relieve weight, E – Examine skin daily, S – Stage ulcer, S – Stop ambulation, U – Use liners, R – Refer for refit, E – Evaluate vascular status.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. MRI of the right lower extremity – Used to rule out osteomyelitis when bone is likely involved (deep, probing bone, chronic wounds). Bone not exposed; superficial ulcer → unnecessary imaging.<br /></br>c. Surgical revision of the amputation site – Reserved for bony prominences or recurrent, non-healing ulcers despite proper socket fit and vascular optimization. Too aggressive here.<br /></br>d. Wound culture and antibiotic therapy – Indicated only if there is clinical infection; none present (no purulence, systemic signs, or cellulitis extending &gt;2 cm). Antibiotics without infection promote resistance.<br /></br>e. X-ray of the right lower extremity – Low sensitivity early in osteomyelitis; also unnecessary without suspicion of bone involvement. May be obtained later if ulcer worsens or fails to heal.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line: Superficial stump ulcers caused by a loose prosthesis are treated by off-loading and refitting the socket; fix the pressure, the ulcer heals.</p>
169. A 40-year-old man comes to the physician 6 days after noticing a nonpainful lump in his neck. He also has a 2-week history of palpitations. He is otherwise asymptomatic. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His temperature is 37.2°C (99°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 134/60 mm Hg. Examination shows a 1.4-cm, firm thyroid nodule. His serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration is less than 0.05 mU/L, and serum free thyroxine concentration is 3.5 ng/dL (N=0.9–1.7). Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Fine-needle aspiration biopsy of the nodule</br>b. Measurement of serum calcitonin concentration</br>c. Operative resection of the nodule</br>d. Radioactive iodine uptake scan</br>e. Ultrasonography of the thyroid gland</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: Fine-needle aspiration (FNA) biopsy of the nodule  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Firm 1.4-cm thyroid nodule” – any palpable, solid thyroid nodule ≥1 cm warrants a malignancy work-up.<br /></br>• “TSH &lt; 0.05 mU/L, free T4 3.5 ng/dL” – confirms biochemical hyperthyroidism, but a low TSH does NOT rule out cancer in the nodule itself.<br /></br>• “40-year-old man, otherwise healthy” – no obvious contraindication to immediate tissue diagnosis.<br /></br>Key point: the presence of hyperthyroidism does not obviate the need to rule out malignancy in a discrete, palpable nodule.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify a discrete ≥1 cm thyroid nodule → must determine if it is malignant.<br /></br>2. The single best test to establish a tissue diagnosis in a thyroid nodule is FNA.<br /></br>3. Hot (“functioning”) nodules are seldom malignant, but “seldom” ≠ “never.” Up to ~5 % of functioning nodules harbor cancer; a firm consistency further raises concern.<br /></br>4. FNA can be done quickly in the office and will directly guide the need for surgery vs. medical/ablative therapy for hyperthyroidism.</p></br><p>Take-home: Any solid thyroid nodule ≥1 cm—regardless of thyroid function tests—requires cytologic assessment with FNA before definitive therapy is chosen.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: record size, consistency, location, and obtain FNA results before scheduling definitive treatment.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Thyroid cancers (papillary &gt; follicular &gt; medullary &gt; anaplastic) usually present as painless, firm nodules.<br /></br>• Autonomous (“toxic”) adenomas result from TSH-independent activation of the TSH receptor → ↑T4/T3, ↓TSH.<br /></br>• Although most toxic adenomas are benign, a small percentage are malignant; hence cytology is still required when a discrete, palpable nodule is present.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic / Treatment Overview<br /></br>First-line diagnostic test for a palpable thyroid nodule ≥1 cm = ultrasound-guided FNA.<br /></br>If FNA shows:<br /></br>• Benign cytology → treat hyperthyroidism (radioiodine ablation or surgery) as indicated.<br /></br>• Malignant/suspicious → surgical lobectomy or total thyroidectomy + appropriate oncologic management.<br /></br>• Nondiagnostic → repeat FNA or consider diagnostic lobectomy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ONE centimeter = ONE needle.”<br /></br>If you can feel a solid thyroid lump ≥1 cm, the next step is to stick a needle in it (FNA) before doing anything else.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Serum calcitonin – screens for medullary carcinoma, appropriate only with a suspicious family history (MEN-2) or characteristic ultrasound findings; it does not evaluate the broader differential.<br /></br>c. Operative resection – definitive but overly aggressive without cytologic confirmation. FNA triages patients to surgery vs. observation.<br /></br>d. Radioactive iodine uptake scan – useful to confirm a toxic adenoma, but it does not answer the malignancy question; a “hot” scan result could delay cancer diagnosis.<br /></br>e. Thyroid ultrasonography – excellent for non-palpable or borderline nodules and for guiding FNA, but cytology (FNA) is still the diagnostic step that actually determines malignancy risk.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core USMLE/ Shelf Takeaway<br /></br>Every palpable thyroid nodule ≥1 cm demands cytologic evaluation with FNA—regardless of thyroid hormone levels—because ruling out cancer always takes priority over characterizing function.</p>
170. A previously healthy 24-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 12-hour history of increasing pain to the left of the center of his chest. Initially, the pain was mild and occurred only when he breathed deeply. Now, the pain is more severe and constant. He rates the pain as an 8 on a 10-point scale. The pain increases when he lies on his back, decreases when he leans forward, and does not radiate. He has not had palpitations, shortness of breath, nausea, vomiting, or sweating. He says that he is "recovering from a head cold," which he has had for 14 days. He appears moderately uncomfortable but is in no respiratory distress. His temperature is 37.6°C (99.6°F), pulse is 66/min and regular, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 152/88 mm Hg. Examination, including cardiovascular examination, shows no abnormalities. His serum creatine kinase activity is 420 mg/dL with an MB fraction of 12% (N<5%). An ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Echocardiography</br>b. CT scan of the chest</br>c. Cardiac MRI</br>d. Coronary angiography</br>e. Pericardiocentesis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Echocardiography  </p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key words/phrases: 24-year-old previously healthy man; sharp chest pain “to the left of center”; worse when lying flat, improved by sitting forward; preceded by a “head cold”; low-grade fever; normal cardiovascular exam; ECG (not shown) in this classic stem is almost always diffuse, concave ST-segment elevation with PR-segment depression.<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: pleuritic, positional chest pain + recent viral URI → classic for acute pericarditis.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no diaphoresis, dyspnea, or radiation; normal vital signs except mild temp; age 24 → extremely low coronary risk.<br /></br>• CK-MB mildly ↑ (12%)—can occur with pericardial inflammation that extends into superficial myocardium (myopericarditis).</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: young patient + viral prodrome + pleuritic/positional pain + diffuse ST elevation → “Acute pericarditis—must look for effusion/tamponade.”</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why echo?)<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Diagnose acute pericarditis clinically/ECG.<br /></br>2. Next priority = determine whether a clinically significant pericardial effusion or early tamponade is present.<br /></br>   • Best initial, non-invasive test = transthoracic echocardiogram (TTE).<br /></br>   • Echo guides need for admission, NSAID vs colchicine alone, serial imaging, or urgent pericardiocentesis.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In acute pericarditis, obtain an immediate bedside echo to look for effusion—even if exam is “normal.”</p></br><p>Documentation: “Bedside TTE ordered to evaluate for pericardial effusion and chamber collapse suggestive of tamponade.”</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Most common U.S. etiology = idiopathic/viral (coxsackie, adenovirus, echovirus).<br /></br>• Inflammation of pericardial layers → pleuritic chest pain (parietal pleura innervation) that’s relieved by sitting forward, worsened supine.<br /></br>• Inflammatory exudate may → effusion → ↑ intrapericardial pressure → impaired diastolic filling (tamponade).<br /></br>• ECG: diffuse concave (“saddle-shaped”) ST elevation + PR depression; no reciprocal changes.<br /></br>• Mild CK-MB rise = epicardial irritation (“myopericarditis”).</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• First-line therapy once effusion ruled out: high-dose NSAID (e.g., ibuprofen 600–800 mg q6-8 h) + colchicine 0.5–0.6 mg bid for 3 mo.<br /></br>• Steroids only if refractory or contraindications to NSAID/colchicine.<br /></br>• Echo: initial and follow-up.<br /></br>• Pericardiocentesis: only for large effusion/tamponade OR unclear etiology (e.g., suspected malignancy, purulent).</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pericarditis 3 P’s”:<br /></br>1. Pleuritic pain<br /></br>2. Positional (better sitting forward)<br /></br>3. Post-viral<br /></br>If you see the 3 P’s → think Pericarditis → order “Peri-cardio-echo.”</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. CT scan of the chest – Useful for aortic dissection or pulmonary embolism; higher radiation, not first-line for effusion assessment in a stable patient with clear pericarditis signs.<br /></br>c. Cardiac MRI – Excellent for pericardial thickness/inflammation but expensive, time-consuming; reserved for equivocal cases or constrictive disease, not initial step.<br /></br>d. Coronary angiography – Indicated for acute coronary syndrome (crushing substernal pain, risk factors, ST elevations in an anatomic distribution, troponin rise). Diffuse ST changes + positional pain in a 24-year-old points away from MI.<br /></br>e. Pericardiocentesis – Therapeutic/diagnostic procedure for large effusion or tamponade, NOT for routine uncomplicated pericarditis with normal hemodynamics.</p></br><p>───────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a young patient with classic acute pericarditis, the immediate next diagnostic step is a transthoracic echocardiogram to evaluate for pericardial effusion and potential tamponade.</p>
171. A 21-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by paramedics because of a 10-hour history of altered mental status. The mother says the patient was “fine” last night. This morning, he did not wake up at his usual time; when she went to check on him, she could not get him to wake up. He has no history of serious medical or psychiatric illness and takes no medications. His mother says he does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. On arrival, he is lethargic but not in respiratory distress. He is responsive to painful stimuli. His temperature is 36.7°C (98.0°F), pulse is 120/min, respirations are 26/min, and blood pressure is 135/85 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 100%. Physical examination shows no other abnormalities.  </br>Laboratory studies are shown:  </br>Serum  </br>Na+ 143 mEq/L  </br>K+ 3.8 mEq/L  </br>Cl- 110 mEq/L  </br>HCO3- 13 mEq/L  </br>Urea nitrogen 14 mg/dL  </br>Glucose 120 mg/dL  </br>Osmolality 290 mOsmol/kg H2O  </br>Urine  </br>Glucose none  </br>Ketones 3+  </br>Arterial blood gas analysis is shown:  </br>pH 7.43  </br>Pco2 22 mm Hg  </br>Po2 96 mm Hg  </br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Alcoholic ketoacidosis  </br>b. Diabetic ketoacidosis  </br>c. Ethylene glycol toxicity  </br>d. Methanol toxicity  </br>e. Salicylate toxicity</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Alcoholic ketoacidosis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• High-anion-gap metabolic acidosis: Na – Cl – HCO₃ = 143 – 110 – 13 = 20 (&gt;12).<br /></br>• Normal measured serum osmolality (290 mOsm/kg) ≈ calculated osmolality (≈298) → no osmolar gap → argues against toxic alcohols (methanol, ethylene glycol).<br /></br>• Large ketonuria (3+) with normal glucose (120 mg/dL) → ketoacidosis not driven by hyperglycemia.<br /></br>• ABG: pH 7.43 (slightly alkalemic), PCO₂ 22 mm Hg (respiratory alkalosis), HCO₃ 13 mEq/L (metabolic acidosis). pH ~normal means the two disorders are partially cancelling each other → classic mixed respiratory alkalosis + metabolic acidosis seen in alcoholic ketoacidosis (AKA).<br /></br>• No history of diabetes; mother “doesn’t drink” is often unreliable—patients with AKA frequently deny or hide alcohol use.</p></br><p>Key pattern: High-anion-gap acidosis + ketones + normal glucose + no osmolar gap ⇒ alcoholic ketoacidosis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the primary disturbance: high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis (HCO₃ 13).<br /></br>2. Apply Winter formula (1.5[HCO₃]+8±2 → 1.5×13+8 = 27.5±2). Expected PCO₂ ≈ 26–29 mm Hg; actual PCO₂ 22 mm Hg → extra respiratory alkalosis.<br /></br>3. Check for osmolar gap to rule in/out toxic alcohols → absent.<br /></br>4. Look for source of anion gap. With large ketones but normal glucose, alcoholic ketoacidosis is most likely.</p></br><p>Take-home: In a patient with unexplained high-AG metabolic acidosis, always check glucose, ketones, and osmolar gap; euglycemic ketosis + no gap → think AKA.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): mental status, vital signs, AG calculation, serum osmolality, alcohol level if available, and initiate dextrose-containing fluids + thiamine.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• AKA develops after a period of heavy alcohol intake followed by poor oral intake or vomiting → depleted hepatic glycogen, low insulin, elevated counter-regulatory hormones.<br /></br>• Lipolysis → ↑ free fatty acids → converted to β-hydroxybutyrate &amp; acetoacetate = ketone bodies → metabolic acidosis.<br /></br>• Hypovolemia + vomiting → respiratory alkalosis from hyperventilation.<br /></br>• Glucose is normal or low because alcohol blocks gluconeogenesis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Management Snapshot (FYI)<br /></br>1. Thiamine 100 mg IV (before glucose) to prevent Wernicke encephalopathy.<br /></br>2. D5NS or D5½NS to stimulate endogenous insulin release and stop ketogenesis.<br /></br>3. Correct electrolytes (especially K⁺, Mg²⁺).<br /></br>4. Treat concomitant alcohol withdrawal if needed (benzodiazepines).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Mnemonic<br /></br>“Alcoholic KETO makes you TELL a LIE”:<br /></br>• K – Ketones present<br /></br>• E – Euglycemic (normal glucose)<br /></br>• T – Thiamine before sugar<br /></br>• O – Osmolar gap absent<br /></br>• LIE – Patient often “lies” about drinking</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Diabetic ketoacidosis – Usually glucose &gt;250 mg/dL (often &gt;400). pH &lt;7.30, no mixed respiratory alkalosis. Normal glucose + no diabetes history argues against.<br /></br>c. Ethylene glycol toxicity – High anion gap + high osmolar gap, no ketonuria. Often causes calcium oxalate crystals and acute renal failure. Osmolar gap absent here.<br /></br>d. Methanol toxicity – High anion gap + high osmolar gap, no ketonuria; visual disturbance (“snowfield” vision) typical. Osmolar gap absent.<br /></br>e. Salicylate toxicity – Produces mixed respiratory alkalosis + metabolic acidosis, but does NOT generate significant ketonuria; often presents with tinnitus, fever, vomiting. Ketones 3+ make this unlikely.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Euglycemic high-anion-gap metabolic acidosis with positive ketones and no osmolar gap points squarely to alcoholic ketoacidosis.</p>
172. A 25-year-old man is brought to the emergency department 1 hour after sustaining a gunshot wound to the abdomen. He has pain at the bullet entry site. He is awake, alert, and appropriately conversant. His temperature is 38.8°C (101.8°F), pulse is 85/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 128/70 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows a bullet wound 5 cm to the right of the umbilicus. There is moderate tenderness over the entry site. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. During operative exploration, 250 mL of succus entericus and eight small bowel holes are found in the abdomen; the bowel holes are treated by resection of 12 cm of proximal ileum. The intestine is reconstructed with a stapled anastomosis. The midline abdominal fascia is sutured closed. Which of the following methods of skin and soft tissue wound management at this point is most likely to decrease the risk for postoperative wound infection?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Delayed primary closure</br>b. Hand-sewn suture closure of the skin</br>c. Use of skin staples</br>d. Application of cyanoacrylate adhesive to the wound</br>e. Split-thickness skin graft</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Delayed primary closure (DPC)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Bullet-wound → emergent laparotomy.<br /></br>• Intra-op findings: 250 mL of succus entericus + 8 small-bowel perforations = gross GI spillage → wound class IV (dirty/infected).<br /></br>• Dirty wounds carry 20–40 % surgical-site infection (SSI) rate if the skin is closed immediately.<br /></br>• Key clues: GI content in peritoneum, multiple enterotomies, stapled anastomosis ⇒ heavily contaminated field.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Classify wound (clean, clean-contaminated, contaminated, dirty). Presence of perforated viscus = dirty.<br /></br>2. For dirty wounds, closing the fascia is fine (keeps hernia risk low), but closing skin traps bacteria → SSI/abscess.<br /></br>3. Most evidence-based method to reduce SSI is Delayed Primary Closure: leave subQ/skin open, pack with moist dressing or negative-pressure device, reassess on POD 3–5; if clean/no purulence, then close. Core takeaway: “If it’s dirty, delay the skin closure.”</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: record wound class, degree of contamination, that skin left open for DPC, and daily dressing-change/NPWT plan.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Succus entericus contains ≥10⁸ CFU/mL gut flora (E. coli, Bacteroides, Enterococcus).<br /></br>• Immediate skin closure converts open contaminated field into a warm, moist, hypoxic pocket—ideal for bacterial proliferation → cellulitis, fascial dehiscence, intra-abdominal abscess.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Initial management in OR:<br /></br>• Copious warm-saline irrigation, resection/anastomosis, close fascia.<br /></br>• Wound left open with wet-to-dry gauze or negative-pressure wound therapy (NPWT).<br /></br>• Inspect daily. Close at bedside/OR once granulation tissue clean (usually POD 3–5).<br /></br>Antibiotics: broad-spectrum (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam) for 24 h if no continuing contamination; longer if peritonitis/sepsis.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Dirty wound? Leave the door open.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>“4th-class (dirty) → 4–5-day delay before closure.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Immediate hand-sewn suture closure: still primary closure; infection risk unchanged.<br /></br>c. Skin staples: faster but same biology—traps bacteria.<br /></br>d. Cyanoacrylate adhesive: essentially seals bacteria inside the incision; contraindicated in contaminated wounds.<br /></br>e. Split-thickness skin graft: used for large skin losses or chronic wounds, not for a fresh midline laparotomy; also would cover bacteria and graft would fail.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home<br /></br>In a dirty/infected abdominal wound, close the fascia but leave skin/subQ open and perform Delayed Primary Closure after contamination is controlled to sharply reduce postoperative SSI.</p>
173. A 32-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-week history of fatigue and increased urination. She has had a 2.3-kg (5-lb) weight loss despite eating more during this time. She has had two urinary tract infections in the past. She takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. The patient is alert and fully oriented. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 52 kg (115 lb); BMI is 19 kg/m2. Her temperature is 36.5 °C (97.7 °F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 100%. The oropharynx is dry. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Na+ 142 mEq/L</br>K+ 5.4 mEq/L</br>Cl– 104 mEq/L</br>HCO3– 16 mEq/L</br>Glucose 800 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.3 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>Specific gravity 1.030</br>Bilirubin none</br>Protein none</br>RBC 0-5/hpf</br>WBC 25-50/hpf</br>Nitrites positive</br>Leukocyte esterase positive</br>Glucose >1000 mg/24 h</br>Which of the following serum hormonal profiles is most consistent with this patient's presentation?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Decreased decreased decreased</br>b. Decreased increased decreased</br>c. Decreased decreased increased</br>d. Decreased increased increased</br>e. Increased increased decreased</br>f. Increased decreased increased</br>g. Increased decreased decreased</br>h. Increased increased increased</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Decreased (insulin)     Increased (glucagon)    Increased (catecholamines/other counter-regulatory hormones)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that point to new-onset diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA) due to type 1 diabetes mellitus:<br /></br>• Age 32 and thin (BMI 19) → favors autoimmune type 1 rather than insulin-resistant type 2.<br /></br>• Polyuria, polydipsia, weight loss in just 2 weeks → classic osmotic diuresis from severe insulin deficiency.<br /></br>• Glucose 800 mg/dL, HCO₃⁻ 16 mEq/L, K⁺ 5.4 mEq/L → marked hyperglycemia with an anion-gap metabolic acidosis and “pseudo-hyperkalemia,” all hallmarks of DKA.<br /></br>• Tachycardia and hypotension with dry mucous membranes → dehydration from osmotic diuresis.<br /></br>• UTI (positive nitrites, leukocyte esterase, and pyuria) → common precipitating infection for DKA.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Absolute insulin deficiency is the fundamental defect in type 1 DM.<br /></br>2. Without insulin, hepatic glucose output, lipolysis, and ketogenesis go unchecked.<br /></br>3. The body perceives a state of starvation and responds by releasing counter-regulatory hormones—principally glucagon and catecholamines (plus cortisol and growth hormone)—to raise plasma glucose even further.<br /></br>Therefore the expected serum profile is:<br /></br>• Insulin ↓ (nearly absent)<br /></br>• Glucagon ↑ (α-cells uninhibited)<br /></br>• Catecholamines ↑ (stress response to infection, volume depletion, and hypovolemic shock risk)</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield points)<br /></br>• Autoimmune destruction of pancreatic β-cells → absolute insulin deficiency.<br /></br>• Insulin normally suppresses α-cell glucagon release and sympathetic output; loss of this brake drives glucagon &amp; epinephrine up.<br /></br>• Elevated glucagon + catecholamines activate hormone-sensitive lipase → ↑ free fatty acids → hepatic ketone production (β-hydroxybutyrate, acetoacetate).<br /></br>• Lack of insulin + high counter-regulators → osmotic diuresis (polyuria), volume depletion, potassium shift out of cells (serum K⁺ high but total stores low).</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Immediate IV insulin (regular insulin) to halt ketogenesis and allow glucose uptake.<br /></br>2. Aggressive isotonic saline for volume repletion, then dextrose once glucose &lt;200 mg/dL.<br /></br>3. Potassium repletion once serum K⁺ &lt;5.0 mEq/L and urine output established.<br /></br>4. Treat precipitating infection with appropriate antibiotics (e.g., nitrofurantoin or TMP-SMX for uncomplicated UTI).<br /></br>Remember: insulin lowers serum K⁺ by driving it intracellularly—hence mandatory K⁺ monitoring.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“DKA = 3 D’s: No in D ulin → ↑ Gluco D on (glucagon) &amp; aD renaline (epi).”<br /></br>Visualize a car with no “INSULIN key,” so the “GAS” (glucagon) pedal and “TURBO” (epinephrine) are floored.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a.
