Decreased/Decreased/Decreased – would occur with exogenous insulin overdose or hypoglycemia, not severe hyperglycemia.<br /></br>b. Decreased/↑/Decreased – fails to account for stress-induced catecholamine surge.<br /></br>c. Decreased/Decreased/↑ – glucagon would not be low when insulin is absent.<br /></br>e-h (any option with ↑ insulin) – incompatible with new-onset type 1 DM where endogenous insulin is virtually absent.</p></br><p>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In DKA from type 1 diabetes, think “absent insulin, all counter-regulators screaming high”—decreased insulin with elevated glucagon and catecholamines.</p>
174. A previously healthy 37-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-year history of her hands and fingers turning a bluish color with exposure to cold. The discoloration has been occurring with increasing frequency and now occurs with exposure to air conditioning and cold water. Examination of the hands shows no abnormalities. The brachial and radial pulses are present bilaterally. When the hands are rinsed in cold water, the fingers become bluish then bright red with rewarming. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. β-Adrenergic blocking agent therapy</br>b. Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitor therapy</br>c. Calcium-channel blocking agent therapy</br>d. Corticosteroid therapy</br>e. Dorsal sympathectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Calcium-channel blocking agent therapy (eg, nifedipine, amlodipine)</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 37-year-old woman (typical demographic for primary Raynaud phenomenon).<br /></br>  – 2-year history of “bluish” discoloration of fingers precipitated by cold or emotional stress (air-conditioning, cold water).<br /></br>  – Triphasic color change described in real time during the cold-water challenge: pallor/blue → bright red with rewarming.<br /></br>  – Normal examination and intact brachial/radial pulses → excludes fixed arterial obstruction.<br /></br>  – No systemic symptoms (skin thickening, arthritis, digital ulcers) → favors primary rather than secondary Raynaud.  </p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No skin thickening, telangiectasias, calcinosis, or ulcers that would suggest systemic sclerosis.<br /></br>  – No neuropathic findings or trauma history that would suggest thoracic outlet or vibration injury.  </p></br><p>Pattern: Symmetric, intermittent, cold-induced digital vasospasm in a healthy young woman = primary Raynaud phenomenon.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify Raynaud phenomenon.<br /></br>2. Decide if primary (benign) or secondary (associated with another disorder). Normal exam and pulses → primary.<br /></br>3. Management hierarchy:<br /></br>   • Lifestyle first (gloves, avoid triggers, smoking cessation).<br /></br>   • If symptoms are frequent or intolerable despite lifestyle → start vasodilatory pharmacotherapy.<br /></br>4. First-line pharmacologic agent for primary Raynaud = dihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker (nifedipine most studied).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Calcium-channel blockers reduce digital arterial smooth-muscle spasm, decrease attack frequency/severity, and are the recommended initial drug therapy for symptomatic primary Raynaud.</p></br><p>Documentation bullets (if this were a real encounter)<br /></br>• Character, duration, and triggers of color changes<br /></br>• Lack of systemic features (skin, joint, pulmonary, renal)<br /></br>• Counseling on cold avoidance and smoking cessation<br /></br>• Plan to start nifedipine 30 mg ER daily and schedule follow-up  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Raynaud = exaggerated vasoconstriction of digital arterioles → transient ischemia (white/blue) → reactive hyperemia (red).<br /></br>Primary: idiopathic, benign; Secondary: due to connective-tissue disease (systemic sclerosis, SLE), occupational vibration, drugs, etc. CCBs decrease intracellular Ca²⁺ in vascular smooth muscle → vasodilation.</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>Lifestyle: gloves, hand warmers, avoid β-agonists/decongestants, smoking cessation.<br /></br>Pharmacologic ladder:<br /></br>1. Dihydropyridine CCBs (nifedipine, amlodipine, felodipine).<br /></br>2. Topical nitrates, phosphodiesterase-5 inhibitors.<br /></br>3. Prostacyclin analogs or endothelin-receptor antagonists for severe secondary disease.<br /></br>4. Sympathectomy or botulinum toxin only for refractory ischemic ulcers.<br /></br>Work-up for secondary causes (ANA, ESR, anticentromere, anti-Scl-70) is pursued only when red-flag findings are present (ulcers, asymmetric attacks, abnormal exam).</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Cold Fingers? Think CALcium blockers.”<br /></br>OR<br /></br>Raynaud color sequence = “White, Blue, Red – WBR = Winter Brings Red.”<br /></br>Management ladder mnemonic “Gloves → Drugs (nifedipine) → Snip (sympathectomy).”</p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. β-Adrenergic blocking agent therapy – Non-selective β-blockers (eg, propranolol) can worsen Raynaud by unopposed α-mediated vasoconstriction. Used for essential tremor, migraine prophylaxis, etc., not for Raynaud.  </p></br><p>b. ACE inhibitor therapy – Helpful for scleroderma renal crisis, hypertension, and diabetic nephropathy. They do not significantly relieve Raynaud vasospasm.  </p></br><p>d. Corticosteroid therapy – Indicated for inflammatory or autoimmune flare (eg, lupus, vasculitis). Raynaud in an otherwise healthy patient is vasospastic, not inflammatory, so steroids offer no benefit and add side-effects.  </p></br><p>e. Dorsal sympathectomy – Surgical interruption of sympathetic fibers decreases vasoconstriction but is reserved for severe, refractory cases with ischemic ulcers or gangrene after failure of maximal medical therapy. Not first-line.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In symptomatic primary Raynaud phenomenon, after simple trigger avoidance, begin a dihydropyridine calcium-channel blocker to relieve vasospasm and prevent attacks.</p>
175. A 60-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. Medical history is remarkable for hypertension and alcohol use disorder. Medications are amlodipine and omeprazole. He has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 40 years, and he drinks approximately twelve beers daily. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination discloses no abnormalities. Results of serum studies are most likely to show which of the following?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Hyperkalemia</br>b. Hypernatremia</br>c. Hypomagnesemia</br>d. Hypophosphatemia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Hypomagnesemia  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Drinks ≈12 beers daily” → long-standing, heavy alcohol use.<br /></br>• “Routine examination…vital signs WNL…no abnormalities on PE” → no acute volume loss, hyperventilation, or refeeding taking place (situations that can push other electrolytes out of range).<br /></br>• No diuretics, ACE-Is, ARBs, or kidney disease listed → makes hyperkalemia, hypernatremia less likely.<br /></br>Key pattern: Chronic alcoholism without other acute illness → think chronic nutritional deficiencies + alcohol-induced renal wasting, classically low magnesium.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify risk factor: chronic ethanol intake.<br /></br>Step 2 – Recall most frequent baseline electrolyte effect of chronic alcohol: renal Mg²⁺ wasting + poor dietary intake = hypomagnesemia.<br /></br>Step 3 – Rule out competitors: hypernatremia requires free-water loss; hyperkalemia requires renal failure, drugs, acidosis; significant hypophosphatemia is typically seen during acute alcohol withdrawal or refeeding, not steady-state drinking.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a stable chronic alcoholic, the single most predictable lab abnormality is low serum magnesium.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Serum electrolytes ordered given history of chronic ethanol use; anticipate Mg²⁺ depletion and will replace as needed.”  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Ethanol directly increases renal Mg²⁺ excretion in the loop and distal tubule.<br /></br>• Poor nutritional intake limits Mg²⁺ ingestion.<br /></br>• Resulting hypomagnesemia impairs Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, leading to refractory hypokalemia and predisposition to arrhythmias, seizures, tremors.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostics<br /></br>• Check serum Mg²⁺ in all patients with chronic alcohol use disorder.<br /></br>• Oral (magnesium oxide) or IV (magnesium sulfate) replacement; correct Mg²⁺ first if concomitant hypokalemia is present because low Mg²⁺ makes K⁺ replacement ineffective.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“MAG” mnemonic for chronic alcoholics:<br /></br>M – Magnesium low<br /></br>A – Alcohol intake chronic<br /></br>G – Gives kidneys a push to Wasting Mg²⁺  </p></br><p>Visual: Picture a beer can spilling out magnesium shavings.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hyperkalemia<br /></br>• Requires renal failure, potassium-sparing drugs, tissue lysis, or acidosis—none present. Chronic alcoholics more often have LOW K⁺ due to vomiting, diarrhea, and Mg²⁺ loss.  </p></br><p>b. Hypernatremia<br /></br>• Needs free-water deficit (diabetes insipidus, severe dehydration). Vitals/PE normal; patient likely euvolemic.  </p></br><p>d. Hypophosphatemia<br /></br>• Seen in refeeding syndrome, diabetic ketoacidosis, or during acute alcohol withdrawal (respiratory alkalosis drives phosphate into cells). In a stable outpatient still drinking heavily, phosphate is usually normal until those acute shifts occur.  </p></br><p>Key differentiator: Mg²⁺ depletion is chronic and steady; phosphate falls during acute shifts.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Chronic ethanol use → renal wasting + poor intake → hypomagnesemia is the most consistent baseline electrolyte abnormality.</p>
176. An 87-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of nausea and general malaise. She has hypertension. One month ago, her blood pressure was 160/95 mm Hg and hydrochlorothiazide therapy was begun. Her only other medication is 81-mg aspirin. She is 157 cm (5 ft 2 in) tall and weighs 50 kg (110 lb); BMI is 20 kg/m2. Today, her pulse is 78/min; her blood pressure is 155/92 mm Hg in the right upper extremity and 153/90 mm Hg in the left upper extremity. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Serum studies show:</br>Na+ 125 mEq/L</br>K+ 3.2 mEq/L</br>Cl− 87 mEq/L</br>HCO3− 25 mEq/L</br>Ca2+ 9.6 mg/dL</br>Urea nitrogen 14 mg/dL</br>Glucose 107 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 0.6 mg/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Add lisinopril to the regimen</br>b. Add losartan to the regimen</br>c. Add triamterene to the regimen</br>d. Begin potassium supplementation</br>e. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>Add lisinopril to the regimen  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Time line &amp; drug exposure<br /></br>   • Hydrochlorothiazide (HCTZ) started 1 month ago.<br /></br>   • Symptoms (nausea, malaise) began 3 weeks ago → temporally related to the new drug.  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Vital signs / BP control<br /></br>   • Still hypertensive (≈155/92 mm Hg) despite adequate HCTZ trial → needs additional BP drug.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Electrolytes<br /></br>   • Hyponatraemia 125 mEq/L (mild/moderate).<br /></br>   • Hypokalaemia 3.2 mEq/L.<br /></br>   • Normal creatinine (0.6 mg/dL) → kidneys working, ACE-I safe.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Pattern recognition<br /></br>   • “Elderly, thin woman + new thiazide + hypo-Na + hypo-K” = classic thiazide side-effect profile.  </p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why lisinopril?)<br /></br>Step-wise logic:<br /></br>a. Current single-agent therapy (thiazide) is not controlling BP ⇒ guideline-directed next step is combination therapy with a drug from a different class.<br /></br>b. ACE inhibitors (e.g., lisinopril) are first-line add-on agents because they:<br /></br>   • Produce additive BP‐lowering via RAAS blockade.<br /></br>   • Raise serum potassium, counterbalancing the thiazide’s K⁺ loss.<br /></br>   • Do not worsen—and may modestly improve—thiazide-induced hyponatraemia (they reduce ADH release through volume/RAAS effects).<br /></br>c. The patient’s renal function and potassium are within a range that makes ACE-I initiation safe (no renal insufficiency, no hyperkalaemia).  </p></br><p>Key takeaway: In an elderly patient whose hypertension remains above goal on a thiazide diuretic, the evidence-based next pharmacologic step is to add an ACE inhibitor (or ARB if ACE-I not tolerated). The electrolyte pattern caused by the thiazide actually makes ACE-I an even better partner because of its potassium-sparing effect.  </p></br><p>What you would document: “Persistent SBP &gt;150 mm Hg on HCTZ 25 mg daily → initiate lisinopril 10 mg daily; monitor BMP in 1–2 weeks for K⁺ and creatinine.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thiazides inhibit the Na⁺/Cl⁻ cotransporter in the distal convoluted tubule → ↑ Na⁺/water loss → mild volume contraction → RAAS activation → K⁺ &amp; H⁺ wasting → possible metabolic alkalosis, hypo-K, hypo-Na (especially in elderly women).<br /></br>• ACE-I block conversion of Ang I → Ang II → ↓ aldosterone → ↓ K⁺ excretion, vasodilation, ↓ afterload.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Test Pearls<br /></br>1. Therapy escalation for essential HTN<br /></br>   – Step 1: thiazide, ACE-I/ARB, CCB.<br /></br>   – Step 2: combine agents from two classes (most effective = thiazide + ACE-I/ARB).<br /></br>2. Monitoring after ACE-I start<br /></br>   – Serum creatinine &amp; potassium 1–2 weeks after initiation; acceptable creatinine rise ≤30%.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ACE-plus-thiazide = A-TEAM”<br /></br>A = ACE inhibitor<br /></br>T = Thiazide<br /></br>E = Electrolytes balance (K⁺ up, Na⁺ neutral)<br /></br>A = Add-on when Alone drug fails<br /></br>M = Monitor BMP  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Add losartan (ARB)<br /></br>• Would also be reasonable if ACE not tolerated, but lisinopril is the typical first-choice add-on. Exam expects ACE-I unless there is cough, angio-edema, or renal artery stenosis.  </p></br><p>c. Add triamterene (K-sparing diuretic)<br /></br>• Corrects hypokalaemia but provides only modest BP reduction and does NOT address persistent hypertension as effectively as RAAS blockade. Still leaves the patient on three diuretic mechanisms (volume depletion ↔ worse hyponatraemia).  </p></br><p>d. Begin potassium supplementation<br /></br>• Treats only the K⁺ number, not the underlying BP issue and does nothing for hyponatraemia. Supplements can be added later if K⁺ remains low after ACE-I initiation.  </p></br><p>e. Discontinue hydrochlorothiazide<br /></br>• Would likely improve the hyponatraemia/hypokalaemia but sacrifices a proven first-line, mortality-benefit antihypertensive that the patient is tolerating overall. Guidelines favor adding a second agent rather than abandoning the thiazide when BP is still above goal and adverse effects are mild.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line:<br /></br>Persistent hypertension on thiazide diuretic in an elderly patient → add an ACE inhibitor such as lisinopril; this provides synergistic BP control and counteracts thiazide-induced potassium loss.</p>
177. A 15-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of a 3-day history of pain with urination and an increasingly frequent and urgent need to urinate. She has had three episodes of urinary tract infection during the past year. She has no other history of serious illness and currently takes no medications. The patient appears subdued and avoids eye contact. The physician asks the mother to leave the office for the remainder of the interview, and she initially is reluctant. When she leaves the room, the physician asks the patient about her sexual history. The patient appears anxious and asks, "If I answer these questions, will you promise not to tell my mom?" Which of the following is the most appropriate physician response?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Ask the patient why she is concerned about confidentiality</br>b. Empathize with the patient's desire to keep her sexual history private</br>c. Inform the patient that her information will be shared only if her safety or that of others is at risk</br>d. Tell the patient that it is worth risking her mother's disapproval to ensure proper treatment of her condition</br>e. Reassure the patient that the need to break confidentiality about her health is unlikely</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection</br>a. Ask the patient why she is concerned about confidentiality</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 15-year-old (minor adolescent) with dysuria, urinary urgency, and a history of multiple UTIs → raises suspicion for unprotected sexual activity or possible sexual abuse<br /></br>• Appears subdued, avoids eye contact, and becomes visibly anxious when the topic of sex is raised → behavioral red flags for potential coercion or abuse<br /></br>• Mother is initially reluctant to leave the room → another possible sign of controlling or abusive dynamics<br /></br>• Patient explicitly asks, “Will you promise not to tell my mom?” → central issue is the patient’s fear about disclosure, not yet the sexual history itself  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. The chief barrier to care is the patient’s fear of breach of confidentiality.<br /></br>2. Before providing generic reassurances or launching into the legal limits of confidentiality, the physician should first explore the reason for the fear.<br /></br>   • “Can you tell me what worries you about me talking with your mom?”<br /></br>3. Understanding that concern might uncover crucial information—e.g., sexual abuse, pregnancy, or fear of retribution—that would change both the medical and legal management.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When an adolescent voices concern about confidentiality, first explore the concern. This keeps communication open, shows respect for autonomy, and may unmask abuse.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (brief)</br>Recurrent UTIs in an adolescent girl are most commonly related to sexual activity. However, sexual abuse can present similarly. Identifying abuse early prevents further physical and psychological harm.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• If evaluation reveals consensual sexual activity → screen for STIs (NAAT for chlamydia/gonorrhea, HIV, syphilis), counsel on contraception and safe sex, treat UTI with culture-guided antibiotics (e.g., nitrofurantoin, TMP-SMX).<br /></br>• If abuse is suspected → mandatory reporting to child protective services, detailed forensic exam, prophylaxis for STIs, emergency contraception if indicated, and psychosocial support.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“ASK before you ASSURE.”<br /></br>A – Assess her concern<br /></br>S – Set confidentiality limits<br /></br>K – Keep communication open  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Empathize with the patient’s desire to keep her sexual history private<br /></br>   • Empathy is good, but it does not elicit the underlying concern that could reveal abuse. It prematurely accepts the secrecy without understanding why it is needed.</p></br><p>c. Inform the patient that her information will be shared only if her safety or that of others is at risk<br /></br>   • This is an important conversation, but it should come after you first explore why she is worried. Jumping straight to the legal boiler-plate may shut her down before you grasp the core issue.</p></br><p>d. Tell the patient that it is worth risking her mother’s disapproval to ensure proper treatment<br /></br>   • Dismisses the patient’s fears and may appear coercive. It does not address the confidentiality worry and may damage rapport.</p></br><p>e. Reassure the patient that the need to break confidentiality is unlikely<br /></br>   • Gives a blanket promise that might be impossible to keep (e.g., if abuse or suicidal ideation is disclosed). Also, like option c, it sidesteps the opportunity to explore her specific fear.</p></br><p>Key Exam Pearl</br>When an adolescent hesitates to talk because of confidentiality concerns, FIRST ask why they feel that way. Understanding the reason guides both clinical care and the depth of confidentiality you can legally and ethically maintain.</p>
178. A 37-year-old man with a 2-year history of intermittent right knee pain comes to the office because of a 2-month history of increased knee pain that began after he collided with another person while skiing. The pain has particularly worsened during the past 2 days and has made bearing weight on his right leg difficult. Immediately following the collision, he felt a sharp pain in the middle of his kneecap and sustained several scrapes and cuts on his kneecap, which stopped bleeding within minutes but have continued to slowly ooze a clear discharge thereafter. Medical history otherwise is noncontributory. His only medications are a daily multivitamin and occasional acetaminophen for pain. His temperature is 38°C (100.4°F), pulse is 76/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Examination shows an erythematous right knee that is warm to touch. The patella is ballotable, and there is moderate joint effusion. Bending the right knee 10 to 15 degrees causes pain; the patient cannot tolerate bending it any further. Which of the following is most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Anterior cruciate ligament tear</br>b. Meniscal tear</br>c. Patellar tendonitis</br>d. Pyogenic arthritis</br>e. Synovial chondromatosis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Pyogenic (septic) arthritis  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point straight to an infected joint:<br /></br>• “Immediately following the collision, he … sustained several scrapes and cuts on his kneecap … have continued to slowly ooze a clear discharge” → communicating abrasion = portal of entry for bacteria.<br /></br>• “2-month history of increased knee pain … particularly worsened during the past 2 days” → subacute course that suddenly flares.<br /></br>• “Temperature 38 °C, erythematous knee, warm to touch, moderate joint effusion, ballotable patella, pain with 10–15° of flexion” → classic description of a hot, swollen, exquisitely painful joint with systemic signs.<br /></br>• Difficulty bearing weight → septic joints hurt at rest and with any movement.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no mention of “pop,” instability, locking/catching, or overuse pattern; these help rule out ligament, meniscal, or tendinous etiologies.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Any acutely painful, warm, swollen joint + fever is septic arthritis until proven otherwise.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Trauma created an abrasion over the patella → inoculation risk.  </li></br><li>Bacteria seed synovial space → intense neutrophilic inflammation → purulent effusion.  </li></br><li>Presentation: warm, red, swollen knee, severe pain with minimal ROM, fever.  </li></br><li>Only diagnosis that integrates systemic signs, joint effusion, and post-traumatic inoculation is pyogenic arthritis.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: “Hot, swollen joint + fever = septic arthritis; aspirate first, antibiotics second.”  </p></br><p>Documentation essentials (real life): Appearance, vital signs, neurovascular status, need for emergent arthrocentesis, empiric IV antibiotics, orthopedic consult.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Usually bacteria (Staph aureus most common) gain access via hematogenous spread, direct inoculation (penetrating injury), or contiguous spread.<br /></br>• Synovium lacks limiting basement membrane → organisms enter easily; neutrophils release proteases → rapid cartilage destruction within days.<br /></br>• Delay in treatment can cause permanent joint damage or osteomyelitis.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: skin breach, prosthetic joint, immunosuppression, IV drug use, diabetes. In this case, cutaneous abrasion is the culprit.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment (high-yield)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Emergent arthrocentesis BEFORE antibiotics:<br /></br>   – Synovial WBC &gt;50,000/mm³ (mostly PMNs)<br /></br>   – Gram stain &amp; culture  </li></br><li>Start empiric IV antibiotics immediately after aspiration:<br /></br>   – Vancomycin (MRSA coverage) + third-generation cephalosporin (e.g., ceftriaxone) while awaiting cultures.  </li></br><li>Possible operative irrigation &amp; debridement if purulent or if no rapid clinical response.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Remember: time is cartilage—treat within hours.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>HOT mnemonic for septic arthritis:<br /></br>H – Hot joint (erythema/warmth)<br /></br>O – Overlying wound or Organism in blood (bacteremia)<br /></br>T – Temp ≥38 °C  </p></br><p>“SEPTIC”:<br /></br>Swollen, Erythematous, Painful, Temperature, Immobile, Culture positive.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Anterior cruciate ligament tear<br /></br>   • Mechanism: sudden pivot → “pop,” immediate swelling (hemarthrosis), instability (positive Lachman).<br /></br>   • No fever or erythema; effusion appears within hours, not 2-month evolution with systemic signs.  </p></br><p>b. Meniscal tear<br /></br>   • Presents with locking, catching, joint-line tenderness, effusion that is usually small and cool, no fever.<br /></br>   • Pain often with twisting, not simple flexion to 10–15°.  </p></br><p>c. Patellar tendinitis (jumper’s knee)<br /></br>   • Overuse injury; pain at inferior patella, worse with resisted knee extension or jumping.<br /></br>   • No effusion, warmth, or systemic symptoms.  </p></br><p>e. Synovial chondromatosis<br /></br>   • Chronic, progressive formation of multiple cartilaginous bodies; painless or mildly painful swelling, mechanical symptoms.<br /></br>   • Extremely rare, no fever, and not linked to recent trauma with infection signs.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>If a joint is acutely hot, swollen, and hurts with minimal movement—especially with fever—think septic arthritis first. Aspirate promptly and start empiric IV antibiotics to prevent irreversible cartilage damage.</p>
179. A 15-year-old girl is brought to the office because of a 2-week history of a facial rash and fatigue. She has a 9-day history of stiffness and swelling of her fingers on awakening in the morning. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She appears tired and has mild chest pain with deep inspiration. Temperature is 38.0°C (100.4°F), pulse is 94/min and regular, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 112/76 mm Hg. A photograph of the patient's face is shown. There are three nontender ulcers over the soft palate. Diffuse 1- to 2-cm lymph nodes are palpated in the anterior and posterior cervical chains. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Heart sounds are normal. Examination of the extremities shows mild erythema, tenderness, and swelling of the proximal interphalangeal and metacarpophalangeal joints, knees, and metatarsophalangeal joints. Urinalysis shows 2+ protein and 1+ blood. Which of the following is most appropriate in determining management for this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Cerebral angiography</br>b. Echocardiography</br>c. MRI of the brain</br>d. Renal biopsy</br>e. X-rays of the knees</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Renal biopsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Demographics: 15-year-old girl (female adolescent = peak age group for systemic lupus erythematosus, SLE).<br /></br>• Key positives:<br /></br>  – “Facial rash” with photo (classic malar/butterfly distribution).<br /></br>  – Oral ulcers (soft-palate).<br /></br>  – Morning stiffness, symmetric small-joint swelling (inflammatory arthritis).<br /></br>  – Low-grade fever, diffuse lymphadenopathy.<br /></br>  – Pleuritic chest pain (serositis).<br /></br>  – UA: 2+ protein, 1+ blood → renal involvement.<br /></br>• Key negatives: Lungs clear, no focal neurologic deficits—so no immediate concern for CNS or pulmonary catastrophe.  </p></br><p>The combination satisfies ≥4 ACR/SLICC criteria for SLE. The new, potentially organ-threatening feature is proteinuria/hematuria, raising concern for lupus nephritis.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize SLE.<br /></br>Step 2: Decide next test that directly influences management.<br /></br>In SLE, treatment intensity hinges on the WHO/ISN class of lupus nephritis (I–VI). The ONLY test that gives that information is a percutaneous renal biopsy. Choice of therapy (high-dose IV glucocorticoids ± cyclophosphamide, mycophenolate, or rituximab) is impossible without knowing the histologic class. Therefore, renal biopsy is essential now.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Immune complex deposition (anti-dsDNA, anti-Smith) → complement activation → glomerular inflammation.<br /></br>• Morphologic classes:<br /></br>  – I-II: minimal/mesangial → mild, usually no aggressive Rx.<br /></br>  – III-IV: focal/diffuse proliferative → aggressive immunosuppression needed (IV steroids + cyclophosphamide or MMF).<br /></br>  – V: membranous → nephrotic; treat if proteinuria heavy.<br /></br>  – VI: sclerotic, ESRD → dialysis/transplant, not immunosuppression.<br /></br>Only biopsy differentiates these.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Indication for biopsy in SLE:<br /></br>  – Proteinuria ≥0.5 g/24 h (≈≥1+ on dipstick).<br /></br>  – Active urinary sediment (RBC casts, hematuria).<br /></br>  – Rising serum creatinine of unclear cause.<br /></br>• Imaging or serologies alone cannot stage lupus nephritis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Rash OR PAIN”: mnemonic for SLE criteria<br /></br>– Rash (malar), Arthritis, Serositis, Hematologic, Oral ulcers, Renal, Photosensitivity, ANA, Immunologic (anti-dsDNA/Sm), Neurologic.<br /></br>“When lupus hits the kidney, Biopsy BEFORE big-gun therapy.” (B for Biopsy, B for Big-gun).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cerebral angiography – Reserved for CNS vasculitis or subarachnoid hemorrhage; patient has no focal neurologic signs or thunderclap headache.<br /></br>b. Echocardiography – Useful for pericardial effusion or Libman-Sacks endocarditis, but these findings would not guide urgent management like proteinuric nephritis does.<br /></br>c. MRI of the brain – Ordered for seizures, psychosis, or other CNS lupus manifestations, none present here.<br /></br>e. X-rays of the knees – SLE arthritis is non-erosive, so films add little and do not affect treatment strategy.  </p></br><p>Take-home Message<br /></br>In a patient with new proteinuria/hematuria from suspected SLE, renal biopsy is mandatory to classify lupus nephritis and tailor immunosuppressive therapy.</p>
180. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of mild pain in her legs with walking and at rest. Her pain is somewhat relieved when she elevates her legs. She has a 3-year history of intermittent swelling of her legs. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She has smoked one-half pack of cigarettes daily for 18 years. She has been able to perform her normal daily activities. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is nonpitting edema of the lower extremities. A photograph of the right lower extremity is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis of the lower extremity findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Echocardiography</br>b. Lymphangiography</br>c. Skin biopsy</br>d. Angiography of the lower extremities</br>e. No further evaluation is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. No further evaluation is indicated  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>6-month history of mild leg pain “with walking and at rest.”  </li></br><li>Pain is “somewhat relieved when she elevates her legs.”<br /></br>   • Venous congestion is relieved by gravity drainage → classic for chronic venous insufficiency (CVI).<br /></br>   • Arterial ischemic pain is made worse, not better, by elevation.  </li></br><li>3-year history of intermittent leg swelling.  </li></br><li>Examination: non-pitting edema, characteristic skin changes on the photograph (hyperpigmentation, stasis dermatitis, lipodermatosclerosis).  </li></br><li>No cardiopulmonary findings, normal activity tolerance → makes CHF unlikely.  </li></br><li>No history of surgery, radiation, lymph-node dissection or filariasis → primary/secondary lymphedema less likely.<br /></br>Together these findings clinch the diagnosis of chronic venous insufficiency, which is made by history and physical exam alone.  </li></br></ol></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><p>Step 1: Is this arterial, venous, lymphatic, or systemic?<br /></br>• Pain better with elevation + long-standing edema + stasis skin changes → venous.<br /></br>• Normal pulses, normal cardiac exam → not arterial disease, not CHF.  </p></br><p>Step 2: Does CVI require further diagnostic testing to be “diagnosed”?<br /></br>• No. Duplex ultrasound is only needed if an intervention (e.g., vein ablation, grafting) is being planned, not to make the diagnosis.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway:  Chronic venous insufficiency is a clinical diagnosis; once typical features are present, further diagnostic work-up is unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record distribution of edema, skin pigment changes, ulcerations, pedal pulses, and response to elevation.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Incompetent deep or perforating venous valves → ↑ venous hydrostatic pressure → extravasation of fluid &amp; RBCs → hemosiderin deposition (brown discoloration) + edema.<br /></br>• Long-standing edema → fibrosis of dermis/subcutis → non-pitting edema (“brawny” induration).  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Management Snapshot (for your memory)  </p></br><p>First-line therapy once diagnosed:<br /></br>• Graduated compression stockings<br /></br>• Frequent leg elevation, ambulation, weight control<br /></br>• Topical steroids for stasis dermatitis, meticulous skin care<br /></br>• Consider duplex ultrasound only if ulcers refractory or surgical/ endovenous therapy contemplated.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Veins Up, Arteries Down”<br /></br>• Venous pain feels better when the legs go UP (elevated).<br /></br>• Arterial pain feels better when legs go DOWN (dangling off the bed).  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Echocardiography – Used to evaluate heart failure–related dependent pitting edema, usually with dyspnea, crackles, JVD. None present here.  </p></br><p>b. Lymphangiography – Reserved for unclear cases of lymphedema (non-pitting, +Stemmer sign, prior oncologic surgery). History/exam doesn’t suggest primary or secondary lymphedema.  </p></br><p>c. Skin biopsy – Indicated if vasculitis, infection, or malignancy is suspected; the photograph/description matches classic stasis dermatitis, so biopsy is unnecessary.  </p></br><p>d. Angiography of the lower extremities – Tests for peripheral arterial disease (PAD) when claudication/rest pain, diminished pulses, or ischemic ulcers exist. Pain in PAD worsens with elevation; no such findings here.  </p></br><p>e. No further evaluation is indicated – Correct. Clinical presentation alone suffices for the diagnosis of chronic venous insufficiency.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line:  Typical venous stasis changes + leg pain relieved by elevation = chronic venous insufficiency, a clinical diagnosis. Do not order additional tests unless planning an intervention—simply move on to conservative management.</p>
181. Ten days after undergoing left above-the-knee amputation for severe diabetic soft-tissue infection, a 78-year-old man in the intensive care unit has noisy, labored breathing.  </br>His postoperative course has been complicated by sepsis, delirium, and respiratory insufficiency.  </br>His temperature is 37.5°C (99.5°F), pulse is 72/min, respirations are 9/min, and blood pressure is 100/50 mm Hg.  </br>Pulse oximetry on 3 L of oxygen by nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 92%.  </br>He is not oriented to person, place, or time.  </br>Breath sounds are coarse and distant bilaterally.  </br>The extremities are cool to the touch and mottled.  </br>After discussions with the patient's family, and consistent with the patient's wishes, the family has chosen to provide comfort care.  </br>The family remains concerned about the patient's current loud breathing and secretions.  </br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Administration of humidified oxygen  </br>b. Administration of morphine  </br>c. Administration of scopolamine  </br>d. Intubation and mechanical ventilation  </br>e. Placement of a nasogastric tube</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Administration of scopolamine (anticholinergic to dry terminal secretions)</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “noisy, labored breathing,” “secretions,” “after discussions … comfort care,” “family concerned about loud breathing.”<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: terminal, delirious, poor respiratory effort (RR 9/min), coarse/distant breath sounds, cool mottled extremities → actively dying patient.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: O₂ sat 92 % on 3 L (so hypoxemia is not driving sx), no mention of pain or air hunger suggesting need for opioid titration.<br /></br>Pattern: “Death rattle” – gurgling respirations caused by pooled oropharyngeal secretions in a patient who is too weak to clear them.</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thinking<br /></br>1. Goal of care = comfort; invasive measures (intubation, NG tube) are inconsistent with this goal.<br /></br>2. Symptom bothering family = audible rattling from secretions, not hypoxemia or pain.<br /></br>3. Best palliative intervention = decrease secretion production with an anticholinergic (scopolamine patch, glycopyrrolate, or atropine SL drops).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Terminal “death rattle” is managed by drying secretions, most commonly with scopolamine.</p></br><p>Documentation: “Patient with terminal secretions → initiate transdermal scopolamine 1.5 mg patch Q72 h; goal symptom relief per comfort-focused plan.”</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• As death approaches, loss of swallowing/cough reflex allows saliva and tracheobronchial secretions to pool above the vocal cords.<br /></br>• Turbulent airflow over these secretions = characteristic rattling.<br /></br>• Anticholinergics block muscarinic receptors → ↓ parasympathetic stimulation of salivary &amp; bronchial glands → drier airway, quieter respirations.</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line for death rattle:<br /></br>• Scopolamine transdermal patch (1.5 mg behind ear; onset 4–6 h)<br /></br>• Alternatives: glycopyrrolate IV/SC, atropine 1% SL eye-drop solution placed orally.<br /></br>No labs or imaging needed; decision entirely clinical in end-of-life setting.</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Silent SCOPe at the end”: SCOPolamine Silences secretions at end-of-life.<br /></br>Visualize crossing out the “rattle” with a pirate’s scope (scopolamine).</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Humidified oxygen<br /></br>   – Helps dryness or mild dyspnea, NOT secretion pooling; moisture can actually worsen rattling.<br /></br>b. Morphine<br /></br>   – Excellent for pain or air-hunger; does nothing to dry secretions. Would not address family’s specific concern.<br /></br>d. Intubation &amp; mechanical ventilation<br /></br>   – Highly invasive, contradicts comfort-care directive, and unnecessary for secretion noise.<br /></br>e. Nasogastric tube<br /></br>   – Invasive, uncomfortable, risk aspiration; suctioning or gastric decompression will not clear oropharyngeal secretions causing death rattle.</p></br><p>──────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a dying patient with loud rattling respirations due to pooled oropharyngeal secretions, the palliative, comfort-focused intervention is an anticholinergic such as scopolamine to dry secretions and relieve the symptom for both patient and family.</p>
182. A previously healthy 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his mother on a winter evening because of the sudden onset of coughing and mild difficulty breathing. His symptoms began late this afternoon when he was playing with other children at a party. His brother has asthma. The patient's temperature is 37.2°C (99°F), pulse is 92/min, and respirations are 36/min. Physical examination shows intense intermittent coughing and mild intercostal retractions. Wheezes are heard bilaterally but are greater on the right. An x-ray of the chest is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Congenital lobar emphysema</br>c. Foreign body aspiration</br>d. Laryngotracheitis</br>e. Pneumonia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Asthma</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – how the clues point to asthma</br>• “Previously healthy 4-year-old” + “brother has asthma” – strong atopic / genetic predisposition.<br /></br>• Trigger: symptoms began while “playing … on a winter evening.” Exercise, excitement and cold, dry air are classic precipitants of an acute asthma flare.<br /></br>• Sudden cough, tachypnea (RR 36) and “mild intercostal retractions” – typical presentation of a mild-to-moderate asthma exacerbation.<br /></br>• Auscultation: “wheezes … bilaterally but greater on the right.”  Asthma produces diffuse expiratory wheezes; intensity often varies from side to side depending on airflow but remains present in both lungs.<br /></br>• Afebrile (T = 37.2 °C) – argues strongly against an infectious cause such as pneumonia or croup.<br /></br>• The chest radiograph (image not shown) on NBME typically demonstrates 1) symmetric hyperinflation, 2) flattened diaphragms, and 3) increased bronchovascular markings – the classic but nonspecific CXR for asthma.   </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise logic:</br>1. Acute lower airway obstruction → expiratory wheeze.<br /></br>2. Bilateral involvement without focal signs → diffuse reactive small-airway disease, not an anatomic blockage.<br /></br>3. Lack of fever or upper-airway noise → not infectious croup or pneumonia.<br /></br>4. Age and family history fit the epidemiology.<br /></br>Core takeaway:  In a preschooler with bilateral wheezing, no fever, episodic symptoms precipitated by activity/cold air, and a suggestive family history, asthma is the most likely diagnosis.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review</br>Asthma = chronic, reversible inflammation of the small airways with episodic bronchospasm.</br>• Etiology: atopy &gt;80 %; viral infections, exercise, cold air and pollutants are common triggers.<br /></br>• Mechanism: IgE-mediated mast-cell activation → leukotrienes, histamine → smooth-muscle constriction + mucosal edema → airflow obstruction predominantly on expiration (→ wheeze, hyperinflation on CXR).<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: cough (often nocturnal), wheeze, dyspnea, chest tightness; intercostal retractions in children.</p></br><p>Management pearls (for real-life encounter)</br>Document: trigger, symptom severity, work of breathing, auscultatory findings, prior episodes, family history.<br /></br>Treatment in ED: short-acting β2-agonist (albuterol) via nebulizer or MDI + spacer; add systemic corticosteroid (e.g., oral prednisolone) if moderate/severe.  Oxygen if SpO₂ &lt; 92 %. Reassess response.</p></br><p>Memorization tip</br>“3 B’s” for asthma exacerbation: Bilateral expiratory wheezes, Bronchodilator response, Brother (family history) of asthma.</p></br><p>Why the other choices are wrong</br>b. Congenital lobar emphysema<br /></br>• Presents in neonates/young infants with persistent—not episodic—respiratory distress.  CXR shows hyperinflation of a single lobe with mediastinal shift, not symmetric bilateral findings.  </p></br><p>c. Foreign body aspiration<br /></br>• Classic triad: sudden cough, unilateral absent (or markedly reduced) breath sounds, unilateral hyperlucency on CXR (air-trapping).  Bilateral wheezes and family history point away; vignette lacks a choking episode or focal exam.  </p></br><p>d. Laryngotracheitis (croup)<br /></br>• Viral illness with barking cough, inspiratory stridor, hoarseness, low-grade fever; CXR may show “steeple sign.”  No stridor or barking cough here, and wheeze is uncommon in croup.  </p></br><p>e. Pneumonia<br /></br>• Fever, crackles/rales, focal consolidation on exam and infiltrate on CXR are typical.  Afebrile child with bilateral wheeze and hyperinflation makes pneumonia unlikely.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: In a preschooler with bilateral expiratory wheezes, no fever, and a positive family history, think asthma first.</p>
183. A 62-year-old man comes to the office with his wife for a follow-up examination 4 weeks after he was hospitalized for management of an acute myocardial infarction that required cardiac catheterization. He has had a 1.4-kg (3-lb) weight gain since discharge. He has not had shortness of breath or chest pain on exertion. He has been sleeping well. His medications are losartan and atorvastatin; he is intermittently adherent to his regimen. He has had irregular attendance at an outpatient cardiac rehabilitation program; he says he sometimes skips sessions because he is tired. His wife says he is irritable and often talks of his father who died of a heart attack at the age of 65 years. The couple has not resumed sexual activity because of the patient’s fear of having another heart attack. The patient enjoys seeing his grandchildren but is no longer interested in playing poker with his friends. He is 168 cm (5 ft 6 in) tall and weighs 85 kg (187 lb); BMI is 30 kg/m2. His pulse is 90/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 130/84 mm Hg. Physical examination shows no pedal edema. On mental status examination, he describes his mood as “blah” and has a constricted affect. He reports no suicidal ideation or suicide plan. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Adjustment disorder</br>b. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>c. Hypoactive sexual desire disorder</br>d. Major depressive disorder</br>e. Post-traumatic stress disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Adjustment disorder  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues pointing to a stress-related, sub-threshold mood syndrome:<br /></br>• Identifiable stressor: acute myocardial infarction (MI) 4 weeks ago.<br /></br>• Temporal relationship: emotional/behavioral symptoms began after the MI and are clearly within 3 months of the event.<br /></br>• Symptoms: irritability, “blah” mood, mild anhedonia (no longer plays poker), intermittent fatigue, avoidance of sex due to fear; NO frank insomnia, hopelessness, worthlessness, psychomotor change, or suicidal ideation.<br /></br>• Functional impact: erratic medication adherence, missing rehab sessions.<br /></br>• Duration &amp; severity: present only a few weeks, cause distress/impairment, but do not meet full Major Depressive Disorder (MDD) criteria.</p></br><p>These elements fit DSM-5 criteria for Adjustment Disorder: emotional or behavioral response to a clear stressor developing within 3 months, out of proportion to the stressor, causing impairment, and not meeting criteria for another major disorder.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Identify the stressor → recent MI.  </li></br><li>Ask: “Are the symptoms better explained by MDD, an anxiety disorder, PTSD, etc.?”<br /></br>   – Needs ≥5 SIGECAPS symptoms for ≥2 weeks for MDD. He has only 2 (low mood, anhedonia).<br /></br>   – PTSD requires intrusive memories/nightmares, avoidance, hyper-arousal ≥1 month; none present.<br /></br>   – GAD requires ≥6 months of excessive worry about multiple domains; symptoms &lt;1 month.<br /></br>   – Hypoactive sexual desire disorder requires absent sexual thoughts for ≥6 months without another explanation; here fear of reinfarction explains avoidance.  </li></br><li>Because symptoms are stress-linked, sub-threshold, and short-lived → Adjustment Disorder.</li></br></ol></br><p>Take-home: Any new-onset emotional disturbance within 3 months of a medical event or life change that does NOT meet criteria for a more specific psychiatric diagnosis = Adjustment Disorder.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real patient): nature of stressor, onset of symptoms, functional impairment, absence of full MDD/PTSD criteria, plan for brief psychotherapy ± SSRI.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Adjustment disorder is not about a biologic neurotransmitter deficit per se; it is an abnormal, maladaptive response to stress.<br /></br>• Prevalence high after cardiac events—up to 20 % show adjustment-type depressive/anxious symptoms.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics  </p></br><p>First-line: brief, problem-focused psychotherapy (e.g., CBT, supportive therapy) targeting coping with MI, fear of exertion/sexual activity, medication adherence.<br /></br>Pharmacotherapy (e.g., SSRIs) reserved if symptoms escalate to full MDD or persist &gt;6 months.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“ADjustment = After a Definable stressor, Doesn’t meet full criteria, Duration &lt;6 months once stressor ends.”<br /></br>Visual: picture a calendar with a big red 3-month arc starting at the MI.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Generalized anxiety disorder – Requires ≥6 months of excessive worry about multiple issues and ≥3 physical symptoms (restlessness, muscle tension, etc.). Symptoms here are &lt;1 month and focused on MI sequelae, not “generalized.”  </p></br><p>c. Hypoactive sexual desire disorder – Must last ≥6 months and reflect low libido, not fear-based avoidance. His sexual interest is inhibited by fear of reinfarction, not true loss of desire.  </p></br><p>d. Major depressive disorder – Needs ≥5 of 9 SIGECAPS symptoms for ≥2 weeks. He has only low mood and anhedonia; no insomnia (sleeping well), no significant appetite loss, no psychomotor change, no guilt, no poor concentration, no suicidality. Sub-threshold → adjustment disorder with depressed mood instead.  </p></br><p>e. Post-traumatic stress disorder – Requires life-threatening trauma PLUS intrusive memories/nightmares, avoidance, negative mood/cognition changes, hyperarousal for &gt;1 month. He has none of the hallmark intrusive/hyperarousal symptoms.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>Sub-threshold depressive/anxious symptoms that begin soon after a medical stressor and do not satisfy criteria for MDD, GAD, or PTSD are classic for Adjustment Disorder.</p>
184. A 17-year-old primigravid patient at 22 weeks' gestation comes to the physician because of a 12-hour history of fever, nausea, and vomiting. She has abdominal tenderness on the right side when she coughs. Her temperature is 38.3°C (101°F). Pelvic and rectal examinations show right-sided tenderness. Fetal heart tones are normal. Her leukocyte count is 18,000/mm3 with a shift to the left. Her serum amylase activity is within the reference range. During the next 8 hours, vomiting, fever, and abdominal pain continue. Ultrasonography of the gallbladder shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Barium enema</br>b. Gallium scan</br>c. Laparoscopy</br>d. Peritoneal lavage</br>e. Amniocentesis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Barium enema</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 22-week pregnancy (uterus already above the umbilicus) → appendix is displaced upward and laterally; pain may be high on the right side, not the classic McBurney point.<br /></br>• 12-h history of fever, N/V, leukocytosis with left shift → acute intra-abdominal infection.<br /></br>• Right-sided abdominal tenderness on cough (“cough test”) → peritoneal irritation.<br /></br>• Normal amylase and normal gallbladder ultrasound → pancreatitis and acute cholecystitis essentially ruled out.<br /></br>Key pattern: Pregnant patient with persistent fever, leukocytosis, and focal right-sided peritoneal signs ⇒ think appendicitis in pregnancy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Most likely diagnosis = acute appendicitis.<br /></br>Step 2: Need confirmatory imaging before subjecting a pregnant patient to surgery.<br /></br>Step 3: Options provided: of those, the historically accepted, low-radiation study that can demonstrate a non-filling/distorted appendix is a limited-field barium (water-soluble contrast) enema.<br /></br>Take-home: In NBME/older Step questions, “barium (or water-soluble) enema showing non-filling of the appendix” is the diagnostic test of choice for suspected appendicitis in the 2nd trimester when ultrasound is non-diagnostic.</p></br><p>If the enema fails to fill the appendix or shows extrinsic compression, surgery is warranted and must be documented in the record.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Appendiceal lumen obstruction → bacterial overgrowth → mucosal ischemia → transmural inflammation → peritonitis.<br /></br>• Pregnancy: enlarging uterus lifts appendix upward; pain localizes to RUQ/R flank, so diagnosis is frequently delayed, increasing perforation risk (≥30% by 3rd trimester).<br /></br>Risk to fetus derives mainly from perforation/peritonitis, not from surgery itself; therefore, timely diagnosis is critical.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Initial: graded-compression right-lower-quadrant ultrasound (already indirectly negative here).<br /></br>2. Next: Limited barium (or water-soluble contrast) enema—radiation ≈0.5 rad, well below teratogenic threshold (&lt;5–10 rads). Positive finding = non-visualization/filling defect at cecal base.<br /></br>3. If still equivocal (modern practice): MRI without gadolinium.<br /></br>Treatment<br /></br>• Prompt appendectomy (open or laparoscopic) plus perioperative IV antibiotics (e.g., piperacillin-tazobactam) once diagnosis is secured.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pregnancy pushes the appendix up—get a contrast cup.”<br /></br>• Pregnancy + persistent right-sided pain → think appendix.<br /></br>• “Contrast cup” = contrast enema as the confirmatory test NBME loves.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Gallium scan – Nuclear study; takes 24–48 h to accumulate, delivers significantly more radiation, impractical in an acute abdomen.<br /></br>c. Laparoscopy – Definitive but invasive and requires general anesthesia; imaging confirmation should precede operative intervention when a safer, low-radiation study exists.<br /></br>d. Peritoneal lavage – Used in blunt abdominal trauma to detect intraperitoneal bleeding; not useful for focal infection.<br /></br>e. Amniocentesis – Evaluates fetal karyotype, lung maturity, or infection (e.g., TORCH), not maternal abdominal pain.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>In a second-trimester pregnancy with suspected appendicitis after a nondiagnostic ultrasound, the NBME “best next step” is a limited barium (water-soluble) contrast enema—quick, low radiation, and diagnostic.</p>
185. An 18-year-old man comes to the clinic because of a 3-hour history of right eye pain with mild light sensitivity and blurry vision that have been present since he awakened this morning. He has had no symptoms in his left eye. He reports being struck in the face by a soccer ball last night but having no symptoms at that time. Medical history is remarkable for admission to the hospital 2 years ago for acute chest syndrome secondary to sickle cell disease; he has taken hydroxyurea since that time. He takes no other medications and does not wear corrective lenses. Family history is remarkable for glaucoma in his maternal grandfather. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the right eye shows blood filling the lower 20% of the anterior chamber. Pupils are equal and reactive to light and accommodation bilaterally, and visual fields are intact. Visual acuity is 20/60 in the right eye and 20/20 in the left eye. Intraocular pressure is 27 mm Hg in the right eye and 18 mm Hg in the left eye (N=12–22). Which of the following factors in this patient most strongly increases his risk for complications of his current condition?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Age</br>b. Family history</br>c. Hydroxyurea use</br>d. Sickle cell disease</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Sickle cell disease  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>Key findings<br /></br>   • Blunt ocular trauma (soccer ball).<br /></br>   • Blood layering in anterior chamber = hyphema (20% height).<br /></br>   • Mildly elevated intra-ocular pressure (IOP 27 mm Hg).<br /></br>   • Photophobia, blurred vision.  </li></br><li>Pertinent negatives/positives<br /></br>   • Pupil reactive, no afferent pupillary defect → optic nerve still intact.<br /></br>   • IOP already above normal (N = 12-22), raising concern for ischemic optic nerve damage or corneal blood staining if it rises further.  </li></br><li>Question stem<br /></br>   “Which factor most strongly increases risk for complications of his current condition?” → ask for a known risk modifier for hyphema-related morbidity.</li></br></ol></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Traumatic hyphema complications<br /></br>• Early: re-bleeding (esp. day 3-5), sudden IOP spike, optic nerve ischemia.<br /></br>• Late: peripheral anterior synechiae, secondary glaucoma, corneal blood staining, vision loss.  </p></br><p>The most potent patient-specific risk factor is sickle cell disease/trait. Sickled erythrocytes obstruct aqueous outflow in the trabecular meshwork, causing higher and more rapid IOP increases and poorer ocular perfusion → optic nerve damage at lower IOP thresholds compared with the general population. Therefore even a “mild” hyphema is managed much more aggressively in patients with Hb S.  </p></br><p>Take-home: Always screen for sickle cell trait/disease in any patient (especially young, Black) with hyphema; they need closer follow-up, lower IOP targets, and often earlier medical or surgical intervention.</p></br><p>What would be documented<br /></br>• Presence of hyphema; % chamber filled.<br /></br>• IOP reading and re-check plan.<br /></br>• Sickle cell status and need for ophthalmology referral within 24 h (urgent).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Hyphema = blood from torn iris/ciliary body vessels collects in anterior chamber after blunt trauma → RBCs circulate through trabecular meshwork into Schlemm canal.<br /></br>• In sickle cell disease/trait, de-oxygenation and acidic aqueous humor promote sickling → rigid RBCs clog outflow → IOP spikes.<br /></br>• Elevated IOP plus vaso-occlusion compromises optic nerve head perfusion, causing optic neuropathy at lower pressures than usual.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><p>Initial management for any hyphema<br /></br>1. Shield eye, limit activity (bed rest, head elevation 30°).<br /></br>2. Measure IOP periodically.<br /></br>3. Ophthalmology consult same day.<br /></br>4. Topical glucocorticoid + cycloplegic to ↓ inflammation &amp; pain.<br /></br>5. Avoid NSAIDs/aspirin (increase re-bleed risk).  </p></br><p>Special for sickle cell patients<br /></br>• Target IOP &lt; 24 mm Hg (lower than usual).<br /></br>• Prompt topical &amp; systemic IOP-lowering meds: timolol ± acetazolamide (but avoid carbonic anhydrase inhibitors if acidosis feared).<br /></br>• Consider exchange transfusion if uncontrolled.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Hyphema SICKS more in SICKle”:<br /></br>S – Sickle cell → Severe complications<br /></br>I – Increased IOP at lower pressures<br /></br>C – Close follow-up<br /></br>K – Keep pressure &lt;24<br /></br>S – Shield &amp; Sick-day rest  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Age<br /></br>• Children &lt;5 yrs have slightly higher amblyopia risk, adults higher re-bleed risk, but neither equals the dramatic IOP/ischemia risk conferred by Hb S.  </p></br><p>b. Family history (glaucoma)<br /></br>• Baseline glaucoma risk ≠ acute complication risk of traumatic hyphema; glaucoma generally develops over years, not acutely post-trauma.  </p></br><p>c. Hydroxyurea use<br /></br>• Hydroxyurea actually reduces sickling crises; it does not worsen hyphema outcomes.  </p></br><p>Only sickle cell disease directly amplifies each major hyphema complication (IOP spike, optic nerve ischemia, re-bleed), therefore it is the strongest predictor of adverse outcome.</p>
186. A previously healthy 16-year-old boy comes to the physician because of constant right-sided scrotal pain for 36 hours. He had sexual intercourse with a new partner 1 week ago. His temperature is 38°C (100.4°F). Abdominal examination shows no abnormalities. There is a scant penile discharge. The right scrotum is swollen and tender, especially posteriorly. The pain is less severe when the scrotum is elevated. Urinalysis and Gram stain of the urethral discharge show leukocytes. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Semen analysis</br>b. Transillumination of the scrotum</br>c. Doppler ultrasonography</br>d. CT scan</br>e. Antibiotic therapy</br>f. Operative procedure</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Antibiotic therapy</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to acute epididymitis rather than another scrotal emergency:</p></br><p>• 16-year-old sexually active male with a new partner → very suggestive of Chlamydia trachomatis or Neisseria gonorrhoeae infection.<br /></br>• Gradual-onset, constant pain for 36 h (torsion classically presents with sudden, severe pain).<br /></br>• Fever 38 °C and leukocytosis in urethral discharge/urinalysis → inflammatory/infectious picture.<br /></br>• Scrotal tenderness maximal posteriorly (where the epididymis sits).<br /></br>• Positive Prehn sign: pain improves when the scrotum is elevated—typical for epididymitis; pain is unchanged or worsens in testicular torsion.<br /></br>• Scant urethral discharge with WBCs on Gram stain → urethritis accompanying epididymitis.</p></br><p>Because the presentation is classic and there are objective inflammatory findings, the next step is empiric antibiotic therapy; additional imaging is unnecessary unless the diagnosis is unclear.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Identify location of pain (posterior testis) → epididymis.  </li></br><li>Determine tempo (subacute) and infectious signs (fever, discharge, WBCs) → infection rather than ischemia.  </li></br><li>Apply Prehn sign → relief = epididymitis, not torsion.  </li></br><li>Management priorities: treat infection promptly to prevent abscess/infertility; imaging only if torsion can’t be excluded.<br /></br>Core Take-away: A typical epididymitis picture in a sexually active man → give empiric antibiotics.</li></br></ol></br><p>Documentation pearl: note onset/duration, sexual history, Prehn sign, urethral findings, and that torsion was clinically ruled out.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Acute epididymitis &lt;35 yr: usually due to C. trachomatis or N. gonorrhoeae ascending via the vas deferens.<br /></br>• Epididymal inflammation → edema and pain, most intense posteriorly.<br /></br>• Reflex cremasteric function preserved (unlike torsion).<br /></br>• Positive Prehn sign due to reduced venous congestion when elevated.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>First-line empiric regimen (CDC/IDSA):<br /></br>• Single dose ceftriaxone 500 mg IM (covers N. gonorrhoeae) PLUS<br /></br>• Doxycycline 100 mg PO bid × 14 days (covers Chlamydia).<br /></br>Advise 1-week abstinence, test/treat partners, and STI counseling.</p></br><p>Imaging (color Doppler US) is reserved when torsion cannot be definitively excluded—e.g., sudden severe pain, absent cremasteric reflex, negative Prehn sign, or no infectious signs.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“PE relief = Positive Prehn = Epididymitis” (PE-E).<br /></br>&lt;35 y: think “COG” → Chlamydia Or Gonorrhea. Treat with “CD” → Ceftriaxone + Doxycycline.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Semen analysis – Used for infertility work-ups, not initial management of an acute painful scrotum.<br /></br>b. Transillumination – Detects hydrocele/spermatocele; does not guide treatment of infectious epididymitis.<br /></br>c. Doppler ultrasonography – Valuable if torsion can’t be ruled out; here, classic findings make imaging unnecessary and would delay therapy.<br /></br>d. CT scan – Not helpful for superficial scrotal pathology; excessive radiation.<br /></br>f. Operative procedure – Emergent surgery is for testicular torsion (&gt;6 h onset) or abscess; no evidence here.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line: In a sexually active adolescent with gradual posterior testicular pain, fever, urethritis, WBCs, and pain relief on elevation, treat presumptive epididymitis immediately with ceftriaxone plus doxycycline.</p>
187. A 52-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe pain and swelling of her right knee. She now is unable to walk because of the symptoms. Five years ago, she fell and hit the knee on the bathroom floor. She has hypertension treated with lisinopril. Her temperature is 36.9°C (98.4°F), pulse is 84/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 132/76 mm Hg. The right knee is erythematous, swollen, and warm. There is diffuse tenderness to light touch; passive and active range of motion is limited by pain. There is no crepitus, but ballottement shows moderate knee effusion. Arthrocentesis is performed, and joint fluid analysis shows a leukocyte count of 50,000/mm3. A Gram stain is negative; rhomboid-shaped crystals are present. An x-ray of the knee is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Febuxostat</br>b. Ibuprofen</br>c. Infliximab</br>d. Methotrexate</br>e. Penicillamine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. Ibuprofen  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Acute monoarticular arthritis: sudden, severely painful, swollen, erythematous knee that limits active and passive ROM.<br /></br>• Synovial WBC ≈ 50,000/mm³: inflammatory but not necessarily septic (often &gt;50–100 k in infection; Gram stain negative).<br /></br>• Rhomboid-shaped crystals: classic for calcium-pyrophosphate deposition disease (CPPD, “pseudogout”).<br /></br>• X-ray (not shown here): exam-writers usually show chondrocalcinosis—linear calcification of articular cartilage—another CPPD clue.<br /></br>Key pertinent negatives: afebrile, Gram stain negative, no crepitus (rules against OA), no tophi, no needle-shaped crystals.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Recognize CPPD from rhomboid crystals ± chondrocalcinosis.<br /></br>Step 2: Decide phase of disease. This is an ACUTE flare.<br /></br>Step 3: Know treatment hierarchy for an acute CPPD attack:<br /></br>• 1st line = NSAID (indomethacin, naproxen, ibuprofen, etc.)<br /></br>• 2nd line = intra-articular or systemic glucocorticoids<br /></br>• Colchicine more useful for prophylaxis or if NSAIDs contraindicated.  </p></br><p>Therefore the most appropriate pharmacotherapy in this setting is an NSAID such as ibuprofen.  </p></br><p>“Take-home”: For any crystalline arthritis flare (gout or pseudogout) think NSAID first, unless contraindications push you to steroids or colchicine.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note joint appearance, ROM limitation, synovial analysis (WBC, crystals, Gram stain negative), and plan for NSAID therapy with GI protection counseling.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (CPPD)<br /></br>• Etiology: Deposition of calcium pyrophosphate dihydrate crystals in cartilage (often idiopathic; risk ↑ with age, prior joint trauma, hemochromatosis, hyperparathyroidism).<br /></br>• Crystal property: Rhomboid, weakly POSITIVELY birefringent (memory: “P”seudogout = “P”ositive, “Pyrophosphate,” “Polygonal”).<br /></br>• Triggers: Trauma, surgery, illness.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Acute attack:<br /></br>1. NSAID (e.g., ibuprofen 600–800 mg q6–8 h with food ± PPI)<br /></br>2. If NSAID contraindicated: intra-articular triamcinolone or oral prednisone taper.<br /></br>3. Colchicine if above not possible.  </p></br><p>Chronic/prophylaxis: low-dose colchicine; there is NO role for urate-lowering drugs (allopurinol, febuxostat).  </p></br><p>Additional work-up only if secondary causes suspected (serum calcium, ferritin, PTH, TSH).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pseudo-3-Ps”: Positive birefringence, Pyrophosphate crystals, Pain treated with NSAids.<br /></br>“Gout gets UA (urate agents); Pseudogout gets Ibuprofen.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>A. Febuxostat – Xanthine-oxidase inhibitor used for CHRONIC gout (urate-lowering). Uric acid crystals are needle-shaped and negatively birefringent; not present here. No role in CPPD.<br /></br>C. Infliximab – Anti-TNF monoclonal antibody for severe rheumatoid arthritis, psoriatic arthritis, ankylosing spondylitis, IBD—not for crystalline arthritides.<br /></br>D. Methotrexate – First-line DMARD for chronic, symmetric polyarthritis of rheumatoid arthritis. Ineffective for acute monoarticular CPPD.<br /></br>E. Penicillamine – Copper-chelating agent for Wilson disease; historically for severe rheumatoid arthritis but rarely used now. Has no benefit in CPPD and carries significant toxicity.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing gout (needle crystals, tophi, urate-lowering therapy) with pseudogout (rhomboid crystals, NSAID first). Keep the crystal shape and color (birefringence) straight!</p>
188. A 28-year-old woman comes to the office for a follow-up examination 2 weeks after undergoing a total thyroidectomy because of a thyroid nodule. Immediately after the operation, the patient had the onset of marked hypocalcemia and was admitted to the intensive care unit. During her hospital stay, intravenous calcium and calcitriol were administered. Today, she has had recurrent numbness around her mouth and muscle cramping. Current medications are calcium carbonate three times daily with meals and calcitriol twice daily. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows spasm of the ipsilateral facial muscles when tapping 2 cm anterior to the ears on the zygomatic arch bilaterally. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies are most likely to show an increase in which of the following in this patient?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Serum calcium concentration</br>b. Serum magnesium concentration</br>c. Serum parathyroid hormone concentration</br>d. Serum parathyroid hormone-related protein concentration</br>e. Urinary 24-hour calcium excretion</br>f. Urine potassium excretion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Urinary 24-hour calcium excretion</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• “Total thyroidectomy” → parathyroid glands may have been removed/damaged → permanent hypoparathyroidism.<br /></br>• “Marked hypocalcemia … IV calcium and calcitriol” → confirms postoperative hypoparathyroidism.<br /></br>• “Recurrent numbness around her mouth, muscle cramping, facial spasm with tapping (Chvostek sign)” → persistent hypocalcemia.<br /></br>• Current outpatient regimen: calcium carbonate (oral calcium) + calcitriol (active vitamin D).<br /></br>These findings tell us the patient lacks endogenous parathyroid hormone (PTH) and is being maintained purely on enteral calcium and calcitriol.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology chain:<br /></br>1. No/low PTH → kidneys lose the PTH-dependent calcium reabsorption in the distal convoluted tubule.<br /></br>2. Calcitriol markedly increases GI calcium absorption → higher filtered load of calcium at the glomerulus.<br /></br>3. Because PTH is absent, the kidney cannot reclaim much of this filtered calcium → calcium spills into urine → hypercalciuria.  </p></br><p>Core “take-home”: In chronic hypoparathyroidism treated with calcium + vitamin D, serum calcium is kept in the low-normal range at the price of increased urinary calcium, predisposing to nephrolithiasis and nephrocalcinosis. Therefore the lab most likely to be elevated is the 24-hour urine calcium.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: record symptoms of hypocalcemia, medication doses, most recent serum/urine calcium, renal function, and counsel on kidney-stone risk.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• PTH normally: ↑ renal distal tubule Ca²⁺ reabsorption, ↓ phosphate reabsorption, stimulates 1-α-hydroxylase → ↑ calcitriol.<br /></br>• Post-thyroidectomy hypoparathyroidism → ↓ PTH, ↓ serum Ca²⁺, ↑ serum phosphate.<br /></br>• Exogenous calcitriol bypasses the lost 1-α-hydroxylase step, powerfully boosting intestinal Ca²⁺ uptake.<br /></br>• Without PTH, the kidney cannot appropriately conserve Ca²⁺, so the additional absorbed calcium is wasted in urine (hypercalciuria).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><p>Management goals in permanent hypoparathyroidism:<br /></br>1. Maintain serum Ca²⁺ low-normal (8.0–8.5 mg/dL).<br /></br>2. Avoid hypercalciuria to protect kidneys.<br /></br>Therapy:<br /></br>• Oral calcium salts (e.g., calcium carbonate).<br /></br>• Calcitriol (1,25-dihydroxy-vitamin D₃).<br /></br>• If hypercalciuria develops → add a thiazide diuretic ± reduce calcium dose; monitor 24-hr urine calcium periodically.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“PTH keeps Ca in Blood; absent PTH, Ca goes to Pee.”<br /></br>Visual: think of PTH as a “kidney gatekeeper.” When the gatekeeper is gone, calcitriol dumps extra calcium into a leaky kidney filter → overflowing bucket (urine).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Serum calcium concentration – LOW or low-normal, not elevated; symptoms prove it.<br /></br>b. Serum magnesium concentration – Usually normal; hypomagnesemia causes low PTH, but patient is already hypoparathyroid. No reason for Mg²⁺ to rise.<br /></br>c. Serum parathyroid hormone concentration – Destroyed parathyroids → low, not high.<br /></br>d. Serum PTH-related peptide – Elevated with malignancy (e.g., squamous cell carcinoma), not in surgical hypoparathyroidism.<br /></br>f. Urine potassium excretion – Not directly altered by calcium/vitamin D therapy or PTH loss.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom line: In hypoparathyroid patients maintained on calcium + calcitriol, the kidney cannot retain the extra absorbed calcium, so urinary calcium excretion rises.</p>
189. A previously healthy 27-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a rash over her chest and back for 5 days. She has a 10-day history of pain with urination, urinary frequency, and nocturia two to three times nightly. She took her father's trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole for 7 days, and her urinary symptoms improved. She currently takes no medications. She does not smoke cigarettes. Her temperature is 37.5°C (99.5°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination of the skin shows an erythematous, maculopapular rash over the trunk. There are no oral lesions. There is no splenomegaly.
