Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12.8 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 3000/mm3</br>Segmented neutrophils 15%</br>Eosinophils 5%</br>Basophils 1%</br>Lymphocytes 54%</br>Monocytes 25%</br>Platelet count 200,000/mm3</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Repeat examination in 1 week</br>b. Granulocyte colony–stimulating factor therapy</br>c. Prednisone therapy</br>d. Vitamin B12 (cyanocobalamin) supplementation</br>e. Skin biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Repeat examination in 1 week  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recent medication exposure: 7-day course of trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole (TMP-SMX), a classic culprit for idiosyncratic drug-induced neutropenia and morbilliform (“maculopapular”) drug rash.<br /></br>• Rash description: diffuse, erythematous, maculopapular on trunk without mucosal involvement → typical simple drug eruption, not Stevens-Johnson syndrome.<br /></br>• CBC: leukopenia 3000/mm³ with marked neutropenia (15% of 3000 = ANC ≈ 450/mm³). Platelets and hemoglobin normal → isolated neutropenia.<br /></br>• Vitals: Afebrile to low-grade 37.5 °C, no hemodynamic instability, no localizing signs of infection.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives (splenomegaly, oral ulcers) reduce concern for marrow infiltration, aplastic anemia, or SLE.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1. Identify adverse-drug reaction pattern (rash + neutropenia after TMP-SMX).<br /></br>Step 2. Remove/withhold offending agent (already stopped).<br /></br>Step 3. Assess severity:<br /></br>  – ANC &gt; 500 but &lt; 1500 = mild/moderate; ANC &lt; 500 = severe. Her ANC ≈ 450 is on the low end, but she is clinically well and improving; most drug-induced neutropenia reverses within 1–2 weeks once drug is stopped.<br /></br>Step 4. Management: observe &amp; repeat CBC in ≈ 1 week to document recovery. Escalate (isolation, G-CSF, IV antibiotics) only if fever ≥ 38 °C or evidence of infection persists/worsens.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Core Concept<br /></br>Simple morbilliform drug eruption with isolated, uncomplicated neutropenia after a short TMP-SMX course usually resolves spontaneously once the medication is discontinued. The first step is watchful waiting with repeat counts, not aggressive therapy.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>TMP-SMX can trigger an idiosyncratic immune-mediated destruction of mature neutrophils (“agranulocytosis”). Bone marrow production is typically intact, so recovery is rapid (3–7 days) once the drug is cleared.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Immediate: stop TMP-SMX (already done).<br /></br>• Follow-up: CBC in 4–7 days to confirm rising ANC; counsel patient to return for ≥ 38 °C fever or infectious symptoms.<br /></br>• No additional diagnostics (biopsy, B12 level) are required unless counts fail to recover.<br /></br>• G-CSF reserved for chemotherapy-related or life-threatening neutropenia, not for mild, transient drug reactions in a stable outpatient.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Sulfa SINKS the segs”<br /></br>Sulfa drug → Skin rash + Isolated Neutrophil Kollapse (drop) → Stop drug &amp; re-check CBC.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Granulocyte colony-stimulating factor therapy – Indicated for profound neutropenia from marrow suppression (e.g., chemotherapy) or septic/unstable patients. This patient is stable and expected to recover spontaneously, so risks/costs outweigh benefits.<br /></br>c. Prednisone therapy – Would be used for severe hypersensitivity (e.g., SJS/TEN) or autoimmune neutropenia. No mucosal lesions or systemic hypersensitivity here. Steroids may worsen infection risk.<br /></br>d. Vitamin B12 supplementation – B12 deficiency causes macrocytic anemia ± mild leukopenia; CBC would show ↑ MCV, not isolated neutropenia; no neurologic signs described.<br /></br>e. Skin biopsy – Indicated for vasculitis, SJS/TEN, or unclear diagnosis. Classic morbilliform drug exanthem is a clinical diagnosis; biopsy wouldn’t change management.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Uncomplicated drug-induced neutropenia with a benign drug rash in a hemodynamically stable patient is managed expectantly—stop the drug and repeat the CBC in about one week.</p>
190. A 57-year-old woman who was admitted to the hospital because of pneumonia has had an increase in her serum creatinine concentration from 1 mg/dL to 2.4 mg/dL during the past 3 days. On admission 7 days ago, she had severe shortness of breath and hypotension; she was intubated and mechanically ventilated for 2 days. Six days ago, a CT scan of the chest with contrast showed pneumonia at both lung bases. She also has type 2 diabetes mellitus. Current medications are ceftriaxone, azithromycin, albuterol, insulin, and lisinopril. She is now breathing 3 L/min of oxygen via nasal cannula and has mild shortness of breath. Her blood pressure has been stable for 6 days. Her urine output has been 1 L/24 h during the past 2 days. Her pulse is 70/min, and blood pressure is 150/90 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on 3 L/min of oxygen via nasal cannula shows an oxygen saturation of 94%. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. Urinalysis shows:  </br>Protein 1+  </br>RBC 10/hpf  </br>WBC 9/hpf  </br>Casts none  </br>A Wright stain of urine shows eosinophils. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Add acetylcysteine to the regimen  </br>b. Add nifedipine to the regimen  </br>c. Add prednisone to the regimen  </br>d. Administer a 1-L bolus of 0.9% saline  </br>e. Discontinue ceftriaxone  </br>f. No intervention is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Discontinue ceftriaxone  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Rising creatinine: 1.0 → 2.4 mg/dL over 3 days = acute kidney injury (AKI).<br /></br>• Urine findings: mild proteinuria, 10 RBC/hpf, 9 WBC/hpf, and—crucially—eosinophils on Wright stain. Eosinophiluria is the classic clue for acute interstitial nephritis (AIN).<br /></br>• Time course: Renal function worsened 5–7 days after starting a β-lactam (ceftriaxone) → typical latency for drug-induced AIN.<br /></br>• Hemodynamics: BP 150/90 mm Hg, good urine output (≈40 mL/hr), no hypotension → rules out pre-renal azotemia.<br /></br>• Other nephrotoxin on history: IV contrast was given 6 days ago, but contrast‐induced nephropathy peaks at 3–5 days and lacks eosinophiluria.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: recent β-lactam, eosinophils in urine, subacute AKI.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no gross hematuria, no systemic vasculitis signs, no prolonged hypotension.  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Patient on a new drug + AKI + eosinophiluria = drug-induced AIN.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify renal syndrome: Urine WBCs + eosinophils → AIN.<br /></br>Step 2 – Determine culprit: Among current meds, ceftriaxone (3rd-gen cephalosporin) is a well-known cause.<br /></br>Step 3 – Management priority: Remove the offending agent; kidney damage is often reversible once the drug is stopped.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In suspected drug-induced AIN, the very first step is discontinuation of the offending drug. Steroids can be considered later if renal function fails to improve.  </p></br><p>Documentation note: “Suspect ceftriaxone-induced acute interstitial nephritis—plan to discontinue ceftriaxone and monitor renal function daily.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Etiology: Hypersensitivity reaction (often type IV) in renal interstitium triggered by drugs (β-lactams, sulfonamides, NSAIDs, PPIs, rifampin, etc.).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Drug acts as hapten → T-cell–mediated inflammation → interstitial edema &amp; tubular cell damage → impaired creatinine clearance.<br /></br>• Typical latency: 3–10 days after first exposure (quicker on re-exposure).<br /></br>• Classic triad (rarely all present): fever, rash, eosinophilia/eosinophiluria.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>1. Stop the offending drug (recovery in days–weeks).<br /></br>2. Supportive care, monitor electrolytes &amp; creatinine.<br /></br>Second-line:<br /></br>• If no improvement in creatinine within 3–5 days or severe initial injury: short prednisone taper (e.g., 1 mg/kg/day × 1–2 weeks).<br /></br>Diagnostic adjuncts: Renal biopsy shows interstitial infiltrates with eosinophils (rarely needed when classic urine findings + drug exposure are present).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“EoSINophils in the urine = Eosinophils Signal Interstitial Nephritis.”<br /></br>“STOP the offending drug before the kidney STOPS.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Add acetylcysteine: Only useful as prophylaxis for contrast nephropathy, given before/soon after contrast. Contrast-induced AKI lacks eosinophiluria and peaks sooner.<br /></br>b. Add nifedipine: No hypertensive emergency or proteinuric kidney disease requiring calcium-channel blockade.<br /></br>c. Add prednisone: May be used later, but discontinuing the causative drug is always the first step.<br /></br>d. 1-L normal saline bolus: Helpful in pre-renal azotemia or contrast prophylaxis, but patient is normovolemic/hypertensive.<br /></br>f. No intervention: Renal function is deteriorating; action is required—continue drug would prolong injury.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>Drug-induced AIN = new drug + AKI + eosinophiluria → FIRST remove the offending agent.</p>
191. A 40-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 1-day history of nausea and vomiting. He has not had fever, chills, abdominal pain, or diarrhea. He says taking a hot shower alleviates his symptoms. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He says he occasionally uses illicit drugs but refuses to provide any additional information. His temperature is 36.7°C (98°F), pulse is 100/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 130/90 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities. Intoxication from which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's current condition?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Cannabis</br>b. Cocaine</br>c. Heroin</br>d. LSD</br>e. Methamphetamine</br>f. PCP (phencyclidine)</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Cannabis (chronic use → cannabinoid-hyperemesis syndrome)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 1-day history of intractable nausea &amp; vomiting → points toward a toxic/metabolic etiology rather than infection (no fever, chills, abdominal pain, diarrhea).<br /></br>• “Taking a hot shower alleviates his symptoms” → classic buzz-phrase for cannabinoid-hyperemesis syndrome (CHS). Patients discover compulsive hot bathing because warm water activates cutaneous TRPV1 receptors, modulating the same pathway that Δ⁹-THC chronically dysregulates in the area postrema.<br /></br>• Occasional illicit drug use + refusal to elaborate → NBME flag that the causative agent is a recreational drug the test taker must identify.<br /></br>No abnormal vitals (aside from mild tachycardia) or exam findings = nothing else in the stem to suggest sympathomimetic, opioid, or hallucinogen intoxication.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Acute vomiting in a young adult → consider gastroenteritis, pregnancy, intracranial process, medication/drug effect.<br /></br>2. Absence of GI infectious signs, and presence of a unique relieving factor (hot shower) drastically narrows to CHS.<br /></br>3. CHS requires chronic—often daily—cannabis use; history purposely vague but “occasional illicit drugs” is enough.<br /></br>Core takeaway: On exams, refractory vomiting relieved by hot baths = cannabinoid hyperemesis → think chronic marijuana.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl (real life): Must ask specifically about frequency/duration of cannabis use, document patient education on cessation, and consider capsaicin cream or anti-dopaminergic agents if symptomatic.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic exposure to THC down-regulates CB1 receptors in the hypothalamus and GI tract → paradoxical switch from anti-emetic (acute) to pro-emetic (chronic).<br /></br>• TRPV1 (vanilloid) receptors in the skin &amp; splanchnic circulation are stimulated by heat; warm bathing temporarily alleviates emesis by modulating the emetic pathway.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate: IV fluids, antiemetics (haloperidol, ondansetron), consider topical capsaicin.<br /></br>Definitive: Complete cessation of cannabis—symptoms resolve within days, relapse with reuse.<br /></br>Diagnostics: Mostly clinical; labs/imaging only to rule out other causes if red flags present (electrolytes, pregnancy test, CT head if neuro signs, etc.).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Hot Pot Syndrome”<br /></br>HOT showers + POT (marijuana) = cannabinoid hyperemesis.<br /></br>Visualize someone standing under steaming water hugging a cannabis leaf.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>b. Cocaine – Sympathomimetic toxidrome (severe hypertension, diaphoresis, chest pain, mydriasis); does NOT improve with hot showers; vomiting is uncommon.  </p></br><p>c. Heroin (opioid) – Presents with miosis, respiratory depression, constipation (not vomiting), altered mental status; warm showers provide no relief.  </p></br><p>d. LSD – Causes visual hallucinations, anxiety, tachycardia, but not repetitive vomiting relieved by heat.  </p></br><p>e. Methamphetamine – Long-acting sympathomimetic: agitation, hypertension, psychosis, bruxism, skin picking. Nausea is minor and unlinked to hot water.  </p></br><p>f. PCP – Vertical/horizontal nystagmus, violent behavior, analgesia, hypertension. Again, no relation to hot bathing or cyclical emesis.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing acute cannabis intoxication (mild euphoria, tachycardia, conjunctival injection) with chronic CHS—the latter is a withdrawal-type paradoxical effect seen only after prolonged heavy use.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Recurrent, severe vomiting that is transiently relieved by hot showers in a habitual cannabis user = cannabinoid hyperemesis syndrome → implicates chronic marijuana (cannabis) exposure.</p>
192. A 62-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 4-month history of a painless mass in her left breast; the mass has not changed in size. Ten years ago, she had a total abdominal hysterectomy and bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy. She has hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide. Her maternal aunt was diagnosed with breast cancer at the age of 55 years. The patient's vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a 2.5-cm hard, immobile mass in the upper outer quadrant of the left breast. Mammography and fine-needle aspiration biopsy specimen show no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Follow-up examination in 3 months</br>b. Repeat mammography in 6 months</br>c. MRI of the left breast</br>d. Tamoxifen therapy</br>e. Excisional biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Excisional biopsy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis (key clues)<br /></br>• 62-year-old woman → post-menopausal age, higher pre-test probability of malignancy.<br /></br>• “4-month history” and “painless, hard, immobile 2.5-cm mass” in the upper-outer quadrant → classic red flags for an invasive breast carcinoma.<br /></br>• Maternal aunt with breast cancer at 55 y → modestly ↑ familial risk.<br /></br>• Mammography AND fine-needle aspiration (FNA) are negative → discordance between highly suspicious clinical finding and benign/indeterminate studies.<br /></br>• No mention of cystic features, infection, trauma, or hormonal fluctuation → reinforces concern for a true solid neoplastic lesion.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: firm, fixed mass, age &gt;50, family history.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no pain, no inflammatory skin changes, no cystic fluctuation, prior oophorectomy (so no estrogen source from ovaries—still, breast Ca risk persists).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Any new palpable breast mass in a woman &gt;30 y is malignant until proven otherwise.<br /></br>Step 2: Initial work-up = diagnostic mammogram ± ultrasound → already done.<br /></br>Step 3: Tissue sampling → FNA was performed but is nondiagnostic / discordant.<br /></br>Step 4: When clinical suspicion remains high despite negative imaging or cytology, obtain a larger tissue sample: core-needle biopsy or excisional biopsy (aka open surgical biopsy). NBME frequently labels the definitive option “excisional biopsy.”</p></br><p>Core concept / takeaway: Imaging or FNA that does not match the physical exam requires escalation to an excisional (open) biopsy to avoid a missed cancer.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: note the discordant results, informed consent discussion regarding open biopsy, and the plan for surgical pathology follow-up.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Invasive ductal carcinoma (IDC) is the most common breast cancer; presents as a firm, immobile mass.<br /></br>• FNA yields only cells (cytology) and can miss the cancer because of sampling error or inability to assess tissue architecture.<br /></br>• Excisional biopsy provides the entire lesion with margins, allowing histology, receptor testing (ER/PR/HER2), and staging information.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Algorithm (high-yield)<br /></br>1. Palpable mass age &gt;30 y → Diagnostic mammogram ± US<br /></br>2. If imaging is suspicious (BI-RADS 4/5) → Core needle biopsy<br /></br>3. If imaging benign but exam suspicious OR biopsy nondiagnostic → Excisional biopsy<br /></br>4. Once malignancy confirmed → receptor testing then surgery ± radiation ± systemic therapy (e.g., tamoxifen for ER+ tumors)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Feel hard, act hard.”<br /></br>If palpation screams cancer but pictures/aspirate whisper benign → “cut it out” (open biopsy).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Incorrect  </p></br><p>a. Follow-up exam in 3 months<br /></br>– Delays diagnosis; contraindicated when a cancer cannot be confidently ruled out. Surveillance is only for clearly benign findings (e.g., simple cyst).</p></br><p>b. Repeat mammography in 6 months<br /></br>– More imaging of the same modality rarely adds new information and still misses lobular cancers; inappropriate in the face of clinical-pathologic discordance.</p></br><p>c. MRI of the left breast<br /></br>– MRI can map extent of known cancer or screen very high-risk patients (BRCA carriers) but is not the next step when tissue diagnosis is lacking. A negative MRI would not exclude malignancy.</p></br><p>d. Tamoxifen therapy<br /></br>– Adjuvant endocrine therapy is started only after histologic confirmation of an ER-positive tumor. Empiric tamoxifen without a diagnosis is inappropriate.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A suspicious palpable breast mass with negative mammogram and nondiagnostic FNA = discordant findings → proceed directly to excisional (open) biopsy to establish a definitive diagnosis and avoid a missed breast carcinoma.</p>
193. A 2-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of bloody drainage from her right ear that the mother first noticed last night when the patient woke up screaming and holding her right ear. The pain improved after the mother cleaned the ear with a cotton swab and gave the patient ibuprofen, but the mother noted dried, crusted blood on the patient's right ear lobe and pillow when she awoke this morning. The patient also has a 3-day history of cough, congestion, and runny nose. She has no history of serious illness and receives no routine medications. She is at the 25th percentile for height and weight and 50th percentile for BMI. Vital signs are within normal limits. The right tympanic membrane cannot be visualized because of seropurulent fluid in the right external auditory canal. The remainder of the examination, including examination of the left tympanic membrane, discloses no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's ear drainage?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Basilar skull fracture</br>b. Mastoiditis</br>c. Otitis externa</br>d. Traumatic injury to the external auditory canal</br>e. Tympanic membrane perforation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>e. Tympanic membrane perforation</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (Pertinent Positives &amp; Negatives)</br>• 2-year-old with 3-day viral URI (cough, congestion, rhinorrhea) → classic predisposing trigger for acute otitis media (AOM).<br /></br>• Sudden awakening at night “screaming and holding her right ear” = acute, severe otalgia.<br /></br>• Pain improved after bloody drainage noted on cotton swab and pillow → relief of pain after drainage is the classic history of spontaneous perforation of an inflamed tympanic membrane.<br /></br>• Seropurulent fluid now filling the external auditory canal prevents visualization of the TM. Purulent ± bloody otorrhea after pain relief strongly points to a ruptured TM due to AOM.<br /></br>• No fever, mastoid tenderness, or neurologic/cranial-base signs are mentioned, helping rule out mastoiditis or basilar skull fracture.<br /></br>• Age, URI prodrome, pain-relief pattern, and appearance of discharge together create the “signature” pattern for TM perforation secondary to AOM.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning &amp; Take-Home Message</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Common pediatric scenario: viral URI ⇒ eustachian tube edema ⇒ AOM.<br /></br>2. A tense, bulging TM can perforate, releasing purulent effusion mixed with blood.<br /></br>3. Key diagnostic clue: dramatic decrease in ear pain once the discharge appears.<br /></br>4. Seropurulent otorrhea that obscures the membrane clinches the diagnosis.</p></br><p>Core concept: In a young child with recent URI, acute ear pain that suddenly resolves with bloody-purulent drainage is almost always a tympanic membrane perforation from AOM.</p></br><p>Documentation points if this were a real patient: onset/time course of ear pain, character of drainage, preceding URI symptoms, degree of pain relief, hearing changes, immunization status, otoscopic findings in both ears, and plan for antibiotic therapy &amp; follow-up.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Viral URI → mucosal inflammation → obstruction of eustachian tube → negative middle-ear pressure &amp; fluid accumulation → bacterial superinfection (S. pneumoniae, H. influenzae, Moraxella catarrhalis).<br /></br>• Increased middle-ear pressure stretches TM; if pressure exceeds tensile strength, the membrane tears, decompressing the middle ear and producing bloody, purulent otorrhea with symptomatic relief.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls</br>1. Oral antibiotics for AOM with perforation: first-line amoxicillin-clavulanate (high-dose amoxicillin if not recently received).<br /></br>2. Add short course of otic fluoroquinolone drops (eg, ofloxacin) if persistent otorrhea ≥48 h.<br /></br>3. Keep ear dry; no ototoxic topical agents (avoid neomycin/polymyxin B in open middle ear).<br /></br>4. Most perforations heal within 2–3 weeks; schedule follow-up otoscopy to confirm closure.<br /></br>5. Audiology referral if perforation persists &gt;6 weeks or if hearing loss suspected.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Mnemonic / Memory Aid</br>“POP and DROP”<br /></br>P = Pain from Otitis media<br /></br>OP = sudden Oozing Purulent/bloody fluid<br /></br>DROP = Dramatic Relief Once Perforated</p></br><p>When AOM “POPs,” the pain “DROPs.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Basilar skull fracture<br /></br>   • Causes hemotympanum or clear CSF otorrhea, usually after head trauma with raccoon eyes/Battle sign. No trauma here.</p></br><p>b. Mastoiditis<br /></br>   • Presents with fever, mastoid tenderness/erythema, ear protrusion, and persistent AOM. None of those findings are present.</p></br><p>c. Otitis externa (“swimmer’s ear”)<br /></br>   • Severe pain with tragus/pinna movement; pruritic watery discharge; no URI prodrome; TM usually normal. Not seen here.</p></br><p>d. Traumatic injury to the external auditory canal<br /></br>   • Can occur with cotton-tip applicators but produces scant blood without purulence and does not relieve otalgia. Here, purulent drainage &amp; pain relief point to middle-ear source.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Key Exam Pearl</br>On NBME/Step 2, remember that sudden cessation of ear pain plus bloody-purulent otorrhea in a toddler with recent URI virtually screams “acute otitis media with tympanic membrane rupture.”</p>
194. A 20-year-old man comes to the student health services on his college campus because of painful urination and scant urethral discharge for the past week. He says that he has had no fever, chills, or back pain. For the past 6 months, he has engaged in unprotected sexual activity with one partner. He has no history of sexually transmitted diseases or other disorders and takes no medications. He appears well. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 64/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 115/70 mm Hg. Examination shows no costovertebral angle tenderness, penile lesions, or expressible discharge. Urinalysis shows no RBCs, 2–5 WBC/hpf, and no casts; throat cultures are pending. Which of the following is the most appropriate management at this time?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Amoxicillin therapy</br>b. Ceftriaxone and azithromycin therapy</br>c. Metronidazole therapy</br>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy</br>e. No treatment until culture and sensitivity test results are known</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Ceftriaxone and azithromycin therapy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Young, sexually active man with dysuria + scant urethral discharge → classic presentation of urethritis.<br /></br>• Negligible pyuria on UA and absence of flank pain/CVA tenderness make cystitis or pyelonephritis unlikely.<br /></br>• Most common causes of male urethritis = Neisseria gonorrhoeae and Chlamydia trachomatis. Both share identical presentation; labs (culture/NAAT) are not back yet, so empiric coverage is required.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify syndrome = urethritis.<br /></br>2. Recall guideline-directed empiric therapy = dual coverage for N. gonorrhoeae (ceftriaxone IM) + C. trachomatis (azithromycin PO single dose or doxycycline x7 days).<br /></br>3. Initiate treatment at point of care; do not delay while awaiting cultures because untreated infection → ongoing transmission, complications (epididymitis, prostatitis, infertility).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Any sexually active patient with urethritis gets immediate dual therapy for GC/CT unless a specific organism has already been ruled in or out.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• N. gonorrhoeae: Gram-negative diplococcus; produces intense neutrophilic inflammation and purulent discharge but can be scant.<br /></br>• C. trachomatis: Intracellular bacterium; often causes minimal discharge and fewer WBCs on UA.<br /></br>• Coinfection rate ≈ 20–30 %, hence dual therapy even if rapid NAAT later returns positive for only one.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line empiric regimen:<br /></br>• Ceftriaxone 500 mg IM once (covers resistant N. gonorrhoeae).<br /></br>• Azithromycin 1 g PO once (covers C. trachomatis and may enhance gonococcal cure).<br /></br>Alternative chlamydial agent: doxycycline 100 mg PO BID ×7 days.<br /></br>Additional management: NAAT of urine/urethra; test &amp; treat sexual partner(s); counsel on barrier protection; screen for HIV, syphilis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Treat the GONO-CHLAM twins together: Rocephin (ceftriaxone) shot + A-Z pack (azithro) snack.”<br /></br>OR think “Dual for DU-ri-al” (dysuria → dual therapy).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amoxicillin therapy – Penicillins no longer reliably cover GC and have no activity against chlamydia; resistance is widespread.<br /></br>c. Metronidazole therapy – Used for Trichomonas vaginalis or anaerobic infections; trich urethritis in men is far less common and not treated empirically here.<br /></br>d. Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole therapy – Good for uncomplicated UTIs or prostatitis caused by GNRs, but ineffective against GC/CT.<br /></br>e. No treatment until culture results – Contraindicated; delays cure, increases transmission and complications.  </p></br><p>Key Documentation Points (real life)<br /></br>• Symptoms, sexual history, condom use.<br /></br>• Empiric GC/CT therapy given (drug, dose, route).<br /></br>• STI counseling and partner notification.<br /></br>• Labs ordered (NAAT GC/CT, HIV, syphilis, etc.).</p>
195. A 62-year-old woman comes to the physician for PPD skin testing prior to beginning volunteer work in a school. She has hypertension treated with hydrochlorothiazide and osteoarthritis treated with acetaminophen. She was born in the USA. She has had no sick contacts. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. A PPD skin test produces 6 mm of induration at 48 hours. Results of liver function tests are within the reference ranges. A chest x-ray shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Provide clearance to begin volunteer work</br>b. Repeat PPD skin testing to assess for booster phenomenon</br>c. Obtain culture of three induced sputum samples for acid-fast bacilli</br>d. Begin isoniazid and pyridoxine therapy</br>e. Begin isoniazid, rifampin, pyrazinamide, and ethambutol therapy</br>f. Begin isoniazid therapy only</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Provide clearance to begin volunteer work</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• 62-year-old, born in the USA, no symptoms and no TB risk factors (no foreign birth or travel, no exposure, not immunosuppressed, not a health-care or correctional facility worker).<br /></br>• PPD (tuberculin skin test) induration = 6 mm.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray and LFTs are normal.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Decide whether the PPD is “positive.”<br /></br> • ≥5 mm: only counts in the highest-risk groups (HIV, recent TB contact, fibrotic CXR changes, organ transplant, or immunosuppression ≥15 mg/day prednisone).<br /></br> • ≥10 mm: medium-risk groups (recent immigrants &lt;5 y, IVDU, residents/employees of high-risk congregate settings, health-care workers, chronic medical disease, children &lt;4 y, etc.).<br /></br> • ≥15 mm: everyone else (LOW-risk, like this patient).  </p></br><p>Her 6 mm induration is well below the 15 mm threshold → a negative test.</p></br><p>Step 2: Management of a negative baseline test in a low-risk individual = document the result and clear her for work. No repeat testing or treatment is indicated.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>The PPD (Mantoux) test detects a delayed (type IV) hypersensitivity reaction to TB antigens. Induration, not erythema, is measured at 48–72 h. Interpretation thresholds are risk-stratified to balance sensitivity and specificity.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• Latent TB infection (LTBI) requires chemoprophylaxis with isoniazid (INH ± rifapentine) only when the test is positive.<br /></br>• Four-drug therapy (INH, rifampin, pyrazinamide, ethambutol) is reserved for suspected or proven ACTIVE disease.<br /></br>• Sputum AFB smears/culture are ordered only if there are symptoms or abnormal CXR suggesting active pulmonary TB.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“5-10-15 Rule”:<br /></br> 5 mm = FIVE categories (HIV, recent contact, fibrotic CXR, organ transplant, immunosuppression).<br /></br> 10 mm = TEN ‘community’ or exposure risks (health-care, immigrants, IVDU, prisons, nursing homes, kids &lt;4, chronic disease, etc.).<br /></br> 15 mm = everyone else.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Repeat PPD (booster phenomenon): Two-step testing is done for employees who will undergo SERIAL testing (e.g., hospital staff) to avoid future false positives. A one-time volunteer in a school does not require the two-step protocol.</p></br><p>C. Three sputum AFB cultures: Indicated only if there is clinical or radiographic evidence of active pulmonary TB—none exists here.</p></br><p>D. Isoniazid + pyridoxine (INH prophylaxis): Appropriate for LTBI (positive test) or high-risk conversion—not for a negative 6 mm result.</p></br><p>E. Four-drug therapy: Used for ACTIVE TB (symptoms or CXR lesions), which she does not have.</p></br><p>F. Isoniazid only (without pyridoxine): Same rationale as D; therapy is not indicated and pyridoxine should accompany INH in adults anyway.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>For a tuberculin skin test, always apply the risk-based cutoffs (5/10/15 mm). Low-risk individuals need ≥15 mm to be positive; otherwise, no further evaluation or treatment is warranted.</p>
196. A 42-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of nausea, vomiting, and malaise. He has bipolar disorder, herpes simplex virus 2, and chronic shoulder pain secondary to a torn rotator cuff injury sustained 7 years ago. His medications are valproic acid, sertraline, acyclovir, hydrocodone-acetaminophen, and docusate. He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. His temperature is 37.2°C (99°F), pulse is 102/min, and blood pressure is 122/84 mm Hg. Physical examination shows dry mucous membranes. Abdominal examination shows tenderness to palpation. On mental status examination, he has a dysphoric mood and congruent affect. Laboratory studies show:</br>Leukocyte count    8000/mm3</br>Serum</br>AST    38 U/L</br>ALT    52 U/L</br>Amylase    465 U/L</br>Which of the following medications is the most likely cause of this patient's current symptoms?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Acyclovir</br>b. Docusate</br>c. Hydrocodone-acetaminophen</br>d. Sertraline</br>e. Valproic acid</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Valproic acid  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 42-year-old with 2 days of nausea, vomiting, epigastric abdominal pain → think “acute pancreatitis.”<br /></br>  – Serum amylase 465 U/L (&gt;3 × upper limit of normal) satisfies biochemical criterion for pancreatitis.<br /></br>  – No alcohol use, gallstone risk factors, hypertriglyceridemia, or recent trauma are mentioned, so look for a medication cause.<br /></br>  – Current drugs: valproic acid, sertraline, acyclovir, hydrocodone-acetaminophen, docusate.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – Normal LFTs (AST/ALT only mildly elevated) → less likely hepatotoxicity from acetaminophen or valproate-induced hepatitis.<br /></br>  – No fever/leukocytosis → favors drug-induced rather than infectious etiology.  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Epigastric pain + vomiting + ↑amylase/lipase in a patient on a classic pancreatitis-inducing drug” → valproic acid.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Diagnose pancreatitis: abdominal pain + amylase &gt;3 × normal.<br /></br>2. Establish etiology: rule out gallstones (no RUQ pain, no gallbladder history, normal LFTs), alcohol (denied), hypertriglyceridemia/hypercalcemia (no data), trauma (none).<br /></br>3. Scan medication list for offenders. High-yield drugs for pancreatitis = “GET SMASHED” mnemonic (Gallstones, Ethanol, Trauma, Steroids, Mumps/Malignancy, Autoimmune, Scorpion, Hyper-TG/calcemia, ERCP, Drugs). Under “Drugs,” valproic acid is one of the most commonly tested culprits.<br /></br>4. Choose valproic acid as the most likely cause.  </p></br><p>Take-away message: Valproic acid carries an FDA black-box warning for acute pancreatitis in addition to hepatotoxicity and teratogenicity. Always think of it when a patient on VPA presents with abdominal pain and elevated amylase/lipase.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Note onset of abdominal pain, associated vomiting, elevated amylase, drug list including valproic acid, and plan to discontinue the medication.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Valproic acid → idiosyncratic injury to pancreatic acinar cells. Exact mechanism unclear (free-radical injury, mitochondrial toxicity). Can occur at any dose, any duration. Mortality can be high if necrotizing.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: concomitant enzyme-inducing AEDs, pediatric age, but it can occur in adults.  </p></br><p>Clinical manifestations: abrupt epigastric pain radiating to back, nausea/vomiting, lipase &amp; amylase elevation, +/- systemic inflammatory response.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. Immediate discontinuation of valproic acid.<br /></br>2. Supportive care for pancreatitis:<br /></br>   • Aggressive IV crystalloid hydration (e.g., Ringer lactate)<br /></br>   • Pain control (IV opioids)<br /></br>   • NPO → advance diet as tolerated when pain subsides<br /></br>3. Monitor labs (lipase, CMP), vitals; consider CT abdomen only if diagnosis unclear or if patient fails to improve.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“VALPROATE ruins the PANCREATE.”<br /></br>or use the “Drugs” branch of “GET SMASHED”:<br /></br>Valproic acid, Azathioprine/6-MP, Loop &amp; thiazide diuretics, Pentamidine, Didanosine, Estrogens, Steroids, … (visualize pills smashing a pancreas).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acyclovir – Classically causes crystalline nephropathy/AKI, neurotoxicity; pancreatitis is extremely rare and not high-yield. Would expect rising creatinine rather than isolated amylase elevation.<br /></br>b. Docusate – Stool softener; no known pancreatic, hepatic, or renal toxicity.<br /></br>c. Hydrocodone-acetaminophen – Acetaminophen overdose causes hepatocellular injury (AST/ALT in the thousands), not pancreatitis. Hydrocodone can cause constipation but not pancreatic inflammation.<br /></br>d. Sertraline – Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors occasionally cause hyponatremia/SIADH, bleeding risk, sexual dysfunction; pancreatitis is not a recognized adverse effect.<br /></br>e. Valproic acid – Only option with a well-established, frequently tested association with acute pancreatitis, making it the correct choice.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-yield takeaway: Any patient on valproic acid who presents with abdominal pain and markedly elevated lipase/amylase → assume valproate-induced pancreatitis until proven otherwise.</p>
197. A 72-year-old woman comes to the office with her son for a follow-up examination. She was diagnosed with Parkinson disease 8 years ago. She says her health has declined substantially during the past 6 months. She feels weaker and has had increasing difficulty walking. She has stopped attending weekly luncheons with her friends. The patient's son says his mother sleeps poorly at night and naps frequently during the day. He has to encourage her to eat and take her medications. The patient's medications are carbidopa-levodopa and donepezil. She appears sad and is tearful as she discusses her difficulties. Examination shows a mild resting tremor of the hands and mild bradykinesia. When asked to stand and walk from a seated position, she moves slowly and pushes on the arms of the chair with her hands; she takes steps with no hesitation but walks slowly. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Add mirtazapine to the medication regimen</br>b. Add rasagiline to the medication regimen</br>c. Add zolpidem to the medication regimen</br>d. Increase the dosage of carbidopa-levodopa</br>e. Increase the dosage of donepezil</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Add mirtazapine to the medication regimen  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 72-y/o woman with long-standing Parkinson disease (PD) who over the last 6 mo has<br /></br>  – Loss of interest in former activities (stopped luncheons)<br /></br>  – Low energy, psychomotor slowing, social withdrawal<br /></br>  – Poor nighttime sleep with daytime somnolence<br /></br>  – Decreased appetite (son must “encourage her to eat”)<br /></br>  – Tearful affect and expresses sadness<br /></br>• Motor exam: only mild resting tremor &amp; mild bradykinesia; gait initiation is intact (no freezing). Motor burden is relatively stable and mild, so the recent functional decline is disproportionately non-motor → screen for a mood disorder.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No mention of hallucinations, cognitive decline, or severe “off” periods that would suggest changing her PD meds.</p></br><p>Pattern: Elderly PD patient + anhedonia + low mood + sleep/appetite changes = Major depressive disorder (a common non-motor complication of PD).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why mirtazapine?)<br /></br>1. Diagnose depression → treat with an antidepressant.<br /></br>2. Select one that also addresses her specific complaints:<br /></br>   • Poor appetite/weight loss → mirtazapine increases appetite (via H1 &amp; 5-HT2 blockade).<br /></br>   • Insomnia → mirtazapine is sedating at the low doses typically used in the elderly.<br /></br>3. Mirtazapine has minimal dopaminergic or anticholinergic activity, so it will not worsen her PD motor symptoms or cognitive status.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In PD, new functional decline is often due to treatable non-motor issues (depression, anxiety, sleep disorders). Targeted antidepressant therapy improves quality of life and overall functioning.</p></br><p>Documentation note: Record DSM-5 criteria met for major depressive disorder, PHQ-9 score, discussion of risks/benefits of antidepressant, and follow-up plan for efficacy &amp; adverse effects (eg, weight gain, sedation).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Depression in PD is multifactorial: degeneration of dopaminergic, serotonergic, and noradrenergic pathways; psychosocial impact of chronic disease.<br /></br>• Prevalence ≈40%. Untreated depression worsens disability more than motor severity alone.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• First-line antidepressants in PD: SSRIs (eg, sertraline), SNRIs (eg, venlafaxine), or mirtazapine; bupropion also reasonable if tremor is not prominent.<br /></br>• Start low, go slow. Monitor for serotonin syndrome if combined with MAO-B inhibitors.<br /></br>• Screen annually for mood disorders in all PD patients.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Mirth-azapine puts the MIRTH back, adds MAss, and lets you sleep”<br /></br>(Mirtazapine → mood, weight gain, sedation).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Rasagiline (MAO-B inhibitor)<br /></br>   – Used to improve motor fluctuations (“wearing-off”) or mild PD symptoms. Does not treat mood; may actually raise risk of serotonin syndrome when combined with other antidepressants.  </p></br><p>c. Zolpidem<br /></br>   – Sedative-hypnotic might aid sleep but ignores the underlying depression and increases risk of confusion, falls, and daytime somnolence in the elderly.  </p></br><p>d. Increase carbidopa-levodopa<br /></br>   – Appropriate for worsening bradykinesia/rigidity (“off” periods) or gait freezing. Her motor findings are stable and mild; increased dose will not address mood and may cause dyskinesias, hallucinations, orthostatic hypotension.  </p></br><p>e. Increase donepezil<br /></br>   – Cholinesterase inhibitor for dementia; patient shows no cognitive decline (no memory loss, executive dysfunction). Will not help depressive symptoms and may worsen bradykinesia.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a Parkinson patient whose recent decline is driven by depressive symptoms—low mood, anhedonia, poor sleep, and appetite loss—the most appropriate next pharmacologic step is to start an antidepressant. Mirtazapine is ideal because it treats depression while stimulating appetite and improving sleep, all without worsening motor symptoms.</p>
198. A 32-year-old primigravid woman at 10 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. She conceived after in vitro fertilization. Three years ago, a bicornuate uterus was noted on diagnostic laparoscopy for primary infertility. She is otherwise healthy, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall. She weighed 50 kg (110 lb) prior to her pregnancy; BMI was 19 kg/m2. She has had a 1-kg (2-lb) weight gain during her pregnancy. Her blood pressure is 110/70 mm Hg. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 10-week gestation. This patient is at greatest risk for which of the following pregnancy complications?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Placenta accreta</br>c. Placenta previa</br>d. Preterm delivery</br>e. Uterine rupture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Preterm delivery  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues:</br>• “Bicornuate uterus” – congenital Müllerian duct anomaly in which the uterine cavity is divided by a central myometrial septum.<br /></br>• “Primigravid … 10 weeks” – very early, uncomplicated pregnancy so far.<br /></br>• No other maternal comorbidities (normal BMI, normal BP, no substance use) → complications should stem from uterine anatomy, not maternal disease.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: known bicornuate uterus.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no placenta‐related risk factors (no prior uterine surgery, no placenta previa on prior US, no multiparity, no C-section scar), no trauma/hypertension/cocaine (abruption risk), no history of uterine surgery or classical C-section (rupture risk).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Müllerian fusion defects (bicornuate, septate, didelphys) leave the uterine cavity smaller and irregularly shaped → less space for growth, abnormal myometrial contractility, and cervical insufficiency. Core complication = inability to stay pregnant to term → recurrent second-trimester loss and spontaneous preterm labor. Therefore preterm delivery is the most likely obstetric complication.</p></br><p>“Take-home” concept: Congenital uterine anomalies are classically tested for their association with early pregnancy loss and preterm birth, not with placental implantation disorders.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: In the note you would flag this patient as high risk for cervical insufficiency and preterm labor; plan serial cervical-length ultrasounds ± cerclage if shortening occurs.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Embryology: failed lateral fusion of the Müllerian ducts → two symmetrical endometrial cavities sharing a cervix.<br /></br>• Obstetric consequence: asymmetric uterine forces + reduced volume → early cervical dilation, malpresentation, preterm contractions.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>1. First-trimester: baseline dating ultrasound (already planned).<br /></br>2. 16–24 wk: transvaginal cervical-length surveillance every 2 wk; consider prophylactic cerclage if length &lt;25 mm.<br /></br>3. 24–34 wk: antenatal corticosteroids (betamethasone) if preterm labor signs.<br /></br>4. Delivery planning: anticipate malpresentation; many need cesarean.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Bicornuate = Baby comes early.”<br /></br>Visual: picture a heart-shaped uterus squeezing the fetus out like toothpaste before 37 weeks.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae – Risks: maternal HTN, trauma, cocaine, smoking, prior abruption. None are present. Uterine anomaly does not increase abruption incidence.<br /></br>b. Placenta accreta – Risks: placenta previa overlying a uterine scar, prior C-section, uterine curettage, Asherman. Congenital anomalies ≠ risk.<br /></br>c. Placenta previa – Risks: multiparity, prior C-section, multiple gestation, advanced maternal age, smoking. Bicornuate uterus is not a known risk factor.<br /></br>e. Uterine rupture – Usually occurs during labor in a scarred uterus (classical C-section, myomectomy). A congenital bicornuate uterus without prior surgery is not prone to rupture.</p></br><p>Bottom Line: The malformed uterine cavity cannot maintain pregnancy to term, making spontaneous preterm delivery the most common and highest-yield complication tested.</p>
199. A 22-year-old man with alcohol use disorder comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of weakness in his right hand. His symptom began after drinking a bottle of whiskey. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He drinks six cans of beer daily. On examination of the right upper extremity, muscle strength is 2/5 in the supinator, wrist extensors, and finger extensors; strength is 5/5 in all other muscles. Sensation to pinprick is decreased over the dorsum of the right hand from the wrist to the base of the thumb and index finger. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Determination of erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>b. Electromyography and nerve conduction studies</br>c. MRI of the brachial plexus</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. MRI of the cervical spine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Electromyography (EMG) and nerve-conduction studies  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Drank a bottle of whiskey … weakness began after drinking” → likely passed out with prolonged pressure on the arm (“Saturday-night palsy”).<br /></br>  – Motor deficit limited to radial-nerve–innervated muscles: supinator, wrist extensors, finger extensors (2/5). All other upper-extremity muscles normal (5/5).<br /></br>  – Sensory loss confined to radial-nerve cutaneous field: dorsum of hand at thumb–index web space.<br /></br>  – No neck pain, no proximal arm weakness, no sensory level, no constitutional symptoms.  </p></br><p>These findings localise the lesion to the radial nerve in the spiral groove/forearm, most consistent with a compressive mononeuropathy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Localise the lesion (peripheral radial nerve).<br /></br>2. Decide what information you still need: extent (neurapraxia vs axonotmesis), prognosis, and need for possible surgical decompression.<br /></br>3. EMG/nerve-conduction studies are the tool that answers those questions and are ideally performed ≥3 weeks after injury—exactly the time point in this vignette.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For a focal peripheral neuropathy of uncertain severity present for ≥3 weeks, the next diagnostic step is electrodiagnostic testing (EMG + nerve-conduction studies).</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Radial nerve wraps around the humerus (spiral groove) and is vulnerable to compression when the arm is draped over a chair/bench during intoxication.<br /></br>• Compression → conduction block (neurapraxia) or axonal injury.<br /></br>• Motor: wrist drop, weak supination, finger extension.<br /></br>• Sensory: dorsal radial hand.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Initial management is conservative (cock-up wrist splint, PT).<br /></br>• EMG/NCS determine:<br /></br>  – Site of lesion (spiral groove vs posterior interosseous branch).<br /></br>  – Degree of axonal loss (prognosis: neurapraxia usually recovers in weeks; axonal injury may take months or require surgery).<br /></br>• Imaging (MRI) is reserved if electrodiagnostics suggest a more proximal or space-occupying lesion.</p></br><p>Memorisation Tips<br /></br>“Saturday-night palsy: Wrist Drop after Whiskey → Test with Wires (EMG/NCS).”<br /></br>(Wrist drop + Whiskey = radial nerve; Wires = electrodiagnostic wires.)</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR)<br /></br>   – Would be relevant for systemic inflammatory or vasculitic mononeuritis multiplex. Single-nerve compression without systemic signs makes ESR low yield.  </p></br><p>c. MRI of the brachial plexus<br /></br>   – Plexopathies cause involvement of multiple nerve roots (mixed motor/sensory deficits in several peripheral-nerve territories); not isolated radial pattern.  </p></br><p>d. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   – A cortical lesion would give upper-motor-neuron signs (spasticity, hyperreflexia) and would not produce a dermatomal/nerve-specific sensory deficit.  </p></br><p>e. MRI of the cervical spine<br /></br>   – Cervical radiculopathy would affect muscles innervated by the same root (e.g., C7) across multiple peripheral nerves and often produce neck pain. Vignette shows pure radial distribution.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping straight to imaging. Always localise the lesion first; if it is peripheral and focal, EMG/NCS is the highest-yield, least-invasive next step.</p>
200. One day after delivery of a healthy newborn at term, a 22-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 2, has fever. Pregnancy and delivery were uncomplicated. Her temperature is 39.4°C (103°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 125/70 mm Hg. Examination shows normal lochia rubra. The uterus is tender, and the fundus extends to the umbilicus. Her leukocyte count is 17,000/mm^3 with a shift to the left. While awaiting results of additional laboratory studies, which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Candida albicans</br>b. Escherichia coli</br>c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae</br>d. Staphylococcus aureus</br>e. Staphylococcus epidermidis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Escherichia coli</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>Post-partum day 1: High fevers that occur within the first 24-48 h after a vaginal or cesarean delivery should make you think “post-partum endometritis.”  </li></br><li>Uterine tenderness + fundus at the umbilicus: A normal fundal height for day 1, but focal tenderness localizes the infection to the uterus rather than lungs (atelectasis), breasts (mastitis), urinary tract, or incision.  </li></br><li>Normal lochia rubra: Rules out retained placental fragments (which would present with excessive or foul-smelling lochia).  </li></br><li>Marked leukocytosis with a left shift supports an acute bacterial infection.</li></br></ol></br><p>Pertinent negatives: No breast pain/swelling (mastitis), no incision erythema (wound infection), and no urinary symptoms (UTI).</p></br><p>Pattern: Fever + uterine tenderness in the immediate post-partum period = endometritis.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step 1: Identify syndrome → Post-partum endometritis.<br /></br>Step 2: Recall microbiology → Infection is polymicrobial, but the single most commonly isolated aerobic pathogen after vaginal delivery is Escherichia coli (anaerobes such as Bacteroides and Peptostreptococcus are frequent co-pathogens).<br /></br>Step 3: Immediate management → Start broad-spectrum IV antibiotics (classically clindamycin + gentamicin) without waiting for cultures.</p></br><p>Take-home: Early post-partum endometritis = think E. coli.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real life): “38+°C fever, uterine fundal tenderness, no incision erythema. Impression: early PP endometritis. Plan: blood &amp; endometrial cultures, start IV clindamycin/gentamicin.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Etiology: Ascending infection of the placental implantation site—the now-necrotic decidua is an excellent growth medium for bowel and vaginal flora.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Prolonged labor, multiple vaginal exams, PROM, internal fetal monitoring, retained membranes, and cesarean section (highest risk).<br /></br>• Bugs: Mixed aerobes/anaerobes. The dominant aerobic Gram-negative rod = E. coli; anaerobic king = Bacteroides.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics  </p></br><p>First-line empiric IV therapy<br /></br>• Clindamycin (good anaerobic &amp; gram-positive coverage) + Gentamicin (excellent gram-negative coverage, including E. coli).<br /></br>• Add ampicillin if enterococcus is a concern.<br /></br>Diagnostics<br /></br>• Blood and endometrial cultures (do not delay treatment).<br /></br>• Imaging only if the patient fails to improve (suspect retained products or abscess).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Endometritis begins with ‘E’ → think ‘E. coli’.”<br /></br>Or visualize: Day-1 mom holding her baby and a big red “E” on the uterus.</p></br><p>Post-partum vs. post-abortal:<br /></br>• Post-Partum → Escherichia &amp; anaerobEs (two big E’s).<br /></br>• Post-Abortal → Chlamydial (A for Abortion, C for Chlamydia).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Candida albicans – Causes fungal vaginitis/thrush, rarely causes high-fever uterine infections; no uterine tenderness pattern.  </p></br><p>c. Mycoplasma pneumoniae – Respiratory pathogen; would present with cough and atypical pneumonia, not postpartum uterine pain.  </p></br><p>d. Staphylococcus aureus – Classic for breast mastitis or incision/wound infections; would expect breast pain or erythematous incision, not a tender, boggy uterus.  </p></br><p>e. Staphylococcus epidermidis – Skin commensal; usually catheter/prosthetic hardware infections, not endometritis.</p></br><p>Key discriminator: Only E. coli matches an ascending, polymicrobial uterine infection immediately after delivery.</p>
